# 通玄真经注

**Annotations to the *Tongxuan Zhenjing***

**Text Title:** *Annotations to the Tongxuan Zhenjing*
Commentator: Xu Lingfu of the Tang (styled Moxizi), circa the eleventh year of the Yuanhe era. Twelve fascicles.
**Source Text:** *Daoist Canon of the Zhengtong Era*, *Dongshen* Section, *Yujue* Category.
**Collated Edition:** *Sibu Congkan*, Third Series—photographic reproduction of the Song dynasty printed edition held in the Tiedan Tongjian Tower of the Qu family of Changshu (hereafter referred to as the *Qu Edition*).

**Table of Contents**

Fascicle 2: Sincerity

Fascicle 12: Supreme Propriety

**Preface to the *Tongxuan Zhenjing***

The great Dao has languished for ages untold. Yet its faint ripples endure, for among the various masters, none fail to trace their lineage to the Dao and virtue, mending the ruptures of a hundred generations.

Wenzi was a man of the time of King Ping of Zhou. He composed a work in twelve fascicles. The *Records of the Grand Historian* states: "Wenzi is also called Jiran, the teacher of Fan Li. His surname was Xin, given name Yan, styled Wenzi, a native of Pu in Caiqiu. A descendant of the Duke of Jin, he twice journeyed abroad, and Fan Li followed and served him. He was also a disciple of Laozi."

King Ping asked Wenzi: "I have heard that you received the Dao from the Old Master. Though a worthy possesses the Dao, if he lives in an age of debauchery and chaos, relying on the authority of one man to transform a people long steeped in disorder—can this truly be done?"

Wenzi replied: "When the Dao and virtue correct the perverse to govern, and restore order from chaos, reviving sagely virtue so that all under Heaven is at peace, the key lies in one man. Thus, the accumulation of virtue brings kingship, while the accumulation of resentment leads to ruin. By this, Yao and Shun flourished, while Jie and Zhou perished."

King Ping trusted his words and employed him, and in that time, all under Heaven was well-governed. Yet whether a state stands secure or falls, its fate does not descend from Heaven—it rests solely on whether the ruler employs the worthy.

Thus, the sage, ever vigilant for the world, cherishes its people as an infant, seeking to bring benefit, remove harm, and secure their peace—not for any private gain. His work first recounts the signs of rise and fall among the sage-kings and hegemons, then traces the causes of the waxing and waning of the Dao, virtue, propriety, and righteousness. In all, it ascends to the profoundest mysteries and extends to the myriad things. Its purport is vast and abstruse, its language refined yet true. Therefore, for those who govern a state, even in an age of decadence and decline, it can return them to the simplicity of primordial purity. For those who cultivate themselves, even burdened by the weight of cares, it can restore them to the spontaneity of their inherent nature. How great indeed!

The gentleman cannot but carve out his heart to receive it.

In the eleventh generation of our Tang, the Emperor, with robes flowing and transformation spreading, harmonizes and nurtures all things, embracing the myriad states with gentle grace, uniting diverse customs under one rule. Thus, those in high office all exhaust their loyalty and devotion, while those dwelling in obscurity also find peace in their labors.

In the fourth year of the Yuanhe reign, I, Moxi, withdrew to the distant slopes of Mount Heng, where I built my hut before the Hua Gai peak. Eight years have since passed, during which I have diligently cultivated the winds of simplicity and unobtrusively savored the subtle purport of the Way. Though I remain unfit for silent reverence, I have nonetheless forced myself to compose this commentary—an endeavor as daunting as measuring the vastness of the Milky Way or gauging the depths of the boundless sea. Thus, I offer this preface.

*Preface by Master Moxi*

**The Scripture of Penetrating the Mystery, Scroll One**
*Commentary by Master Moxi*

**The Origin of the Dao**

Of all things esteemed, none surpasses man.
Yet failing to settle the mind’s restlessness and illuminate its clarity, or to sever the net of attachment at the crossroads of desire, man’s nature drifts with craving, his life dissolving into the flux of things—much to the sorrow of the perfected.
Thus, the origin of the Great Dao is set forth here at the head of these chapters, that one may trace the source to ascend the Way and, in every encounter, touch the real. How could this not be fitting?

Laozi said:
There is a thing, chaotically complete, profoundly still and ever-abiding—hence it is called “a thing.”
It shapes and nurtures the ten thousand kinds, hence it is called “chaotically complete.”
Born before heaven and earth, it stands foremost among all things.
Its image is formless, like the heavens’ height bearing great signs, while the Dao’s vastness knows no fixed shape.
It congeals in emptiness as the One, and as qi disperses, it unfolds into the ten thousand things.

Abysmally dark, profoundly still, utterly serene and indifferent—such is the nature of the Dao, subtle and unfathomable.
No sound is heard, for it transcends sound; no form is seen, for it transcends color. Yet I am compelled to name it, calling it the Dao.
Since it is neither sound nor color, it is nameless, wordless, beyond speech or description.
What is spoken here is not its true designation, hence it is called a “forced name.”

The Dao is lofty beyond reach, deep beyond measure.
Devoid of form or image, how could its height or depth be gauged?
It enfolds heaven and earth, bestowing without shape.
Its source flows ceaselessly like water, ever full yet never overflowing, ever drawn yet never depleted.
*Chuochuo*: the appearance of water welling forth.
When turbid, it gradually clarifies through stillness—so too does motion settle into quiet, and murkiness into clarity.
Its bounty is inexhaustible, unbound by time, its generosity extending beyond any single dawn or dusk.
Though manifest, it does not fill a grasp; though tangible, it eludes form.
Contracted, it can expand; obscure, it can illuminate; supple, it can be firm.
It embraces yin and exhales yang, revealing the three luminaries.
Spoken in obscurity, its brightness rivals the three celestial lights.
Spoken in softness, its sharpness cuts through metal and stone.
Thus, it can be yin or yang, soft or hard, great or small, short or long.
Turn toward it, and it abides; turn away, and it vanishes.
It is neither this nor that, its transformations boundless.
By it, mountains rise in height, abysses plumb their depths.
By it, beasts run, birds fly, unicorns swim, phoenixes soar, and the stars traverse their courses.
How could the loftiness of mountains, the profundity of abysses, the swimming of unicorns, the soaring of phoenixes, or the unerring movements of celestial bodies arise from anything but the mysterious resonance of perfect governance?

Through nonbeing, it attains being; through humility, it gains honor; through retreat, it takes the lead.
This means: by relinquishing life, one preserves the self; by humbling oneself, one is exalted; by yielding, one advances.
The nature of the Dao delights in modesty, and thus modesty brings benefit.

In ancient times, the Three Sovereigns grasped the thread of the Dao, standing at the center and roaming in spirit with transformation to govern the four directions—the Three Sovereigns being Fuxi, Shennong, and the Yellow Emperor.
In ruling the world, their spirit moved within, while their virtue and grace overflowed without.
Thus, they could emulate the heavens’ motion and the earth’s stillness, their wheel turning without cease, their waters flowing without end, accompanying all things from beginning to end.
Winds arose and clouds gathered, thunder resounded and rain descended, all responding without limit.
*The heavens move; the earth rests.*
This speaks of the sage who models the heavens’ activity and the earth’s tranquility, aligning with the beginnings and endings of the ten thousand things.
Issuing decrees and commands, thunder rolls and winds rise, clouds drift and rain falls, nurturing and sustaining all beings, responding to change without end.

Carved and polished, it returns to simplicity.
Cultivating truth and prudence in conduct—this is what is meant by “polishing.” Yet when one severs dependence and congeals in emptiness, one naturally reverts to simplicity.
Acting without acting, it harmonizes with life and death.
The Great Dao is without intent, allowing things to follow their nature—hence it is called “nonaction.”
Life cannot but live, for life is natural.
Death cannot but die, for death is natural—thus it harmonizes with life and death.
Speaking without speaking, it communicates virtue; tranquil and unassuming, it attains harmony.
This means: without words, it reaches virtue; without affectation, it achieves harmony.
Though the ten thousand things differ, they all thrive in accord with their nature.
It harmonizes yin and yang, regulates the four seasons, and adjusts the five phases.
Yin and yang harmonize the four seasons, which in turn regulate the five phases—this is the constant Way.
This does not mean the sage alters or creates anew, but rather that he humbly observes human affairs, emulates the heavens’ timing, and discerns the people’s sentiments, preventing wickedness and ushering in great peace.
It moistens grasses and trees, seeps into metal and stone.
Birds and beasts grow large, their fur lustrous; eggs do not spoil, and animal fetuses do not miscarry.
*Sound: dú.*
The Dao’s efficacy prevails, and all things fulfill their growth without harming one another.

Fathers know no grief of losing sons, elder brothers no day of weeping for younger brothers. Above, governance flows smoothly; below, none rebel. Children are not orphaned, nor do they die untimely. Women are not widowed, all secure in their bonds. Rainbows and miasmas vanish, evil qi conceals itself. Bandits and thieves cease their ways, and the world attains great harmony—all brought forth by the power of embodied virtue.

The blessings just named all arise from the sage’s nurturing influence. The constant Way of heaven generates and transforms without end, giving life yet possessing nothing, perfecting transformations yet ruling nothing. In "possessing nothing" lies the wondrous presence of being; in "ruling nothing" appears the true ruler. The ten thousand things depend on it to live, yet none know its virtue; they depend on it to die, yet none can resent it. Life is not mine to claim—whom should I thank? Death is not mine to inflict—whom should I blame? It gathers and stores yet grows no richer, distributes and bestows yet suffers no poverty. Hoarded, it never overflows; scattered, it never empties.

Vague and elusive, it defies representation; fleeting and indistinct, its use knows no limit. Profound and obscure, it responds to transformations without form. In the space between fleeting and indistinct, its responses are inexhaustible. At the threshold of the profound and obscure, its changes are boundless. Traced to its source, it seems to hold something; pursued further, it has no shape. Flowing and unobstructed, it never acts in vain. Its spiritual workings complete, its principles never respond falsely.

It coils and unfurls with hardness and softness, rises and falls with yin and yang, bending and stretching with the seasons. Laozi said: "The great man who embodies the Dao is serene, without thought; tranquil, without worry. Moving with the Dao, why trouble with thoughts and concerns?"

Taking heaven as his canopy, earth as his chariot, the four seasons as his horses, and yin and yang as his charioteer, he travels where there are no roads, roams without rest, and exits where there are no gates. With heaven as his canopy, nothing is left uncovered; with earth as his chariot, nothing is left uncarried. With the four seasons as his horses, nothing is left uncommanded; with yin and yang as his charioteer, nothing is left unprepared. The great man rides the uprightness of heaven and earth, harnesses the movements of yin and yang, traverses boundless realms, and roams the inexhaustible Dao—how magnificent is this!

Thus, swift yet unshaken, distant yet untiring, his four limbs unmoved, his clarity and insight undiminished, he illuminates all under heaven—grasping the essence of the Dao to observe the limitless. Holding fast to the Dao’s essence, he surveys the eight directions as if in his palm. The subtlety of perfect governance resides in the square inch of the heart. How could he exhaust himself racing near and far?

Therefore, the affairs of the world cannot be forced; those who force them invite failure. Push forward by following their natural course, and nothing will fail to flourish. The changes of the ten thousand things cannot be rescued; grasp their essence and return to it. Though their transformations are manifold, their essence can be precisely discerned.

Thus, the sage cultivates his root within and does not adorn his branches without. He rectifies his heart within and rejects extraneous arts without. He sharpens his spirit to attain inner clarity and stills his knowledge and perceptions to halt what is not the Dao. Thus, he is effortlessly inactive yet accomplishes everything, governs without governing yet nothing is left ungoverned. The so-called "non-action" does not precede the things themselves; "non-governance" does not alter the natural. "Nothing is left ungoverned" follows the mutual arising of things. When things are allowed to be as they may, then nothing is not as it should be. When governance follows what can be governed, then nothing is left ungoverned.

Laozi said: "He who holds the Dao to govern the people responds to matters as they arise, follows things as they move. The transformations of the ten thousand things all find their response; the changes of a hundred affairs all find their match." This means that when the sage governs the world, he stabilizes human affairs according to their convenience, and thus the people cannot but be transformed, and all things cannot but harmonize.

Thus, the Dao is empty and formless, level and easy, clear and still, soft and weak, pure and unadorned—these five are the forms and images of the Dao. Without possessing these five virtues, one cannot perceive the form of the formless or the image of the imageless.

Emptiness and formlessness are the dwelling of the Dao. "Dwelling" means residence. Levelness and ease are the simplicity of the Dao. "Simplicity" means essence. Clarity and stillness are the mirror of the Dao. "Mirror" means illumination. Softness and weakness are the function of the Dao. "Function" means penetration. Reversal is the constant of the Dao. The common use is action; the Dao’s use is non-action. Softness is the hardness of the Dao; weakness is the strength of the Dao. Accumulated softness becomes hardness; accumulated weakness becomes strength. Purity, simplicity, and unadornedness are the trunk of the Dao.

Emptiness means the center holds nothing; levelness means the heart bears no burdens. Desire and craving do not burden—this is the perfection of emptiness. Having no likes or dislikes—this is the perfection of levelness. Unchanging unity—this is the perfection of stillness. Unmixed with things—this is the perfection of purity. Free from sorrow and joy—this is the perfection of virtue. These resolve the five meanings.

In the governance of the perfected man, he discards his cleverness and does not adorn his wisdom. He erases his embellishments and preserves his simplicity. Following the Dao, he abandons cunning and fulfills the way of clarity and emptiness, casting off deluded and false wisdom. He emerges with the people from the public, without private motives. He restricts what he holds, reduces what he seeks, relinquishes his temptations and longings, removes his desires and avarice, and casts aside his thoughts and concerns.

Restricting what he holds leads to discernment; reducing what he seeks leads to attainment. When the heart attains unity, it masters the arts of the ten thousand things. Restriction enables subtle understanding; reduction enables the governance of the many. Thus, controlling the external with the internal, nothing is left undone. If the internal attains it, the external can be shepherded. The attainment of the internal—"internal" refers to the ruler in a state and the heart in a person. When the ruler is enlightened, the state is at peace; when the heart is upright, the body is governed. Thus, controlling the external with the internal, the world has no equal. Controlling the internal with the external leads to success or failure. Knowing both internal and external, no action fails.

The great man rides the uprightness of heaven and earth, harnesses the movements of yin and yang, traverses boundless realms, and roams the inexhaustible Dao—how could this not be magnificent?

The five viscera at peace, thoughts and reflections balanced, sinews and bones firm and strong, ears and eyes keen and bright—all arise from guarding the center. The great Dao is smooth and broad, not far from the self; those who seek it afar go forth only to return. To wade far and lose the ford, not knowing to seek it near within oneself—

Laozi said: The sage forgets governing others and attends to self-cultivation; values forgetting power and rank and attends to self-attainment. In self-attainment, the world attains me. Never has one whose own person is ungoverned been able to govern others, nor one in power who does not overbear others. Only in self-attainment does one not overbear. In self-attainment, what I love, others also love. With my feelings, I grasp theirs—thus it is called self-attainment. In self-attainment, the feelings of the world are all attained by me. Joy forgets wealth and nobility and resides in harmony; only one who does not overbear preserves harmony and joy amid wealth and nobility.

To know the greatness of the self and make the world small is near to the Dao. Attaining the Dao, though the self is slight, one may possess the world—thus the self is great. Losing the Dao, though the world is vast, there is nowhere to contain the self—thus the world is small.

Thus it is said: Utmost emptiness, profound stillness—all things arise together, and I observe their return. Things arise from emptiness and stillness, thus returning to the root is called stillness. Stillness is called the restoration of life, meaning the endless cycle of going and returning, where the myriad things never end.

The Dao shapes and nurtures the myriad things, beginning and ending without form, motionless and silent, vast and boundlessly obscure. The Dao is tranquil and vast, a great furnace molding clay, beginning in formlessness. Within it lies something, yet it ends without form. Who knows its limit? It resides in boundless obscurity, beyond the ken of gods and spirits.

Vast and deep, broad and great, it cannot be made external; minute and subtle, it cannot be made internal. Without walls or enclosure, the greatest cannot be sought outside, the smallest cannot be grasped within. It gives rise to the general name of being and non-being. Speak of non-being, and all categories teem forth; speak of being, and no shape or sign appears. This is the essence of non-action, the subtlety of existing things—the general name of the myriad things arises between being and non-being.

The true person embodies emptiness and non-being, simplicity and ease, purity and stillness, softness and weakness, unadulterated plainness, not mingling with things. This is the virtue and Dao of heaven and earth—thus they are called true persons. Their body aligns with emptiness and non-being, their virtue harmonizes with heaven and earth—thus they are true persons.

The true person knows the greatness of the self and makes the world small, values governing the self and disdains governing others. From the human perspective, the world seems vast; from the Dao’s perspective, the world is small. Viewed from afar, governing others seems base; viewed from near, governing the self is noble.

Not allowing things to disturb harmony, not allowing desires to disorder the feelings—if the heart is not moved by things, how can feelings be disordered by desires? They conceal their name and identity, drifting with the times, flowing with the俗, yet none know them—this is the utmost concealment. When the Dao prevails, they withdraw and remain silent; when the Dao is absent, they exert themselves and cultivate it. They act without acting, engage in affairs without affairs, know without knowing. They act without reliance, engage without pride, know without display.

Embracing the heavenly Dao, encompassing the heart of heaven, they exhale and inhale yin and yang, expel the old and take in the new. With yin, they close; with yang, they open. With hardness and softness, they contract and extend; with yin and yang, they bow and rise. Their heart is one with heaven, their body one with the Dao. Exhaling and inhaling follow principle, contraction and extension are fitting, movement and stillness have rhythm, bending and stretching accord with the seasons.

No joy, no sorrow, no delight, no anger—the myriad things are profoundly the same, without right or wrong. Sorrow and joy do not cling to the heart, delight and anger do not show on the face. Encountering things, they remain true—thus it is called profound sameness.

When the body is harmed by the ravages of cold, heat, dryness, and dampness, the form is exhausted and the spirit blocked. When the spirit is harmed by the afflictions of joy, anger, thought, and worry, the spirit is spent and the form remains in excess. Cold and heat assail from without, joy and anger arise within. As the spirit departs, though the remaining form persists—how can it endure long?

Thus the true person employs the heart-mind to restore nature, relying on spirit for mutual support, and so attains wholeness from beginning to end. Therefore, their sleep is dreamless, and their waking is carefree.

The true person knows how yin and yang harm the upright, discards partial and biased emotions, and nurtures a nature of tranquility and indifference—thus body and spirit sustain each other, and worry and dreams do not intrude.

When Confucius asked about the Dao, Laozi said: "Correct your form, unify your gaze, and the harmony of Heaven will arrive. When the form is unified and upright, the four limbs are all proper; when the heart-mind is level, no deviant influences oppose it; when perception is unified, all encounters are real. Cut off all deliberation, and the harmony of Heaven will come of itself.

"Gather in your knowledge, rectify your measure, and spirit will come to dwell. Virtue will become your countenance, the Dao will become your dwelling. Without other knowledge, abiding by constant measure—then spirit will respond without fail, virtue will embrace all, and the Dao will be present everywhere.

"Bright-eyed as a newly born calf, without seeking its former state—this is called concentrating qi to attain suppleness, seeking only nourishment from the mother, with no other concerns.

"Form like withered wood, heart-mind like dead ashes—unaware of form as form, heart-mind as heart-mind. Is it withered wood? Dead ashes? Truly, they know reality without distortion, thus they maintain a vast and unfettered state, with no heart-mind to scheme, luminous in all four directions—can they be without knowledge?

"This is called the formless form, the heart-mindless heart-mind, which cannot be described in appearance or sought in location. Vast and unbounded, it cannot be planned; measured, it attains its singularity in all four directions—can it be without knowledge? This is called true knowledge."

Laozi said: "Those who attend to life respond to change and act; change arises from the times, and those who know the times follow no fixed course. They must respond to what comes, adapting to the moment—what is prized is knowing the moment; how could there be a constant way?

"Thus, the Dao that can be spoken is not the constant Dao; the name that can be named is not the constant name. Responding to the times, how could there be a fixed method? Writing arises from words, and words emerge from wisdom—but the wise do not know, for it is not the constant Dao.

"By 'writing' is meant the *Odes*, *Documents*, *Rites*, and *Music*; by 'words' are meant the sayings of former kings and sages. All are like straw dogs used in sacrifice—not the constant Dao. The name that can be named is not the one who treasures books.

"Inscribing on bamboo and silk, engraving on metal and jade—this is the name that can be named. Yet once names arise, there is truth and falsehood. Thus, writing does not convey true names; it is not real.

"Much learning leads to exhaustion; it is better to guard the center. Much learning exhausts one’s strength; guarding the One requires no labor. Abandon learning and there is no worry; discard sagacity and renounce knowledge, and the people benefit a hundredfold. Cease what is not the Dao, remove ostentation and bias, know and rely on the One Origin—thus there is a hundredfold benefit.

"Human beings are tranquil at birth—this is Heaven’s nature. Stirred by things, they move—this is the desire of nature. When things arrive and one responds, this is the movement of wisdom. When wisdom engages with things, likes and dislikes arise; when likes and dislikes take shape, wisdom is externalized, and one cannot return to oneself—Heavenly principle is extinguished.

"Nature is tranquil, but desire moves; when things are sensed, harm follows. Wisdom gives rise to calamity, desire disorders the real; likes and dislikes are born, and gain and loss arise. Unable to reflect and return to true nature, one brings about self-destruction.

"Therefore, the sage does not exchange Heaven for man, externally transforming with things while inwardly not losing their true state. Thus, those who penetrate the Dao return to clarity and stillness; those who possess the Dao are clear and still. Those who exhaust themselves in things ultimately attain non-action. External things—hence, non-action.

"Nurture wisdom with tranquility, unite spirit with indifference—then one enters the gateless gate. Wisdom does not arise without stillness; spirit does not respond without quiescence. Response emerges from the gateless gate. One roams the gateless gate, following Heaven and wandering with the Dao—this means guarding emptiness and stillness. Those who follow others exchange with the vulgar—this means clinging to fame and power.

"Thus, the sage does not disturb Heaven with affairs, does not disorder the true state with desires, does not deliberate yet is fitting, does not speak yet is trusted—like the Heavenly Dao, without intention, like the four seasons, mysteriously attuned. Without thought, they attain; without action, they accomplish. Attainment is not through toiling thought; accomplishment is not through effort.

"Therefore, though they dwell above, the people do not find them burdensome; though they stand before others, none are harmed. The world turns to them, and the treacherous and perverse fear them—because they do not contend with the myriad things, none dare contend with them. Where utmost virtue extends, the treacherous decline and hide. The myriad people bear them without burden; none in the world dare contend with them."

Laozi said: "When people follow desire and lose their nature, their movements are never upright. With this, to govern a state is to bring chaos; to cultivate the self is to bring defilement. The harm of desire—destroying states and ruining lives—its response is like an echo. How can one not be warned?

"Thus, those who do not hear the Dao cannot return to their nature; those who do not understand things cannot attain clarity and stillness. Unclear about the principles of the Dao, unenlightened about the conditions of things, they cannot return to the origin and restore simplicity.

"The original nature of human beings is without depravity or defilement. Long immersed in things, it changes; changed and forgetting its root, it merges with what seems like its nature. The original human nature is utterly still and unaware; stirred by things, it moves—this is desire harming the real, decline obscuring the upright. The deluded do not awaken, taking this as their true nature. If they do not transform throughout their lives, how grievous is this?

"The nature of water desires clarity, but sand and stone defile it; the nature of human beings desires equilibrium, but cravings and desires harm it. Only the sage can forsake things and return to the self. The nature of water is originally clear—defilement lies in sand and stone. The nature of human beings is originally balanced—harm lies in cravings and desires. Only the sage can forsake things and return to the self.

"Therefore, the sage does not employ wisdom to enslave things, does not use desire to disturb harmony. Their joy is not exultant, their sorrow is not mournful. *Wū guàn qiē*—taking joy in joy, when joy reaches its extreme, sorrow follows."

Those who take sorrow as sorrow never forget their sorrow. Thus, they stand high yet do not peril, rest secure yet do not topple, preserving emptiness and purity—what fear have they of collapse or danger?

Therefore, when good counsel and sound plans are heard, even fools delight in them; when the sagely virtue and lofty conduct are praised, even the unworthy yearn for them. If fools still know to turn toward and admire such things, how much more the worthy and virtuous? Many delight in such words, yet few put them into practice; many admire such ways, yet few walk them. Though those who rejoice and yearn number in the tens of thousands, none advance in cultivation. Why is this? Because they are shackled by things and struck by custom, ensnared by gluttony for flavors and fettered by the shackles of fame and profit.

Thus it is said: "I practice non-action, and the people transform themselves; I remain without affairs, and the people enrich themselves; I love stillness, and the people rectify themselves; I am without desire, and the people return to simplicity." This makes clear that when the ruler models these four principles, governing from above, the people are transformed below.

Stillness and purity are the perfection of virtue; softness and weakness are the function of the Dao. Emptiness, tranquility, and joy are the ancestors of the ten thousand things. When these three are practiced, one merges into formlessness—formlessness is called "the One." The One is the mindless union with all under heaven. The One is non-being. It speaks of the formless moving through the world, pervading the ten thousand things without end.

Virtue is spread without exhaustion, employed without toil; no moisture can moisten it, no mind can weary it. It cannot be seen, cannot be heard. So subtle it transcends sound—how could it be heard or seen? From formlessness, form arises; from soundlessness, the five tones resound; from tastelessness, the five flavors take shape; from colorlessness, the five colors emerge. Thus, being arises from non-being, substance from emptiness.

The number of tones does not exceed five, yet the variations of the five tones cannot be fully heard; the number of flavors does not exceed five, yet the variations of the five flavors cannot be fully tasted; the number of colors does not exceed five, yet the variations of the five colors cannot be fully seen. Form, sound, color, and flavor all arise from nothing. Tracing them to their fundamental number, they do not exceed five. Yet from these five, endless transformations arise, losing their constancy. Pushed to excess and decadence, they surpass what sight and hearing can fathom, what constant observation can discern.

When the tone *gong* is established, the five tones take shape; when sweetness is established, the five flavors are fixed; when whiteness is established, the five colors are formed; when the One is established, the ten thousand things are born. All the above take the One as their foundation. Thus, things attain constancy through the One, and people attain transformation and brilliance through their master. The Dao unifies as One, and the ten thousand things flourish abundantly. Therefore, the principle of the One extends throughout the four seas without difference; the scrutiny of the One pervades heaven and earth.

Those who comprehend the One understand the root of heaven and earth’s creation and the nature of the ten thousand things. In its wholeness, it is thick and uncarved; outwardly, it appears foolish to others. When dispersed, it is vast and turbid, its traces obscured by custom. Though turbid, it gradually clears; though empty, it gradually fills. Turbid yet able to clear, empty yet able to fill. Vast and still like the great sea, boundless and without shore. Drifting like floating clouds, whither does it go? As if non-being yet being, as if perishing yet enduring. Neither non-being nor being, yet able to endure or vanish—this describes the one who embodies the Dao.

Laozi said: "The sum of the ten thousand things all passes through a single aperture; the root of a hundred affairs all emerges from a single gate." The ten thousand things and ten thousand affairs all issue from the gate of manifold subtleties. Thus, the sage follows a single track, never altering its constancy, never changing its regularity, aligning with the plumb line and following the carpenter’s square, bending to accommodate its constancy.

The sage follows the origin of the great Dao, examining the nature of the ten thousand things, not forcing land-dwellers into the depths or tree-nesters into caves—this is not altering their constancy. Each attaches to what brings ease, all benefiting their nature—this is bending to accommodate its constancy.

Joy and anger are the deviations of the Dao; sorrow and grief are the losses of virtue; likes and dislikes are the transgressions of the heart; cravings and desires are the burdens of life. These eight are what the great man does not dwell in. Great anger shatters *yin*; great joy topples *yang*; thin *qi* brings dimness; terror and fear induce madness; sorrow and grief scorch the heart, giving rise to accumulations of illness. If one can eliminate these five, they align with spirit illumination. These five are the utmost sincerity in cultivating the self.

People know to refine gold and smelt stone in pursuit of longevity, yet cannot moderate desires and pacify harmony to preserve their heavenly nature. Moreover, joy and anger arise recklessly—what use are medicinal stones? If one truly grasps this principle, they naturally align with spirit illumination. Spirit illumination is attained internally. Those who attain it internally have peaceful organs, balanced thoughts, keen hearing and sight, and strong sinews and bones.

Those who attain internally embrace emptiness and guard the One, their spirit at ease and thoughts serene—thus their ears and eyes are keen, their sinews and bones strong. Open and unobstructed, they do not contend with things; firm yet not exhausted, they employ softness without lack. Never excessive, never deficient. Desires do not exceed measure, and nothing they do fails to succeed.

Nothing in the world is softer or weaker than water, yet water embodies the Dao. This is a metaphor to convey principle, using water to clarify the Dao. Its breadth cannot be fathomed, its depth cannot be measured; its length extends without end, its reach pervades without limit. Its ebb and flow, increase and decrease surpass all reckoning. Above, it becomes heaven’s rain and dew; below, it becomes earth’s moisture and nourishment. The ten thousand things cannot but live, the hundred affairs cannot but succeed. It embraces all life without partiality, bestowing its blessings even upon the lowliest creatures without seeking repayment. It enriches all under heaven without exhaustion, bestows virtue upon the people without cost. Its course cannot be exhausted, its subtlety cannot be grasped. Struck, it is not wounded; pierced, it is not injured; severed, it is not cut; scorched, it is not burned. Supple and flowing, it cannot be scattered. It penetrates metal and stone, overcomes all under heaven. Abundant or scant, it allows all to take or give. It endows the ten thousand things without precedence or delay, without partiality or impartiality, vast and equal with heaven and earth—this is called utmost virtue.

That by which water fulfills its utmost virtue lies in its supple smoothness. Thus it is said: "The softest under heaven courses through the hardest under heaven, entering where there are no crevices." Water, foremost among the Five Phases, attains the One and thus the Dao succors all under heaven, its virtue harmonizing with the ten thousand kinds. Its benevolence extends to grasses and trees, its righteousness hardens like metal and stone, its trustworthiness aligns with the four seasons, its wisdom flows without end. Thus the soft cannot be severed, the hard cannot be broken; in motion it commands awe, in strength it knows no rival. Scattered, it becomes rain and dew; gathered, it forms springs and fountains. Used, it is never exhausted; bestowed, its reach is boundless. Defiled, it remains unstained; purified, it preserves its wholeness. How vast is the virtue of water, in harmony with the Dao!

That which is formless is the great ancestor of things; that which is soundless is the great progenitor of kinds. All things arise from the formless, the five tones emerge from the soundless. Thus the utmost void, though ungiving, gives birth—hence the void is the ancestor and progenitor of things. The Perfected Person, penetrating the numinous treasury, becomes one with the Creator, holding fast to dark virtue in the heart, transforming with spirit-like swiftness. Therefore, the Dao that is not a Dao—how vast and indistinct! Without issuing commands or decrees, it shifts customs and alters mores—this is the work of the heart’s conduct.

All things have their source yet alone return to their root; all affairs have their origin yet alone guard their gate. Thus one may exhaust the inexhaustible, reach the unreachable, illuminate things without dazzling, respond to echoes without knowing. Within, one attains the unity of the heart; without, one connects with the ten thousand existences. The Dao of submerging and floating transforms like a spirit, its responses boundless—who knows its limit?

Laozi said: "Those who attain the Dao have wills that are weak yet affairs that are strong, hearts that are empty yet responses that are fitting." A weak will is gentle, tranquil, and serene, concealed in non-acquisition, acting in incapacity, tranquil in non-action, moving without missing the moment—this is what it means to attain. The Dao of non-action follows what is fitting for things, moving in accord with the proper time. Thus the noble must take the lowly as its root, the high must take the low as its foundation. Dust gathers to form mountains, streams accumulate to become seas. Entrusting the small to encompass the great, residing within to govern without, acting with softness yet firmness—strength knows no defeat, no enemy can overpower. With an empty heart, one precedes things: though without force, one is strong, and wherever one goes, all is accomplished, none dare encroach or insult. Adapting to transformations and measuring the times, none can harm. Moving in tandem with the Dao, what thing could cause harm?

Those who desire firmness must guard it with softness; those who desire strength must preserve it with weakness. Accumulate softness and it becomes firm; accumulate weakness and it becomes strong. Observe what is accumulated, and you will know survival or ruin. Know firmness, know softness—then virtue is truly cultivated. Know survival, know ruin—then the self will surely flourish. Strength can overcome those weaker than oneself, but when it encounters its equal, it is checked. The strong cannot be overcome, the weak cannot be oppressed—this is the Dao of acting with softness. Softness can overcome those stronger than oneself—its power is immeasurable. Do not lightly scorn, for it may surpass you.

Thus when armies are strong, they perish; when wood is hard, it snaps; when leather is stiff, it splits. Those who rely on strength do not preserve their material whole. Teeth are harder than the tongue yet perish first—observing the principle of teeth and tongue, one may discern the Dao of firmness and softness. Thus the firm meets ruin, while the soft obtains wholeness. Therefore, softness and weakness are the pivot of life, while firmness and strength are the companions of death. Circumstances summon one another, and life and death may be verified.

The one who leads falls into exhaustion; the one who follows reaches the source. Holding to the latter ensures one is not subdued. Those who grasp the Dao to pair with change—whether first or last, each controls the other. How can one fail to master others when others cannot master them? This is to seize the mechanism of transformation, which none can comprehend. Preceding others invites no resistance; following others accords with what is fitting. Mastering both precedence and subsequence, acting and resting in non-action—this is grasping the Dao to pair with change.

The so-called "following" is to harmonize one’s measures with the proper time. When the time changes, there is no gap for even a breath. Preceding it is excessive; following it is insufficient. Affairs have their proper moments, things have their successes and failures, movements in perilous mechanisms allow no pause for a breath. It lies in harmonizing measures and awaiting the time, aiming for the mean without error.

The sun revolves, the moon orbits—time does not tarry with man. Thus the sage does not value a foot-long jade but treasures an inch of time. Time is hard to obtain yet easily lost, so the sage acts in accord with the time and establishes merit based on resources. The sage does not prize amassing treasures but values preserving the Dao. Cherishing the fleeting light of time, the rare encounter of opportunity—when acting to achieve success, one must not miss the moment.

Abide by the pure Dao, restrain oneself with feminine restraint, follow along and respond to change, always remaining behind and never ahead. Soft and weak in stillness, composed and settled in tranquility—great achievements know no firmness, none can contend with them. Thus embodying purity and stillness, holding to feminine weakness, one attacks the strong under heaven—does this not mean seizing the proper time without contention?

Laozi said: "When the heart harbors the mechanisms of cunning, even purity and whiteness are not unsullied." If scheming and cleverness are not forgotten, the dark Dao grows ever more distant. When numinous virtue is not complete within the self, how can one know what distant harm may befall? Near, one loses the self; far, one loses others.

When the heart forgets the desire to harm, even a starving tiger may be tailed—how much more so with people! When the desire to harm is forgotten within, though one treads upon a starving tiger’s tail or stands before a violent man, one will ultimately suffer no harm.

Those who embody the Dao are at ease yet never exhausted; those who attain the governance of the Dao never rest even in death. Those who rely on calculations labor without achievement. Those who exhaust numerological arts labor without benefit.

Ruling through harsh laws and punishments is not the work of emperors and kings; frequent use of the whip and rod is not the way to drive far. Excessive punishments breed resentment among the people; frequent whipping makes horses unruly. Excessive likes and dislikes invite calamity to follow—this is the principle of nature.

Thus, the laws of the former kings were not what they created but what they followed; their prohibitions and punishments were not what they enacted but what they upheld. They did not rely solely on execution and killing to seek the people’s welfare—expelling the wicked, meting small punishments to admonish great faults, which is the blessing of petty men.

Therefore, following brings greatness, while contriving brings pettiness; upholding brings stability, while acting brings ruin. As when Great Yu governed the waters: he followed the people’s plight, for their suffering could not be endured. He dredged rivers along the mountains, cut channels to guide the flow, rescuing the times and saving from peril, ensuring no harm befell them. How majestic was his success—this was the power of following. When Shang Yang of Qin created laws and altered standards, rewarding those who obeyed the present and punishing those who violated the statutes, in a single day he executed over seven hundred, turning the Wei River red. Later, he himself died by dismemberment—what he harmed was great, what he achieved was small, and what he upheld was not secure. Those who act bring ruin.

Relying on ears and eyes to hear and see wearies the mind yet brings no clarity; governing through cunning and calculation exhausts the heart yet yields no success. If one’s ears and eyes, mind and thoughts are spent, how can one speak of sufficiency? To hear without listening, to see without looking, to act without intention, to succeed without deliberation—this is what the perfected person values.

Relying on the talent of one person is insufficient to achieve perfect governance; the ability of one person cannot manage even three acres of land. If one overreaches, strength is dispersed and cannot suffice. Following the principles of the Dao and the natural order of heaven and earth, even the six directions are not too vast to harmonize. By adapting to what is fitting and measuring one’s strength, even the vastness of the six directions can be balanced, and the multitude of the ten thousand things can be thoroughly mastered.

Hearing is misled by praise and blame, confused by likes and dislikes. The eyes are seduced by colors and beauty; ritual and propriety are insufficient to restrain desire, but sincerity of heart can embrace what is distant. Words and rites cannot guard against transgression, but the heart alone can illuminate the subtle.

Thus, no weapon is sharper than the will, yet *Mo Ye* and *Po Ya* are inferior; no enemy is greater than yin and yang, yet war drums are trivial. The so-called great enemy leaves corpses without restraint; the middling enemy hides in the mountains; the petty enemy lurks among the people. The five weapons are but the dregs of the Dao. Yin and yang are the greatest enemies—this is the Dao. *Mo Ye*, *Po Ya*, drums, and gongs have form and sharpness, sound and might. The supreme Dao has no sharpness yet can sever, no might yet can subdue. Thus, though *Mo Ye* is sharp, the Dao is inferior to it; though drums retain their sound, they are trivial beside the Dao.

Yin and yang are the love and aversion of male and female. Wherever desire for profit arises, the heart follows emotion, and in the space between acceptance and rejection, there must be the peril of life and death. Thus, the great enemy hides in the breast, and the petty enemy lurks among the people. Therefore, the perfected person is cautious within and restrained without.

Thus it is said: when the people abound in cleverness and skill, strange things proliferate; when laws and decrees multiply, thieves and bandits increase. Abandoning one to take the other prevents heavenly calamity from arising. Governing a state with excessive laws brings chaos to the people—chaos is the omen of ruin. Governing the self with excessive rules wearies the body—weariness is the sign of decay. Abandoning that cleverness and those laws, embracing this clarity and stillness, heavenly calamity naturally ceases.

Thus, governing a state with cleverness is the state’s bane; not governing a state with cleverness is the state’s virtue. The perfected person sees cleverness as a bane; the worldly see it as virtue. Using it brings harm; not using it brings blessing.

The formless is great; the formed is small. The formless is many; the formed is few. The formless is strong; the formed is weak. The formless is solid; the formed is hollow. Where there is great and small, there is many and few; where there is few, form is small—this is the smallness of the formed. Where there is no great or small, there is no many or few. Where there is no few, there is no form—this is the greatness of the formless.

The great and small, many and few, strong and weak, solid and hollow—all arise from this. The formed is the completion of affairs; it originates from the formless, whose name cannot be known. Following things, it is named the completion of affairs. The formless is the beginning of creation; the completion of affairs is the making of vessels. The beginning is simplicity; simplicity scatters and becomes vessels.

The formed has sound; the formless has no sound. The formed is born of the formless—thus, the formless is the beginning of the formed. The Dao is not nothing; what is born is something. Through birth, one awakens to the Dao; when the body exists, there is nothingness. Thus, sound emerges without inquiry; form exists with a beginning.

Vastness and thickness have names—what has a name is prized for its wholeness. This speaks of the named as the name of the nameless, not seeking a name. If one seeks a name, how can wholeness be prized? Frugality and thinness have no name—what has no name is base and light. This speaks of the nameless not as the name of the nameless, but as one who cuts oneself off in ignorance, not knowing the name of the great Dao.

Abundance and wealth have names—what has a name is honored and cherished. Poverty and scarcity have no name—what has no name is lowly and despised. With the Dao, one is honored; without the Dao, one is disgraced. The male has a name—what has a name is manifest and bright. The female has no name—what has no name is hidden and subtle. If not for the use of the male, how could the name be whole? If not for embracing the female, how could the nameless be whole? Obscurity and clarity, concealment and display—all rest in my having or lacking, my abiding or stagnating.

Surplus has a name—what has a name is exalted and wise. Deficiency has no name—what has no name is tasked with the lowly. Not prizing one’s name is surplus; not exalting oneself yet being exalted is not wisdom. Not cultivating one’s name is deficiency; not lowering oneself yet being lowly is folly.

Merit brings a name; lack of merit brings no name. The Dao does not respond in vain. The named is born of the nameless—the nameless is the mother of the named. Thus, the Dao gives rise to being and non-being, difficulty and ease complete each other.

Being does not arise from being but is born of non-being. Difficulty does not arise from difficulty but is completed through ease. Knowing that being is insufficient, one must guard the mother to preserve the child.

Knowing that difficulty does not arise from difficulty, one must make the difficult through the easy.
Yet things do not move in isolation—matters depend on mutual support.
Thus the sage holds to the Dao in emptiness, stillness, subtlety, and wonder, thereby perfecting their virtue.
Forgetting the mechanism, illumination arises.
Therefore, where there is the Dao, there is virtue; where there is virtue, there is merit; where there is merit, there is a name; where there is a name, it returns once more to the Dao.
When Dao and virtue are cultivated, merit and name naturally follow.
What is possessed is not truly mine—it is possessed of itself.
Not knowing to whom it belongs, it returns to the Dao.
Merit and name endure long, and one remains free from blame throughout life.
Preserving merit and name wholly, one is naturally free from regret.
Kings and dukes possess merit and name; the orphaned and widowed possess no merit and name.
Thus the sage calls themselves orphaned and widowed, returning to their root.
Only the sage can establish merit without merit, a name without a name.
Though possessing such merit and name, they still style themselves orphaned and widowed—this is to guard the feminine, the yielding, and return to simplicity.

Merit is achieved yet not possessed.
Thus merit is used for benefit, and namelessness is employed for function.
Merit benefits things yet does not display itself; namelessness is constantly employed yet inexhaustible.
In ancient times, the people were simple and unlearned.
Though their words were established, they remained like children.
They knew not east from west, making no distinctions.
Their appearance did not depart from their nature, and heaven’s harmony followed.
Words did not exceed actions; actions proceeded without pretense.
Movement accorded with the Dao, and speech was unadorned—valuing substance.
Their clothing was warm yet without ornament, adding no embellishment.
Their weapons were blunt and without edge, not fashioning instruments of harm.
Their gait was halting and deliberate.
Their gaze was dim and unfocused, like that of an infant.
They drank from the well they dug, ate from the fields they tilled.
Beyond clothing and food, they sought nothing more.
They did not bestow charity nor seek virtue.
High and low did not vie with one another; long and short did not compare.
Externally, they sought no repayment; internally, they did not pray for virtue.
Silently aligning with the true Dao, they obscurely accorded with heaven’s principle.
When the wind conforms to custom, it may be followed; when affairs match one’s ability, they are easily done.
Commands need not be issued, yet customs naturally align; people have no desires, and affairs naturally simplify.
Those who flaunt deceit to delude the world, or act perversely to mislead the multitude—
the sage does not take them as the people’s norm.
For to engage in deception and falsity, to act with perversity and crookedness, is to delude the world and mislead the multitude—
this is what the sage does not do.

Purity and sincerity—purity is clarity, sincerity is trust. Sincerity is heaven’s nature; purity is man’s clarity. Sincerity is upheld by the will, clarity is distinguished by discernment. Without the utmost sincerity under heaven, how could one fully realize the natures of all beings and unite with heaven and earth’s virtue? Thus it is said: without purity and sincerity, one cannot move others—this is what it means.

Laozi said: Heaven attains its height, earth attains its depth; the sun shines, the stars gleam; yin and yang harmonize—not through deliberate action. What is achieved by non-action arises not from desire. Align with the Dao, and things naturally follow. When the ruler rectifies himself, the people are transformed as if by spirit; the teaching of no words has no equal.

The four seasons and yin-yang do not generate the myriad things; timely rain and dew do not nourish grasses and trees. Yet when spirit and clarity connect, when yin and yang harmonize, the myriad things are born. When heaven and earth are harmonious and at peace, when spirit and clarity intermingle and descend—this is not a matter of intention. The four seasons cannot but follow their course, and the myriad things cannot but be born.

The Dao conceals purity within, harbors spirit in the heart, abides in stillness and tranquility, delights in harmony within the breast. Vast and formless, silent and soundless—this is how the sage embraces heaven’s heart, bestows virtue, and nurtures the Dao. Inwardly, spirit is stored; outwardly, no trace of beings appears. Without omen or sign, how could there be form or sound?

Government offices are as if empty of affairs; the court is as if empty of people. There are no harsh policies, no flatterers, no recluses, no idle folk. The court is filled with loyal and steadfast men, the wilderness holds no hidden worthies. There are no corvée labors, no unjust punishments; the people are employed in season, laws are applied without excess.

Under heaven, none do not look up to the ruler’s virtue, emulate the sovereign’s intent. Even distant lands and foreign customs, none do not come through repeated translation. The ruler possesses the Dao, and the people rely on his virtue. His influence extends to the eight directions, flowing even to alien customs. It is not that he visits every household in person—he merely extends his sincere heart to all under heaven. Thus, though distant peoples and foreign customs come to submit, it is not that the ruler visits each home himself; rather, his sincere heart issues forth, and distant peoples naturally arrive.

Thus, rewarding the good and punishing the violent is proper governance, but what enables it to be carried out is purity and sincerity. Though commands may be clear, they cannot act alone—they must await purity and sincerity. Purity is clarity; sincerity is trust. Without clarity and trust, none can reward the good and punish the evil.

Therefore, when the Dao is universally applied yet the people do not follow, it is because purity and sincerity have not reached them. As when Yu attacked the Miao, they did not submit, and he withdrew—only when he danced with shields and feathers did the Miao come to terms. This was the power of purity and sincerity.

Laozi said: Heaven establishes the sun’s brightness, arrays the stars, extends the four seasons, regulates yin and yang. By day it exposes things, by night it lets them rest; the wind dries them, rain and dew moisten them. In generating things, none see how they are nourished, yet the myriad things grow. In destroying things, none see how they perish, yet the myriad things die—this is called spirit and clarity. The Dao of heaven operates in hiddenness; it is difficult to express in words.

When things are born, it is their time, and they cannot but be born. When things die, it is their time, and they cannot but die. Thus, the living align with life, the dead align with death. Therefore, the living do not thank heaven, the dead do not resent the Dao—it is simply natural. This is what is meant by spirit and clarity.

Thus, the sage emulates this. When fortune arises, none see how it begins; when misfortune is removed, none see its cause. It cannot be traced, yet it is not false. Above, it was said that heaven follows the Dao; here, it is said that the sage follows heaven. Heaven’s life and death correspond to the ruler’s rewards and punishments. "Fortune arising" refers to employing the worthy; "misfortune removed" refers to eliminating the evil. The evil cannot but be punished; the worthy cannot but be advanced. Thus, the worthy advance of themselves, not because I advance them; the violent are punished of themselves, not because I punish them. Therefore, fortune does not arise from me, nor does misfortune originate from me. None know its source; none see its form.

Daily reckoning seems insufficient, but yearly reckoning shows surplus. The sage does not scheme over trifles; daily use may seem inadequate, but yearly accounting shows abundance. Silent and soundless, a single word stirs all under heaven—this is how heaven’s heart moves and transforms.

When the sage is still, all under heaven is silent. When he moves, the myriad things return to him. Thus, Gaozong did not speak for three years, yet when he spoke, his words were revered. Such was the case.

Thus, purity and sincerity take form within, moving heaven. Auspicious stars appear, yellow dragons descend, phoenixes arrive, sweet springs flow, fine grains grow. Rivers do not overflow, seas do not surge—this is how the sage embodies the Dao and nurtures beings. Only virtue moves heaven. Issuing from the heart, it responds above to heaven. Thus, dragons and phoenixes soar and gather; rivers and seas become clear and still. Without pure sincerity, how could this be achieved?

To defy heaven and harm beings brings solar and lunar eclipses, wandering stars, seasons out of joint, days darkened and nights lit, mountains collapsing and rivers drying, winter thunder and summer frost. Heaven and man communicate thus. Above, it was shown how heaven responds with auspicious omens; here, it is shown how defiance brings calamity and disaster.

Thus, know that misfortune and fortune have no fixed gate—they are summoned by man. Heaven and man communicate; qi and kindred types resonate—this cannot be mistaken. Therefore, when a state is about to perish, celestial signs change, customs grow chaotic, rainbows appear. All things are interconnected; pure qi presses upon one another. When perverse qi prevails, calamities transform beings, responding with misfortune.

Thus, the sage examines his own person and understands the myriad kinds. Diligent and cautious, he dares not be negligent. He would rather align above with heaven’s heart to pacify the black-haired people than dare to be remiss. Therefore, matters of spirit and clarity cannot be achieved by cleverness or force. Spirit and clarity are upright—how could they tolerate deceit and falsity?

Only with utmost sincerity can there be response—how could force ever penetrate? Thus, the great person’s virtue merges with heaven and earth, his clarity merges with sun and moon, his numinosity merges with ghosts and spirits, his trustworthiness merges with the four seasons. Only the supreme sage can embody the perfect Dao and align with heaven’s heart; thus, his power leaves nothing unencompassed, his illumination leaves nothing unillumined, his numinosity responds without emptiness, his trustworthiness never transgresses the proper time. One who can attain this may be called great indeed.

He embraces heaven’s heart, holds earth’s qi, grasps the balanced and contains harmony. When heart and qi communicate, heaven and earth unite in peace—how could one who does not embody balance and harmony ever reach such a state? Without descending the hall, he travels the four seas—this is the work of spiritual transformation. Changing taboos and customs, the people are transformed toward goodness as if it arose from themselves—this is the power of spiritual transformation.

The sage does not alter himself for high or low, nor change his feelings for barbarian or Chinese. Thus, without descending the hall, he transforms diverse customs; without extending his spirit, envoys from distant lands arrive. His virtue extends to others as if it arose from themselves.

Laozi said: "For the person who follows the Dao, preserving nature and safeguarding authenticity, nothing harms his person. One who comprehends the Dao’s essence and aligns with heaven’s principle remains unharmed by things and uninjured in himself. When pressed by urgent peril, his essence communicates with heaven. Sincerity reaches its utmost without deviation."

Should one never depart from their fundamental source, what could they not accomplish? Death and life share the same domain, and none can coerce or oppress them. Never departing from their source means the mind merges with the Dao, the body equalizes with transformations—where could they not go, what could they not achieve? Death and life are already dissolved; how could they be coerced or oppressed?

Moreover, to govern heaven and earth, to encompass the myriad things, to return to the workings of creation, and to contain perfect harmony—this is to have never died. The Dao models itself on heaven and earth, embraces yin and exhales yang, distributes the five agents, and envelops the ten thousand kinds. Alone, it operates above the potter’s wheel of creation, circulating beyond the bounds of transformation—it has never been born, so who could speak of its death?

Sincerity takes form within and outwardly resonates in the hearts of others—this is the untransmitted Dao. This speaks of nurturing sincerity within and externally moving others; can it be transmitted? When the sage resides above, embracing the Dao without speaking, his beneficence extends to the myriad people. Thus, the teaching without words is vast indeed.

When ruler and minister diverge in heart, deceit and treachery manifest in heaven, and the correspondence of spirit and qi is evident—this is called the eloquence of the wordless, the Dao of the unspoken. The supreme Dao is without speech; its mysterious workings do not dominate. Therefore, when ruler and minister preserve one another, chanting and extolling virtue, it reaches heaven above and communicates in secret with the spirits. The eloquence of the wordless, the Dao of the unspoken—these are what they attain.

To summon the distant, let there be non-action; to draw near, let speech be without affairs. The height and remoteness of heaven can only be moved by non-action to respond, and by non-affairs to be made close. Only those who walk in the night can attain this—walking in the night means diligent practice, like traveling at night, not yet reaching the destination, yet accompanying the journey without ceasing. Thus, the spirit is inwardly aroused and ascends to heaven.

Therefore, dismiss the galloping horse to manure the fields—"dismiss" means to cease. The "horse" is the heart. The heart, like a runaway horse, is difficult to control. People all gallop after their hearts, chasing distant fame and profit to glorify themselves. I, however, do not act thus: I cease the galloping of my heart, preserving emptiness and stillness to nourish my person.

When the tracks of chariots do not reach beyond distant lands, this is called "sitting in motion and sinking into obscurity." To arrive without traveling is called "sitting in motion"; to remain hidden without emerging is called "sinking into obscurity." The Way of heaven is impartial in approach and departure; the capable have abundance, the inept fall short. The Way of heaven is impartial: those with virtue it approaches, those without it departs.

Observe how choosing this and abandoning that involves partiality, yet when forsaking evil and embracing good, the principle aligns with nature. Without desire, there is abundance; with desire, there is insufficiency. Following it brings benefit; opposing it brings misfortune. Only through impartiality and non-action is there neither benefit nor merit.

Thus, those who govern with cunning find it difficult to maintain the state—this was already explained in the previous section. Only those who harmonize with the great harmony and uphold natural responsiveness can possess it. Only by preserving the great harmony and naturally responding can one attain it.

Laozi said: The Dao and virtue are like rawhide and leather—distance makes them seem near, closeness makes them seem far. Scrutiny finds nothing, yet investigation is not in vain. Rawhide and leather form a drum: strike it, and it responds. The Dao and virtue sustain the person: employ them, and they function. The resonance is inexhaustible, the spiritual transformation boundless. Thus, examination finds nothing, yet investigation is not in vain.

Therefore, the sage is like a mirror: neither advancing nor retreating, responding without concealing. The myriad things do not harm him; his heart is like a mirror, reflecting all without omission. He does not seek to illuminate by approaching things but constantly reflects and responds to them. No image escapes; what harm could come from encounters?

What is gained is thereby lost; what is lost is thereby gained. Gain and loss reveal insubstantiality; without gain or loss, there is true unity. Thus, one who penetrates the great harmony is dim as if drunk, sweetly reclining and roaming within it—never having departed from their source, this is called great penetration. Embracing the Dao and containing harmony, forgetting form and relinquishing burdens, like drinking fine wine, delighting in its midst, merging indistinguishably—this is the great Dao. This is the use of non-use to accomplish its function. This is awakening to the Dao through non-birth, like forgetting form through drunkenness.

Laozi said: In ancient times, the Yellow Emperor governed the world by harmonizing the movements of the sun and moon, regulating the qi of yin and yang, moderating the measures of the four seasons, rectifying the calculations of pitch pipes and calendars, distinguishing male and female, clarifying superior and inferior, ensuring the strong did not oppress the weak, and the many did not tyrannize the few. The people preserved their lives without premature death; the years were abundant without famine. The hundred officials were upright and impartial; superiors and inferiors were harmonious without blame. Laws and commands were clear and not obscure; assistants were just and not obsequious. Farmers yielded boundaries; roads were free of lost items; markets did not engage in speculative pricing. Thus, at that time, the sun, moon, and stars did not deviate from their courses; wind and rain came in season; the five grains flourished; phoenixes soared in the courtyards, and qilin roamed the outskirts. Such was the Yellow Emperor’s governance of the world through the Dao, and the transformation through virtue.

Fuxi ruled the world resting his head on stone and sleeping on ropes, killing in autumn and executing in winter, bearing the square earth and embracing the round heaven, embraced by yin and yang. Where stagnation and blockage did not flow, the qi of obstructed principles and perverse things that harmed the people and accumulated excessively were cut off. His people were like unlearned children, not knowing east from west, their vision dim, their steps faltering, yet content and self-assured, unaware of their drifting, floating without knowing their origin, nurturing themselves without knowing where to go.

At that time, birds, beasts, insects, and snakes all sheathed their claws and fangs, concealed their stings and poisons. Though they harbored the nature of venom and sting, they had no intent to harm—such was the reach of supreme virtue.

At the time of the Yellow Emperor, the essential subtleties remained obscure beneath the Great Ancestor, yet his achievements went uncelebrated, his name unproclaimed. Concealing the Way of the True Person, he followed the inherent constancy of heaven and earth. What, then, of the Dao and its virtue? When the upper reaches are penetrated, cunning and artifice vanish.

The Great Ancestor preceded the Yellow Emperor. His people were unadorned, their nature untamed. Though he accomplished works, he claimed no virtue; though he bore a name, he did not exalt it. Thus it is said: he concealed the Way of the True Person, severed the clamor of superficial wisdom, and accorded with the natural communion of heaven and earth.

Laozi said: If heaven is unsettled, the sun and moon have nothing to bear them; if earth is unsettled, grasses and trees have nowhere to stand; if the self is not at peace, right and wrong take no shape. When the Three Powers are not at peace, the myriad things lose their place. If one does not cultivate single-minded devotion, turning inward to listen and reflect, then the True Person remains unseen, and true wisdom does not arise.

Thus, only with the True Person does true wisdom emerge. If what one holds is not illuminated, how can one know whether what I call knowledge is not ignorance? If what one preserves is not clear, how can one know the Dao?

This is to know that the knower does not fail to know the Dao.

Accumulating kindness and valuing wealth, bringing joy to the myriad people so that they delight in life—this is *ren* (benevolence). Achieving great deeds, making one’s illustrious name known, embodying the proper relationship between ruler and minister, rectifying the hierarchy of high and low, clarifying the distinctions of near and far, preserving imperiled states, continuing severed lineages, and establishing heirs where none exist—this is *yi* (righteousness). Through *ren* one establishes things, and through *yi* one preserves sincerity; thus, none fail to cherish it, and no affair goes unaccomplished. This is the Way of kings and hegemons in governing the world.

Closing the nine apertures, concealing one’s will and intentions, relinquishing cleverness and insight, returning to non-discernment, wandering in boundless vagueness beyond the dust and grime, roaming freely in the realm of non-action, inhaling yin and exhaling yang to harmonize with the myriad things—this is *de* (virtue/power). Inwardly obscure yet outwardly compliant, concealing essence to nurture the true, acting without contrivance while remaining untouched by external defilements—thus, the Dao disperses and becomes *de*, and *de* overflows into *ren* and *yi*. Yet once *ren* and *yi* are established, the Dao and *de* are abandoned. What was once pure essence scatters, leaving only dregs.

Laozi said: When the spirit scatters, words become florid; when *de* dissipates, conduct becomes false. When the utmost essence is obscured within, yet words and actions are scrutinized without, one cannot avoid being enslaved by things. When inner essence overflows, external embellishments arise; when the mind is ensnared by affairs, how can the body escape harm? Essence may be exhausted, but actions know no end; if what one preserves is unstable, one becomes externally entangled in worldly customs. If inner preservation is unsteady, one reaches a dead end and sinks into mire; if external pursuits never cease, one is perpetually tormented by storms and waves. Therefore, the sage cultivates the arts of the Dao within and does not outwardly adorn himself with *ren* and *yi*. He understands the proper use of the nine apertures and four limbs, yet roams in the harmony of spirit—this is the sage’s wandering.

The sage inwardly guards the true essence while outwardly responding to things as they are; thus, he attains the harmony of spirit and roams in the boundless. Laozi said: As for the sage’s wandering, he moves in perfect emptiness, his mind roams in the great void, gallops beyond the four directions, acts without gateways, listens without sound, and sees without form. Unconstrained by the world, unbound by custom—moving in perfect emptiness, there is nothing he does not penetrate; roaming in the great void, there is nothing he does not possess. What gate or door could confine him? How could sight or hearing be clouded? Thus, what the sage employs to move the world is not what the True Person surpasses, and what the worthy use to correct世俗 is not what the sage observes. The traces of the True and the sagely differ, their achievements and undertakings diverge—like ancestral sacrifices and kitchen duties, each attends to their proper place.

Those bound by世俗 must manifest their form and dissipate their spirit, and thus cannot escape entanglement. None who dwell in glory and prominence avoid enslavement of the spirit; having received their emoluments, they must also bear their burdens. If I can be fettered, it is because my fate lies beyond myself. Once shackled, my fate is no longer mine.

Laozi said: When the ruler’s thoughts do not scatter in his breast, nor his wisdom extend beyond the four domains; when he cherishes a heart of benevolence and sincerity, sweet rains fall in season, the five grains flourish, spring brings growth, summer brings maturity, autumn brings harvest, winter brings storage, and monthly reviews and seasonal assessments culminate in annual tribute—such is the ruler with the Dao. He does not exhaust his spirit in deliberation nor flaunt his wisdom and ability, yet distant lands are drawn to him. Thus, he aligns above with the heart of heaven and below with the proper order of things, and the myriad people offer their allegiance, their tribute unceasing. He nurtures the people with impartiality, his majesty is awe-inspiring yet not harsh, his laws are few and punishments lenient. Transformation occurs as if by spirit, laws are broad and penalties mild, prisons stand empty, the world shares a single custom, and none harbor treacherous hearts—this is the sage’s grace.

Impartial yet not selfish, majestic yet not fierce, laws concise and punishments mild, people follow his transformation—this may be called the utmost spirit. Thus, all directions are united, diverse customs follow a single path—this is the sage’s grace in governing the world. If those above covet without measure, those below will vie for merit without restraint; the people grow impoverished and distressed, disputes arise, toil yields no fruit, cunning and deceit sprout, and bandits and thieves proliferate. Above and below resent one another, decrees go unheeded. The aforementioned conditions are not how the sage governs the world. To rule thus is to court ruin without delay.

When water is turbid, fish gasp; when governance is harsh, the people rebel. When those above are full of desires, those below are full of deceit; when those above are restless, those below are unsettled; when those above make many demands, those below contend with one another. To neglect the root and seek remedy at the branches is no different from digging a channel to stop water or carrying firewood to extinguish a fire. Grasping the root to govern the branches yields effortless success and a well-ordered world; clinging to the branches to seek the root brings labor to the self and chaos to the world—like trying to stop overflowing water by digging channels or quell a raging fire by adding fuel, it cannot be saved.

The sage’s affairs are few yet well-ordered, his demands are few yet sufficient. Without acting, he is benevolent; without speaking, he is trusted; without seeking, he obtains; without striving, he accomplishes. Embracing the natural, preserving the utmost true, holding to the Dao and extending sincerity, the world follows him as echo follows sound, as shadow follows form—what he cultivates is the root. The principle of the utmost sage lies in simplicity and ease, and thus the world reveres him, responding like echo to sound, like shadow to form—none fail to answer.

Laozi said: When the spirit scatters outward and wisdom and deliberation dissipate within, one cannot govern. When the uses of form and spirit extend too far, what is neglected lies close at hand. Pursuing what is not one’s portion and indulging without restraint injures the spirit within and abandons the body without. Thus, one who does not step beyond the door yet knows the world, who does not peer through the window yet knows the Way of heaven— the farther he ventures, the less he knows. This speaks of sincerity issuing from within and spirit and qi moving heaven.

Grasping the essentials to observe the world, the sentiments of people can be discerned. Holding the jade pivot to observe the great movements, the Way of heaven can be illuminated. When sincere words issue from within, essence and qi respond to heaven. What one guards is near, yet what one illuminates is distant; the more one busies oneself, the less one knows.

Laozi said: The yang of winter’s sun, the yin of summer’s shade—things turn to them without being driven to excess. This is the spontaneous arrival of utmost refinement, unsummoned yet come, departing without going. In the profound and obscure, though one knows not how it is done, success is achieved of itself.

The yang of winter’s sun—those who are cold draw near. The yin of summer’s shade—those who are scorched find relief. Thus the sage governs the world: yin and yang are without sentiment, and the sage too is without sentiment. Things embrace of themselves, people return of themselves.
