Thus they come without being summoned, depart without being sent away.

To await the eye before seeing, to await speech before issuing commands—governance becomes difficult. Gaoyao was mute yet served as Grand Judge, and the world knew no cruel punishments—what need was there for words? Shikuang was blind yet served as Grand Minister, and the state of Jin knew no disorder—what need was there for sight? The command that requires no speech, the vision that requires no seeing—this is how the sage becomes a teacher.

To transform without speaking, so that below there are no cruel punishments—why trouble with words? To govern without seeing, so that Jin knows no disorder—what need is there for sight? Thus, without awaiting the eye to see, without needing speech to command—this is how the sage becomes a teacher.

The people’s transformation by their superior does not follow his words but follows his conduct. In high antiquity, one acted without speaking; in later ages, one speaks without acting. Thus when a ruler favors valor, though he does not incite strife, the state will face many crises, and gradually there will arise the chaos of robbery and murder. When a ruler favors beauty, though he does not encourage licentious talk, the state will fall into darkness and disorder, and over time there will come the calamity of debauchery and ruin.

The people’s transformation by their superior is like water flowing downward. One must block the source and be vigilant in small matters. Thus Duke Zhuang of Qin suffered the disaster of forced abdication, and Jie of Xia brought about the chaos of Daji. Therefore the sage refines sincerity within and clarifies likes and dislikes without. His words match his feelings, his decrees make his intent clear. Within, there is no partiality; without, there is no favoritism or aversion. Words issue and echoes respond; commands go forth and the wind carries them.

Thus punishments are not enough to shift customs, executions are not enough to suppress villainy. One may transform through virtue, but it is difficult to control through penalties. Only spiritual transformation is precious. Utmost refinement is spirit. Where refinement moves, it is like the qi of spring giving life or the qi of autumn bringing death. In life, it is warm as spring things receiving their growth. In death, it is solemn as autumn things reaching their end.

Thus in life there is no seeking of reward, in death no harboring of resentment. Life and death come from its being without intent. Therefore the noble person is like an archer: here, a hair’s breadth; there, a span’s mark. Thus one who governs others must be cautious in how he influences them.

Therefore the noble person governing others is like an archer: when the arrow is loosed, he aims for the target, and when it reaches the butt, he measures the span’s mark. A deviation of a hair’s breadth is as distant as heaven from earth.

Laozi said: To hang laws and set rewards yet fail to shift customs and change habits is because sincerity is not embraced. Laws cannot restrain the people; only utmost virtue can transform customs. Thus by listening to their music one knows their customs; by observing their music one knows their manners; by seeing their manners one knows their transformation.

Examine the sound to know the music, examine the music to know the governance, observe the governance to know the customs, observe the customs to know the transformation. One who embraces truth and offers sincerity moves heaven and earth, and spirits transcend beyond the four directions. Commands are obeyed and prohibitions observed. Sincerity penetrates the Way and conveys its intent. Though not a word is spoken, the myriad people of the world, birds and beasts, ghosts and spirits, all change with him.

Thus the sage’s governance of the world: the people follow his transformation as if rolling a ball. To forbid villainy and error is like halting a square object. Therefore, with virtue that does not rely on force, with teachings that require no words, even birds and beasts, spirits and ghosts, have none who are not pleased and submissive—how much more so for people?

Thus the highest is spiritual transformation, returning to spontaneity. Next is enabling people not to do wrong, establishing laws to regulate. The lowest is rewarding the worthy and punishing the violent. When the Way and virtue are abandoned, rewards and punishments begin. When the path to advancing the worthy is opened, the unworthy also find places. When their positions are lost, the seeds of violence arise, and even the worthy may suffer execution.

Thus one knows that without clear laws, those who uphold them become excessive; without an even balance, those who use it become weak.

Laozi said: The great Way is non-action. Non-action is non-possession. One who does not possess does not abide. One who does not abide dwells in formlessness. One who is formless does not move. One who does not move does not speak. One who does not speak is still and without sound or form. Without sound or form, it cannot be seen when looked at, cannot be heard when listened to. This is called the subtle and wondrous, the utmost spirit. Continuous as if existing—this is called the root of heaven and earth.

Non-action is acting without reliance, thus it is called non-action. No speech is speaking without pride, thus it is called no sound. Without form, what is there to hear? Without color, what is there to see? This may be called spiritually subtle. Standing alone without change, continuous and ever-present—it is the root of heaven and earth.

The Way is without form or sound, thus the sage forcibly gives it form, using a single word to name the Way of heaven and earth. This speaks of grasping the essentials of the Way. The great takes the small as its root, the many take the few as their beginning. Being arises from non-being, the many arise from the one.

Tracing back to the origin returns to formlessness; pursuing the branches leads to confusion by multiplicity. Thus knowing to return takes non-being as its foundation; sensing multiplicity seeks the one as its master.

The Son of Heaven takes heaven and earth as his standard—this speaks of their vastness. He takes the myriad things as his resources—there is nothing he does not possess. His merit and virtue are utmost, his power and name are most noble. The beauty of these two virtues matches heaven and earth. Thus he cannot but follow the great Way as the mother of the world.

From the honor of the four seas to matching heaven and earth—how could he not follow it to avoid disorder? This is the heavenly constant.

Laozi said: Relieving poverty and aiding the urgent brings forth fame and profit, yet one should not practice petty kindness. Removing harm is where merit is accomplished. Not claiming merit as one’s own—this is goodness.

When the world knows no disasters or harms, even sages have no virtue to apply; when superiors and inferiors are in harmony, even the worthy have no merit to establish. When disasters arise, sages pacify them; when a state falls into calamity and chaos, the worthy settle it. Yet if disasters do not arise and chaos does not stir, even sages have no throne from which to act as sages, and even the worthy have no ground on which to establish merit.

Thus the governance of the perfected person embraces virtue and holds to the Dao, extending sincerity and delighting in bestowal. With boundless wisdom, they rest in silence and do not speak, and none in the world know the value of their non-speaking. They drum their bellies and strike the earth, unaware of the emperor’s power.

Thus the Dao that can be spoken is not the constant Dao; the name that can be named is not the constant name. The Dao without name is the utmost Dao; the Dao with name is the name of the world. Therefore, the Dao that can be spoken is not the Dao; the name that can be named is not the name.

That which is inscribed on bamboo and silk, carved into metal and stone, and transmitted to others—all these are but its coarse aspects, flowery and overly adorned. The Three Sovereigns, Five Emperors, and Three Kings followed different affairs yet shared the same heart, took different paths yet reached the same destination. Though their words differed and their governance varied with the times, their return to the Dao was one.

Scholars of the latter age do not know that the Dao is embodied in unity, that virtue gathers in essentials. They seize upon the immediacy of accomplished matters, kneel and sit to discourse upon them—though learned and widely informed, they cannot escape disorder. Scholars of today do not trace back to the root, do not embody the essentials, do not investigate the principle, but instead follow the traces, striving for broad knowledge, only adding to the chaos.

Laozi said: The essence of the heart can work spiritual transformations but cannot be spoken of as the Dao. Its spiritual transformations cannot be explained as non-Dao. The sage does not descend from his seat yet rectifies the world, his care surpassing even a shout. A shout can be heard, but only by a few; when a decree is issued, the whole world submits.

Thus when words are the same, trust lies before the words; when commands are the same, sincerity lies beyond the command. Sharing the people’s sorrows, words reach trust, for trust is established beforehand—none dare not respond. Sharing the people’s benefits, commands are carried out with sincerity, for sincerity arises externally—none dare disobey.

When the sage is above, the people are transformed as if by spirit, for sentiment precedes them. Grasping their intent, responses come as if by spirit. If one acts above yet receives no response below, it is because sentiment and decree diverge. The hungry seek food, the cold long for fur. If one gives fur to the hungry and food to the cold, the decree is already contrary—how can the people’s sentiment attach to it?

A three-month-old infant knows not of benefit or harm, yet the loving mother’s care grows ever more earnest—this is sentiment. The heart of a newborn child does not speak of hunger or fullness, yet the compassionate mother observes its signs, discerns its dryness or dampness, and nurtures it with kindness. If the ruler of men cherishes the hundred clans as he loves a newborn child, what worry is there that the world will not be governed, that the four seas will not be at peace?

Thus the use of words brings change—change in small matters. The use of non-words brings change—change in great matters. Words that speak of words are small; words that speak of no-words are great. Trusting a noble person’s words, being loyal to a noble person’s intent, loyalty and trust take shape within and move responses without—this is the transformation of the worthy and sage.

Loyalty and trust arise from the noble person’s original intent, not from forgetfulness. Thus when they take shape within and move without, even the worthy and sage cannot but follow.

Laozi said: A son’s death for his father, a minister’s death for his lord—these are not acts of seeking fame through death. The heart’s kindness is stored within, and they do not shirk from difficulty. A son dies for his father’s peril, a minister dies for his lord’s peril—not to feign virtue for the world’s praise, but because kindness covers them so deeply that they forget themselves, rushing straight into peril. Their sincerity arises from within.

The noble person’s sorrow is not for outward correctness but arises from within. One must examine their conduct.

Sorrow and distress refer to penal law. Execution and punishment are not the proper Way. They are employed only to punish evil and encourage good, as a last resort, and must not be wantonly applied. The sage feels no shame before the sun and moon; the noble do not commit wrongdoing. The noble person is cautious in solitude, meaning they do not deceive even in darkness and seclusion. To abandon what is near and seek what is distant is to invite obstruction. To neglect self-cultivation while demanding it of others is to invite blockage.

Thus when the sage is in high position, the people delight in their governance; when in low position, the people admire their intent, for their resolve never wavers from desiring to benefit others. With their resolve set on benefiting others, all people rejoice and admire them.

Laozi said: When a brave warrior lets out a single cry, the entire army retreats—such is the power of his sincerity. A single shout from the brave strikes terror into ten thousand. The worthy govern the age, and all under heaven look to them. If one sings and none respond, or offers counsel and none bear it, there must be discord within. For their principles differ from others’. One who corrects all under heaven without leaving their seat seeks the cause within themselves. When joy and sorrow are shared with the people, and likes and dislikes are aligned with them, then by examining oneself, one can transform others.

Thus where persuasion does not reach, one’s demeanor does; though it may not convey the subtlest essence, it can approximate one’s bearing. Where demeanor does not reach, subtle influence does—arising from the heart and taking form, it moves through spiritual resonance, not through outward appearance. The utmost refinement can be perceived through form, yet cannot be forced into clarity. Though form arises from the formless, the purest essence gives birth to all forms, making them manifest. One does not seek refinement, yet refinement resides within.

Laozi said: Words have their source, affairs have their root. Lose the source and root, and no matter how many skills one possesses, it is better to speak little. Take the Dao as the source and virtue as the root; depart from them, and abundance is no match for simplicity. To harm the many, Yu the craftsman would sever his own finger to demonstrate that great skill cannot be forced. True skill resides in the heart, not in the fingers—cutting off what cannot be attained. When the Dao is in the heart, clarity does not depend on grand words. To value words as if they were the Dao is to hope in vain for the Dao.

Thus the craftsman’s wisdom lies not in their ability but in knowing when to close. The craftsman—an artisan—closes with skill, for the most adept know how to seal tightly, so none can open. The unskilled rely solely on brute force, and though strong, they will inevitably fail. This illustrates the exhaustion of subtle wisdom, for strength alone is insufficient.

Laozi said: The sage’s undertakings may follow different paths, yet they converge in the same end. Though past and present differ, governance remains the same. Preservation and ruin, stability and collapse—if one’s resolve never wavers from desiring to benefit others, then the ruined are preserved, the collapsed are stabilized. Is this concern only for oneself? It is always for the benefit of others.

Thus the songs of Qin, Chu, Yan, and Wei differ in sound yet all express joy; the wails of the Nine Yi and Eight Di differ in sound yet all convey sorrow. Song is the manifestation of joy, weeping the expression of sorrow—concealed within, it emerges without, and thus the means of moving others. Sound and breath resonate, sorrow and joy summon one another. Therefore, though songs differ by nation, all share in joy; though wails vary by region, all share in grief.

The sage’s heart never ceases, day or night, from desiring to benefit others. The reach of their virtue is thus vast. By unceasingly benefiting others, their influence extends far and wide.

Laozi said: When people act without contrivance, there is governance. To act is to harm the governance of non-action. To practice non-action is to act; those who cannot act without contrivance cannot truly act. To speak of non-action is still to act; to act is to depart from the governance of non-action. Only by acting without acting is there true non-action.

When people remain silent, the spirit speaks—yet to cling to silence harms the spirit’s true nature. The spirit values silence; even the sage does not force speech. Words reveal traces, actions reveal the coarse. Only when both are unharmed can the Dao be preserved whole.

Wenzi said: A name can be forcibly established, merit can be forcibly achieved—this is the way of encouragement. Formerly, Nanrong Chu, a disciple of Laozi, felt shame that the sagely Dao was absent within himself. He traveled south to see Laozi and received a single teaching. His spirit became enlightened, and though he endured hardship and toil, fasting for ten days, he savored the Dao as if feasting on the finest delicacies. Having received a single teaching, he meditated for ten days, forgetting hunger and tasting the Dao as if enjoying a grand banquet.

Thus his wisdom illuminated all within the seas, his name endured through later generations, his intelligence and strategy spanned heaven and earth, his discernment was as keen as distinguishing autumn down. His praises and eloquent words continue unceasing to this day—this is what it means for a name to be forcibly established. The ultimate teaching, once received, dissolves all doubts like the sun’s reflection illuminating a dark room.

Thus the farmer who does not exert himself will not fill his granary; the official who does not strive will not refine their sincerity; the general and minister who do not exert themselves will not achieve great deeds; the king and lord who are indolent will leave no name for posterity. From commoners to kings and lords, none have filled their granaries or made their names illustrious without diligence and effort.

The perfected person acts in concealment, like thunder hidden away. They act in accordance with the times, establish merit through available resources, advance and retreat without difficulty, and nothing is impassable. The great person’s Dao is hidden and still—none under heaven can perceive their actions. Yet when thunder strikes, none under heaven fail to hear it. They advance without elation, retreat without shame—this is the great Dao.

The perfected person’s essence and sincerity take form within, their virtue flowing to the four directions. Seeing benefit for all under heaven, they rejoice yet do not forget the harm that may befall them, trembling as if in mourning. They welcome benefit yet fear encountering harm. One who sorrows with the people’s sorrow will have the people sorrow with their sorrow; one who rejoices with the people’s joy will have the people rejoice with their joy. Thus to sorrow with all under heaven and rejoice with all under heaven—yet fail to become king—has never been.

To sorrow with the people is to have the people sorrow with you; to rejoice with the people is to have the people rejoice with you. One who shares sorrow and joy with the people yet fails to win their allegiance has never been.

The sage’s law begins where it cannot be seen and ends where it cannot be reached. Their virtue and righteousness know no bounds, their beginning and end no limits. They dwell in unshakable ground, stand where non-action prevails. They amass from inexhaustible granaries, using without depletion; they draw from unfailing treasuries, circulating without end.

Their decrees flow like the source of a river, guiding the people to offices of no contention. When commands are issued, the people know the prohibitions; when affairs are simplified, officials have no disputes. They open doors that must be entered, secure the people in their occupations. They do not attempt what cannot be accomplished, do not seek what cannot be obtained, do not dwell where they cannot endure, do not act where they cannot recover.

The great person’s decrees bring only benefit when enacted, and what they establish endures. They do not dwell where they cannot last, do not seek what cannot be regained. The great person enacts governance that brings joy, and none fail to follow their commands. When commands are obeyed, one advances from small to great; when commands are defied, good becomes harm and success becomes failure.

In their governance and teaching, obedience turns misfortune to fortune, while defiance reduces the great to the small. The one called a true great person is inwardly strong and outwardly luminous—internally as steadfast as heaven and earth, externally as radiant as sun and moon. Heaven and earth cover and bear all, sun and moon illuminate all. To be like heaven and earth in covering and bearing, like sun and moon in illumination—this is the true great person.

The great person shows goodness to others without altering their fundamental ways, without changing their constant principles. All under heaven follow their commands as grass bends to the wind. To alter the fundamentals unsettles customs; to follow nature makes the people easy to govern. Grass bends to the wind—none dare defy it.

When governance errs in spring, Jupiter waxes and wanes, abandoning its regular path. When governance errs in summer, Mars moves in reverse. When governance errs in autumn, Venus appears where it should not, its movements erratic. When governance errs in winter, Mercury fails to appear in its proper place. When the four seasons lose their order, Saturn sways unsteadily, the sun and moon display omens, the five planets move in chaos, and comets appear. When the seasons are disordered and the five planets lose their regularity, celestial omens appear in the heavens, and disaster befalls humanity.

If spring governance is not lost, millet and grain flourish; if summer governance is not lost, rain falls in season; if autumn governance is not lost, the people prosper; if winter governance is not lost, the state enjoys peace and well-being. Thus the enlightened ruler must not lose proper governance. When the ruler loses governance, heaven sends down a hundred calamities. When the ruler upholds governance, heaven sends down a hundred blessings. The joy of one becomes the joy of ten thousand.

**The Nine Guardians**

This chapter contains nine sections, hence the title *The Nine Guardians*. Nine is the culminating number of the *Yi*. When clarity reaches its limit, change occurs, and change leads to deviation from the Dao. Those who guard it abide in stillness—like the dragon at its zenith, free from regret—and may transcend the Three Pure Realms. Those who neglect it, like a barren mare, will sink beneath the Nine Dark Abysses. Thus, one must guard the Dao and never lose constancy.

Laozi said: Before heaven and earth took form, all was dark and indistinct, merged as one, the qi and its manifestations undivided. Silent and limpid, the heavy and turbid settled as earth, the refined and subtle rose as heaven—thus were shapes and substances established. Separating, they became the four seasons: spring’s birth, summer’s growth, autumn’s harvest, winter’s storage. Dividing, they became yin and yang, and hardness and softness were set forth. Refined qi became humans, coarse qi became creatures. Their origin is one, yet their endowment differs; qi has purity and turbidity, things have refinement and coarseness. Through the interplay of hardness and softness, the myriad things are born. From this, transformations extend endlessly.

Spirit and essence derive from heaven; bones and flesh root in earth. That which derives from heaven inclines upward; that which derives from earth inclines downward. When spirit enters its gate and bones return to their root, what remains of me? Entering the gate of the boundless, returning to the root of creation, who, if not utterly sincere in their truth, can claim a self? Thus, the sage models heaven and follows earth, unbound by custom, unenticed by others. Heaven is their father, earth their mother; yin and yang their warp, the four seasons their weft. Soaring between heaven and earth, they tread the warp and weft, roaming in the realm of self-attainment, dwelling in the abode of non-action. Custom cannot bind them, the world cannot entice them.

Heaven is still and clear; earth is settled and tranquil. The myriad things that oppose them perish; those that accord with them live. Heaven has no intent regarding opposition or accord—yet humans bring upon themselves calamity and ruin. Thus, stillness and silence are the dwelling of spirit and clarity; emptiness and nothingness are the abode of the Dao. The Dao resides in quietude; spirit wanders in the utmost void.

Spirit and essence are received from heaven; bones and flesh are endowed by earth. Heaven’s qi, clear and transformative, becomes spirit; earth’s qi, heavy and congealing, becomes bones and flesh—hence the term "endowment." The Dao gives birth to the One: heaven. The One gives birth to the Two: earth. The Two give birth to the Three: humanity. The Three give birth to the myriad things. Their transformations are vast. The myriad things bear yin and embrace yang, harmonized by the qi between them. All are softened and harmonized by qi.

Laozi said: Humans receive the transformations of heaven and earth and are born between them, dwelling above the myriad things. In the first month, the essence congeals. In the second, blood and vessels form, and the embryo’s shape appears. In the third, the embryo takes form, and the residence of spirit is fixed. In the fourth, the fetus develops; in the fifth, the sinews; in the sixth, the bones; in the seventh, the form is complete, and the orifices open, granting clarity. In the eighth, movement begins, and spirit descends with its numinous power. In the ninth, restlessness arises as the palaces and chambers arrange themselves to secure the essence. In the tenth, birth occurs, and the myriad images are complete.

Once the physical form is fully shaped, the five viscera divide. The liver governs the eyes, the kidneys the ears, the spleen the tongue, the lungs the nose, and the gallbladder the mouth. Here, only four viscera are mentioned—modern accounts differ, and the details remain unclear. The exterior is the surface; the interior is the core. The head is round, modeling heaven; the feet are square, resembling earth. A single human body encompasses the myriad images—it must not be treated lightly.

Heaven has four seasons, five phases, nine luminaries, and three hundred sixty days; humans have four limbs, five viscera, nine orifices, and three hundred sixty joints. Heaven has wind, rain, cold, and heat; humans have taking and giving, joy and anger. The gallbladder corresponds to clouds, the lungs to qi, the spleen to wind, the kidneys to rain, and the liver to thunder. Humans resemble heaven and earth, with the heart as their master.

The heart governs the body, overseeing the five viscera, six bowels, four limbs, and nine orifices. Above, it connects to heaven; below, it responds to earth; in between, it harmonizes with the myriad things. This is what is called "spirit." The common people use it daily yet do not know it; those who understand this Dao are few.

The ears and eyes are the sun and moon; blood and qi are wind and rain. When the sun and moon lose their course, they dim and eclipse; when wind and rain are untimely, they destroy and bring disaster. Heaven has sun and moon, which must not be dim; wind and rain, which must be regulated. When they are untimely or unregulated, disaster follows. Humans have ears and eyes, which must be clear; blood and vessels, which must be balanced. When they are disharmonious or unbalanced, illness arises.

When the five stars lose their course, the states and regions suffer their misfortune. Where the five stars trespass, the corresponding lands endure calamity. When the five viscera are afflicted by evil, the whole body falls ill. The Dao of heaven and earth is vast and great, yet it still regulates its brilliance and cherishes its spirit and clarity. How can human ears and eyes endure constant stimulation without rest, or spirit and essence gallop without exhaustion?

Heaven and earth, though vast, regulate the four seasons and harmonize the five planets, protecting their spirit and clarity. How much more so for humans, who employ their ears and eyes and let their hearts and thoughts race—can they preserve their nature and spirit? Thus, the sage guards the inner and does not lose the outer, preserving spirit within and protecting the physical form without.

Blood and qi are humanity’s splendor; the five viscera are humanity’s essence. When blood and qi focus inward and do not stray outward, the chest and abdomen are full, and desires diminish. Observing the surface reveals the interior; seeing the root reveals the branch. When desires arise within, splendor fades without; when essence and qi harmonize within, the flesh and skin flourish without.

When desires are few, the ears and eyes are clear, and hearing and sight are keen—this is called "clarity." Clarity leaves nothing unperceived; brightness leaves nothing unilluminated. All arise from restraining passions and cutting off desires. When the five viscera remain aligned with the heart and do not stray, qi and intent prevail, and conduct is not perverse. When spirit is abundant and qi does not scatter, hearing leaves nothing unheard, sight leaves nothing unseen, and nothing is left unaccomplished.

By rightly settling the heart, the five viscera remain free from evil, wayward qi disperses, and the function of spirit and essence becomes bright. Nothing subtle is left unilluminated, nothing hidden is left unexamined, no affair is left unfinished, and no task is left unaccomplished.

Troubles and misfortunes have no means to enter, and wayward qi cannot invade. In movement and action, constancy in rectitude ensures calamities vanish of themselves. When inner essence does not dissipate, external evils cannot penetrate. Thus, those who seek much gain little, and those who perceive broadly know narrowly. To seek abundance from scarcity, to infer the great from the small—this is clarity.

The orifices are the doors and windows of spirit; blood and qi are the messengers and sentinels of the five viscera. Open the doors and windows, and spirit becomes luminous, its dark mirror bright; unobstruct the messengers, and the five viscera flow freely, free from disorder. When ears and eyes are indulgent in sounds and sights, the five viscera tremble and cannot settle, blood and qi surge and do not rest, spirit gallops and does not abide. Though blessings and misfortunes arrive like mountains, there is no means to perceive them.

Bewildered by sounds and sights, one enslaves the spirit, forgetting both self and other. Like still water, where even a hair’s breadth is visible, when one is obscured, even mountain-like calamities go unseen. Thus, the sage cherishes without transgressing; though desires may flow, they are ever restrained and regulated, never seeking what lies beyond. If the sage ensures ears and eyes are keen and profoundly penetrating, free from allure and longing, intent and qi unerring in clarity, with few desires, the five viscera find peace. Spirit guards within the form without straying, and one may observe beyond past ages, within future affairs, and between fortune and misfortune—all becomes fully visible.

The sage knows that desires obscure and obstruct clarity, so they unify heart and qi to halt disorder, guard spirit without transgression, and thus the truths of inner and outer become visible, the omens of fortune and misfortune naturally clear. Therefore, the farther one strays, the less one knows. Chasing distant things, prizing the near and valuing the self—what is discarded is great, what is gained is small. This speaks to spirit: it must not be allowed to stray outward. The five colors confuse the eyes, rendering them unclear; the five tones enter the ears, dulling their acuity; the five flavors disorder the mouth, causing sores and decay.

External desires bring internal harm. Pursuits and aversions disturb the heart, making conduct reckless. Desires cause qi to overflow, likes and dislikes exhaust the essence. If not swiftly abandoned, aspiration and qi daily diminish. When essence and qi leak away, form and spirit daily fade. The reason people cannot fulfill their natural lifespan is their excessive indulgence in life. "Excessive indulgence" means insatiable craving, seeking only gratification—truly, self-estrangement.

Only those who do not treat life as paramount attain longevity. Not treating life as paramount means not indulging excessively; not indulging excessively means not dwelling in places of certain death. Heaven and earth move and interpenetrate; the myriad things converge as one. To know the One is to know all; not to know the One is to know nothing. From heaven and earth to the myriad things, endless cycles all ride upon the One, giving rise to the ten thousand kinds. Though diverse, they share a root in the One. Thus, to know the myriad things as One is to comprehend all principles; not to know them as One is to fail in every encounter.

I dwell in the world as one thing among things—yet things are also things. How can things be distinguished from one another? This reveals the profound unity of self and other, where preferences and aversions have no ground. Thus, I dwell in the world as but one thing among the world’s things. Being of the same kind, how can they be distinguished? When self and other are both forgotten, right and wrong dissolve, and the blade glides through emptiness—this is the great mastery of preserving the true.

Desire for life cannot be served; aversion to death cannot be refused. Desires cannot be indulged, affairs cannot be multiplied. The lowly cannot be despised, nor the noble rejoiced in. Nobility and baseness find their proper place—where then lie likes and dislikes? Follow one’s endowment and find tranquility, daring not to exceed. Not exceeding, one attains the utmost joy.

This passage summarizes the Nine Guardians as the utmost sincerity for governing the state and cultivating the self. Those who turn toward the Dao should carefully ponder its essence.

**The Nine Guardians of Unity**
Laozi said: The so-called sage accords with the times and finds peace in their position, delights in their work amid the age. What others find uneasy, they make supremely peaceful; what others find joyless, they make supremely joyful. Grief and joy are deviations of virtue; likes and dislikes are burdens of the heart; pleasure and anger are transgressions of the Dao. The common person has them; the Daoist does not. In life, they follow the movement of heaven; in death, they transform with things. Life is not mine to possess—thus it is called "heaven’s movement." Death is not my end—thus it is called "transformation with things."

At rest, they unite with yin in virtue; in motion, they harmonize with yang in its current—this is movement and stillness without losing their rectitude. The heart is the life of the form; spirit is the treasure of the heart. Spirit is the abode of the heart, what people hold most precious. When the form labors without rest, it withers; when essence is used without cease, it depletes. Thus, the sage honors this and dares not transgress. The form is the dwelling of spirit; essence is the numen of qi. They depend on one another to preside; if they part, death follows. The sage values them and dares not use them lightly.

Responding to being with non-being, one must exhaust its principles; receiving the full with emptiness, one must fathom its limits. The myriad things arise from nothing, and nothing is not possessed. Heaven and earth receive with emptiness, and nothing is not contained. Serene, joyful, empty, and still, one fulfills their mandate. Preserving emptiness and stillness, one penetrates life and death. Nothing is estranged, nothing is favored. In nobility, one is not favored; in baseness, one is not estranged. Embracing virtue and kindling harmony, one accords with heaven, joins with the Dao as their boundary, and neighbors with virtue. They do not initiate fortune, nor precede calamity. Death and life bring no change to them—thus they are called "utterly spirit-like."

Accordant with the Dao and virtue, equal in death and life, fortune cannot aid them, nor calamity overthrow them. If not utterly spirit-like, how could one be thus?

The spirit-like attains all it seeks and accomplishes all it undertakes. Able to align with the Dao of heaven and earth, to unite in virtue with the numinous, whatever it seeks is attained, whatever it does is accomplished—this may be called "utterly spirit-like." Abiding in nothingness, Laozi said: "Lightening the world, the spirit remains unburdened; refining the myriad things, the mind is not deluded. If one does not treat the world as a thief of the myriad things, how can the spirit be burdened? If one is not a thief of the myriad things, how can the mind be deceived?"

Equalizing life and death, the will is not cowed; harmonizing with transformation, clarity is not dazzled. Knowing that life and death are but provisional names, one is not terrified; recognizing that transformation is but empty illusion, one is not startled. The perfected person leans upon an unshakable pillar, walks an unbounded path—the unshakable pillar is the Dao, the unbounded path is virtue. With the Dao as their pillar, they do not topple; with virtue as their path, all destinations are fitting.

Drawing from an inexhaustible storehouse, the spirit’s use is limitless. Learning from an undying teacher, their foundation is non-arising. There is nowhere they do not succeed, nothing they do not penetrate. In bending and stretching, bowing and rising, they embrace their mandate without confusion, navigating fortune and misfortune with ease. Profit and harm cannot trouble their heart; life and death do not press upon them—how could profit and harm weigh upon their mind?

Those who uphold righteousness may be pressed by benevolence but cannot be coerced by force; those who preserve righteousness may be exhorted by the Way of benevolence but cannot be threatened by military might. They may be guided by righteousness but cannot be lured by profit—valuing righteousness, they disdain profit. The noble person dies for righteousness and cannot be detained by wealth or honor; they would rather tread the path of righteousness unto death than live in ignoble prosperity. Those who act for righteousness cannot be frightened by death, for they die for righteousness and forget life—how much more so for those who practice non-action?

The non-acting are without burden; the unburdened take the world as a shadow pillar—a shadow pillar is emptiness and nothingness. Devoid of form and substance, where can burdens cling? If those who preserve righteousness cannot be threatened by force, how much less can those who embody the Dao be frightened by death?

Gazing upward at the ranks of the perfected, deeply tracing the intent of the Dao and virtue, and examining below the conduct of the vulgar world, one finds ample cause for advancement. To advance is to progress. Observing the past and present, embracing the Dao and guarding virtue, deeply understanding the purport—this is sufficient for cultivation.

Those who do not treat the world as their own are like striking a drum to silence it. The rulers of high antiquity, without exception, took the world as their own, not contemplating the ultimate Dao, acting with blatant self-interest—they were like one who beats a drum seeking silence, yet no response comes.

Laozi said: "High rank and great profit are what people covet, yet compared to the self, they are base. Those who value rank esteem the world and belittle the self. Those who cultivate the Dao treasure the self and disdain wealth. Thus, the sage eats enough to fill the void and join qi, wears enough to cover the body and ward off cold, satisfies their nature and rejects excess, neither coveting gain nor hoarding abundance, content with sufficiency and discarding the surplus.

"With eyes clear, they do not see; with ears still, they do not hear; with mouth closed, they do not speak; with mind released, they do not ponder. They discard cleverness and return to primal simplicity, rest the spirit and abandon knowledge, without likes or dislikes—this is called 'great penetration.' To remove defilement and cast off burdens, nothing surpasses never having departed from one’s origin. What could not be accomplished? Never having departed from one’s origin means abiding in original emptiness and quiescence, without craving or attachment—thus the myriad entanglements, all defilements and burdens, arise. Therefore, to dispel and cleanse, return to the origin and restore simplicity.

"Those who know the harmony of nurturing life cannot be lured by profit; those who align the inner and outer tokens cannot be enticed by power. Coveting profit harms life; yearning for the world obstructs the Dao—these are what the perfected do not do. The outer of the without-outer is supremely great; the inner of the without-inner is supremely noble. Able to know the great and noble, where do they not succeed? The great Dao emerges without outer, enters without inner—nothing it does not penetrate, and this may be called 'supremely noble.'"

Laozi said: "The ancients who embodied the Dao ordered their nature and emotions, governed their mind and techniques, nurtured them with harmony, and maintained them with suitability. Delighting in the Dao, they forgot baseness; content in virtue, they forgot poverty. Attaining the harmony of nature and emotions, they forgot the distinctions of poverty and lowliness.

"Nature has what it does not desire; without desire, nothing is unattained. The heart has what it does not delight in; without delight, all that suits is achieved. With desire, what is desired is never enough; without delight, all that is fitting is fulfilled. What does not benefit nature is not used to burden virtue; what does not aid life is not used to disturb harmony. Fame and profit harm virtue; cravings and desires injure life—thus they are not pursued.

"Not indulging the self or giving free rein to desires, yet their standards can serve as models for the world. Naturally able to be reverent and cautious, they may serve as exemplars. Eating according to the belly’s measure, clothing according to the body’s form, dwelling to shelter the self, acting to suit the emotions, possessing the world without claiming it, entrusting the myriad things without profiting from them—how could they lose their nature and life over poverty or wealth, nobility or baseness? Those who are thus may be said to embody the Dao.

"Only those who embody the Dao and know contentment possess the world in surplus without profiting from the myriad things—how could they follow desires and harm their nature and life?"

Laozi said: "People receive qi from heaven—the ears and eyes in relation to sounds and sights, the nose and mouth in relation to fragrances and odors, the skin and flesh in relation to cold and warmth—their nature is one. Noble and base share this. Some die, some live; some become noble persons, some petty persons—the means by which they are governed differ. What they desire is the same; what they attain is different. Following the heart’s desires, they are controlled by things.

"Nature has worthy and foolish, emotions have thick and thin. Thus some are noble, some base; some die, some live—it is not uniform. The spirit is the abyss of wisdom; when the spirit is clear, wisdom is bright, and nothing is left unreflected. Wisdom is the storehouse of the heart; when wisdom is impartial, the heart is level."

The mind moves without private gain.
No one gazes into flowing water to see their reflection,
but gazes into still water—
because it is clear and tranquil.
Water does not become clear by itself to reflect,
nor does the spirit reside without clarity.

Thus, when the spirit is clear and the mind is level,
one can discern the true nature of things.
Only clarity and levelness
can perceive the essence of things.

Therefore, those who act must rely on those who do not act.
This is the interplay of *you* and *wu* (having and non-having),
of form and spirit in mutual preservation.

A mirror’s clarity means dust cannot soil it,
a spirit’s clarity means desires cannot mislead.
Clarity preserves the body from defilement,
purity preserves the spirit from error.

When the heart reaches its limit,
the spirit responds with presence—
heart and spirit in communion,
so when the heart arrives, the spirit abides.

Returning to emptiness,
agitation dissolves, breath hides—
this is the play of the Sage.

The heart knows no restlessness, the spirit’s luminosity drifts freely.
Thus, to govern the world,
one must first grasp the nature of life and destiny.

**On Upholding the True:**
Laozi said:
The so-called Sage adapts to nature alone.
Eating to fill the belly, clothing to suit the body,
restraining oneself—
thus, the heart of greed cannot arise.
Cutting off greed, nature finds its ease;
restraining food, vitality remains whole.

Therefore, those who can possess the world
must not cling to the world.
Those who can possess the world
do not cling to it;
those who earn renown
do not seek it through transgression.
Those who attain great renown and govern the world
do not act with arrogance.
Thus, the world willingly follows without exhaustion,
and though praised, the Sage does not boast—
so the people revere him without burden.

When one truly understands life and destiny,
benevolence and righteousness follow naturally.

Unless one discerns the divisions of success and failure,
the dispositions of people and things,
the world will come of its own accord,
and the people will attach themselves naturally.

As for the spirit unobstructed, the heart unburdened,
open and uncluttered, serene and unconcerned—
power and profit cannot tempt,
sounds and colors cannot seduce,
eloquence cannot persuade,
wisdom cannot sway,
courage cannot frighten—
this is the play of the True Person.

The spirit’s clarity is such that things cannot obscure it,
affairs cannot confuse it.
Though power may topple kings and nobles,
wealth accumulate like mountains,
sounds linger like clouds,
colors bewitch nations,
eloquence flows like chains,
wisdom rushes like rivers,
courage surpasses lifting weights—
even an ordinary man cannot be swayed,
how much less the True Person?

The ever-generating does not generate,
the ever-transforming does not transform.
Only the non-generating can generate,
the non-transforming can transform.
Neither generating nor transforming,
yet it is the root of all generation.

Those who do not grasp this Dao,
though they may organize heaven and earth,
illuminate sun and moon,
unravel intricate arguments,
or polish words like gold and stone—
it profits nothing in governing the world.

Not knowing the principles of life and destiny,
not heeding the subtle shifts of danger—
though they may revel in qi that swallows the cosmos,
or debate like rivers—
though called divine, they are rotten.
What use are they to the world?

Thus, the Sage does not abandon his foundation.
What he despises is power and fame;
what he values is virtue and the Dao.

**On Cultivating Stillness:**
Laozi said:
Stillness, tranquility, and simplicity—
these nourish life.
Harmony, ease, and emptiness—
these establish virtue.

Without tranquility and ease,
how could one preserve the Dao?
External chaos does not disturb the internal—
nature finds its proper place.
Stillness and non-action bring harmony—
virtue settles into its rightful position.
Nourishing life to sustain the world,
embracing virtue to complete one’s years—
this is to embody the Dao.

External things cannot disrupt,
internal harmony arises naturally.
Nurtured with substance,
it returns to its natural state.

Thus, blood and vessels flow without stagnation,
the five viscera gather no excess qi.
Calamity and fortune cannot sway,
praise and blame cannot soil.
Body and mind are open and unrestrained,
emotions and qi flow freely.
Fortune and misfortune are indistinct,
praise and blame are irrelevant.

Without its age, who could save it?
Even with talent but no fitting time,
the body itself cannot escape—
how much less without the Dao?

When ruler and subject meet,
it is like dragons responding—
extraordinary rulers use extraordinary people,
a rare encounter, once in a thousand years.
Now, talent may suffice for the age,
yet the times lack virtue—
the heart should transcend conventions,
without further seeking.
Thus, Wen Zheng met his end,
Fan Li drifted on the river.

Hence, the wise and foolish differ by vast distances.

He who examines the tip of autumn hair
cannot hear the thunder’s roar.
He who tunes to the sounds of gold and jade
cannot see Tai Shan’s form.
Thus, when one focuses on the small,
the great is forgotten.

Concentrating the eyes blinds the ears,
fixing the gaze dulls hearing.
Though thunder’s roar is not faint,
the deaf do not hear it—
not because they are deaf,
but because the heart does not divide its attention.
Though Tai Shan’s form is not small,
the blind do not see it—
not because they are blind,
but because the will does not serve multiple purposes.

Thus, those who see profit forget the Dao,
who submit to things abandon their bodies—
many are such cases.

Now, all things come and seize our life,
pluck our essence like springs—
though one might wish to avoid them,
how could it be done?

The pleasures of sound and color,
the delights of taste,
the allure of gold and jade—
they bewilder and dim the mind,
obscure the spirit.
They gather like wine cups,
deepening into vast chasms.
Unless one is a True Person,
how could they rise like wings to the heavens,
or soar like hairpins over the sea of wisdom?

Now, if water in a basin is made clear,
it takes days to see one’s eyebrows—
yet if muddy, even a moment’s disturbance
prevents seeing even a square inch.

Human spirit is hard to clarify but easy to muddy—
like water in a basin.
Common emotions are easily stained by convention.
Knowing that which is easily stained,
one must fortify the difficult path.

Water’s nature resists clarity in a vessel.
Understanding that which resists clarity,
one must remove the easy-to-dim mirror.

**On Upholding the Law:**
Laozi said:
The highest Sage follows Heaven,
doing nothing.

「Next, they honor the worthy—this is artificial teaching. Below that, they entrust their ministers. Entrusting ministers is the path of peril and decline, for political power lies with men, and peril and decline have no fixed day.

Honoring the worthy is the root of folly and bewilderment, for when the ruler honors the worthy, those beneath him will distort their nature to conform. Those who distort their nature are not true to their essence, hence the saying: "folly and bewilderment."

Following the Dao of Heaven is the Way to govern the cosmos. The Way of ruling the world is to align with Heaven’s movement. To be empty and tranquil is the foundation—empty, so as not to receive; tranquil, so as to hold. By upholding purity and stillness, one can sustain the world and become its sovereign.

Knowing the Way of emptiness and tranquility, one may achieve both beginning and end. Therefore, the Sage rules through stillness and is undone by action. Those who remain in stillness have minds unperturbed; those who govern rightly thus prevent the world from chaos.

Hence it is said:
Do not disturb, do not entangle—
and all things will naturally clarify.
Do not alarm, do not terrify—
and all things will naturally settle.
This is called the Dao of Heaven.

Disturbance is agitation; alarm is disorder.
To speak of the Way to govern the people thus,
then all the myriad folk and myriad things will not lose their place.

**On Guarding Weakness:**
Laozi said:
The Son of Heaven, princes, and dukes
treat the world and a single state as their home,
and all things as their livestock.
With the vastness of the world in their embrace,
and the abundance of all things,
their *qi* becomes overbearing, and their wills grow arrogant.
They boast of their greatness—
though great, they must perish;
they fear their peril—
though perilous, they may endure.

The great act with military force against the small,
while the small defy and oppress those beneath them.
Those who rely on strength perish,
and those who oppress others are extinguished.

If one’s heart is extravagant and broad,
it is like a gusting wind or violent rain—
unable to last long.

The Sage knows that strength and violence cannot endure,
thus he governs with Dao and virtue,
holding to the One without action,
and does not harm the natural flow of *qi*.
Only the Sage understands that strength and violence cannot last,
so he restrains them with virtue and power,
aligning with the natural inclinations of love and dislike—
thus the harmonious *qi* is unharmed, and great peace is attained.

To see the small and uphold the weak,
to retreat without leaving traces,
is the way to avoid ruin and achieve grandeur.

Model oneself after the rivers and seas:
the rivers and seas do not force their way,
so their achievements and renown naturally manifest.
They do not rely on strength,
so they can fulfill their sovereignty.
The Sage’s virtue is not seized,
and all things come to him naturally.

As the female principle of Heaven,
he endures without death.
The Sage’s spirit—
unclouded in the Nine Abysses,
unfading in the Three Purities—
originates from the boundless beginning,
how could it have an end?

He cherishes himself,
so his nobility is complete.
The might of a thousand chariots,
with all things as his achievements and renown,
holds the heaviest of responsibilities—
he cannot treat himself lightly.
To treat oneself lightly is to lose one’s achievements and renown.

The Dao of the Sage is vast,
yet it completes itself through the small,
and abundant through the few.
The Dao achieves the great through the small,
and all things follow the multitude while revering the One.

Thus, the Sage governs the world with the Dao:
the weak, gentle, and subtle—
this is seeing the small.
The frugal and restrained—
this is seeing the few.
Seeing the small, he achieves the great;
seeing the few, he fulfills the perfect.

He who holds the world
does not abandon the ministers of small states,
so he achieves the great.
He who governs himself
does not discard even a fragment of good,
so he attains perfection.

The Dao of Heaven
presses down the high and raises the low,
reduces the surplus and supplements the deficient.
Heaven’s Dao abhors excess while favoring humility,
so the rivers and seas occupy the world’s lowly places,
and thus all things return to them and revere them.

The Sage practices humility, purity, and stillness,
declining honors—
this is seeing the low.
The rivers and seas regard vast capacity as their greatness,
and the Sage regards humility as supreme.

To be empty of the heart and without possession—
this is seeing insufficiency.
Seeing the low, one attains the high;
seeing insufficiency, one fulfills one’s worth.

The proud cannot endure;
the extravagant cannot last;
the forceful and rigid perish;
the full and self-satisfied are doomed.
Those who preserve emptiness and weakness endure long,
while the proud and extravagant meet swift ruin.

Gusting winds and violent rains do not last a day,
small valleys cannot remain full even for a moment.
Gusting winds and violent rains embody the *qi* of forcefulness,
so they cannot endure and are extinguished.
Small valleys occupy the land of the forceful,
so they cannot help but be seized.

Thus, the Sage holds to the female principle,
rejecting extravagance and arrogance,
daring not to act with the *qi* of forcefulness.
By holding to the female principle,
he establishes his masculine sovereignty.
By not daring to be extravagant or arrogant,
he endures long.

Only when a man does not indulge in arrogance,
holding to the female principle while embodying the masculine,
does he become the King of the Many Heroes.

Laozi said:
The Dao of Heaven reaches its extreme and turns back;
excess leads to depletion.
The sun and moon are examples of this.
Thus, the Sage daily diminishes himself,
so his natural *qi* does not dare to be full.
He daily advances with the female principle,
and his virtue and merit do not decline—
this is the Dao of Heaven.

Heaven’s Dao abhors excess while favoring humility,
so the Tang and Yu followed it and became great men.
The Dao abhors violence and forbids arrogance,
so Jie and Zhou disregarded it and met ruin.

Human nature and inclinations
all favor the high and abhor the low,
favor gain and abhor loss,
favor profit and abhor harm,
favor honor and abhor disgrace,
favor nobility and abhor meanness.
The common folk act thus,
so they cannot achieve greatness.
To grasp thus is to lose what one seeks.

Therefore, the Sage follows Heaven:
he does not act yet achieves,
he does not grasp yet attains.
The common folk follow convention,
favoring high and honor,
abhorring low and disgrace,
so they seek to be high yet cannot elevate themselves.

The Sage follows Heaven,
neither favoring high and honor
nor abhorring low and disgrace,
so he is not honored yet is naturally honored,
not high yet is naturally high.

He shares emotions with others yet differs in the Dao,
so he endures long.

He dims his brilliance,
aligning himself with the dust of the common.
Hence, the Three Sovereigns and Five Emperors had vessels of warning,
called *yòuzhī* cups.
When they were empty, they stood upright;
when they were full, they overturned.

Such vessels exist even today,
to preserve the utmost warning.
Hence, knowing emptiness brings self-preservation,
while excess cannot endure long.»

The Way of things is such:
When full, it decays;
when the sun is at its zenith, it declines;
when the moon is full, it wanes;
when joy reaches its end, sorrow follows.

The Dao of Heaven has its waxing and waning,
The Way of humanity has its rise and fall—
some begin with fortune yet end in misfortune,
some find joy in the front yet sorrow in the back.

Thus, the wise and learned guard their brilliance with simplicity,
for excess in knowledge leads to overanalysis.
Those with vast learning and eloquence guard with frugality,
for unrestrained speech harms what is true.

The strong and valiant guard with caution,
for relying on courage leads to recklessness.
The wealthy and noble guard with humility,
for clinging to arrogance brings many regrets.

The Sage who bestows virtue upon the world guards with modesty—
these five principles are how the ancient Kings preserved the world.

Those who embody this Way do not seek excess,
for only by avoiding excess can one prevent ruin from arising anew.
Diligently guarding one’s flaws and shortcomings,
not allowing them to swell into arrogance—
one who upholds this Way can preserve the world,
how much more so a single life?

Laozi said:
The Sage closes with yin and opens with yang.
What can be hidden is hidden,
what can be revealed is revealed.

To reach the state of no joy,
one must first attain the utmost joy—
no joy, then the highest joy is realized.
This is the joy within,
not the joy without;
thus, there is self-joy,
and thereby self-purpose.

To be cherished by the world,
one must first be the world’s necessity.
What is prized is not clanging music,
nor the eight tones,
nor refined colors or ornate beauty.

What is prized is clarity and emptiness,
the absence of action and desire—
this is the highest joy, yet not sought.

The joy within, outward,
brings joy to others,
and aligns one with Heaven.
The joy without, inward,
brings joy to oneself alone,
and becomes an enemy to the body.

Thus, to follow one’s own joy in joying others,
this is the essential Way of the world.
It does not lie in the external,
but in the self;
not in others,
but in the body.
When the body is at ease,
all things are gathered in harmony.

To seek it externally is to oppose the Dao;
to cultivate it within is to neighbor virtue.
Hence, those who grasp the principles of the heart’s cultivation
turn their cravings and aversions outward,
and thus find:
no delight, no anger,
no joy, no sorrow—
all things merge in the mysterious Oneness,
neither affirming nor denying.
When the heart’s cultivation is clear,
virtue and power will naturally form.

Sorrow and joy both forgotten,
likes and dislikes left in suspension—
all things unchanging,
naturally merge in the mysterious.

Thus, the scholar has fixed principles,
the woman has unyielding conduct.
The scholar’s Way is unshaken—
a thousand deceits cannot alter his resolve.
The woman’s conduct is steadfast—
a thousand gold cannot change her virtue.

He does not need rank to be honored,
nor wealth to be rich,
nor strength to be powerful.
He does not profit from goods and money,
nor crave worldly fame.
He does not find security in nobility,
nor peril in lowliness.
Form and body, qi and purpose,
each abides in its proper place.

Honoring the Dao, enriching virtue,
lightening rank, relinquishing profit—
purpose and qi clear, form and body harmonized.

The body is the dwelling of life,
qi is its foundation,
the spirit is its ruler.
If one loses its place,
all three are harmed.

These three—form, spirit, qi—
if the spirit departs,
form and qi wither.
If one loses its proper place,
where can the three find support?

Thus, those who take the spirit as master,
the body follows in benefit.
Those who take the body as master,
the spirit follows in harm.
Nurturing the spirit as master,
peace and stillness reside in the root—
then the spirit flows and qi is complete.

Nurturing the body as master,
if desires and harm have not yet taken hold,
the body perishes and the spirit flees.

As for life:
The gluttonous, greedy, and desirous,
are deluded by rank and profit,
bewitched by fame and position,
and though they possess others’ wisdom,
their spirit is consumed by worldly pursuits—
indulging without return,
the body’s gates closed in resistance,
and thus no way to enter.
Hence, there are times of utter forgetfulness and self-loss.

The spirit and purpose are hard to govern,
rank and fame are easy to be deceived by.
One must guard against inner resistance,
never opening to external influences—
yet still fear losing the means to serve the world,
and thus never be saved in life.

The spirit and purpose:
stillness makes them grow strong each day,
agitation makes them wither and grow old.
Stillness returns to no agitation,
and thus loses life.
Therefore, the Sage nurtures his spirit,
harmonizes and weakens his qi,
levels and smooths his form,
and rides the Dao’s rise and fall—
thus, all transformations of things
occur in perfect accord,
all changes of affairs
respond without fail.

When the spirit is harmonized,
qi is level,
purpose is strong,
and form is serene,
one can ride the Dao’s rise and fall,
adapt to the times’ changes,
and respond without doubt.

**Embodying Simplicity**

Laozi said:
The True Person’s nature aligns with the Dao—
thus, though he has, he seems without,
though he is real, he seems empty.
He cultivates within yet does not govern without,
illuminates the Great Simplicity,
acts without action and returns to the root.
He holds the origin and embraces the spirit,
wandering in the root of Heaven and Earth—
dimly resembling the dust and filth,
freely roaming in the business of no business.

To return to the root and embody truth,
is to wander in the root of Heaven and Earth.
To act without action, to have no business,
is to freely roam in the dust and filth.

Mechanical cleverness and contrived skill
are not carried in the heart.
He is certain in the unassumed,
does not shift with things.
True nature is already established,
external things cannot alter it.
Seeing the transformations of things,
he guards their essence—
his mind and intent focused within,
penetrating the unity of calamity and fortune.
Guarding the root without change,
seeing transformations without doubt,
fortune and calamity naturally clear—
how can elevation or decline bring burden?

He dwells without knowing what to do,
moves without knowing where to go—
his cultivation is effortless, without intent.
He knows without learning,
sees without looking,
accomplishes without doing,
discerns without governing.

Knowledge comes from the unknown,
vision from the unseen,
action from the inaction,
discernment from the undiscerned.

To know the unknown is true,
to know the known is false.
To respond and move,
to be pressed and act—
not to find one’s own way yet go,
like light’s reflection,
like shadow’s imitation—
moving like light,
flowing without stagnation.

The stillness is like a shadow,
dwelling while following the heart’s intent.

With the Dao as his guide,
he seems to have needs yet remains natural—
vast and empty,
clear and tranquil, yet nothing in particular.

He dwells alone in boundless openness,
yet suddenly appears to have needs.

He drifts without attachment,
yet truly has no concern at all.

A thousand lives as one transformation,
ten thousand forms as one root—
though the thousand lives differ,
they all ride upon the same transformation;
though the ten thousand forms vary,
they all emerge from the same emptiness.

He holds his essence yet does not expend it,
possesses spirit yet does not employ it,
preserving within an undisturbed depth,
without outward use or labor.

He upholds the pristine simplicity of *Taihun*,
establishing the utmost refinement within.
Nurturing truth and cultivating spirit,
his essence is pure, his spirit refined.

His sleep knows no dreams,
cutting off all thought.
His wisdom does not sprout,
holding no other concerns.
His movement is formless,
his spirit’s work subtle.
His stillness is bodiless—
existing yet as if nonexistent,
alive yet as if dead—
entering and exiting without obstruction,
commanding ghosts and spirits,
ascending to the Dao’s borrowed forms—
those who now say "existing is nonexistent,
living is death"
call it abandoning the body, discarding intelligence,
forsaking form and severing wisdom.

Thus, entering and exiting without obstruction,
commanding ghosts and spirits—
this is ascending to the Dao’s borrowed forms.

He lets his spirit flow freely,
yet does not lose his original *yuan*—
the *yuan* being the refined *qi*.
Day and night without pause,
he harmonizes with all things as spring—
like the sun of spring illuminating the ten thousand forms,
how could there be any boundary to shift?

This is the merging of life and transformation within the heart.
Not relying on external techniques,
only the heart aligning with the Dao.

Thus, though the body may weaken,
the spirit never transforms;
though the body may vanish,
the spirit remains ever present.
With the untransformed responding to transformation,
through myriad changes and endless turns,
yet never reaching an end—
only the untransformed can transform,
so hidden and revealed are boundless,
changes and transformations endless.

Transformation returns to the formless,
the untransformed coexists with Heaven and Earth—
so the ever-emerging never truly emerges,
its emergence is the very transformation;
transformation never truly transforms,
its transformation is the very transformation.
All things receive life and transformation,
yet cannot help but be born and transformed.
Thus, it sinks into the formless,
yet Heaven and Earth can generate and transform without ceasing.
Therefore, it is always generating, always transforming.

This is the true person’s wandering,
the pure and refined Dao.

Speaking of pure *qi* and exquisite subtlety,
wandering the path of neither birth nor transformation—
thus he is called the true person.

**End of Scroll Three of *Tongxuan Zhenjing***
#1 Love: Original *"Shòu"* (授), corrected to *"Zhě"* (者) per the Qú edition.
#2 *Zhě*: Original *"Yě"* (也), corrected per the Qú edition.

**Scroll Four of *Tongxuan Zhenjing*, with notes by Mòxī Zǐ.**

**Scroll Four of *Tongxuan Zhenjing* (Continued)**

**On the Symbol (符):**
The *symbol* is the *contract* (契).
The *word* is the *principle* (理).
Thus, through the subtle interplay of words and principles,
one awakens to the Dao and forgets the words—
this is indeed profound.

Laozi says:
The Dao is most exalted, without surpassing above,
most profound, without limit below.
Above, it transcends the highest;
below, it reaches the lowest—
thus it can be both lofty and profound,
ascending and descending.

Level as the plumb-line, straight as the square.
It is not the balance that makes it level,
yet there is no place not even.
It is not the square that makes it straight,
yet there is no place not upright.

Round as the compass, square as the carpenter’s rule—
it is not roundness that makes it round,
yet there is no roundness;
it is not squareness that makes it square,
yet there is no square.

It encompasses Heaven and Earth,
yet has no exterior or interior.
Its greatness knows no outer bound,
its subtlety knows no inner void.
Piercing as a covering, yet unobstructed—
the great circle has no edge,
the great passage has no hindrance.

Thus, those who embody the Dao:
neither angry nor joyful,
sitting without care,
sleeping without dreams,
seeing things yet naming them not,
actions arising yet responding naturally.
*(As previously explained.)*

Laozi says:
Those who cling to fame as a corpse
must generate affairs—
when affairs arise, they abandon the public for the private.
Those who cling to fame as a master
must have affairs—
when affairs arise, they lose harmony.
Thus, abandon fame and seek harmony.

To double the Dao and trust oneself,
to see praise and act virtuously,
to stand and be called "accomplished"—
*double* means to betray.
Betraying the Dao to seek praise is not true joy.
Conforming to trends to gain fame is not true virtue.

If governance does not align with principle,
and actions do not follow the times,
governance misaligned with principle brings many reproaches,
actions misaligned with the times yield no merit.
To align with principle is to use little effort yet achieve great deeds;
to ride the times is to expend much force yet see little gain.

To contrive for centrality—
when success is achieved, it is insufficient to fulfill obligations,
when failure occurs, it brings ruin to oneself.
Those who seek fame and immediate results,
who pursue harmony with the times—
their undertakings are unfinished,
their failures extend to themselves.

Laozi says:
Do not be a corpse of fame,
do not be a treasury of schemes,
do not be a burden of affairs,
do not be a master of cleverness.
Hide in the formless,
act without weariness.
Do not precede blessings,
do not initiate calamities.
Move without taking the lead—
then there is no form,
and no form means no welcome of fortune.
Respond only when called—
then there is no weariness,
and no weariness means no future calamities.

Begin in the formless,
act only when unavoidable.
To desire blessings first is to invite calamities;
to seek profit first is to court harm.
To govern before signs appear is to invite blessings;
to cut off extraordinary gains is to avoid harm.

Thus, those who act without action yet find peace—
if they lose what they rest in, they become perilous.
Those who govern without action yet rule—
if they lose what they govern, they bring chaos.
To lose what one rests in means
abandoning inner peace to seek it externally—
then one is constrained.
To lose what one governs means
forsaking oneself to govern others—
then one is deluded.

Thus, do not be like jade, polished and coveted,
nor like stone, rejected and ignored.
For jade and stone have their distinctions,
yet contention arises from them.
The finest carvings strip the skin,
the most beautiful horns bring slaughter.
Sweet springs will dry up,
straight trees will be felled.
Things of beauty invite harm,
people who crave fame summon misfortune.

Fine words bring later regret—
first to adorn speech,
then to invite ruin upon oneself.
Mountains yield jade, yet their peaks are scarred;
mountains that hide treasure are dug up.
People who speak carelessly invite calamities.
The commoner’s woes begin with idle words.

Moreover, the virtuous must be cautious—
even then, they have threefold silence.
The petty-minded, with their loose tongues,
how can they avoid disaster?

Laozi says:
The times move, and actions follow—
those who do not know Dao turn blessings into calamities.
People follow the times in their actions,
yet those who do not know Dao perish in their blessings.

The sage governs through the Dao:
knowing beforehand life and death,
measuring beforehand gain and loss.
Thus, expanding or contracting is without fixed form,
honor and disgrace do not disturb—
only then does one attain completion,
and good fortune preserves the body.

As for the ignorant,
they often receive blessings yet act tyrannically—
thus, when blessings reach their peak, calamities arise.
It is not that blessings and calamities cancel each other,
but that their actions are misaligned with their use.

Heaven is the covering, Earth is the axle—
those who skillfully use the Dao will never be exhausted.
Earth as the axle, Heaven as the covering—
those who skillfully use the Dao will never be harmed.
To use Earth as the axle is to cover without limit, all beneficent.
To use Heaven as the covering is to move without end, yet cause no harm.

The Five Phases are arranged—
each has its victor:
Metal and Fire attack each other.
The King of Chu succeeded in turn.

What Heaven covers is without exception,
Heaven’s Dao is vast and all-inclusive.

Thus, to know without knowing is superior,
to not know while knowing is illness.
To know the unknown is virtue,
to not know while knowing is folly.

Laozi says:
Mountains produce metal,
stones produce jade—
yet they erode each other.
Trees breed worms,
which devour them.
People create affairs,
which destroy themselves.
Fame brings the Dao’s decline,
undertakings invite harm.

Those who love affairs never fail to be entangled,
those who seek profit never fail to be exhausted.
No one crosses water without wetting their feet,
no one walks in dust without staining their body.
Skilled swimmers drown,
skilled riders fall—
each perishes by their own inclination.

Boasting of ability destroys their merit,
flaunting strength destroys their lives.
It must be so.

Success lies in timing, not in struggle,
governance lies in the Dao, not in the sage.
Timing is naturally obtained,
no need for forced contention.
The Dao lies in self-respect—
why bother with boasting of wisdom?

Earth dwells low yet does not vie for height,
thus it is stable and not perilous.
Water flows downward yet does not compete for speed,
thus it departs and is not delayed.

What makes the Dao precious,
what the virtuous hold in esteem—
is to be high without contending,
to be swift without haste.

Thus, the sage holds nothing fast and so loses nothing,
acts without action and so meets no defeat.
The Dao has no form or shape,
cannot be grasped—
to seize it is to lose it.
It is not a physical body,
cannot be carved—
to force it is to fail.

Laozi says:
One word cannot be exhausted,
two words make the world’s foundation,
three words make the feudal lords mighty,
four words make the world’s pairs.

Sincerity and trust cannot be exhausted,
virtue and Dao make the world’s foundation,
promoting the worthy makes the feudal lords mighty,
hating the few and loving the many makes the world’s pairs.

「Whoever attains all four phrases,
ascends to become the **Emperor** or **Son of Heaven**,
while those who grasp only one
descend to become **Conquerors** or **Assistants**.

Laozi says:
Humans have three deaths,
though not by fate.

Those who speak of "not by fate"
are themselves the cause.

Excessive indulgence in food and drink,
despising one’s own worth,
brings illness that kills;
unrestrained pleasure,
endless craving,
invites punishment that kills;
to assault the many with few,
to challenge the strong with weakness,
invokes war that kills.

Thus, death and life lie within us,
misfortune and blessing have no gate—
they do not descend from Heaven,
but arise from human deeds.

Laozi says:
The greater the gift,
the finer the return;
the deeper the grudge,
the graver the calamity.

To give little yet expect much,
to harbor resentment yet seek no harm—
such things do not exist.

Observe how one proceeds,
and one shall know whence they came.

Great deeds bring great rewards,
deep grudges invite great woes;
according to their weight,
one leaves behind neither favor nor enmity.

Laozi says:
Fathom **Heavenly Fate** (*tiān mìng*),
refine one’s **inner cultivation** (*xīn shù*),
balance **affection and aversion** (*hào zēng*),
harmonize **emotions and nature** (*qíng xìng*),
and the **Dao** is thus mastered.

To fathom **Heavenly Fate** (*tiān mìng*)
is to remain undisturbed by misfortune and blessing;
to refine one’s **inner cultivation** (*xīn shù*)
is to neither recklessly rejoice nor rage;
to balance **affection and aversion** (*hào zēng*)
is to avoid wasteful greed;
to harmonize **emotions and nature** (*qíng xìng*)
is to restrain desire without excess;
to remain undisturbed by misfortune and blessing
is to move and rest in accordance with **Nature’s Way** (*lǐ*);
to neither recklessly rejoice nor rage
is to neither favor nor punish arbitrarily;
to avoid wasteful greed
is to not let desire harm one’s **True Nature** (*xìng*);
to restrain desire without excess
is to nurture life by knowing contentment.

All these four principles
require no external seeking,
no reliance on others—
they are attained by turning inward.

To comprehend these four is to be **greatly enlightened** (*dà tōng*).

Do not depend on others,
only simplify and purify oneself.

Laozi says:
Do not seek to justify one’s actions,
do not resent others for criticizing you—
if one acts without deceit,
why would others blame you?

Cultivate the **virtue of benevolence and forgiveness** (*rén shù*),
and you will have no cause to reproach others.
If you strive for **unfailing reputation** (*rú*),
yet do not seek others’ praise,
and trust only in your own self-cultivation—
you cannot ensure misfortune will not come,
nor that blessing will always arrive.
You cannot prevent unforeseen calamity,
nor can you compel fate to grant your wishes.
Blessing that must come,
you must receive when it arrives.
Misfortune that comes,
is not of your own making—
so though in straits, why worry?
Blessing that comes,
is not of your own doing—
so though prosperous, why boast?

Thus, one finds joy in **idleness and ease** (*xián jū*),
governs without **action** (*wéi*),
and drifts content in **tranquility and freedom** (*yōu yóu*).

Laozi says:
The **Dao**-practitioner guards what they already possess,
and does not seek what they have not yet attained.
To seek what they have not yet attained
is to lose what they already possess;
to follow what they already possess
is to gain what they desire.

What one already possesses is the **spirit of the body** (*shēn qíng*),
what one has not yet attained are **multifarious techniques** (*qí shù*).
Now, if one abandons the **spirit of the body** (*shēn qíng*)
and begs for **multifarious techniques** (*qí shù*),
the unattained will not arrive,
and what was attained will be forgotten.
One cannot safeguard the **origin of attainment** (*dé yī*),
nor can one undo the **regret of double loss** (*liáng shī*).

Thus, the **Perfect Person** (*zhì rén*) holds fast to their **root** (*běn*),
does not chase after **external methods** (*mò*),
values what is **innerly attained** (*nèi*),
and governs what is **externally imposed** (*wài*).

If governance is not yet **firmly established** (*wèi gu*),
yet one acts as if governing—
this is perilous.
If conduct is not yet **free from fault** (*wú fēi*),
yet one rushes to seek fame—
this is self-destructive.

When the **root is firm**, the state is at peace;
