when conduct is **comprehensive**, one suffers no disgrace.

Thus, no blessing is greater than **freedom from misfortune**,
no gain is greater than **avoiding loss**.

To be free from misfortune is the **height of blessing**,
to avoid loss is the **greatest of gains**.

Thus, some things may seem to bring **benefit** yet cause **harm**,
while others may seem to bring **harm** yet cause **benefit**.
Only when free from misfortune and blessing
does one escape both **harm** and **benefit**.

What cannot be **urged** to seek **gain**
is the way to **settle the spirit** (*ān shén*)
and **avoid peril**.

The **Dao** cannot be **lured** by **gain**,
for without **gain**, there is no **harm**—
thus the spirit is naturally at ease,
and the **Dao** comes effortlessly.

Therefore, one who **tastes no misfortune** does not **taste blessing**,
one who **commits no wrong** does not **earn merit**.

To be free from misfortune and blessing,
free from wrong and merit—
this is called **great enlightenment** (*dà tōng*).

The **Dao** speaks thus:
**"Vague and indistinct** (*máng máng mèi mèi*),
follow **Heaven’s majesty** (*tīan wēi*),
align with **Heaven’s qi** (*tīan qì*),
without thought or calculation,
without scheming or storing."

The **Dao** says:
"Is the **Dao** a **ruler** (*jūn*)?"

**"Vague and indistinct"** (*máng mèi*)
refers to the **Dao’s profound mystery** (*yǎo míng*), unseen by mortals.

Now, if one merely **imitates Heaven** (*fǎ tiān*) with **emptiness** (*xū*),
**preserves the body** (*shēn*) with **non-action** (*wú*),
and **refines the heart** (*xīn*) without **scheming** (*lǜ*)—
then **virtues accumulate** (*wù jī*) without effort,
and **things manifest** (*shí chéng*) without labor.

**Departing** is not **forced away**,
**arriving** is not **summoned**—
one simply **allows it to unfold naturally** (*rèn zī zì dé*).

Though humans move **east, west, south, or north**,
they stand **alone at the center** (*zì yú zhōng yāng*),
their **bodies respond to things** (*shēn yìng wù*) yet **endure without limit** (*wú qióng*),
while the **Dao abides in the center** (*zhēn qì liú zhuǎn*) and **moves alone** (*dú yùn*).

Thus, though surrounded by **crookedness** (*qū*),
one does not lose their **straightness** (*zhí*),
for **completeness in flexibility** (*qū quán*) is **greatness**.

To flow with the **world** (*tiān xià*) yet **never stray** (*bìng liú bù lí qí yù*)
is the **Dao’s vital breath** (*zhī qì liú zhuǎn*),
its **true essence** (*zhēn jīng*) ever preserved.

Neither **pursuing goodness** nor **avoiding ugliness**,
one follows **Heaven’s Way** (*tiān dào*),
does not **begin with oneself** (*bù wéi shǐ*),
does not **cling to self** (*bù zhuān jǐ*),
conforms to **Nature’s principles** (*tiān lǐ*),
does not **prematurely plot** (*bù yù móu*),
does not **reject the appointed time** (*bù qì shí*),
aligns with **Heaven’s decree** (*yǔ tiān wéi qī*),
does not **seek what is obtained** (*bù qiú dé*),
does not **spurn what is blessed** (*bù cí fú*),
and follows **Heaven’s natural order** (*cóng tiān zhī zé*).

**Heaven** has no **intent** (*wú xīn*) nor **speaks** (*wú yán*),
yet all **things are born** (*wàn wù shēng rén*).
**Non-action** (*wú wéi*) and **no scheming** (*wú móu*)
yet all **affairs are accomplished** (*bǎi shì suì*).

Within, there is no **extraordinary blessing** (*qí fú*),
without, there is no **extraordinary misfortune** (*qí huò*),
so misfortune and blessing **do not arise** (*wú huò fú*),
and thus there is no **human thief** (*rén zéi*).

All blessings inevitably bring misfortune,
but now misfortune and blessing are **concealed** (*yǐ míng*),
who then **harms others** (*zéi hài*)?

Thus, **great virtue** (*zhì dé*)—
its words are **like a single axle** (*shìng biāo*),
its deeds are **like a single blessing** (*shìng fú*),
the **hearts of all** (*shàng xià yī xīn*) are unified,
there is no **divergence in paths** (*wú qí dào*),
and those who **perceive things partially** (*piān jiàn*) are **drawn back to error** (*tuì zhī yú xié*).

Open the **Way** to **goodness** (*kāi dào zhī yú shàn*),
and the people will **turn toward uprightness** (*mín xiàng fāng yǐ*).

Partial perception is insufficient to **persuade the common folk** (*huà sú*),
but the **true Way** (*zhèng dào*) can **guide the people** (*yǒu kě yòu mín*).

Laozi says:
To **do good** (*wéi shàn*) is to **exhort** (*quàn*),
to **do evil** (*wéi bù shàn*) is to **observe and restrain** (*guān quàn*).
To **exhort** (*quàn*) is to **invite censure** (*shēng zé*),
to **observe and restrain** (*guān*) is to **incite resentment** (*shēng huàn*).

**Exhortation** (*quàn*) means **encouraging oneself** (*rén wéi shàn*),
while **observation and restraint** (*guān*) means **seeing one’s own faults** (*chá jiàn jǐ guò*).
If one sees their own faults,
they will **turn toward uprightness** (*xiàng fāng yǐ*).

One cannot avoid doing good.

If one examines their own goodness while seeking out others’ faults to censure them, then misfortune will arise.
Thus, exhorting to goodness while failing to practice it—this is misfortune.

Therefore, the Way cannot be advanced to seek fame,
but can be withdrawn to cultivate the self.
Thus, the Sage does not act to seek fame,
nor does he display knowledge to gain praise.
Governance follows natural order,
and he remains uninvolved in external affairs.

To advance without embellishing wisdom to seek fame,
to withdraw and cultivate the self to govern oneself—
to push matters along naturally,
how could one seek human praise?

The doer may fail to succeed,
the seeker may fail to obtain,
while humans may reach their limits,
the Way remains unobstructed.

Humans may seek without attaining the Way,
but non-action (*wuwei*) suffices to encompass all.

With wisdom yet without action, one’s efficacy equals that of the unwisely;
with ability yet without exertion, one’s virtue equals that of the incapable.
Having wisdom yet appearing unwisely,
having ability yet appearing unable—
when the Dao is realized, human talent is extinguished.

The Sage’s supreme virtue fills within,
while human talent vanishes without—
thus, having yet appearing as lacking,
substantial yet seeming empty.

Humans and the Way cannot both be manifest;
if humans love fame, they will not use the Way.
If the Way surpasses humans, fame will cease;
if the Way ceases, human fame will flourish—then danger and ruin follow.

The Way must be unified;
affairs cannot be fully dual.
Laozi says:
To let honest men divide wealth,
it is better to fix portions by casting lots—
why?
Those with intent in leveling wealth
are inferior to those without intent.

To let virtuous men guard wealth,
it is better to seal the doors and keep it intact—
for those with desires among the common folk
are inferior to those without desires.

Casting lots resolves doubt through non-intent,
while the common folk are constrained by desire.

If one’s flaws are exposed, resentment arises;
if one’s ugliness is reflected, one will improve.
The wise, when their faults are pointed out, reflect and amend.
The foolish, self-praising, claim goodness.

If one interacts with others without entangling oneself,
one avoids burdens.
Prioritizing others over oneself,
one will never incur blame.

Laozi says:
When dealing with people,
one does not rely on treasures or gifts,
but on humble words—
simple offerings yet desires are never exhausted.
The noble do not value treasures;
they use modest attire and demeanor to show respect.
If one practices this,
over time, desires will not be sated.

Humble bearing avoids reproach;
discourse without entanglement—
binding oaths and alliances,
once agreed, are fulfilled as originally promised.
The noble’s bonds are not forged by oaths;
a single word suffices to settle matters for life.
The petty’s bonds rely on pledges,
yet within days, they betray their former agreements.

Thus, the noble do not externally display virtue and righteousness
while internally cultivating morality and virtue.
Internally, they embody sincerity and purity;
externally, they have no false adornment.

They govern affairs within their domain,
expand their territory’s reach,
exhort the people to defend to the death,
strengthen their walls and fortifications,
and unite the people to protect the ancestral altars.
They do not attack the innocent for gain,
nor do they assault what is difficult to obtain—
this is the Way of absolute preservation,
the principle of absolute benefit.

Sharing benefits with the people,
the people will gladly die for them.
Sharing intentions with the people,
the people will steadfastly defend them.

Those who seek fame are not greedy;
those who seek profit do not overstep boundaries—
this is the Sage-King’s Way,
the shared defense of the ancestral altars,
the unified fortification of borders.

Laozi says:
The Sage does not conquer his heart,
while the common folk do not conquer their desires.
When the heart prevails, the Way is complete;
when desires prevail, the heart is in peril.

The noble act with upright qi,
the petty act with perverse qi.
Internally, it aligns with nature;
externally, it conforms to righteousness—
moving according to principle,
unconstrained by external things—this is upright qi.

Indulging in tastes and sounds,
erupting in joy and anger,
without regard for future consequences—this is perverse qi.

Perverse and upright qi harm each other;
desires and nature conflict—
they cannot coexist,
one flourishes while the other declines.
Thus, the Sage reduces desires and follows nature.

The eyes love color,
the ears love sound,
the nose loves fragrance,
the mouth loves flavor—
when combined, they bring delight,
yet never separate from harm and desire.

The ears, eyes, nose, and mouth
know not what they truly desire—
all are governed by the heart,
each finding its place.

From this, it is clear that desires cannot be fully satisfied.

The six senses’ desires are all governed by the heart.
If qi is upright within,
desires will not harm nature.
If the heart is perverse externally,
falsehood will confuse the true.

Thus, uprightness and perversity lie within oneself;
gain and loss depend on the heart.
If the heart is upright,
how can external things burden it?

Laozi says:
To govern the body and nurture nature,
one must regulate sleep and rest,
moderate eating and drinking,
harmonize joy and anger,
adapt movement and stillness—
what is internal to oneself is attained,
and goodness is not sought externally.
Thus, perverse qi has no way to enter.

Adorning the exterior harms the interior;
indulging emotions harms the spirit—
those who see only surface appearances obscure the true.

He who never forgets his virtue as an honorable man
will surely exhaust his nature.
To speak of a man’s virtue without pause,
if even for a moment he is separated from it,
he will surely suffer humiliation.

Within a hundred paces, one must not forget they are a guest,
lest they burden their form.

To assist the body and govern nature,
one must moderate drinking and maintain harmony,
so that the heart’s qi remains balanced within,
and the spirit may be preserved.
The noble are cautious of subtle matters,
not because they are distant—
even within ten steps,
one must consider the danger of rotting wood;
in the blink of an eye,
unexpected misfortune may strike—
how can one be negligent?

Thus, those with splendid plumage are harmed by their bones,
those with lush foliage are harmed by their roots—
none under heaven can possess dual perfections.

The phoenix is harmed by its plumage,
spring blossoms are plucked for their fragrance.
Things with dual beauties,
affairs with dual benefits—
such things do not exist.

Laozi says:
Heaven has light,
yet does not fear the people’s darkness.
Earth has wealth,
yet does not fear the people’s poverty.

Heaven’s Way is bright and expansive,
penetrating even the deepest obscurity.
Earth’s bounty nourishes all things,
its benefits are boundless and reach everywhere.

The Sage’s supreme virtue and Way
stands like a towering mountain, unmoving—
the traveler takes it as their destination.
Straightening oneself while fulfilling others,
not bestowing, nor receiving in return—
thus, it is stable and enduring,
uninvolved with Heaven and Earth,
and thus cannot be taken,
cannot be repaid,
and thus cannot incur resentment.

The Perfect Person,
by name and reputation cannot be swayed,
by harm and desire cannot be toppled—
steadfast alone, immovable as a mountain.

With their constant sufficiency, they do not accept gifts;
when they take, they suffer no loss—
thus, when given to, they lack no virtue,
when taken from, they incur no resentment,
and thus they endure forever.

The skillful in anger must bear many grudges;
the skillful in giving must be skillful in taking.
Only by following the natural Way of Heaven and Earth
can one overcome the rules of the world.

Transcending the realm of joy and anger,
forgetting the feelings of giving and taking,
letting things unfold naturally,
to preserve the fullness of Heaven’s Way.

Thus, praise arrives and destruction follows,
good fortune comes and misfortune follows—
benefits become harms at their outset,
blessings become calamities before they unfold.
Not seeking benefit means no harm,
not seeking fortune means no misfortune—
the body remains whole as its natural state,
wealth and honor are but transient lodgings.

Praise is what people admire,
good fortune is what people covet.
But if one does not crave display,
there will be slander and resentment—
unless one utterly rejects praise and good fortune,
there will be no slander or resentment.
If one neither boasts nor flaunts,
naturally there is no calamity nor blessing,
and virtue and Way remain intact.

The body remains whole as its natural state,
wealth and honor are but transient lodgings.

Laozi says:
The Sage wears no strange or wondrous garments,
nor adopts bizarre or ostentatious conduct.
Their attire is simple, their actions unobserved—
they do not startle the crowd, nor act contrary to others.
They are open yet not extravagant,
humble yet not fearful,
honored yet not flamboyant,
hidden yet not humiliated,
different yet not strange—
poverty and prosperity are but fate.

Thus, they are not extravagant nor constrained.
Honor and obscurity are but the times—
thus, they are neither flamboyant nor humiliated.
Though they differ from others,
what is there to find strange?
To act in harmony without distinction—
this is called Great Harmony.
When actions are without distinction,
there is no obstruction—
this is Great Harmony.

Laozi says:
The Way is to straighten oneself and await fate,
when time arrives, one cannot force it forward,
when time departs, one cannot summon it back—
thus, the Sage does not advance to seek nor retreat to yield.
Straightening the body, awaiting fate,
following the Way, conforming to time—
not leading, not resisting.

Three years in accordance with time,
time departs and I follow,
three years in its absence,
time returns and I linger behind—
neither going nor coming,
steadfast in their place.

This speaks of the excess of going too far ahead,
the inadequacy of falling too far behind—
only by meeting time without resistance
can one stand firm in their place.

The Dao/Way has no favorites,
only virtue does it bestow.
When fortune arrives,
it is not what one sought—
thus, they do not boast of their merit.
When calamity comes,
it is not what one created—
thus, they do not regret their actions.
This has already been explained.

The mind remains tranquil and unburdened,
virtue is not weighed down—
without praise, they do not strive,
without honor or disgrace, they are not shaken.
The barking of dogs does not disturb them,
they trust in their own nature—
truly without excessive desires.
Self-illuminated, they have no falsehood—
thus, they are not shaken nor afraid.

Therefore, those who understand the Way are not confused,
those who know fate are not anxious.
To know the Way and know fate,
what anxiety, what fear?

When the Emperor’s death occurs,
their remains are left in the wild—
their spirits are enshrined in the Hall of Brightness,
for the spirit is nobler than the form.
This shows the ancient Emperors returning their remains to the wild,
not sealing nor erecting tombs,
to teach the people that all things must end;
enshrining spirits in the Hall of Brightness,
not flattering nor being extravagant,
to teach the people to know solemnity.

Thus, when the spirit governs the form, it prospers;
when the form overcomes the spirit, it declines.
Though wisdom is employed,
it must return to the spirit—
this is called Great Harmony.

Relying on the spirit, the form remains whole;
indulging desires, the spirit departs.
Unless one is truly enlightened,
how can they preserve it?

Laozi says:
The ancients who preserved themselves
rejoiced in virtue and forgot disgrace—
thus, their names did not sway their resolve.
They rejoiced in the Way and forgot poverty—
thus, benefits did not sway their hearts.
Hence, they could be humble yet joyful,
quiet yet unperturbed.

Virtue and Way complete the body,
poverty and deficiency bring no shame—
the heart and resolve remain unbroken,
benefits and fame do not confuse them.
Thus, they can be humble,
joyful in stillness,
and unperturbed.

Counting life by numbers,
worrying over the world’s chaos,
is like worrying over the drying of the Yellow River—
weeping to increase it, yet to no avail.
Thus, they do not worry over the world’s chaos,
but rejoice in their own self-order—
such a person can speak of the Way.

It is like one who does not worry over their life’s end,
but worries over the world’s disorder—
weeping a few drops of tears,
desiring to augment the Yellow River’s flow—
this is of no benefit.
Only by forgetting to rule others
and instead ordering oneself
can one speak of the Way.

Laozi says:
People have three resentments—
those of high rank are envied by others,
those of great office are despised by their superiors,
those of abundant wealth are resented by the people.
High yet able to be humble,
abundant yet able to disperse—
this is how one preserves their elemental good fortune.

The higher the rank, the lower the intent,
the greater the office, the smaller the heart,
the richer the wealth, the broader the giving—
cultivating these three, resentment does not arise.

Thus, nobility takes humility as its root,
height takes lowliness as its foundation.
If these three are not cultivated,
calamity befalls oneself.

Laozi says:
Words are used to convey one’s own nature to others,
listening is used to receive others’ nature into oneself.
Expressing one’s own nature to reach others’,
obtaining others’ intentions to harmonize with one’s own.

If one is both blind and deaf,
the Way of humanity is blocked—
thus, those afflicted with blindness and deafness
do not know how to harmonize.
Is it only the body that can be blind and deaf?
The heart also has its own obstructions—
the eyes cannot see the great mountains,
the ears cannot hear the thunder—
this is the body’s blindness and deafness.
To discern a deer or a horse but not their reflections,
to know chili and wheat yet not their distinctions—
this is the blindness and deafness of humanity.

If one cannot discern even ordinary matters,
how much less can they grasp the Great Way?
Those who do not know how to harmonize
are of the kind blind and deaf.

The Way as the ultimate source,
all things with form arise from it—
its kinship is vast.
Those who nourish on grains and breathe on *qi*,
all attain longevity—
its lordship is vast.
All who possess wisdom learn from it—
its guidance is vast.

Life arises from the Way as its kin,
formless yet forming—
its kinship is great.
Grains and *qi* are its lords,
not conferring longevity—
its lordship is vast.
Wisdom is its teacher,
not conferring enlightenment—
its guidance is vast.

The virtue’s generosity is profound,
wisdom takes learning as its teacher—
not a teacher yet teaching—
its clarity is supreme.

All men harm the useful with the useless,
toiling in vain while wounding deep affections.
Thus knowledge unbroadened knows no end,
the noble man ever laments his limits,
striving to spur himself onward.

To ask of *weiqi* while playing *yìshí*,
one’s hearing and seeing grow profound.
To redirect *yìshí*’s efforts toward Dao and virtue,
one may attain profound subtlety.

Not to hear nor ask is like the blind and deaf among men.
To hear yet not question—this is true blindness and deafness.

Laozi said:
*"By nature, men’s hearts submit to virtue,
not to force—
one may govern with virtue,
but never with force."*

Virtue comes or does not come of its own accord.
Virtue bestowed upon others expects no return.

Thus the Sage who desires to be honored by others
first honors them,
who desires to be esteemed by others
first esteems them,
who desires to overcome others
first overcomes himself,
who desires to be humble before others
first humbles himself.
Hence nobility and humility, esteem and lowliness—
Dao governs them.

The ancient Sage-Kings spoke humbly to the people,
placed themselves behind them,
and though the world joyfully pushed them forward,
they bore it without resentment,
though the world burdened them,
they carried it without weariness—
this is virtue’s abundance and *qi*’s harmony.
Thus, to give is to take,
to follow is to lead—
this is the subtle Dao.

To honor others is not to take honor from them,
to precede others is not to take precedence from them—
this is *qi* harmonized with Dao,
virtue returning to oneself.
Thus they are pushed forward without resentment,
borne without burden.

Laozi said:
*"Few virtues yet many honors—reproach,
little talent yet high rank—danger,
great rewards without great deeds—disaster.
Thus some gain is loss,
some loss is gain.
When talent and office do not align,
ruin is inevitable.
Gain and loss follow one another—
calamity and fortune verify this."*

The common man knows only to profit from profit,
yet does not know that harm is profit’s disease.
Only the Sage knows harm as profit,
profit as harm.

The common man knows profit as profit,
yet does not know profit as harm’s root.
The Sage knows profit as harm,
takes non-harm as profit.

Thus twice-planted trees wound their roots,
houses hoarding wealth invite later calamity.
Great profit turns to harm—
this is Heaven’s Way.
Trees replanted must wound their roots;
houses storing treasures must invite ruin upon themselves.
What comes not by intent—
first profit, then harm—
this is Heaven’s Way.

Laozi said:
*"The petty man acts for ‘mere gain,’
the noble man acts for ‘mere righteousness.’
He who does good does not seek fame,
yet fame follows him.
Fame does not await profit,
yet profit comes to him.
Their aims are the same,
their ends differ.
The petty man acts for ‘mere gain’ as profit,
and profit follows him.
The noble man walks the straight path,
not awaiting profit,
yet profit comes to him.
Thus receiving profit is the same,
yet meeting harm differs.
Hence action brings benefit—
loss follows it.
Words without fixed truth,
actions without fixed propriety—
this is the petty man.
Unsteady in virtue,
he invites shame.
To perceive one thing,
to master one skill—
this is the mediocre man.
His vision unbroadened,
he is confined to one domain.
To encompass all and possess all,
to wield skills and employ talents—
this is the Sage.
To dismiss the wicked, banish evil,
to employ the worthy, use the capable—
this is the Sage.

Laozi said:
*"Life is what we borrow,
death is our return.
Thus in times of order, one guards the body with righteousness;
in times of chaos, one guards righteousness with the body.
The day of death is the end of one’s path.
In ordered times, one preserves the body with righteousness;
in chaotic times, one dies for righteousness.
Thus the noble man benefits others—
though he dies, he regrets nothing.
He views death as returning home,
just as life’s years.
Thus the noble man need only be cautious in one thing:
to act in accordance with Dao,
never straying from the true path.

Life is what we receive from Heaven,
fate what we encounter in time.
To have talent yet not meet the age—
this is Heaven.
To seek it has its method,
to obtain it lies in fate.
To meet the time—
not to meet Heaven—
to gain it without joy,
to lose it without blame.
The noble man can do good,
yet cannot guarantee fortune.
He cannot bear to do wrong,
yet cannot be certain to avoid calamity.
The noble man does good,
yet does not necessarily receive blessing.
To abandon evil does not necessarily distance calamity,
yet he never forsakes righteousness to seek fortune.
Easy to practice, easy to escape calamity—
why?
Because righteousness cannot be altered,
and the heart harbors no duplicity.
Thus the noble man meets the time and advances,
obtaining it through righteousness—
what fortune is there?
He does not meet the time and retreats,
yielding with propriety—
what misfortune is there?
Thus though he dwells in poverty and lowliness,
he never regrets it—
he has found what is truly noble.
The noble man does not consider advancement fortune,
for righteousness has given it to him.
He does not consider misfortune shame,
for what is there to regret?
What is cherished—Dao and righteousness—
how can it compare to poverty and lowliness?

Laozi said:
*"Man has harmonious and discordant *qi*,
born from the heart.
When the heart is orderly, *qi* is harmonious;
when the heart is disordered, *qi* is discordant.
The heart’s order or disorder lies in Dao and virtue.
To attain Dao, the heart is orderly;
to lose Dao, the heart is disordered.
An orderly heart brings yielding,
a disordered heart brings contention.
Yielding brings virtue,
contention breeds bandits.
Virtue brings harmonious *qi*,
bandits bring discordant *qi.
Unified in heart, *qi* is harmonious and upright;
divided in *qi*, it is discordant and perverse.
Uprightness elevates Dao,
perversity breeds harm.
Dao persists, the spirit clears,
clarity brings harmony and order.
Bandits arise, *qi* grows turbid,
turbidity brings contention and chaos.
Once turbid and chaotic,
ruin is inevitable.
Harmonious *qi* diminishes itself to nourish others,
discordant *qi* diminishes others to nourish itself.
These two *qi* may be mastered by Dao
and governed by oneself."*

The governance of trifles is difficult,
yet it can be mastered through Dao.

The Dao of Heaven is like the echo responding to sound:
virtue accumulates, and blessings arise;
calamity accumulates, and grievances arise.
Humans may act upon it,
Heaven may observe it—
good and evil will surely manifest,
just as echo follows sound.

Official virtue declines when office prospers,
filial piety wanes when wives and children thrive.
Calamity arises from neglecting worry,
disease recurs when near recovery.
Thus, to treat the end as the beginning,
there will be no failed undertakings.

Official virtue fails when law is abandoned,
filial piety fades when hidden in private matters.
Though worry may briefly ease,
calamity still lurks.
When near recovery, restraint must still be observed—
thus, to treat the end as the beginning,
there will be no failed undertakings.

Laozi says:
*"To raise the crooked with the straight—
how can this not be wrong?
To raise the straight with the crooked—
do not let it proceed,
for this is like mixing filth with different muds."*

Knowing people is not easy;
when selecting them, clarity is essential.
Now to raise the crooked as straight,
the foolish as wise—
how can this be like mixing filth with different muds?

Laozi says:
*"The sage unifies life and death,
the fool also unifies life and death.
The sage unifies life and death,
knowing the distinctions of principle;
the fool unifies life and death,
unaware of where harm and benefit lie.
The sage transcends life and death,
neither benefiting nor harming others,
thus there is no life or death.
The fool distinguishes life and death,
fixating on gain,
thus he fears death and life."*

Dao hangs from Heaven,
things spread across Earth,
harmony rests in humans.
If the ruler is not harmonious,
then Heaven’s *qi* does not descend,
Earth’s *qi* does not ascend,
yin and yang are unbalanced,
winds and rains are untimely,
and the people suffer hunger and sickness.
Dao depends on Heaven,
things arise from Earth,
harmony lies in humans.

Humans are Heaven’s essence,
Earth’s spirit.
Thus, as ruler over humans,
one must cultivate harmony,
stabilize *qi*,
nurture all things—
then winds and rains will not falter,
and calamities will not arise.

Laozi says:
*"To command ten thousand soldiers
is not as valuable as hearing one correct word;
to possess the Huisi pearl
is not as valuable as knowing the origin of things;
to possess the He Shi jade
is not as valuable as knowing the proper course of things.
One beneficial word surpasses ten thousand soldiers,
one proper action surpasses precious jade.
Though the world be vast,
those who love war perish;
though a state be secure,
those who love conflict are endangered.
Thus, small states with few people,
though possessing tenfold weapons,
should not use them.
Great states should cultivate virtue,
small states should serve others—
then conquest and war will not arise,
and all will be at peace."*

Laozi says:
*"Those who can achieve hegemony and kingship
must be certain victors—
they do not lead unless first.
Those who can defeat enemies
must be strong—
without virtue, they cannot prevail.
Those who are strong
must rely on human strength,
and those who rely on human strength
must win the people’s hearts—
using the worthy’s strength,
gaining the masses’ loyalty.
Those who win the people’s hearts
must first be self-possessed—
self-possessed, they are gentle and yielding,
able to conquer those weaker than themselves,
yet when facing equals, they yield.
Gentle strength emerges from yielding to equals,
and their affairs cannot be altered—
thus, though many oppose them,
they achieve great victory.
Only through preserving humility and gentleness
can they not be bent by the masses,
thus securing their triumph."*

The *Tongxuan Zhenjing* concludes, Volume Four. #1 [□], the original text unclear.

The *Tongxuan Zhenjing* concludes, Volume Five. **Defaulted Son’s Annotation** on *Dao/Way and Virtue/Power*:

This chapter first inquires into *Dao/Way and Virtue/Power*, then returns to *rites and wisdom*.
Though the previous volume has already made this clear, the inquiry is raised again—
for its profound and subtle nature,
it demands meticulous examination,
to establish the path for future disciples to awaken to the Way.

The *Wenzi* asked of *Dao/Way*,
and the *Laozi* replied:
*"Learning and inquiry must be precise,
listening to the Way must be profound.
Without learning, one does not know;
without precision, one does not reach.
All who listen seek to attain wisdom,
to perfect action,
or to achieve merit and renown.
Doubt leads to inquiry,
listening demands scrutiny—
like striking a bell,
its resonance must not be hollow.
Only then will there be true benefit
in attaining merit and renown.
Without precision, it remains unclear;
without depth, it is not comprehended.
Thus, the highest learning listens with the *spirit*#1,
scanning the profound without omission.
The middle learning listens with the *heart*,
sometimes retaining, sometimes losing.
The lowest learning listens with the *ears*,
fleeting as the wind passes.
Those who listen with the *ears*
have learning only on the surface,
those who listen with the *heart*
have learning in the sinews,
those who listen with the *spirit*
have learning in the marrow.
Thus, if one’s listening is not profound,
one’s knowledge will be unclear;
if one’s knowledge is unclear,
one cannot exhaust its precision;
if one cannot exhaust its precision,
one’s actions will not be perfected.
The *Dao/Way* and *Virtue/Power* are lofty and wondrous—
if one sees them clearly,
then one’s achievements will stand firm.

The principle of listening is this:
an empty mind, serene and tranquil,
diminished *qi* without excess,
no thought, no concern,
eyes never forgetting their gaze,
ears never heedlessly listening—
concentrating with unwavering focus#1,
internalizing intent fully.
Once obtained, it must be steadfastly held,
enduring forever.
This is the method of *spirit-listening*,
the path to awakening to the Way.
Once obtained, it must be guarded well—
good listeners do not forget,
good embrace#2 does not slip away.

The *Dao/Way* originates from the beginningless;
to listen to its principles, one must first clarify its source.
It begins with *gentleness and yielding*,
yet culminates in *firmness and strength*—
begins with *fewness and smallness*,
yet culminates in *abundance and greatness*.
A ten-encircled tree begins with a sapling,
a hundred-foot-high terrace begins with a foundation.
This is the *Dao/Way* of Heaven.
From non-being to being,
from subtlety to manifestation,
Heaven’s *Dao/Way* is ever thus—
how much more so for humans?

The Sage follows this *Dao/Way*,
and thus the humble *lower themselves*#3,
the retiring *step back*#3,
the frugal *remain modest*#3,
the self-diminishing *keep their numbers few*#3.
By humbling themselves, they are exalted;
by stepping back, they take precedence;
by remaining modest, they grow vast;
by keeping their numbers few, they become great—
this is the *Dao/Way* of Heaven.

Most people exalt themselves while belittling others,
thus losing what others hold dear.
The Sage places others before himself,
thus gaining what others hold dear.
Thus, to oppose the natural order brings no response,
while to align with Heaven brings spontaneous devotion without coercion.

The *Dao/Way* is the source of *virtue/power*,
the root of Heaven,
the gateway to blessings—
all things await it to arise,
await it to flourish,
await it to rest in peace.
The *Dao/Way* is the sovereign of life and transformation,
*virtue/power* is the nourishment for cultivation.
The root of all things must await it to manifest,
the gateway of all blessings must pass through it to unfold.

The *Dao/Way* is actionless and formless,
yet through actionlessness all things arise,
through formlessness all things transform.
Internally, it cultivates the self;
externally, it governs the people—
achievements stand firm,
affairs are established.
In harmony with Heaven,
it does nothing yet accomplishes all.
Cultivating the self, governing the people—
actionless and formless,
in harmony with Heaven,
in unity with the *Dao/Way*,
and thus all is at peace.
None know its essence,
none know its truth—
yet within its depths, faith resides.
Though its substance is not perceptible,
nor its truth recognizable,
yet within its mysterious expanse,
faith is indeed present.

If the *Son of Heaven*#4 has the *Dao/Way*,
then all under Heaven submit,
and he endures with the *altars of soil and grain*#5.
If the *dukes and marquises*#6 have the *Dao/Way*,
then the people are harmonious,
and their states remain unlost.
If the *commoners and scholars*#7 have the *Dao/Way*,
then they preserve their lives,
and safeguard their kin.
From the *Son of Heaven* above
to the commoners below—
all should guard the *Dao/Way*,
secure the state,
harmonize the people,
preserve their lives,
and safeguard their kin.

The strong and mighty with the *Dao/Way*
conquer without battle;
the weak and small with the *Dao/Way*
gain without strife;
undertaking affairs with the *Dao/Way*
brings success and blessings.
Rulers and ministers with the *Dao/Way* are loyal and benevolent,
fathers and sons with the *Dao/Way* are affectionate and filial,
scholars and commoners with the *Dao/Way* love one another—
thus, with the *Dao/Way* there is harmony#2,
without the *Dao/Way* there is harshness.

From this, it is evident:
the *Dao/Way* is fitting for all people.
To practice the *Dao/Way* in small ways brings small blessings,
to practice it in great ways brings great blessings,
to practice it fully brings all under Heaven’s submission—
submission leads to devotion.
All who practice the *Dao/Way*
trace their blessings to its roots.
Thus, the *Emperor*#8 is the fitting destination of all under Heaven,
the *King*#8 is the path all under Heaven follows—
if all under Heaven do not find fitting destination or path,
then one cannot be called *Emperor* or *King*.
This is because they lack the *Dao/Way*—
the people do not come of their own accord.
Though they occupy their positions,
how long can they endure?
Thus, without the people, even *Emperors* and *Kings* cannot succeed—
the state depends on the people,
and a stable foundation ensures lasting peace.
To gain the people yet lose the *Dao/Way*
is also unable to endure.
To have the people but lack the *Dao/Way*
is called an *empty state*.

The greatest crime is to lose the *Dao/Way*,
the deepest grudge is to lose *virtue/power*—
this is the *Dao/Way* of Heaven.
Great crimes and deep grudges
cannot fail to bring ruin to those who hold states,
cannot fail to bring death to those who hold lives—
for their *Dao/Way* is lost,
their *virtue/power* extinguished—
this is Heaven’s reason for their destruction.

The *Laozi* says:
*"Those who practice the *Dao/Way*
make others, though bold,
unable to pierce them with a blade;
though cunning,
unable to strike them.
If they are bold yet do not dare to pierce,
or cunning yet do not dare to strike—
this is better than making them, though bold,
suffer humiliation without fear of piercing,
or though cunning,
suffer scorn without fear of striking.
For those who do not dare to act,
it is not because they lack intent—
but rather to make others lack intent entirely."*

「Thus, those who lack intent
are without hearts of affection or harm—
the *Dao/Way*-practitioner is bold yet unharmed by piercing,
is cunning yet never struck.
Though called harmless,
they are already shamed by the deaf and mocked by the vulgar,
while the extraordinary lies in the *Dao/Way* itself,
and such is termed child’s play.

Better is to make others not even conceive the intent to pierce or strike,
while I hold no affection for gain or harm,
forgetting the artifice of the world—
thus the *Dao/Way* remains whole.

Better still is to make all men and women of the world
willingly embrace love for gain and harm,
as if they were naturally inclined—
for then, without territory, I may be lord,
without office, I may be chief.
All under Heaven willingly seek security and benefit,
and thus the *Regan* (Regan) of *Shi* (Shi) lineage
and the *Sucai* (Sucai) of *Kong* (Kong) family
are but such men.

Thus, boldness in daring brings death,
boldness in not daring brings life.
Boldness in daring leads to ruin,
boldness in not daring ensures survival.

When *Wenzi* (Wenzi) inquired about *virtue/power*,
*Xiang* (Xiang) already knew the *Dao/Way*.
Now he asks again of *virtue/power*,
which includes *ren* (仁) and *yi* (义),
and follows with *li* (礼) and *zhi* (智).
Unless he asks broadly,
how could he grasp the whole?

The *Laozi* says:
*"To nurture and rear,
to fulfill and grow—
comprehensive benefit without discrimination,
aligning with Heaven and Earth—
this is called *virtue/power* (德).
To nurture and complete,
without action or selfishness,
spreading beneficence to all things,
harmonizing with Heaven and Earth—
this is called *ultimate virtue/power* (至德).

What is *ren* (仁)?
It is: when ruling, not boasting of one’s achievements,
when serving, not shaming one’s faults.
Toward the great, not boasting,
toward the small, not deceiving—
comprehensive love without selfishness,
enduring without decay—
this is called *ren* (仁).
Though noble as a Son of Heaven, one does not grow proud,
though lowly as a commoner, one does not fret—
kindness and grace without favor,
broadly bestowing and aiding all—
this is *ren* (仁).

What is *yi* (义)?
It is: when ruling, to support the weak,
when serving, to uphold one’s principles.
When prosperous, not to indulge recklessly,
when in adversity, not to abandon one’s integrity—
one course in accordance with reason,
never perverting or distorting for private gain—
this is called *yi* (义).
To uphold the fallen and rescue the drowning,
to remain steadfast in hardship and guard one’s principles,
to follow what is appropriate and act in accordance with reason—
this is *yi* (义).

What is *li* (礼)?
It is: when ruling, to be reverent and stern,
when serving, to be humble and respectful.
To withdraw and yield, to uphold softness,
to be the feminine complement to the world—
to stand where one does not dare,
to establish where one cannot—
this is called *li* (礼).
To honor and cherish, to comfort those below,
to humble oneself and prioritize others,
to embody the *virtue/power* of modesty and gentleness,
without slackness or arrogance—
this is *li* (礼).

Thus, refining one’s *virtue/power* makes those below obey,
cultivating *ren* (仁) makes those below not contend,
practicing *yi* (义) makes those below fair and upright,
observing *li* (礼) makes those below honor and respect.
When all four are refined,
the state is secure and peaceful.
If any of the four are lacking,
ruling with them brings state collapse,
practicing them brings self-ruin.

Thus, *Dao/Way* is the source of all things,
*virtue/power* is what sustains growth,
*ren* (仁) is what brings love,
*yi* (义) is what brings order,
*li* (礼) is what brings reverence.
When all five are cultivated,
there is none under Heaven who can rival them.

Without nurturing or rearing,
one cannot fulfill growth.
Without kindness or love,
one cannot achieve completion.
Without order or rectification,
one cannot endure long.
Without reverence or esteem,
one cannot attain nobility and worth.
Thus, *virtue/power* is what the people hold dear,
*ren* (仁) is what the people cherish,
*yi* (义) is what the people fear,
*li* (礼) is what the people revere.
These four are the harmonious sequence of *Wenzi* (Wenzi),
the means by which the Sage governs all things.

Possessing these four *virtues/powers*,
one is called a Sage—
thus they can follow Heaven’s will,
and rule all beings.

If a *junzi* (君子) lacks *virtue/power*,
those below will resent them.
If they lack *ren* (仁),
those below will contend.
If they lack *yi* (义),
those below will act violently.
If they lack *li* (礼),
those below will rebel.
When all four are absent,
they are called without *Dao/Way* (无道).
None without *Dao/Way* can endure.

When the *Dao/Way* is hidden,
the four *virtues/powers* are abandoned.
*Wenzi* (Wenzi) inquired of its beginning and end,
and the *Laozi* expounded its gains and losses.
If all four are neglected,
resentment and violence will arise,
contention and disorder must follow—
thus, without *Dao/Way*,
ruin is immediate.

The *Laozi* says:
*"In the age of *ultimate virtue/power*,
merchants find profit in trade,
farmers take joy in the fields,
great officials are content in their duties,
scholars refine their *Dao/Way*,
and the people delight in their work.
Without the transformation of *ultimate virtue/power*,
how could each find joy in their station,
and take delight in their work?
Thus, the winds and rains do not wither or break,
grass and trees do not die young,
the Yellow River bears the *Tuxing* (图) [i.e., *Yijing* (易经) eight trigrams],
the Luo River yields the *Hongfan* (洪范) [i.e., *Shu* (书) Nine Categories].
Only *virtue/power* moves Heaven,
and beneficence touches all things—
this is the Sage’s ultimate governance achieved.

As the age declines,
taxes and levies are without measure,
slaughter and executions know no end,
and those who counsel with punishments
kill the virtuous scholars.
Thus, mountains crumble and rivers dry up,
serpents writhe without rest,
and the wilds lack even a hundred stalks of grain.
The rulers of the *Jiji* (季) age
undermine the fundamental principles,
destroy the traditions,
slay the virtuous and favor the flatterers,
hoard wealth without restraint,
and neglect duties without end.

Defying *qi* and provoking calamity,
their deeds reach Heaven above,
while stars and constellations behave strangely,
and their effects manifest on Earth below—
thus, mountains crumble and rivers run dry,
people have no means to survive,
and all insects and plants lose their proper place.
Only the ruler must be warned against this.

Thus, in times of order,
the foolish cannot act alone in disorder,
and righteousness does not tolerate the crooked.
In times of chaos,
the virtuous cannot rule alone.
Few cannot overcome many.

The Sage is harmonious, joyful, and tranquil—
this is their life.
The *Dao/Way* of *ultimate virtue/power* is realized—
this is their fate.
Thus, life meets fate,
and only then can they act.
Fate is obtained,
and only then can they be clear.
There must be their age,
before there can be their person.

Life meets fate,
and the Sage arises.
The lofty *wudi* (梧) tree naturally draws the *lingfeng* (灵凤),
while the shallow pool cannot contain the great scales.
*Wenzi* (Wenzi) inquired of the Sage’s wisdom,
asking of the Sage and of wisdom.

The *Laozi* says:
*"To hear and know is wisdom (圣);
to see and know is insight (智).
Thus, the Sage always hears where misfortune and fortune originate,
and chooses their *Dao/Way*.
The wise always observe where misfortune and fortune take form,
and choose their actions.
To act when one sees opportunity,
to know difficulty and cease—
The Sage knows the *Dao/Way* of Heaven’s auspicious and inauspicious,
and thus knows where misfortune and fortune originate..."」

The wise see the form before it takes shape,
and thus know the gates of misfortune and fortune.

The Sage discerns Heaven’s auspicious and inauspicious *borrowing and covering* (倚伏),
observing their formlessness before they manifest,
and thus governs before disorder arises.

The wise know misfortune and fortune *leaning against each other*,
and guard against their signs once they appear,
so they do not wander into their gates.

To hear what has not yet emerged—this is the Sage.
To see the form before it takes shape—this is wisdom.
Those who neither hear nor see are truly *qumi* (愚迷).

To hear of what has not yet emerged,
who but the Sage could do so?

To perceive the misfortune already formed,
who but the wise could recognize it?

Without hearing or seeing,
one is indeed *qumi* (愚迷).

The *Laozi* says:
*"If a ruler cherishes *virtue/rightness* (義),
he will trust the times and rely on himself,
abandon the *fate/number* (數) and employ *kindness* (惠).
People act upon the *virtue/rightness* (義) of a single moment,
without considering future calamities,
neglecting the great *Dao/Way*’s *fate/number* (數),
and boasting of clever *kindness* (惠).

This is not the way of an *accomplished ruler* (賢君).
To have vast knowledge yet shallow wisdom,
and to nourish vastness with shallowness—this is impossible.
Pointing to a cup and calling it the sea,
using a short rope to draw deep water—
how could this ever succeed?

To rely solely on one’s wisdom,
one’s failures will surely multiply.
Relying solely on wisdom leads to ruin;
seeking counsel from many yields approval.
Cherishing wisdom exhausts one’s *skills/arts* (術);
cherishing valor is the path to peril and destruction.
To dazzle in what one knows will bring exhaustion and frustration;
to act solely on valor without prudence will invite danger and ruin.

To give freely without fixed measure—
if the upper levels lack fixed measure,
then the lower levels’ expectations will have no end.
If one takes much and gives little,
the people will become his *enemies* (仇).
To take little and give much—
if the *fate/number* (數) is not just,
then giving will be the path to resentment.

In all giving,
one must first take.
To take is to have limits;
to give is to exhaust.
To provide from what is already exhausted
to meet boundless demands—
this is difficult to sustain.

Yet to exchange this with that,
one gains here and loses there.
What if taking is not by the *Dao/Way*,
and giving is not to those who deserve it?
The recipient will not rejoice,
the giver will make an enemy.

Thus, the *virtuous/powerful* (志) Sage abandons the mind of taking and giving,
and upholds the balanced measure.
Where would resentment arise from?

By this, it is clear:
*Wealth* (財) is insufficient to govern the *Dao/Way*,
but the *Dao/Way* and *skills/arts* (術) can be followed for illumination.

Observing the measures of taking and giving,
one sees that they are the source of enmity and resentment.
Thus, when *wealth* (財) cannot save the times,
only the *Dao/Way* can assist the multitudes.

*Wenzi* asked:
*"The ancient kings ruled the world through the *Dao/Way*—
how did they do so?"*

Asking about the *Dao/Way* of the ancient kings,
and admonishing the rulers of their time;
speaking of the present’s flaws,
yet not mentioning the governance of the past—
what should they do?

The *Laozi* said:
*"To hold fast to the One without *action* (為),
and to follow the transformations of Heaven and Earth—
the world is a great vessel.
The sovereigns of antiquity did not rule through *divine wonders*,
but by conforming to Heaven and Earth,
holding fast to the One without *action*,
and harmonizing with the *times* (時).

The world is a great vessel—
it cannot be grasped,
it cannot be made.
To make is to ruin it;
to grasp is to lose it.
The *Dao/Way* is subtle yet formless—
it cannot be grasped;
to grasp is to err.
It is minute yet without fixed shape—
it cannot be made;
to make is to ruin it.

To hold fast to the One is to see the small—
to see the small is to achieve the great.
Only through the One can one govern the multitudes and control things;
only through the great can one see the small without neglect.

To act without *action* is to guard stillness—
to guard stillness is to bring order to the world.
To move without overstepping one’s measure,
to remain still without stagnating—
this is the utmost stillness,
and thus one can bring order to the world.

To dwell in fullness yet not overflow,
to occupy high position yet not be arrogant—
to dwell in fullness without overflowing,
to be full yet not depleted;
to occupy high position without arrogance,
to be elevated yet not perilous.
To be full yet not depleted,
this is how one long guards *wealth* (富);
to be elevated yet not perilous,
this is how one long guards *nobility* (貴).

*Wealth* and *nobility* do not depart from one’s person,
and blessings extend to one’s descendants.
The ancient kings’ *Dao/Way* was complete in this.

To align with the *Dao/Way* of non-*action*,
and to resonate with the ultimate *Dao/Way*—
to dwell in fullness yet not overflow,
to tread in high position yet not be perilous,
to let *virtue/rightness* (德) permeate all things,
and to bestow *virtue/power* (德) upon descendants—
the bright kings of old
all upheld this *Dao/Way* to transform the world."*

The *Laozi* says:
*"The people have a shared *Dao/Way* to follow,
and shared laws to uphold.
*Virtue/Rightness* (義) cannot fix them,
and *authority* (威) cannot compel them.
Thus, a ruler is established to unify them.

In corrupt customs and *thin-hearted* (浇薄) people,
there is no adherence to the *Dao/Way*,
no unity in the laws.
Externally, they adorn themselves with *virtue/rightness* (義) to seek praise,
internally, they wield *authority* (威) to subdue the multitudes.
Without establishing a ruler,
how could they be brought into order?

When the ruler holds fast to the One, governance is achieved;
when there is no constancy, disorder arises.
If the One is unclear,
the ten thousand people lose their foundation.

The ruler’s *Dao/Way* is not about *action*,
but about non-*action*.
The wise do not use *virtue/power* (德) for personal gain,
the brave do not use strength to oppress,
the benevolent do not use their position to bestow favors—
this can be called the One.

Not to choose *idle wanderings* (逍) and act recklessly,
not to rely on one’s position to indulge in *kindness* (惠),
but to preserve all five—
this can be called the One.

The One is the *Dao/Way* of no *adaptation* (適),
the root of all things.
The One is the law.
*Adaptation* means *going* (往).
If the ruler implements the law to govern,
then all things will naturally turn toward the ruler,
and there will be none that do not *adapt*.

If the ruler frequently changes his laws,
the state will frequently change its rulers,
and laws will frequently shift,
while rulers will frequently change.

If the ruler lacks the One,
then the people and things will toil in exhaustion,
and the world will not be at peace.
If the ruler has no fixed laws,
and alters them with each passing time,
then there will be no fixed ruler,
and this will also ruin the people.

By one’s position,
one’s *likes and dislikes* (好惡) are revealed,
while the lower classes fear and tremble,
and cannot be governed.

For all who would be rulers,
it is proper to steady them with *virtue/power* (德) and *Dao/Way*,
and not to indulge in arbitrary *likes and dislikes*.
To indulge their hearts and minds,
to exceed proper *rewards and punishments* (賞罰),
if this is not appropriate,
then the lower officials will tremble in fear,
and fear will lead to excessive *punishments* (刑).
How could this be governed?

Thus, when a ruler loses the One,
his disorder is worse than having no ruler.
The ruler must hold fast to the One,
and only then can the multitudes be united.

The world submits to the ruler above
because the ruler has the *Dao/Way*.
Now, if a state has a ruler yet lacks the *Dao/Way*,
it is as if the people have no master.
Though they have their master,
if *wicked ministers* (奸臣) steal his authority,
and the *virtuous* (賢) suffer harm,
if *taxation* (敛) is excessive without end,
and the people and things labor in suffering—
thus it is said that this is worse than having no ruler."*

「文子问曰：王道有几？
*Wénzǐ asked: What are the number of the Kingly Dao/Way?*

老子曰：一而已矣。
*Lǎozǐ replied: There is but one.*

皇王之号虽殊，古今之道唯一也。
*The titles of the sovereigns differ, yet the Dao/Way of antiquity and the present is singular.*

文子曰：古有以道王者，有以兵王者，何其一也？
*Wénzǐ asked: In antiquity, there were those who ruled by the Dao/Way and those who ruled by force—how can they be singular?*

唐虞揖让，汤武征伐，其不一也。
*The Sage Kings of the Shang and Zhou dynasties, Tang and Yu, yielded power through humility, while the later kings, Tang of Shang and Wu of Zhou, conquered through warfare—how they differ!*

曰：以道王者，德也；
*He replied: Those who rule by the Dao/Way do so through virtue/power;*

以兵王者亦德也。
*those who rule by force also do so through virtue/power.*

道无升降，时有浇淳，
*The Dao/Way does not rise or fall, yet times shift from corruption to purity,*
理在变通，义非胶柱，
*principles lie in adaptability, and righteousness is not rigid like a bound pillar,*
故适时而举，因资济物，
*thus, actions are raised to suit the times, and resources are harnessed to benefit the people,*
大矣哉，其谁知之。
*great indeed! Who truly understands it?*

且结绳而理，用道以化者，德也。
*Moreover, even in the age of knotted cords for record-keeping, governing through the Dao/Way is virtue/power.*

夷暴殄逆，用兵而治，亦德也。
*To pacify the violent and extinguish rebellion through warfare is also virtue/power.*

动不逾正，静不乖道，
*When in action, one does not exceed propriety; when at rest, one does not stray from the Dao/Way,*
虽曰凶器，实为至德也。
*though called a cruel tool, it is truly the utmost virtue/power.*

用兵有五：有义兵，有应兵，有忿兵，有贪兵，有骄兵。
*The use of arms has five categories: righteous warfare, defensive warfare, vengeful warfare, greedy warfare, and arrogant warfare.*

夫兵者，动有危亡，用有可否也。
*Warfare, when employed, carries risk of ruin; its use may be just or unjust.*

诛暴救弱，谓之义；
*To punish the violent and aid the weak is called righteousness;*
敌来加己，不得已而用之，谓之应；
*when an enemy attacks and one must defend oneself, using force is called defensive;*
争小故不胜其心，谓之忿；
*to fight over petty grievances without overcoming the enemy’s resolve is called vengeful;*
利人土地，欲人财货，谓之贪；
*to covet another’s land or wealth is called greedy;*
恃其国家之大，矜其人民之众，欲见贤於敌国者，谓之骄。
*to rely on one’s own nation’s strength or populace, seeking to appear more virtuous than the enemy, is called arrogant.*

义兵王，应兵胜，忿兵败，贪兵死，骄兵灭，
*Righteous warfare brings kingship; defensive warfare brings victory; vengeful warfare brings defeat; greedy warfare brings death; arrogant warfare brings annihilation—*
此天道也。
*this is the natural order of the cosmos.*

国有五兵，轻用则死。
*A nation with these five types of warfare, if used recklessly, will perish.*

败身有五，贼轻用之，则危亡。
*Self-destruction has five forms: when a ruler employs warfare lightly, his nation faces peril and ruin.*

天道赏善惩奸，其理不差，
*The Dao/Way of Heaven rewards the virtuous and punishes the wicked—its principles never falter,*
仁者慎之也。
*the benevolent must take heed.*

老子曰：释道而任智者危，
*Lǎozǐ said: To abandon the Dao/Way and rely on cleverness brings danger,*
弃数而用才者困。
*to discard the natural order and employ ingenuity leads to constraint.*

舍平夷之道，专巧诈之智，
*To forsake the path of pacification and focus solely on cunning and deceit,*
遗祸福之数，骋谲诡之才，
*to neglect the rhythms of fortune and misfortune, and to unleash the talents of trickery,*
抑本趋末，得不危亡也？
*to suppress the fundamental and pursue the superficial—how could such a ruler not face ruin?*

故守分循理，失之不忧，
*Thus, one who adheres to one’s proper role and follows principles,*
得之不喜，
*does not grieve over loss nor rejoice in gain,*
成者非所为，得者非所求。
*success is not something one strives for, nor is gain something one seeks.*

不惊得失，自无忧喜，
*Unperturbed by gain or loss,*
入者有受而无取，
*those who enter receive without coveting,*
出者有授而无与。
*those who depart give without resentment.*

受无贪取之心，与无矜出之态。
*Receiving without greed, giving without pride—*

因春而生，因秋而杀，
*to act in accordance with the seasons—life flourishes in spring, death comes in autumn—*
所生不德，所杀不怨，
*the born do not blame, the fallen do not resent,*
则几於道矣。
*then one approaches the Dao/Way.*

春秋无心，生杀有时，
*The seasons act without personal intent—*
人主无为，赏罚必当，
*yet a sovereign who governs without interference*
远违其理，近失其道。
*will stray far from principle and lose the Dao/Way nearby.*

文子问曰：王者得其欢心，为之奈何？
*Wénzǐ asked: How does a kingly ruler win the people’s affection?*

帝王之理，何以得百姓欢心？
*The principles of sovereigns—how do they earn the joy of the common folk?*

老子曰：若江海即是也，
*Lǎozǐ replied: It is like the vastness of rivers and seas—*
淡兮无味，
*calm and without ostentation,*
用之不既，
*their use is inexhaustible,*
先小而后大。
*beginning with the humble and culminating in the grand.*

夫明王之德，湛若江海，
*The virtue/power of a wise sovereign is deep like the rivers and seas,*
来者不逆，酌者不竭。
*those who come are not repelled, and those who draw from it are never drained.*
淡然无味，五味成焉。
*Calm and without favoritism, all five flavors are harmonized within it.*
施之无穷，万物赖焉。
*Its bounty is boundless, and all things depend upon it.*

故得万姓欢心，子孙不绝也。
*Thus, the people’s affection is won, and their descendants endure.*

夫欲上人者，必以其言下之，
*He who seeks to lead the people*
欲先人者，必以其身后之，
*must place their words below the people’s needs and his own person behind theirs,*
天下必效其欢爱，
*then the world will emulate his joy and love,*
进其仁义，
*advance his benevolence and righteousness,*
而无苛气，
*without harshness,*
居上而民不重，
*though he stands above,*
居前而众不害，
*though he leads,*
天下乐推而不厌，
*the world willingly follows without weariness,*
虽绝国殊俗，蛆飞蠕动，
*even in distant lands of strange customs,*
莫不亲爱，
*all feel close and cherish him,*
无之而不通，
*without his guidance, nothing flourishes,*
无往而不遂，
*without his influence, nothing succeeds,*
故为天下贵。
*thus, he is honored by all under Heaven.*

欲上人者，非有欲上之心，
*He who seeks to lead the people*
有欲人之不上矣。
*does not harbor a desire to rule above them but to ensure their welfare does not suffer.*
先人者，非有允人之心，
*He who leads the people*
则推先而不害。
*does not seek personal glory but pushes forward what is beneficial without harm.*
若然者，德惠动天地，
*If such is his way,*
况於人乎？
*how much more so for the people?*

老子曰：执一世之法籍，
*Lǎozǐ said: To cling to a single set of laws for all ages*
以非传代之俗，
*and reject the customs of successive generations*
譬犹胶柱调瑟。
*is like binding a pillar to a lute to tune it—*
执一隅之说，非通代之典，
*to adhere to one partial doctrine and disregard the comprehensive traditions of successive ages,*
其犹胶柱调瑟，
*it is still like binding a pillar to a lute—*
何典节之能全也？
*how could such rigid adherence preserve the fullness of governance?*

圣人者应时权变，
*The Sage adapts to the times and exercises flexible measures,*
见形施宜，
*observing conditions and applying appropriate actions,*
世异则事变，
*when the world changes, so do the affairs;*
时移则俗易，
*when times shift, customs transform,*
论世立法，随时举事。
*he assesses the world to establish laws and acts in accordance with the times.*

夫圣王救时济物，
*The Sage King rescues the times and aids the people,*
众人仰止，
*all look up to him in reverence,*
犹饥而待食，
*like the hungry awaiting nourishment,*
渴而思饮，
*or the thirsty yearning for drink,*
人谁不愿也？
*who would not wish for such a ruler?*

上古之王，法度不同，
*The sovereigns of ancient times had differing laws and measures,*
非古相返也，
*not because they rejected antiquity,*
时务异也，
*but because the affairs of the times were distinct,*
是故不法其已成之法，
*thus, they did not follow the laws already established,*
而法其所以为法者，
*but followed the reasons behind those laws,*
与化推移。
*adapting them to the changing world.*

道无隆替，而俗有变革，
*The Dao/Way does not wax or wane, yet customs undergo transformation,*
是以五帝不同治，
*that is why the Five Emperors ruled differently,*
三王不共法。
*and the Three Kings did not share the same laws.*
非欲相返，
*Not because they sought to contradict one another,*
因时宜者也。
*but because they followed what suited the times.*

圣人法之可观也，
*The Sage establishes laws that are observable,*
其所以作法，不可原也，
*yet the reasons behind them cannot be traced back.*
法未然，人不可知，
*Before a law is enacted, people cannot foresee it;*
政已洽，众有可观。
*once governance is harmonized, the people see its efficacy.*

其言可听也，
*His words are heard,*
其所以言，不可形也。
*yet their underlying intent cannot be defined.*
言可听者，当时用也。
*Words that are heard serve their immediate purpose.*
不可形者，不可以当时之言，
*That which cannot be defined*
为后时之用。
*cannot be applied as a fixed doctrine for future generations.*

三皇、五帝轻天下，
*The Three Sovereigns and Five Emperors treated the world lightly,*
细万物，
*and regarded all things with care,*
齐死生，
*equalizing life and death,*
同变化，
*and aligning with transformation.*
轻天下者，非鄙薄也。
*To treat the world lightly was not to hold it in contempt,*
细万物者，非简贱也。
*nor to regard all things as insignificant.*
言非有欲取天下，
*They did not seek to seize the world,*
而天下归；
*yet the world submitted to them;*
无心利万物，
*they had no intent to benefit all things,*
万物自附者也。
*yet all things naturally attached to them.*
齐死生则忧惧不能入，
*Equalizing life and death, their fears could not penetrate,*
同变化则诡异不能移也。
*aligning with transformation, their strange schemes could not sway them.*

抱道推诚，
*Embracing the Dao/Way and extending sincerity,*
以镜万物之情，
*like a mirror reflecting the sentiments of all things,*
神而为镜，
*a divine mirror,*
照无不得。
*nothing escapes its illumination.*

上与道为友，
*Above, he befriends the Dao/Way,*
下与化为人。
*below, he aligns with transformation to be among the people.*
上与道交，
*He exchanges with the Dao/Way above,*
下与化游。
*and plays with transformation below.*

「Now wishing to learn their Dao/Way, one cannot attain their pristine clarity and lofty sanctity, nor uphold their legal codes and enact their decrees, and thus will assuredly fail to govern effectively.

Indeed, preserving their classics and implementing their laws and institutions relies upon the pristine clarity and lofty sanctity to expound and guide, so that later scholars may know to honor their guidance, applying it internally to cultivate their own bodies and externally to govern the nation.

When Wenzi asked about politics, he meant: *politics and teaching*.

Laozi said: *Rule them with the Dao/Way, nurture them with virtue/power, do not display your wisdom, do not exert your strength. Teach them with the Dao/Way, yet do not show them your cleverness. Approach them with virtue/power, yet do not flaunt your authority or might. Subtract from excess and hold fast to the singular, so that there is no advantage to be exploited, no desires to be gratified. The essence lies in purity and emptiness; desires and benefits will naturally perish.*

Square yet unsevered, sharp yet not piercing—
*Square yet unsevered,*
*sharp yet not piercing—*
*the upright does not harm things,*
*the sharp does not injure righteousness.*
*Without arrogance, without self-aggrandizement.*

Rule them with the Dao/Way and the people will attach themselves,
Nurture them with virtue/power and they will submit,
Do not display your wisdom and they will suffice,
Do not exert your strength and they will remain sincere.

Do not display your wisdom—*this is frugality*,
Do not exert your strength—*this is restraint*.
Below, he gathers them with generosity,
Above, he takes them with guile.
Frugality preserves himself,
Restraint keeps him secure.

If he does not gather below, they will disperse,
If he does not nurture above, they will rebel,
If he displays his wisdom, they will contend,
If he exerts his strength, they will resent him.
Their dispersion weakens the nation’s might,
Their rebellion leaves the ruler without authority,
Their contention makes virtue/power seem trivial,
Their resentment endangers the ruler’s position.

If these four are truly cultivated—
*frugality, nurturing, wisdom without display, authority without violence—*
then the true Dao/Way is nearly achieved.

Frugal yet self-preserving,
Nurturing to win the people’s affection,
Wise yet not serving self-interest,
Authoritative yet not cruel—
if these four are all cultivated,
then the true Dao/Way is preserved.

Laozi said: *The words spoken above are used below, the words spoken below are used above. The words spoken above are constant usage, the words spoken below are expedient usage.* Only the sage can know how to use expedient measures, speaking yet always trustworthy, setting terms yet always meeting them. Above, the sage speaks of the Dao/Way; below, he employs expedient measures. Only the sage can know how to use them without losing the Dao/Way, skillfully applying expedient measures.

The petty man uses them and loses his life—
*he does not understand expedient measures.*
Only expedient measures are trustworthy without words,
and meet expectations without prior agreement.

What is the highest conduct in the world?
*To be upright yet honor one’s father,*
*to be trustworthy yet die for one’s daughter—*
who could value such things?

The world knows that honoring one’s father is wisdom,
dying for one’s daughter is trustworthiness,
yet none can truly esteem them.
This is not so.
To force one’s nature for uprightness,
to restrain conduct for sincerity—
these are but the deceptive methods of the end times,
not the true intent of supreme virtue/power.
Thus, they are not worthy of honor or trust.

Therefore, the sage judges matters as crooked or straight,
bending or extending with others,
without fixed forms or appearances,
adjusting to the occasion.
If a ruler is praised as virtuous yet drowned,
or a father reviled as cruel yet buried,
this is due to circumstances alone.
*"Drowned"* is pronounced *nà* (祚骨切).
A ruler praised as virtuous is not so by nature—
*only when praised does he seem virtuous*,
*a father reviled as cruel is not so by nature—*
*only when drowned does he seem acceptable*.
Circumstances dictate the event—
who claims that this is fitting to propriety?

Expedient measures are what the sage alone understands.
*To first oppose then later conform*—
this is called expedient measures.
To first conform then oppose—
this is not to understand expedient measures,
and not to understand them is to invite disaster.

The skilled user of expedient measures—
*first deceptive, then enlightening*.
The unskilled user—
*begins fortunate, ends calamitous*.

Wenzi asked: *Master, without virtue/power and the Dao/Way, one cannot govern the world. The rulers of antiquity, inheriting their predecessors’ achievements, also had those without the Dao/Way yet did not suffer ruin or defeat. How could this be?*

Laozi replied: *From the Son of Heaven down to the common people, each lives as they will. Yet their vitality differs in measure—some flourish, others decline. When nations fall and families break, it is not due to external calamity but to losing the Dao/Way.*

If virtue/power and the Dao/Way exist,
then day and night one remains vigilant,
ever fearful of peril and ruin.
If they do not exist,
then desires run wild and laziness prevails—
their ruin comes without warning.

To dwell as though already ruined is to leave no remnant of calamity for the nation.
To rest in safety and forget danger is to court death at any moment.
If Jie and Zhou followed the Dao/Way and practiced virtue/power,
even if Tang and Wu were wise,
they could not have established their achievements.
With the Dao/Way, one becomes king;
without it, one faces ruin.
It is known that good and evil do not determine kingship.
The rise or fall lies with the people—
the August Heaven aids virtue/power,
this is the natural order.
How could it be otherwise?

Virtue/power and the Dao/Way are what nurtures and sustains life,
what fosters growth and affection,
what inspires reverence and esteem.
Even the deaf worm and blind turtle,
though lacking ears,
do not harm what they love.
If all under heaven harbored hearts of benevolence and righteousness,
how could calamity and disaster arise?

The Dao/Way is vast and encompassing,
thick and heavy—
it gives life, sustains it,
nurtures it, loves it,
without distinction among things,
fully extending itself to all.
If all things were thus,
even the deaf worm’s stupidity would feel the grace of benevolence—
why fear the rise of calamity and disaster?

Without the Dao/Way yet free from harm—
this is because benevolence has not yet been extinguished
and righteousness has not yet perished.
Though benevolence may linger
and righteousness not yet vanish,
the feudal lords will already scorn their ruler.
When feudal lords scorn their ruler,
the court will not show proper respect,
even if they do not openly defy him.

If a king lacks the Dao/Way,
yet his lineage and position remain unbroken,
it is because benevolence and righteousness still linger.
Yet the seeds of misfortune and calamity have already taken root here—
the people’s resentment toward their ruler has begun.
Thus, the ruler of Qi descends from his hall to meet the feudal lords,
and the Duke of Jin convenes alliances at the borderlands—
these are signs of a declining age.

When benevolence is extinguished and righteousness perishes,
the feudal lords rebel,
and the common people seize power through force and coercion.
The strong oppress the weak,
the great encroach upon the small—
the people take up arms as their livelihood.
Disasters multiply,
calamities arise—
their ruin is inevitable.
Why speak of being free from misfortune?»

「When the Dao/Way is lost and virtue/power extinguished,
when benevolence is extinguished and righteousness perishes,
there are rulers who are not true rulers,
and ministers who are not true ministers.
Superior and inferior lose their proper stations,
the strong oppress the weak—
thus the Second Emperor of Qin and the later sovereigns of Han,
these are the times when states are destroyed and perish.

Laozi says:
*"When laws are intricate and punishments severe,
the people will resort to deceit.
When laws are intricate, they cannot be fully followed,
and when they are not fully followed,
one enforces severity through punishments—
but when punishments are not just,
the innocent are ensnared in guilt."*

Thus, the common people grow reckless, risking death to defy the law,
and the peril of the world arises from this.
The more the ruler acts, the more the subjects rebel;
the more he seeks, the less he attains;
the more he prohibits, the fewer obey.
He creates troubles through his actions,
yet seeks to suppress them through further actions—
this is like fanning flames to extinguish them;
using wisdom to breed calamity,
then using wisdom to defend against it—
like stirring water to seek its clarity.

When people are burdened with many affairs, their hearts grow restless,
and when a state enforces many prohibitions, its people grow weary—
just as fire cannot be quenched by constant fanning,
nor water purified by repeated stirring.

Laozi says:
*"If a ruler favors benevolence,
he rewards those without merit
and pardons those with crimes.
If he favors severity,
he discards those with merit
and punishes the innocent.
Without fixed preferences,
he executes without resentment,
and gives without expecting virtue in return.
When a ruler has no fixed preferences,
his ministers face no unjust punishments,
those rewarded do not avoid their fate,
and those executed hold no grudges.
He follows the square and the plumb line,
uninvolved in affairs,
like Heaven and Earth—
why should they not sustain and cover all?"*

When he unites and harmonizes, he is the ruler;
when he separates and punishes, he is the law.
If the people are punished without complaint,
this is called virtue/power and Dao/Way.

Actions follow law and measure,
virtue aligns with Heaven and Earth—
when the ruler is enlightened, principles are never obscured,
and when the law is just, the people suffer no wrongful fate.

Laozi says:
*"The world’s judgments of right and wrong are not fixed;
each age upholds what it deems good
and rejects what it deems evil.
Those who seek what is right
do not seek the Dao/Way,
but what aligns with themselves.
Those who reject what is wrong
do not reject evil,
but what contradicts their hearts.
If I wish to choose what is right and dwell in it,
or reject what is wrong and discard it,
I do not know what the world calls right and wrong.

The world’s people praise what I uphold as right,
and condemn what I reject as wrong;
they also condemn what I praise as good,
and reject what I uphold as right.
If what I uphold is not truly right,
and what I praise as good is not truly good,
then good and evil are undefined,
and right and wrong are nowhere to be found.
Yet those who satisfy their own desires,
though they condemn, still consider it good,
and though they praise what is good,
they do not abandon decay.
What they reject as wrong
is what contradicts their own hearts—
thus, there is no right without wrong,
and no wrong without right.
There is no good without evil,
nor evil without good—
so the enlightened do not fixate on good and evil,
nor on right and wrong.
Thus, they govern great states like simmering a delicate broth—
do not stir it, lest it spoil.
Great states cannot endure chaotic governance?
Delicate broths cannot bear repeated stirring?
Those who seek harmony
must speak truthfully to foster closeness,
yet when their bodies are distant and they plot,
they invite suspicion.
Seeking harmony means aligning with the ruler’s favor.
If their words are truthful yet their bodies are distant,
the ruler will not fully trust them,
and they will surely be suspected.
Now I wish to uphold my integrity
and await the world’s response—
how can I know what the world will use to measure me?
If I rush to conform with the world,
I am like one fleeing rain without shelter—
yet still, I am drenched.
Now I wish to be the world’s measure and model,
yet others will also make me their teacher and guide—
like one sprinting to avoid rain,
exhausted and labored,
yet still unable to escape being wet.
To dwell in emptiness is impossible,
unless one does not seek emptiness and yet emptiness comes naturally—
this is how desires are fulfilled without effort.
Emptiness is without desire;
desire is not emptiness.
Without the heart’s attachment,
one attains all things.

Thus, those who grasp the Dao/Way
are like a cart’s axle that does not turn for itself,
yet enables the wheel to reach a thousand miles,
turning endlessly on the boundless plain.
The adept of the Dao/Way
is the hub of the cart—
his body mingles with the world yet finds rest,
his spirit dwells in the center yet remains ever still.
He does not stray beyond words,
nor toil for himself—
thus he turns upon boundless paths,
and wanders in realms of utter darkness.
Therefore, the sage embodies the Dao/Way and returns to its source,
does not transform to await transformation,
and acts without contrivance.

The sage internally returns to truth,
externally adapts to change,
touched by emotions yet unstained—
he moves and acts without contrivance.

Laozi says:
*"He who often wages war and frequently wins
will surely bring his state to ruin.
Frequent warfare exhausts the people,
frequent victories breed arrogance in the ruler—
with an arrogant ruler governing exhausted subjects,
how few states can avoid ruin?
Arrogance leads to excess,
excess leads to depletion of resources,
exhaustion breeds resentment,
resentment leads to extreme measures—
when both ruler and subjects reach extremes,
no state remains unfallen."*

Thus, when achievements are complete, the sage withdraws—
this is the Dao/Way of Heaven.
War should not be waged often,
the ruler should not grow arrogant,
the people should not be exhausted,
resources should not be depleted—
for when extremes are reached,
they return to extremes without ruin.
When King Ping asked Wenzi:
*"I have heard that you attained the Dao/Way from Lao Dan.
Now, though wise men may possess the Dao/Way,
they face a depraved and chaotic age—
with the authority of one man alone,
how can he transform the long-suffering people?
Is this possible?"*
King Ping refers to the Zhou King Ping.
The "one man" speaks of the king himself.
Wenzi, the wise man, is the speaker here—
*"Though the Dao/Way of virtue/power
rectifies evil to establish order,
restores chaos to bring governance,
and transforms depravity to return to simplicity,
pure virtue revives,
and the world attains peace—
this depends on one man.
For righteousness resides in the heart,
and governance stems from the ruler.
When the heart weakens, decay follows;
when the ruler governs rightly, order prevails.
Thus, prosperity and decline are not decreed by Heaven,
success and failure rest within ourselves—
they do not depend on external things,
but on the Dao/Way we uphold.
The value lies in the Dao/Way."*

The Dao/Way rests with one man,
then licentious customs may be transformed,
pure virtue revived—
what cause for worry that governance will fail?

The sovereign is the people’s teacher,
the ruler, their model.
When the ruler is virtuous, the people follow;
when the ruler possesses virtue/power,
the people cultivate benevolence and righteousness,
and thus there is no age of licentious chaos.

Hence, all under Heaven, bright and clear,
take the ruler as their teacher,
look to him as their model—
as water from a thousand-fathom gorge
must flow downward, so too do all turn to him.

Accumulate virtue/power and become a sage-king,
accumulate resentment and court ruin,
accumulate stones and mountains rise,
accumulate water and seas form—
nothing great is achieved without accumulation.
Virtue unaccumulated is insufficient to earn renown,
evil unaccumulated is insufficient to destroy oneself.
Thus, the sage-king follows what is accumulated.

He who accumulates virtue/power
receives Heaven’s favor,
gains Earth’s assistance,
and is aided by spirits and gods—
phoenixes soar in his courtyard,
qilin roam his suburbs,
and dragons repose in his ponds.
Thus, by accumulating virtue/power,
he moves Heaven and Earth,
and the four spirits manifest auspicious signs,
while all things rejoice in their proper roles.

To govern under Heaven with the Dao/Way
is the virtue/power of the world;
to govern under Heaven without the Dao/Way
is the world’s ruin.
To set oneself against the world as an enemy,
even if one seeks longevity, it cannot be attained.

*Governing* (莅) means to preside over.
When the ruler governs under Heaven with the Dao/Way,
the world willingly bears him without burden.
When one governs without the Dao/Way,
the world resents him and his rule does not endure.

Emperor Yao and Shun flourished through this,
while Jie and Zhou perished by it.
Observe the good and the evil
to discern survival and ruin.

King Ping said: "Your servant reverently obeys."
King Ping, a virtuous ruler of Zhou,
grieved over the decline of the Dao/Way in his age,
and thus questioned Wenzi,
