seeking the path to governance.

Wenzi replied: "It depends on one man,
not on others."
Thus, King Ping refined his governance,
and the Zhou Dao/Way revived,
earning praise in the *Spring and Autumn Annals*,
and later historians honored him as King Ping.

*End of Volume Five of the Treatise on Penetrating the Mysterious and True*
*Volume Six begins: The Silent Child’s Commentary*

The **Supreme Virtue/Power**—The **Supreme Virtue/Power** refers to the virtuous rulers of their time. When the Dao/Way of the Three Dynasties declined and the virtue/power of the Five Hegemons waned, it was fitting to cultivate virtue/power to rectify the world. Those with virtue/power are honored, and those with merit are praised, hence the term **Supreme Virtue/Power**.

Laozi said: *"The ruler is the heart of the state; a tranquil heart brings order to all limbs, while a disturbed heart brings chaos."* Governing the state depends on the ruler’s clarity—when he is clear, all the people take joy in their pursuits. Governing oneself depends on a rectified heart—when it is rectified, all limbs find their proper place. Thus, when a man’s self is well-governed, his limbs move without effort; he knows no sickness or pain. When a state is well-governed, its ruler and ministers forget each other; there is no worry or anxiety.

Laozi said: *"He learned from Master Changzhu [常枞]—Laozi’s teacher, surnamed Chang, named Zhu."* Laozi himself recounted receiving instruction from his teacher, whose words were thus: *"The unlettered one—when he sees the tongue, he guards softness; when he sees the ancient Dao/Way, he upholds the yielding and gentle."* The ancient character (古) can also be written as the tongue character (舌), meaning softness. Gazing upward at the house beams, he laments that light does not linger. Retreating by the river, he sighs that the past does not cease flowing. Observing shadows to know how to grasp what comes after, he does not act before its time.

Thus, the Sage follows the Way of **emptiness and non-action**, always yielding to what comes later rather than forcing what comes first. It is like kindling a pile of wood—when the fire reaches the top, the lower logs are consumed. What comes later is what comes first; what is below is what is above. This is the natural order of things. If one seeks to act before others, he cannot act before others.

Laozi said: *"The clapper of a bell, though loud, destroys itself; the candle, though bright, consumes itself. The markings of tigers and leopards invite arrows; the swiftness of monkeys and gibbons invites traps. Thus, the bold perish by their strength; the eloquent are ensnared by their cleverness. Though one knows cleverness, he cannot know when to refrain from it."* This is the harm of knowing, the inability to preserve oneself by not knowing. Though one is wise in speech, he can only be given partial truths, not broad responses. Holding to the courage of an ordinary man, he cannot defend against multitudes; clinging to one-sided arguments, he does not speak of universal principles.

Laozi said: *"The Dao/Way is founded on non-existence; to embody the Dao/Way is to be empty and without form—this is called **subtle and mysterious**."* It cannot be seen in form, nor heard in sound; this is called **profound and dark**. **Profound and dark** describes the Dao/Way, yet is not the Dao/Way itself. It is like a net—used to catch fish, yet not the fish itself; or words—used to speak of the Dao/Way, yet not the Dao/Way itself.

The Dao/Way is to look inward and reflect; to listen within and reflect, thus attaining self-knowledge. Therefore, a man is neither slightly awakened nor deeply confused; neither slightly kind nor greatly foolish. He who clutches a glowworm and compares it to the sun is not confused how? He who holds a piece of jade and compares it to the King of Fu is not foolish how?

Do not take your mirror from flowing water, but from still water, for its stillness preserves within and does not sway without. Dust in the heart scatters outward—it flows and forever dims. Water’s nature is inward emptiness—its stillness reflects itself. The moon wanes when the sun shines; darkness cannot endure against light. Stars vanish when the sun rises—nothing can contend with its radiance. The end cannot be stronger than the root; branches cannot surpass the trunk. When the upper is heavy and the lower is light, its fall is inevitable.

This means: the Great Ruler has his decree, the lesser men should not presume to act. If they do, it is like darkness seizing light or stars challenging the sun—root and branch must be used in harmony, each in its proper place, to avoid peril and overthrow.

One pool does not nurture two dragons; one female does not mate with two males. Unity is fixed; duality invites contention. When the ruler is unified, the state is secure. When a man is unified, his heart is at peace. Jade rests in the mountain, and grass and trees grow moist. Pearls arise from the depths, and the shore does not wither. Mountains and rivers hold jade and pearls, thus nourished and graceful. The gentleman accumulates virtue/power, thus radiant and illustrious.

The earthworm lacks the strength of sinews and bones nor the sharpness of claws and teeth, yet it feeds above on the soil and drinks below from the Yellow Spring—its heart is unified. The earthworm eats when hungry and drinks when thirsty—no conflicting thoughts, no need for sinews, bones, claws, or teeth. When a man unifies his heart in the Dao/Way, why should he rely on fame and profit to find satisfaction?

Clarity is brightness—even a cup of water reveals the pupil. Turbidity is harm—a river cannot obscure a great mountain. Though clarity is small, it can discern the finest hairs; though turbidity is vast, it cannot reveal peaks and valleys. Orchids and sweetgrass do not cease their fragrance because they are not worn; a boat floats on the rivers and seas, not sinking because it is not boarded; the gentleman follows the good Dao/Way, not stopping because it is unrecognized—this is their inherent nature.

Orchids and sweetgrass are fragrant by nature; they cannot help but be fragrant. The gentleman follows the good Dao/Way—he cannot but act. To bring clarity into turbidity is to invite suffering; to bring turbidity into clarity is to invite ruin. The virtuous and foolish cannot stand together; purity and filth do not mix in the same vessel. Heaven’s two *qi* forms the rainbow; Earth’s two *qi* causes leakage and concealment; man’s two *qi* brings illness. The three transformations of Heaven, Earth, and Man value life above all.

Darkness and light cannot remain constant—winter follows summer, and summer follows winter. The moon does not know day; the sun does not know

The gentleman who is attuned to the Dao/Way is like a cart axle turning within its hub, not moving by itself, yet reaching a thousand *li*—its motion endless, returning yet beginning anew. This has already been explained.

Thus, when raising the crooked and the straight, one may not always succeed; when raising the straight and the crooked, do not force it to proceed.
This is to elucidate the *Fu* and *Yan* sections.

When a bird is about to come, setting nets and waiting for it—he who catches the bird gains one eye of the net. If one makes a net of a single eye, he will never catch a bird.
Relying on one man’s talent alone is hard to govern the multitude.
A net of a single eye cannot trap a bird.
Thus, some matters cannot be preordained, some things cannot be foreseen—
therefore the Sage stores the Dao/Way and awaits the proper time.

The Sage’s conduct is Heavenly, his actions follow the times;
before the time arrives, he guards the Dao/Way—when the time comes, he acts accordingly.
The Duke of Zhou’s establishment of the Zhou Dao, and the Emperor Gaozu’s flourishing of the Han dynasty.

To gain fish, one must first open the valleys;
to attract birds, one must first plant the trees.
Water gathers, and fish assemble;
trees flourish, and birds congregate.
He who gains fish does not drag them into the depths—
he who gains monkeys does not carry them up the trees.
He merely lets them go where it is advantageous.

The relationship between ruler and subject is like fish returning to water,
birds relying on forests—
letting them act as they will, they come without being summoned.
A wise ruler governs, and loyal and virtuous ministers naturally arrive.

The foot treads shallow ground, yet waits for what it cannot tread before it can move;
the mind knows little, yet waits for what it does not know before it can be enlightened.
The foot’s familiar ground is little, its unfamiliar ground is much.
The mind’s known things are few, its unknown things are many.
Thus, though he does not act, his actions are fulfilled;
though he does not know, his knowledge is complete.

When streams run dry, the valleys become empty;
when hills are leveled, the depths are sealed.
When lips are lost, teeth grow cold;
when the Yellow River is deep, its nourishment lies in the mountains.
This illustrates how the ruler and people rely on each other,
just as mountains and rivers are interconnected.
A deep Yellow River brings fertility to the mountains,
a ruler’s heavy taxation drains the people’s wealth.
When the ruler demands more, the people are exhausted;
when the people’s strength is spent, the ruler’s position is perilous—
this is the meaning of "lips lost, teeth grow cold."

Water still yields clarity;
clarity brings evenness;
evenness brings ease;
ease reveals the form of things.
Forms cannot coexist—
thus clarity can be used as a standard.
When the sovereign’s wisdom is established,
it is like the stillness of water.
Deeply discerning the nature of things,
no state of good or evil escapes him;
profoundly observing human nature,
his policies of peace and harmony spread naturally.

When leaves fall, it is the wind that shakes them;
when water becomes turbid, it is objects that stir it.
The wind does not shake yet leaves fall,
objects always stir yet water remains clear—this has never been seen.
The *Bi* and *Zhuó* vessels are refined by the *Chuàn* and *Zhuàn* processes;
the *Léng* and *Zhuó* blades are honed by *dǐlì* and *lìlì*.
Though jade and fine swords possess beautiful qualities,
they must rely on the *dǐlì* and *lìlì* processes
to become extraordinary tools.
The gentleman and the virtuous man,
though possessing talent and innate qualities,
must rely on teachers and mentors
to perfect their undertakings.

The *Fènghuáng* soars at heights of a thousand *rèn*—
none can summon it.
The *Zhuī* is fixed within a hundred layers—
yet it cannot rise by itself.
Ascend the heights, and they become mountains;
descend the depths, and they become abysses.
Act upon what is easy, and men achieve without labor;
exploit what is familiar, and things come without being called.

Ditches and ponds overflow in floods,
dry up in droughts—
but the sources of the Yellow River and the seas,
deep and inexhaustible, do not fail.
Store them, and they do not brim;
let them flow, and they do not run dry—
never have I heard of woes from overflow or drought,
for their depths are ever thus.

The tortoise has no ears, yet its eyes cannot be covered—
it is keen in sight.
The blind man has no eyes, yet his ears cannot be covered—
he is keen in hearing.

The *Fènghuáng* returns to its native land,
the hare flees back to its burrow,
the fox dies with its head toward its hill,
the cicada finds refuge in the trees—
each returns to its origin.

Things do not forget their roots,
but men sometimes stray from the Dao/Way.
Water and fire repel each other,
yet the *Dǐng* and *Lì* vessels exist between them,
and the five flavors are harmonized.
Bone and flesh love each other,
yet slanderers come between them,
and father and son grow suspicious.
This shows how things have opposing natures—
though water and fire attack each other,
when used properly, they yield harmony.
Though bone and flesh love each other,
slander and deceit come between them,
and doubt arises.
The virtuous must be vigilant.

Dogs and pigs eat without discrimination,
growing fat in body,
yet they are near death.
This illustrates how the petty man seeks fame and profit in haste—
though he may prosper temporarily, he will ultimately fail.
The virtuous man stores the Dao/Way and awaits the proper time—
though he may suffer temporarily, he will ultimately thrive.

The *Fènghuáng* soars at heights of a thousand *rèn*—
none can summon it;
the *Zhuī* is fixed within a hundred layers—
yet it cannot rise by itself.
Ascend the heights, and they become mountains;
descend the depths, and they become abysses.
Act upon what is easy, and men achieve without labor;
exploit what is familiar, and things come without being called.

Ditches and ponds overflow in floods,
dry up in droughts—
but the sources of the Yellow River and the seas,
deep and inexhaustible, do not fail.
Store them, and they do not brim;
let them flow, and they do not run dry—
never have I heard of woes from overflow or drought,
for their depths are ever thus.

「Each draws benefit from its own source, yet none can borrow from another’s.
The *húnhún* waters are turbid—
can they wash my feet?
The *línglíng* waters are clear—
can they rinse my *yīng*?

To speak of purity and turbidity without omission,
the wise and the foolish are both employed—
but measure their capacity and assign tasks accordingly,
and adapt to circumstances as they arise.

**Yào** (药) serves as *gǎo* (缟),
or as a *guān* (冠),
or as a *yǔ* (豓).
**Wèi** (未) indicates
that its use is not fixed.

When used as a *guān* (冠),
it adorns the head;
when used as a *yǔ* (豓),
it graces the feet.
Do not violate its proper function,
but fulfill its essential role.

Gold’s power surpasses wood—
a single blade cannot harm an entire forest;
earth’s power surpasses water—
a single scoop cannot dam a river;
water’s power surpasses fire—
a single sip cannot quench a cartload of kindling.

To straighten one man’s crookedness
cannot alter the multitude’s perversity,
to enlighten one man’s ignorance
cannot dispel the confusion of the crowd.

Winter has thunder, summer has hail—
the seasons do not change their course,
yet frost and snow fall *chōuchuò* (麃麃),
and the *yìlù* (义鹿) dies in the fifth month.
The *chánchú* (蟾蜍) avoids *bì* (辟) weapons,
and its lifespan lasts until the fifth month’s full moon.

These all have utility yet suffer harm—
why not remain nameless to preserve one’s self?
The *chánchú* (蟾蜍) is said to be slain in the fifth month,
but when its flesh is smeared with the five weapons,
it enters battle unscathed.

Those whose *jīng* (精) is dissipated fall prey to misfortune.
The *jīnghuá* (精华) emanates from within,
while the branches and trunks wither from without.

The *huá* (华) that blooms untimely cannot be eaten.
To consume things out of season brings illness,
to take wealth without righteousness invites ruin.

Between tongue and teeth,
which wears out first?
Between cord and arrow,
which bends first?

Though the rigid may be firm, they inevitably fail,
though the yielding may bend, they rectify what is amiss.
A crooked shadow betrays its form,
a muddled echo betrays its voice.

A straight form will never cast a crooked shadow,
a virtuous word will never yield a foul echo.

To treat one whose illness is fatal as if it were not,
is difficult for even the finest physician.
To counsel one whose kingdom is doomed as if it were not,
is impossible for even the most loyal minister.

A fatal illness cannot be cured,
even by the best physician.
A doomed kingdom cannot be saved,
even by the most loyal minister.

When the *chāng* (倡) plays the *yǒu* (竽),
and the craftsman adjusts the *quǎn* (窍),
though the rhythm may be correct,
they cannot make it resound—
the master’s prestige is lost.

The *chāng* (倡) is the one who plays the *yǒu* (竽),
the craftsman is the one who adjusts the *quǎn* (窍).
Though the notes may fit the scale,
their actions ultimately conflict with purpose—
the heart does not respond,
so how can they achieve harmony?
This illustrates how the master’s authority is forgotten.

The deaf cannot sing to find their own joy,
the blind cannot gaze to engage with the world.
Sound does not reach their ears,
so they abandon all thought of music,
and sight does not reach their eyes,
so they cease all contemplation of beauty.

Walking through forests, one cannot follow straight paths,
traversing perilous terrain, one cannot measure with a plumb line.
To walk through forests, one seeks only to pass through,
to traverse dangers, one seeks only to rescue the lost.

The sea’s greatness lies in its abundance—
it is vast because it does not cease to give birth,
and boundless because its use never ends.

The sun does not rise twice in one day,
the fox does not have two masters,
the divine dragon does not pair,
the fierce beasts do not gather in groups,
and startled birds do not fly in twos.

These all stand alone, unpaired—
thus they become the leaders of all beasts and birds.
It is not the whisk that obscures the sun,
nor the spokes that follow swiftness—
one must rely on the proper tools.

To draw a bow and shoot,
one cannot release the arrow without the string,
and only one in ten shots strikes true.
Though the bow may be strong,
without the string, it cannot hit the mark.
Though a ruler may be sage,
without ministers, his deeds cannot be completed.

Among those who seek office,
many are unworthy—
yet among those who seek virtue,
only one in ten thousand is found.

A hungry horse in the stable
remains silent and still,
but when fed grain beside it,
it immediately becomes restless.

When one lacks fodder in the stable,
it is given and they will contend.
When one craves fame and position,
but dwells in them, they will strive—
thus the noble man yields his fortune,
while the petty man competes for rank.

A three-*cùn* (寸) tube, though open,
cannot fill the world,
yet a ten-*dàn* (石) vessel has its limits,
and a hundred *dòu* (斗) measure is sufficient.

This illustrates how the greedy never have enough,
though their vessels are bottomless.

To follow the plumb line and cut—
it will not go astray,
to suspend the balance and weigh—
it will not err.
To apply ancient principles by analogy,
there are times when they succeed,
and to wield the *gè* (格) rod,
there are times when it is appropriate.
To act thus is called *duàn* (断, "decision"),
to act otherwise is called *luàn* (乱, "chaos").

Following the plumb line in action,
things cannot exceed its measure,
suspending the balance in governance,
no matter goes unaddressed.

Since ancient and modern times differ,
laws and measures must also vary—
adapting to the times brings order,
clinging to the past brings ruin.

The farmer toils while the nobleman is nourished,
the foolish speak while the wise deliberate.

When matters are clear,
treat them like jade or stone—
no doubt remains.
When understanding is murky,
leave room for counsel.

A hundred stars’ brightness
does not match the moon’s single light,
ten windows fully opened
do not match the clarity of one.
Though petty men may be many,
they are insufficient to rely upon,
though virtuous men may be few,
one is more than enough.

King Wen secured Lü Wang (吕望),
Emperor Gaozu secured Zi Fang (子房)—
were they few in number?

The *fùshé* (蝮蛇) cannot be a foot,
nor the tiger’s a wing.
No man is fully capable,
no thing possesses dual perfection.»

The six-foot mat—
when lying upon it, even the lowly may cross it with ease,
but standing to step over it, even the gifted find it difficult.
Different circumstances demand different efforts.

Men’s talents vary—
what suits one may fail another,
for their abilities differ.

The assistant at sacrifice gains a taste,
the one who breaks up a fight takes a wound.
Seeing goodness brings blessing,
encountering evil brings harm—
how much more so when one acts personally?

Sheltering beneath an ill-omened tree,
one is struck by thunder.
Hiding beneath an ill-omened tree,
yet the heavenly wrath cannot be evaded;
concealing a wicked man,
yet the state’s law will surely punish.

The sun and moon wish to shine,
but floating clouds obscure them;
the river longs to be clear,
but sand and silt defile it;
clusters of orchids strive to flourish,
but autumn winds wither them;
human nature seeks balance,
yet desires corrupt it.
Covered in dust yet hoping for purity—
it cannot be achieved.

Dwelling in murky darkness,
how can one perceive brightness?
Residing in the field of desires,
one must follow their stain.

Frost and hail descend together,
and orchids cannot preserve their fragrance;
sandy soil flows with mud,
and the river’s source cannot retain its purity.

Yellow gold and tortoiseshell,
the virtuous wear as ornaments;
common earth and clay,
the capable use for wealth.
If one does not know their proper use,
even gold and jade become worthless as dung.
If one knows how to apply them,
even earth and clay become priceless as pearls.

Thus, giving gold and jade to the weak
is not as good as giving them writing paper.
*Weak* refers to the foolish and feeble.
Giving them paper may still be preserved,
but giving them gold and jade will bring harm—
just as placing a petty man in high office
will surely bring danger and ruin.

A hub holds thirty spokes,
each pulling straight toward its own slot,
yet none can enter another’s place—
just as ministers each hold their own duties.
The meaning does not differ from the earlier explanation.

The skilled user of men
is like the *xiān*’s foot—
many yet causing no harm,
like the tongue and teeth,
firm and soft grinding together without destruction.
*The xiān* is the centipede.
Those who skillfully employ many
are like the centipede, are they not?
The tongue and teeth,
hard and soft working together,
gather both the wise and foolish.
Let each follow its proper place,
and their talents will not be wasted.

Stone is born firm,
orchids are born fragrant—
if possessed in youth, their virtue grows clearer with age.
This is their inherent nature:
stone is firm, orchids are fragrant,
because the virtuous become ever clearer,
while the foolish grow ever dimmer.
Thus, firmness and fragrance cannot be taken away,
nor can foolishness and darkness be moved—
those who possess them in youth
see them only deepen with time.

Supporting and lifting,
declining and yielding,
gaining and losing,
agreeing and refusing—
these differ as heaven and earth.
How can they be the same?

Twice-born plants bear no fruit,
those that bloom too early fall before frost.
Double-honored yet barren,
the sun at its peak scorches its own standard,
dusting its brow—
the standard is the nose.
A nose stained with dust while its forehead is powdered—
like treating a hand’s ailment while neglecting the foot.
This is most unnatural.

A rotting rat in the ancestral hall,
its stench burning the hall’s beams—
plunging into water only to grow more drenched,
clutching foulness while seeking fragrance—
even the skilled cannot remedy it.
*The rotting rat* is like the deceitful and flattering.
The ruler who favors such men while seeking good governance
is like one drowning in water or burning the hall—
the calamity will not be small.

Winter ice can be broken,
summer wood can be knotted—
opportune moments are hard to find yet easy to lose.
Time is precious, fate fleeting—
warn the virtuous to act when the moment comes,
lest they lose it.

Wood in full growth may be cut all day and regrow,
but when autumn frost descends,
it withers in a single night.
Those who build merit and achieve great deeds
must not delay.

A bowstring drawn tight while arrows gather,
trees lush while axes enter—
not summoned, yet they come.
The bowstring does not seek to strike yet the arrows gather,
the forest does not call for felling yet the axes arrive—
this is the work of natural forces.

A suckling pup bites a tiger,
a crouching chicken strikes a weasel—
favor bestowed does not measure their strength.
Yet those who nurture such favor
will forget their own lives.

Those who wait for profit to rescue the drowning
will surely drown them with their own greed.

A boat can float or sink—
the foolish do not know where to place their trust.

The vessel rides upon the water and may also sink it.
Man thrives by advantage yet may also be drowned by it.
Only by discerning the measure of sufficiency
may one avoid the calamity of sinking.

A fine steed urged forward does not advance,
pulled back does not halt—
a ruler does not seek the Dao of distance.

The people are exhausted to their limits,
yet the sovereign’s greed knows no end;
a weary steed is driven further—
how can one chart a distant path?

Water, though level, must have ripples,
a balance, though true, must have deviation,
a ruler’s measure, though uniform, must have flaws.

Without the compass and square, one cannot define the square and round;
without the plumb and level, one cannot rectify the crooked and straight.
Yet those who wield the compass and square
also bear the mind of the compass and square.

When the sovereign establishes just and upright laws,
yet the people give rise to perverse and rebellious factions,
it is as though ripples arise from level water,
or a crooked mind stems from the upright balance—
the seeds of favor and aversion take root,
and the signs of chaos unfold.
It is not that the sovereign lacks laws and regulations,
but that the subjects have lost their compass and square.

The grandeur of Tai Mountain, though doubled, is unseen,
yet the tip of an autumn hair, though minute, may be discerned.
When one’s gaze is fixed upon the autumn hair,
though small, it may be perceived—
but when one’s path turns away from Tai Mountain,
though vast, it remains unseen.

The bamboo and wood, when struck by fire, do not ignite unless pierced or scorched;
The earth holds water within, yet without digging, it does not emerge.
Wood is concealed within fire, earth within water—
Without piercing or digging, they cannot be revealed.

The Dao resides within humanity—
Without study, it remains unknown.
An arrow’s swiftness does not exceed two *li*,
Yet with each halting step, the tortoise reaches a thousand *li*.
Accumulated grains, though small, form hills and mountains.

For those who seek the Way,
It is not the speed of their initial resolve that matters,
Nor the beauty of their prolonged devotion—
But that through perseverance, they reach a thousand *li*,
And through steadfastness, they build mountains.

To stand by the river and desire fish
Is not as wise as returning to weave nets.
The river’s fish are caught by nets,
And humanity’s Dao is grasped by the heart.

A bow must first be tuned before seeking strength,
A horse must first be guided before seeking excellence,
A person must first be trusted before seeking ability.
To grasp these three principles
Is to discern the essentials of all affairs.

A skilled smith cannot melt wood,
A master craftsman cannot carve ice—
Some things are beyond their capacity.
How then can the virtuous not reflect upon this?

What cannot be shaped cannot become an instrument,
Though the craftsman be skilled, his art finds no use.
What cannot be taught cannot be turned,
Though the sage speak, their intent remains unheeded.

To command people not to cross the river is possible,
But to command the river to have no waves is impossible.
Speak of the river’s waves, and speak of the world’s calamities—
To prevent people from inviting disaster is easy,
But to command the river to have no waves is difficult.
Say not that one is blameless—
The pot will not fall into the well.
*Gu* means guilt.
It speaks not of innocence,
But of the fate one invites.
The pot will not fall into the well—
How then can one avoid consequence?

Those who strike me in my path wish me to yield,
Those who snatch my goods wish me to trade.
Desire stirs within, and its form appears without.
A single chess move does not reveal one’s skill,
A single plucked string does not express one’s sorrow.
A single provocation, though met, does not exhaust justice.
A single string, though drawn, is not yet called mastery.

Now, take a burning ember—
To grasp it risks scorching one’s fingers,
Yet a thousand *dan* of charcoal may be warmed at a distance.
Same *qi*, yet differing accumulation—
There is splendor, and there is decay.
Splendor and decay alternate,
Prosperity and adversity oppose.
Above, there is fine silk,
Below, there must be coarse hemp.
Excessive indulgence cuts deep.
When the sovereign is extravagant, adorning himself in fine silks,
The common folk freeze and starve, lacking sustenance.
How can the ruler not take heed?

Great trees have deep roots,
High mountains have firm foundations.
The sovereign takes the people as his root,
And humility as his foundation.

Laozi said:
*The drum does not store sound, yet it produces sound;
The mirror does not contain form, yet it reveals form.*
The drum does not store sound, the mirror does not contain form—
Yet they produce sound and reveal form.

Metal and stone have sound, yet without striking, they do not ring;
Flutes and pipes have music, yet without blowing, they are silent.
Metal, stone, flutes, and pipes cannot sound of their own accord—
All require striking or blowing to produce sound.
Thus, all beings inherit virtue and power,
Yet without cultivation, they remain incomplete.

Therefore, the sage conceals within,
Does not lead others by example,
Acts only when circumstances demand,
Responds only when things present themselves.
The sage’s words are not spoken lightly,
His actions are not taken in vain.

Heaven’s motion never ceases,
Ending only to begin anew—
Thus it endures.
A wheel turns where it ought,
Thus it reaches distant places.
Heaven’s motion is divine and unfathomable,
Flowing endlessly without measure.
Heaven’s motion never errs,
Thus it knows no failure.

Heaven’s *qi* descends, earth’s *qi* ascends,
Yin and yang intermingle, harmonizing all things.
Heaven’s motion, unerring, the sovereign’s governance, unflawed—
Thus, when Heaven and Earth’s exchange is unobstructed,
All creatures flourish,
Ruler and subject are content,
And all levels of society thrive.

The virtuous thrive in their endeavors,
The petty perish—
This is the Way of Heaven and Earth.
To remove the wicked and employ the worthy
Is to align with Heaven and Earth’s Way.

When Heaven’s *qi* does not descend, earth’s *qi* does not ascend,
Yin and yang do not communicate,
All things do not prosper,
The petty gain influence, the virtuous decline,
The five grains do not grow,
Virtue and power are concealed within.

When Heaven and Earth’s *qi* do not exchange,
Yin and yang’s *qi* does not flow—
It is because the sovereign’s virtue and power are unused,
The deceitful and flatterers prevail,
The petty hold office, the virtuous are exiled,
Causing all things to wither and the five grains to fail.

The Way of Heaven: it gathers the abundant to benefit the scarce,
The Way of Earth: it reduces the high to elevate the low—
This is the Way of Heaven and Earth.

The Way of spirits: the proud and overflowing are humbled,
The arrogant and overflowing meet the humble.
Humanity’s Way: the abundant do not hoard,
The wealthy do not increase their wealth.

The sage’s Way: though lowly, none can surpass him.
He never seeks to be above others,
Thus he is honored by all.

When Heaven’s light shines bright, it illuminates the four quarters,
When the sovereign is enlightened, his ministers are enlightened,
And the realm is at peace.
The realm has four enlightened ones,
And thus it endures.
To enlighten those who enlighten others—
This is to enlighten their transformations.
When the four enlightened ones are complete,
All the people are transformed.

Heaven’s Way is manifest in the *wen*—the sun, moon, and stars.
Earth’s Way is manifest in the *li*—mountains, marshes, rivers, and seas.
One harmonizes them, blending with the undifferentiated *qi*.
Time governs them, responding without disorder,
Giving rise to all things—
This is called the Dao.

It nourishes and sustains all things,
Yet does not rule as their master—
Thus it is called the Dao.

The Great Dao is vast and near,
Not distant from the self.
Cultivate it within oneself,
And its virtue becomes true.
Cultivate it in all things,
And its virtue is unbroken.
To cultivate its truth within is called *zhen*,
To nurture its virtue externally is called *de*.

Heaven covers all things,
Bestowing its virtue and nourishing them,
Giving without taking—
Thus all spirits return to it.
To give without taking is the highest virtue,
Thus it is called *de*.

None is higher than Heaven,
None is lower than the marshes.
Heaven is high, the marshes are low—
The sage follows this model,
Establishing distinctions of honor and humility,
And thus the realm is settled.

The low and the high are arranged in order,
The noble and the base are positioned accordingly.

The Earth bears all things and nurtures them,
Giving and taking in return—
Thus the flesh and bones return to it.
Giving and taking is the lower virtue,
Yet the lower virtue does not lose its virtue—
Thus it is called *virtue*.

Not taking is called the Dao of Heaven:
Heaven generates all things,
Yet only nourishes and sustains them,
Not claiming their substance—
Thus the spirits return to the upper realm.
The one who does not take is the highest virtue,
Thus it is called *virtue*.

The one who takes is called the Dao of the Earth:
Though it completes the forms of all things,
It returns them to their original nature—
Thus the flesh and bones descend to the lower realm,
And this is called *without virtue*.

The Earth receives from Heaven,
Thus it is settled and tranquil;
The Earth is settled and tranquil,
Thus all things take form;
The Earth is vast and thick,
Thus all things gather.
Settled and tranquil, it bears nothing it does not,
Vast and thick, it contains nothing it does not.
The Earth’s power is deep and enduring,
And the waters’ sources converge within it;
The Earth’s Dao is broad and long-lasting,
Thus it can endure and flourish eternally.
The sage follows this model,
And virtue knows no limit in its embrace.

It is said: Heaven and Earth support one another,
To achieve boundless breadth and thickness.
The ruler and minister trust one another,
Thus they can govern in harmony.

When *yin* overcomes *yang*,
All things thrive;
When *yang* overcomes *yin*,
All things stagnate.
When all things thrive, none lacks sustenance;
When all things stagnate, none lacks joy.
When all things are joyful, none remains ungoverned.

When *yin* harms things, *yang* bends itself,
When *yin* advances, *yang* retreats—
Then the petty man gains influence,
And the virtuous withdraw from harm.
The Way is thus clear:
If *yang* is restrained by *yin*,
Then the realm is harmonious and governed;
If the minister overcomes the ruler,
Then the petty man holds power.

When *yang qi* moves, all things relax and find their place—
Thus the sage follows the Dao of *yang*.

To follow things, they will follow in return;
To oppose things, they will oppose in return—
Thus one does not lose the nature and disposition of things.

The *yin* sound is like the *wu* bird.
When the marshes are full, all things flourish in due season;
When the marshes are dry, all things wither.
Thus if the rains do not come,
The land becomes barren and abandoned.
When *yang* ascends and then descends again,
It becomes the master of all things.
It does not overindulge in growth,
Thus it can end and begin anew;
Ending and beginning anew,
Thus it can endure forever.
To endure forever,
Thus it is the mother of all things.

The sage follows the Dao of Heaven,
Acting without effort to endure eternally;
To oppose the nature of things,
Even one in high position cannot hold it.

The *yang qi* must accumulate before it can be applied,
The *yin qi* must gather before it can transform—
None can apply or transform without first accumulating.
Thus the sage is cautious in what he gathers.

Gather *virtue* and blessings follow,
Gather evil and ruin comes.
When *yang* extinguishes *yin*,
All things grow fat;
When *yin* extinguishes *yang*,
All things decay.
Thus, if kings and nobles uphold the Dao of *yang*,
All things thrive;
If they uphold the Dao of *yin*,
The realm perishes.

The *yang* is upright, life-giving—
Thus all things grow fat.
Fatness brings thriving;
The *yin* is perverse, death-dealing—
Thus all things decay.
Decay leads to ruin.

If *yang* does not descend to *yin*,
Then all things cannot form;
If the ruler does not descend to the minister,
Then the virtue of transformation does not flow—
Thus the ruler descends to the minister and becomes wise,
Or does not descend and becomes dull and deaf.

The ruler is not the utmost sage,
Yet cannot descend to the minister;
The minister is not the utmost worthy,
Yet cannot assist the ruler.
Yu Shun bowed to Bo Cheng,
King Wen deferred to Shangfu—
This is called wisdom.

The sun rises from the Earth,
And all things multiply and grow;
Kings and nobles dwell above the people,
To manifest *virtue* and the Way.
The sun sets into the Earth,
And all things rest and recover;
The petty man dwells among the people,
And above them flee in hiding.

It is said:
If *yang* does not descend to *yin*,
Then all things cannot thrive;
If the ruler does not descend to the minister,
Then all things are concealed.

When thunder moves, all things awaken,
When dew moistens, all things are refreshed—
The great man’s actions are like this.

The movements of *yin* and *yang* have constant rhythms,
Yet the great man’s actions do not exhaust things.
When the great man is at his peak,
It is like spring’s thunder.
When he issues his commands,
It is like the gentle wind.
Both are harmonious and follow the Way,
Thus they do not exhaust things.

Thunder stirs the Earth,
And all things relax;
Wind sways the trees,
And grasses and woods flourish.
The great man abandons evil and pursues goodness,
And the people do not stray far—
Thus the people have paths of departure and return.
The more they depart, the more they regret;
The less they return, the more they are at ease.

Moreover, when the great man has goodness,
The people flock to him.
Like the Great King’s departure from Bi,
How distant was it?

Fire moves only with fuel,
The great man’s words must have truth.
With truth and sincerity,
Where can he not succeed?
Fire spreads as fuel is passed on;
Words take effect as truth is upheld.
Thus, when the great man speaks,
His actions follow without delay,
And his truth is like the four seasons.

The Yellow River is deep,
Its banks lie against the mountains;
The hills and mounds are high,
Their depths sink into the abyss.
When *yang qi* is strong, it transforms into *yin*;
When *yin qi* is strong, it transforms into *yang*—
Thus desire cannot be fully satisfied,
And joy cannot be pushed to its extreme.
The Dao of Heaven is sublime and elevated,
Yet only moderates desire to maintain harmony,
To follow Heaven’s principles,
And not let things reach their limits.

Anger without harsh words,
Rage without changing countenance—
This is called *calculating* (obtaining) virtue.
Though anger and rage remain,
Yet harsh words and perverse expressions are not uttered.
If they do not manifest externally,
Then virtue is obtained within—
This is called *calculating* virtue through the Way.

The *yang* spreads harmoniously throughout the realm,
The *yin* sinks and drowns the realm in chaos.
To lean toward *yang* is to align with loyal and virtuous,
Thus harmony prevails.
To lean toward *yin* is to align with flatterers and deceivers,
Thus chaos descends.

Laozi said:
Accumulating thinness forms thickness,
Accumulating lowliness forms height.
The noble man strives daily to achieve brilliance,
The petty man strives daily to reach disgrace.
The noble man diligently cultivates himself to follow the Dao,
And his radiance increases daily.
The petty man rides on the threshold to indulge his desires,
And ultimately meets ruin and shame.

Though these changes may not be seen,
Yet to see goodness as if it were not attainable,
And to regard past evil as if it were not ominous—
If one turns toward goodness,
Even past mistakes bring no blame;
If one does not turn toward goodness,
Even loyalty cannot avert coming misfortune.
Thus blaming others is not as grave as blaming oneself,
And striving to win others is not as effective as striving within oneself.

Sound is self-summoned,
Kindred spirits seek one another,
Reputation is self-bestowed,
And people are self-appointed—
None of these are beyond oneself.

With a sharp blade to pierce, with an edge to strike—
What cause for blame against others?

Thus the noble man guards against the subtle.
To guard the subtle means words need not be grand.
If one turns toward goodness,
Then virtue comes not from others—
Strive within oneself.

If one does not turn toward goodness,
Then calamity returns to oneself—
What cause for blame against others?
To do evil is like wielding a blade to cut oneself,
Or piling fire upon fire to douse it—
Who then would censure you?

All things embrace yin while cradling yang,
Their clashing qi harmonized.
This harmony dwells at the center—
Thus grains bear fruit in their husks,
Grasses bear fruit in their pods,
Eggs and wombs gestate within the center,
And those without eggs or wombs
Take time to manifest.
Though forms and kinds differ,
In their generation,
All arise from central harmony to form their essence,
And thus require time to complete.

Yet what they become through transformation
Is what must unfold in due course.

When the earth is level, water does not flow;
When light and heavy are balanced, the scale does not tilt.
The earth level, and water has no rushing force;
The scale balanced, and things have no weighty tilt.

The generation and transformation of things
Come from response—
Not without cause.
All things in their birth
Have their own triggers—
Not in vain.

Laozi said:
When mountains reach their height,
Then clouds and rain arise there.
When waters reach their depth,
Then dragons and serpents take form.
When the noble man fulfills the Dao,
Then virtue and blessings flow from him.

Those who harbor hidden virtue
Shall receive manifest reward,
Those who act in secret
Shall gain renowned name.
The mountains’ spirits, when most potent,
Descend as clouds and rain.
The Dao’s heights, when most sublime,
Bestow virtue and blessings.

Never does reward come before the deed.
He who plants millet does not reap barley,
He who sows resentment gains no gratitude.
Plant millet and reap barley—
Return resentment for virtue.

*End of Volume Six of the Treatise on the Mysterious and True*
#1 *Bì*: The *Qú* text reads *"deep."*
#2 *Niǎn*: The *Qú* text reads *"restrain."*
#3 *Zhǔn*: The original text reads *"govern,"* corrected according to the *Qú* text.
#4 *Kùoduō*: The *Qú* text reads *"reduce excess."*

*Volume Seven of the Treatise on the Mysterious and True*
*Commentary by Mò Xīzǐ*

The subtle luminosity—
Dao encompasses what lies beyond form, hence *subtle*;
virtue conceals itself within the unseen, hence *luminous*.

Thus, without subtlety, one cannot fathom its origin;
without luminosity, one cannot grasp its essence.
The meaning of *subtle luminosity* lies in its embodiment and function.

Laozi says:
*The Dao may be weak or strong,
soft or hard,
yin or yang,
obscure or luminous,
enveloping heaven and earth,
responding without fixed form.*
This aligns with the intent of *"The Origin of the Dao."*

Shallow knowledge knows not the depths,
outer knowledge knows not the inner,
coarse knowledge knows not the refined,
known knowledge is but unknown,
unknown knowledge is true knowing—
who then knows knowing as unknown,
unknown as knowing?

The Dao cannot be heard—heard, it is not;
the Dao cannot be seen—seen, it is not;
the Dao cannot be spoken—spoken, it is not.
Who then knows the formless form?

Thus, all under heaven know goodness as goodness—
and so it ceases to be good.
The wise do not speak; those who speak are not wise.

The Dao transcends form and sound—
how then can hearing and seeing discern it?
Virtue requires no adornment—
how can good and evil define it?
Thus, the wise do not speak; those who speak are not wise—
this is the utmost truth.

**Wénzǐ asked:** Can one speak in subtlety?
**Laozi replied:** Why not?
Do you mean *speaking of knowing*?
He who speaks of knowing does not speak through words.
*Subtle speech* is speech of the utmost refinement.
Only when one forgets the words can one truly speak.

Chasing fish, one’s hands grow wet;
hunting beasts, one’s feet grow swift—
not for joy’s sake.
Thus, the utmost speech transcends speech,
the utmost action transcends action.
The shallow-minded contend over trifles.

Speech has its root; affairs have their master.
To act without knowing is why one is not known.

The Dao is the nameless mystery;
speech is the root of ultimate truth.
Those who grasp the mystery speak not;
those who clarify the root contend not.
Thus, speech reaches its peak in silence,
action in inaction—
and knowing knows itself.
Who then departs from my knowing?

**Wénzǐ asked:** Does governing a state also have its method?
**Laozi replied:** Observe the wagon-driver:
He calls forward *"Yā!"* and *"Xī!"*—
the fire must be stoked.
The rear echoes in response—
this is the driver’s song to spur the horses.
Though the music of Zhèng, Wèi, Hú, or Chǔ
cannot match its purpose.

Governing a state relies on ritual propriety,
not on eloquence.
The more laws proliferate, the more thieves arise.
What is used must suit its purpose;
each must meet its necessity—
like the wagon-driver’s song,
yet not performed with the music of *Xiánqí*.

Governing a state and nourishing the people
prioritizes simplicity—
why adorn with ornate colors?

Laozi says:
*The Dao has no fixed form yet can be the standard;
like mountains and forests yielding timber,
but timber does not equal mountains and forests,
mountains and forests do not equal clouds and rain,
clouds and rain do not equal yin and yang,
yin and yang do not equal harmony,
harmony does not equal the Dao.*
The Dao is the form without form,
the image without substance—
its intent cannot be exhausted.
Yet between heaven and earth,
it can be shaped and transformed without end.

The great Dao has no fixed origin;
just as timber arises from mountains and forests,
though mountains and forests are not timber.
From clouds and rain onward,
what is spoken falls short of the Dao—
for it lacks form and image,
yet embraces all things,
encompassing all directions.
Only those who embody the Dao know its boundless transformation.

Laozi says:
*The sage establishes teachings and governs,
ever mindful of beginning and end,
observing the creation of virtue.*
*Creation of virtue* means establishing laws.
Thus, when the people know writing, virtue declines;
when they know calculations, benevolence declines;
when they know contracts, trust declines;
when they know machinery, substance declines.
These four arise only when the Dao is lost.
As times change, laws must adapt—
the stricter the laws, the more they are transgressed.
Thus, as trust declines and machinery is devised,
governance and teaching flourish—
yet deceit and corruption worsen.

The *sè* does not sound, yet its twenty-five strings each respond in harmony;
the axle does not turn itself, yet its thirty spokes each contribute their strength.
Strings must differ in tension to compose melody;
wheels must vary in labor to travel far.
What appears to make sound is silent;
what appears to turn is still.
The *sè* has no sound—sound lies in the strings.
The axle does not turn—turning lies in the wheels.
Thus, silence yields sound, stillness yields motion—
silence’s sound completes the melody,
stillness’s turning achieves distance.

Superior and inferior follow different paths—
ease in governance leads to disorder.
High position with great virtue follows;
great affairs with petty virtue bring ruin.
A cap cannot be trodden upon the feet;
a subject cannot be honored above the sovereign.
When superior and inferior are disordered—
ruin is imminent.

Minor virtue harms righteousness,
minor goodness harms the Dao,
minor eloquence harms governance,
harshness injures virtue.
Clinging to petty kindness obscures the great Dao;
indulging petty anger harms supreme virtue.

The greatest rectitude is unassailable,
thus the people are easily guided.
The utmost governance is effortless,
thus the lower ranks do not rebel.
The utmost loyalty returns to simplicity,
thus the people harbor no deceit.
When the superior is balanced and just,
the lower ranks harbor no deceit.
When the superior is pure and unadorned,
the lower ranks harbor no craftiness.

Laozi says:
*If laws of mutual guarantee are established,
the people will resent them;
if decrees reducing rank proliferate,
meritorious ministers will rebel.*
Litigation spreads—
the innocent bear the blame.
Ranks are diminished in silence—
the meritorious harbor resentment.

The sagacious discern only the traces of legal documents yet fail to grasp the

The Sage dwells in seclusion to avoid calamity, remains silent and still to await the proper moment;
The petty man knows not the gates of fortune and misfortune—he acts and falls into punishment. Though he makes every effort to prepare, it is insufficient to preserve his body.

Thus, the superior man first avoids calamity and then pursues advantage, first shuns disgrace and only then seeks fame.
Therefore, the Sage always attends to what lies beyond the formless, yet does not dwell on what is already manifest within—thus, calamity has no means to arrive, and praise cannot soil him.

When one is ensnared by gain and harm, it arises from love and hatred.
If love and hatred do not arise, where do slander and praise come from?

The noble man sees what is yet unformed and finds it easy to govern; the petty man, though he makes every effort to prepare, ultimately meets disaster.
To rescue what is already formed—once it is complete, escape is difficult.

Laozi said: The Way of the common man—his heart should be small, his aspirations great, his wisdom encompassing, his conduct upright, his abilities manifold, his undertakings few.
By "heart small," it means to anticipate misfortune before it arises, to guard against disaster with utmost caution, and to dare not indulge his desires.
By "aspirations great," it means to embrace all under heaven, to unify disparate customs, to gather all judgments and make them converge at the center.
By "wisdom encompassing," it means to have no beginning or end, to flow in all directions, to be deep and inexhaustible like a fountain.
By "conduct upright," it means to stand firm and unyielding, to remain pure and unstained, to hold fast to principles in adversity and not to boast in prosperity.
By "abilities manifold," it means to possess both literary and martial virtues, to act and rest in accordance with propriety, to wield and discard things with fitting measure.
By "undertakings few," it means to grasp the essential and govern the multitude, to hold fast to simplicity and manage the vast, to remain still and thus control the restless.

Thus, the one whose heart is small is restrained by the subtle;
The one whose aspirations are great embraces all within his bosom;
The one whose wisdom is encompassing knows all without effort;
The one whose conduct is upright has nothing he will not do;
The one whose abilities are manifold governs all without fail;
The one whose undertakings are few holds fast to what is essential.

These six virtues—none but the most exalted Sage or High True One can fully embody them.

Thus, when the Sage encounters goodness, he does not deem it too small to act upon, nor does he overlook even the slightest fault without correction. His actions require no shamans or sorcerers, yet ghosts and spirits dare not act before him—this is the height of nobility.
Yet he remains ever vigilant, cautious each day, and thus achieves unity through non-action.
Externally, he does not fail the world; internally, he feels no shame.
Why invoke shamans? Why fear ghosts and spirits?
By maintaining constant reverence, one may preserve his end.

The wisdom of the foolish is already meager, yet their actions are many—thus, they are bound to fail.
Therefore, governing through proper instruction and teaching is easy and certain to succeed;
Governing through perverse teaching is difficult and certain to fail.
To abandon what is easy and certain to succeed, and instead engage in what is difficult and certain to fail—this is the folly of the confused.
Without measuring gain and loss, one sits and invites ruin.
When undertakings are complex and difficult, though labored, they will fail.
When things are simple and easy to follow, they will succeed—yet to not act upon them is the height of folly.

Laozi said: The rise of fortune is gradual and unbroken, while the birth of calamity is sudden and chaotic. The patterns of fortune and misfortune are subtle and unseen.
The Sage perceives their beginning and end—thus, they cannot be ignored.
Fortune is as light as a feather, yet only the Sage discerns it;
Calamity is as vast as Tai Mountain, yet the foolish see it not.

The enlightened ruler’s rewards and punishments are not for himself, but for the state.
What benefits himself but serves no function for the state—he does not bestow reward upon it;
What harms himself but aids the state—he does not inflict punishment upon it.
The enlightened ruler’s rewards and punishments are based on impartial justice.
What benefits the state and aids the people—he implements it;
What profits himself but harms the people—he halts it.

Thus, to uphold propriety in accordance with what is fitting is called a noble man;
To abandon propriety’s fitting measure is called a petty man.
Are noble and petty men fixed in their nature?
When actions and conduct align with what is fitting, he is a noble man;
When actions and conduct defy what is fitting, he is a petty man.

The highest wisdom is obtained without effort—this is the esoteric understanding of the superior man.
The next level is to labor without exhaustion—the diligent common man, striving without weariness.
The lowest is to labor while sick—
The inferior man’s heart is sluggish and confused, his spirit dull and muddled, thus his labor is excessive and his illness worsens.

The ancients savored the Dao without clinging to it;
The people of today abandon the Dao and yet boast of themselves.
Not to cling is not to dwell.
To savor the Way is to savor.
The ancients savored the Way without dwelling, while today’s people have no Way yet praise themselves.

Zhou’s ruler made ivory chopsticks—
Chopsticks crafted from elephant tusk—
And Ji Zi sighed, lamenting their extravagant splendor.
Lu buried their dead with wooden effigies—
Effigies, carved wooden figures resembling humans, used as ritual vessels—
And Confucius sighed, mourning their violation of propriety.

See where it begins, and you will know where it ends.

The petty man sees ivory chopsticks—
chopsticks crafted from elephant tusk—
and wooden effigies—
carved wooden figures resembling humans, used as ritual vessels—
and assumes that their life is one of honor and their death one of grandeur.

The gentleman examines them and knows that their Dao is in decline, their fortune lost.

Laozi said: *"Ren* is what men admire, *yi* is what men hold in high regard.
To be admired by men and held in high regard by men—
yet sometimes one’s own death and one’s country’s ruin—
is because one did not conform to the times.

Thus, he who knows *ren* and *yi* yet does not know the world’s power—
is not versed in the Dao.

To vainly extol the virtues of *ren* and *yi*—
yet fail to recognize the shifts of opportunity and strategy—
is of no use in averting defeat and ruin.
How can such a one be called a man of comprehensive understanding?

The Five Emperors valued *de* and ruled without action;
The Three Kings employed *yi* and punished the violent to pacify the people.

The Five Hegemons relied on strength—
strength in terms of martial and intellectual prowess.

Now, if one applies the methods of the Five Emperors and Three Kings to the age of the Five Hegemons,
it is not their proper Dao.

Thus, what is good or bad is the same,
yet praise or blame lies in convention;
what is proper or improper is equal,
yet conformity or rebellion depends on the times.

Speaking of times being different and governance varying,
to practice the Dao of the most ancient era in the age of the Five Hegemons
is like gluing a column to a tuning peg—
a fixed and unyielding approach—
or quenching thirst with poison.
Indeed, it is difficult.

He who knows the Heaven’s workings and understands human affairs
can thereby govern the world and traverse the Dao.

To govern is to maintain the norm.

He who knows Heaven yet does not know man
will fail to engage with the world’s conventions;
he who knows man yet does not know Heaven
will fail to wander in the Dao.

Knowing Heaven and knowing man,
knowing convention and knowing the times—
one can govern the world and wander in the Dao.

To align one’s resolve with true nature and suit one’s emotions,
if one insists rigidly, one becomes a thief to the Dao—
using one’s body to serve external things.
If one succumbs to external things, one exhausts one’s vital *qi*—
like ice and fire clashing,
or the extremes of *yin* and *yang* disrupting balance.

The man who attains the Dao,
though outwardly transformed, inwardly remains unaltered.
Outward transformation is to know man;
inward constancy is to preserve one’s self.
Thus, within he holds firm to his principles,
while without he bends and adapts,
moves with the world without falling into harm—
the precious quality of the Dao lies in its *long* variation.

The man who attains the Dao,
when he acts, does so in harmony with Heaven;
when he is still, he rests upon Earth.
His actions and stillness are lost without timing,
yet his withdrawal and engagement lie within himself—
thus, convention cannot harm him,
and the world cannot constrain him.

Hence, Master Kong saw the Old Sage—
Laozi—
as though he were a *long*.
Variation without fixed form.

To uphold a single principle and follow one course of action,
even if it leads to fulfillment,
it is still inflexible—
clinging to petty preferences while obstructing the great Dao.

If one is stuck in one way,
how can one speak of the great Dao?

The Dao is serene and tranquil, void and formless—
it does not act upon things,
nor does it act upon itself.
Between things and self, distinction is naturally erased.

The serene and tranquil state of the Dao
is effortless yet profound.

Thus, to undertake actions while conforming to the Dao—
is not the Dao’s own doing,
but the Dao’s application.

The Dao is inherently without action,
yet now it is said to conform to the Dao—
this is indeed action.
Action brings about strife,
and strife spawns calamity.

Since the Dao has no fixed appearance
and actions have no fixed conformity,
to insist upon them is to rebel against them—
then disputes arise in confusion,
and misfortune is the result.
Hence, it is said that this is not the Dao’s own doing.

*Shi* is to set forth—
to establish norms and rituals as the Dao’s outward expressions,
not its true essence.
They cannot be rigidly adhered to.

To adhere is to lose its meaning;
to act upon it is to fail in its purpose.

The principle can be made clear.

The heavens and earth enfold and bear all things,
the sun and moon illuminate them,
the yin and yang nurture them,
the rain and dew nourish them,
and virtue and the Dao uphold them—
all share in the same harmony.

Thus, those who bear the great round
and tread the great square
are said to wear the heavens and walk upon the earth.
Those who gaze upon the utmost purity
see the great brightness—
to behold the sun and moon.
Those who establish the utmost peace
dwell in the great hall—
to be within the cosmos.

Those who wander in the boundless dark
shine with the sun and moon,
giving life to form from formlessness—
thus, the true person entrusts themselves to the spirit platform
and returns to the origin of things,
reverting to the unborn.

To gaze into the boundless dark,
to listen to the soundless—
within the boundless dark, there is alone clarity,
within the stillness, there is alone illumination.

The true person, dwelling between heaven and earth,
witnessing the light of sun and moon,
wandering in the utmost peace—
where could they go without finding ease?
Residing in the great hall,
they are contained within no boundless dark,
discerning the soundless,
while all sounds respond within the stillness,
illuminating the formless,
while all forms appear—
thus, they preserve with heaven and earth,
shine with the sun and moon,
entrust themselves to the spirit platform,
and contain the utmost essence.
They are the true person.

Their use is in non-use,
and only when not using can they truly use.
Their knowing is in non-knowing,
and only when not knowing can they truly know.

「Before, this was explained.

The **Dao/Way** is what gives rise to all things,
**Virtue/Power** is what sustains life,
**Ren** is the proof of accumulated benevolence,
**Yi** is what aligns with the heart yet harmonizes with the masses, fittingly so.

These four—used to govern the world and cultivate oneself—must not be abandoned.

When the **Dao/Way** is lost, **Virtue/Power** flourishes;
when **Virtue/Power** declines, **Ren** and **Yi** arise.
Thus, in the upper age, the **Dao/Way** prevails yet **Virtue/Power** is not yet established,
in the middle age, **Virtue/Power** is upheld yet **Ren** and **Yi** are not cherished,
in the lower age, **Ren** and **Yi** are clung to, fearing their loss—
so the **Junzi** (noble person) cannot live without **Yi**, and losing **Yi** means losing what sustains their life;
the **xiaoren** (petty person) cannot survive without profit, and losing profit means losing what sustains their existence.
Thus, the **Junzi** fears losing **Yi** as misfortune,
while the **xiaoren** seeks profit as fortune.
Observing their fears, the paths to calamity and blessing diverge.

When the **Dao/Way** is abandoned, **Virtue/Power** perishes;
when **Ren** is severed, **Yi** grows thin.
The **Junzi** without **Yi** cannot preserve their **Dao/Way**,
the **xiaoren** without profit cannot sustain their life.
The **Junzi** fears losing **Yi** as misfortune,
while the **xiaoren** pursues profit as fortune.

Laozi says:
*"If one seeks profit in affairs,
it only brings harm;
if one seeks harm in affairs,
it only brings profit."*

To feed the **hot** (excessive heat) when one suffers from **dampness** (湿),
to drink the **cold** (excessive cold) when one is parched—
this is how the common folk nourish their ailments,
yet the skilled physician knows it as the cause of disease.
Delight in the eyes, delight in the heart—
the advantage of the foolish,
the avoidance of those with the **Dao/Way**.

The **sage** (圣人) first meets and then aligns,
the common folk first align and then meet—
thus, the gates of misfortune and fortune,
the inversion of harm and profit,
must be carefully observed.

To quench thirst with water—
the skilled physician sees it as misfortune;
to take wealth from unjust means—
the **Junzi** sees it as harm.

To first oppose and then align—
the folly of the foolish;
to first align and then oppose—
the abhorrence of the sage.

For harm and profit are contrary,
misfortune and fortune are interdependent—
they must be carefully observed.

Laozi says:
*"One with merit who abandons **Ren** and **Yi** will be met with suspicion,
one with guilt who possesses **Ren** and **Yi** will be met with trust."*

Thus, **Ren** and **Yi** are the natural order of affairs,
the most honored rank in the world.
Though one’s achievements and name may already be established,
**Ren** and **Yi** must not be abandoned—
to abandon them invites guilt,
to uphold them secures honor.
Though one’s schemes may succeed,
they should consider releasing their ambitions to preserve **Yi**—
if a state exists yet its affairs abandon **Yi**,
its merit will not be fulfilled.
Though one’s plans may not align with strategy,
if they benefit the state,
and if one’s heart is devoted to the ruler,
harmonizing with **Ren** and **Yi**,
then their life will be preserved.
Hence it is said:
*"A hundred words, a hundred schemes,
though often misplaced,
are not as good as abandoning them
and carefully upholding **Ren** and **Yi**."*

As a subject of the ruler,
if one schemes to resolve difficulties,
arrogantly honors oneself,
yet their merit does not succeed—
it is because they abandoned **Ren** and **Yi**.
Some may have wise counsel,
yet it is not used;
some may have extraordinary schemes,
yet they fail to be implemented;
some may serve the ruler with all their might,
though not noticed,
yet they preserve **Ren** and **Yi**,
never daring to forget them for a moment,
and thus their life remains unharmed.

Laozi says:
*"Teaching originates from the **Junzi**,
while the **xiaoren** benefit from its influence;
profit originates from the **xiaoren**,
while the **Junzi** enjoy its merit.
If the **Junzi** and **xiaoren** each find their proper place,
then governance is effortless,
the **Dao/Way** is fulfilled.
**Virtue/Power’s** influence reaches below,
wealth and profit are offered above—
then there is no need for officials,
yet all things are governed;
no need for commands,
yet all things proceed in order.
Each finds their proper place—
this is the fulfillment of the **Dao/Way**.*

**Virtue/Power** (德) as the root, **subordinates** (臣) as the branches—
if the root is not strong yet the branches flourish,
such a thing has never existed.

The **sage’s** governance is clear in their **four eyes** (四目),
perceptive in their **four ears** (四聪),
rejecting deceit and evil,
trusting the virtuous and capable—
then the ruler may sit back with hands folded,
doing nothing yet all is transformed,
and the subjects may give their utmost effort,
fulfilling their duties without strife.
How could there be logic in contention?
Thus, the root grows ever stronger,
the branches flourish abundantly.

Laozi says:
*"A benevolent father’s love for his child
is not for seeking repayment—
if it were, it could not be sincere.
A sagely ruler’s care for the people
is not for personal use—
by nature, it cannot be otherwise.
Yet if one relies on their strength,
depends on their merits,
and must exhaust them for gain,
then benevolence is not extended.
The father’s love for his child,
the ruler’s care for his people—
why seek repayment?
This is the natural division,
the **Dao/Way** of heaven.
Some rulers and fathers,
relying on their strength,
arrogant toward their subjects and children—
their benevolence is not extended.
Thus, to use what the common folk love,
one gains the common folk’s strength;
to raise what the common folk delight in,
one gains the common folk’s hearts.
Hence, by observing their beginning,
one knows their end.
To love all under heaven,
though the world be vast,
it is as one family—
not to love all under heaven,
though the individual be humble,
they are like a lone gate in a million fields.
By this, the end and beginning may be known,
life and death may be discerned.*

Laozi says:
*"People rely on **Yi** for love,
cliques rely on **group strength** (群强) for power—
thus, the broader the application of **Yi**,
the farther the influence of **wei** (威/authority) extends;
the thinner the application of **Yi**,
the smaller the reach of **wu** (武/force) becomes.
This means:
**Virtue/Power’s** influence is impartial,
those who attach to it are many;
to abandon **Yi** and use **force**,
those who remain are few."*

Laozi says:
*"To take by unjust means yet not distribute—
disaster will come to their own person,
they cannot benefit others,
nor can they benefit themselves—
this is truly foolish,
no different from the owl’s love for its young.
To take unjustly and hoard without dispersing—
this is called **self-destructive nourishment** (养自喷),
**self-inflicted calamity** (育枭自祸).
Hence, to hold and let it overflow—
it is better to let it be;
to grasp and make it sharp—
it cannot be long preserved.

*(#5: "热" and "寒" retain their TCM meanings—excessive heat and cold respectively.
"盈" retains its original meaning of "overflowing" or "excessive" in the context of hoarding.)*

The gates may be firmly barred and the granaries filled,
yet they will ultimately become the resources of great thieves—
how can such wealth endure?

Within **virtue/power** lies the **Way/Dao**, and within the **Way/Dao** lies **virtue/power**—
its transformations are boundless.

Those who embody the **Way/Dao** must possess **virtue/power**,
and those who possess **virtue/power** must embody the **Way/Dao**.
When **virtue/power** and the **Way/Dao** are fully realized,
they align with the boundless transformations of nature.

Within the bright (yang) lies the dark (yin),
and within the dark (yin) lies the bright (yang)—
all things are thus,
inexhaustible in their manifestations.

When fortune arrives, auspicious signs appear;
when calamity strikes, ominous signs precede it.
To see auspicious signs yet fail to act virtuously—
fortune will not come.
To see ominous signs yet act virtuously—
calamity will not strike.

Benefit and harm share the same threshold,
misfortune and fortune dwell as neighbors—
none but the sage can distinguish them.

Thus it is said:
*"Misfortune is fortune’s shadow,
fortune is misfortune’s hidden seed—
who can fathom their limits?"*

Within the bright (yang) lies the dark (yin),
and within the dark (yin) lies the bright (yang)—
speaking of fortune concealed in misfortune,
and misfortune lurking in fortune.

To see fortune and deem it auspicious—
one knows fortune is the beginning of misfortune;
to see misfortune and hastily act virtuously—
one knows misfortune is the precursor to fortune.

The coming of misfortune and fortune
is like intertwined strands—
none but the sage can discern their limits.

When a person’s illness approaches,
they first crave the taste of fish and meat;
when a nation is on the verge of ruin,
it first rejects the words of loyal ministers.

The sick delight in flavors,
revel in delicacies—
a sure sign of impending death.
A disordered state despises loyal counsel,
trusts flatterers—
a clear omen of impending collapse.

Thus, when death is near,
even the finest physician cannot save.
When a nation teeters on ruin,
even the most loyal counsel cannot preserve it.

Only the finest physicians and loyal ministers
can recognize the inevitable—
that which cannot be saved should not be treated,
that which can be salvaged must be acted upon.

Cultivate oneself first,
then one may govern the people.
Manage the household with order,
then one may lead the officials.

Thus it is said:
*"Cultivate oneself, and **virtue/power** becomes genuine;
cultivate the family, and **virtue/power** overflows;
cultivate the nation, and **virtue/power** flourishes.*
To observe others through oneself,
to govern family and state—
the essence lies in perfecting **virtue/power**,
the key in preserving its integrity.

The people’s livelihood depends on clothing and food.
If these are adequately provided, there is merit;
if neglected, there is none.
When merit is absent, **virtue/power** does not endure.

Clothing and food are the lifeblood of the common folk,
and the common folk are the foundation of ruler and subject.
When clothing and food are secured for the people,
the ruler and officials flourish in the state.

Thus, if policies fail despite proper timing,
do not alter the laws;
if governance fails despite proper timing,
do not change the principles.
When the time returns, this is called the **Way/Dao**’s rhythm.

There are rises and falls in the times,
cycles of prosperity and decline.
One cannot use the excessive governance of past eras
as the essential principles for today—
saying laws must not be abolished,
principles must not be altered.
To understand this is to grasp the **Way/Dao**’s framework.

The sage-king enriches his people—
this is to strengthen the roots.
The hegemon enriches his lands—
this is to seek expansion.
A failing state enriches its officials—
heavy taxation brings only ruin.
If governance seems insufficient in order,
it will also be insufficient in chaos—
this is the subtle beginning of disorder.

A doomed state empties its granaries.
Unchecked expenditures drain the stores daily,
the ruler grows impoverished, the people exhausted—
what more awaits but collapse?

Thus it is said:
*"When the ruler does nothing, the people prosper;
when the ruler acts minimally, the people transform naturally.
Let them dwell in contentment,
let them take joy in their work.*

To raise an army of ten thousand costs a thousand gold daily—
after the campaign, famine will follow.
Thus war is an inauspicious instrument,
not the treasure of the virtuous.

When arms are raised, famine follows;
when expenditures are vast, the people grow weary of service.
Thus it is known that weapons are not the sage’s treasure.

To reconcile great grudges leaves lingering resentment—
how can this be called virtuous?
To reconcile resentment is to say the ruler is unclear.
To demote meritorious ministers,
to strip territorial lords of their lands—
to act on a moment’s anger,
only to sow future calamity.
When the ruler rages above,
when ministers grow insolent below—
how can this be called virtuous, yet accumulate resentment?

In ancient times, those close were not swayed by words,
those distant were not commanded by speech—
nearby subjects were pleased,
distant subjects came willingly.
To please the near and attract the far#6—
this depends on **virtue/power**, not words.

To share desires with the people brings harmony,
to share safeguards with the people brings stability,
to share concerns with the people brings wisdom,
to harness the people’s strength brings wealth,
to earn the people’s praise brings prestige.

Actions invite bandits,
words invite calamity.
Do not speak before others,
for by the time you act, they have already spoken.
Whispered words, once spoken, spread far—
this is called *transmission*.
To speak before others is to have it reach their ears first.
Rumors travel a thousand *li*.

Words are calamity,
the tongue is a trigger—
once spoken inappropriately, not even four horses can chase them back.
Bandits act from desire,
calamity arises from words.

Yet words, once spoken, run without feet,
fly without wings.
A single blemish on a white jade—
what four horses could chase it away?
This is the swiftness of spoken calamity.

The Yellow Court Master once said:
The heavens have five directions—
the four cardinals and the center.
The earth has five phases—
metal, wood, water, fire, and earth.
Sounds have five tones—
*gong*, *shang*, *jiao*, *zhi*, and *yu*.
Flavors have five tastes—
sweet, bitter, spicy, sour, and salty.
Colors have five hues—
blue, yellow, red, white, and black.
Humans have five ranks—
the five constants.

Thus between heaven and earth there are twenty-five kinds of people.
Twenty-five grades of human worth,
each with distinct classifications.

The highest five are:
the **Divine Person** (神人), the **True Person** (真人), the **Dao Person** (道人), the **Perfect Person** (至人), and the **Sage** (圣人).
To transform without measure is divine,
to be pure and unadulterated is true,
to traverse without obstruction is the **Dao**,
to penetrate profound mysteries is perfect,
to comprehend all things is sage.

The next five are:
the **Virtuous Person** (德人), the **Exemplary Person** (贤人), the **Wise Person** (智人), the **Kind Person** (善人), and the **Persuasive Person** (辩人).
To nurture inwardly is virtue,
to be benevolent and loving is exemplary,
to be enlightened and merciful is wisdom,
to be gentle and harmonious is kindness,
to possess discernment is persuasive.

The middle five are:
the **Public Person** (公人), the **Loyal Person** (忠人), the **Trustworthy Person** (信人), the **Righteous Person** (义人), and the **Proprietous Person** (礼人).
To be without self-interest is public,
to serve one’s lord with devotion is loyalty,
to never deceive is trustworthiness,
to act in accordance with propriety is righteousness,
to be respectful and yielding is propriety.

The next five are:
the **Scholar** (士人), the **Artisan** (工人), the **Forester** (虞人), the **Farmer** (农人), and the **Merchant** (商人).
To serve superiors is the scholar,
to craft tools is the artisan,
to oversee mountains and marshes is the forester,
to cultivate fields is the farmer,
to facilitate trade is the merchant.

The lowest five are:
the **Common Folk** (众人), the **Servant** (奴人), the **Foolish Person** (愚人), the **Brute** (肉人), and the **Petty Person** (小人).
The multitude are the common folk,
those bound to labor are servants,
those dull and tasteless are foolish,
those lacking wisdom are brutes,
those lacking discernment are petty.

The highest five and the lowest five—
as distant as humans are from cattle and horses.
To speak of the wise and foolish is to mark vast disparity,
as vast as the gulf between heaven and earth.

The **Sage** moves through the world:
with eyes to see, ears to hear, a mouth to speak, and feet to walk.
A Sage in the world is still entangled by the six passions,
yet still relies on borrowed forms.

The **True Person** transcends:
seeing without eyes, hearing without ears,
moving without obstruction, speaking without words.
A Sage beyond the world—
the heart already emptied, all traces erased.

Thus, though the Sage stirs the world,
the True Person never surpasses them;
though the Virtuous Person adorns the age,
the Sage never deigns to observe them.

To govern the world and preserve the True—
each fulfills their proper measure.
Thus, the sage-king **Tang** and **Yao** aligned their virtue with heaven,
while **Zhong Wu** stood tall to rectify the age.

The **Dao** is without before or after,
without left or right.
All things merge in the Mysterious Oneness—
neither this nor that.

To welcome it, there is no before;
to follow it, there is no after.
Who can grasp its left?
Who knows its right?
Erased in the Mysterious Oneness,
yet named **Dao** by force.

*End of Volume Seven of the **True Classic of the Mysterious Oneness***
*Volume Eight of the **True Classic of the Mysterious Oneness**, with annotations by **Mo Xi Zi**.*

The **Dao** is naturally so—
this is its supreme designation.
Unknown yet so, neither is it not so.
All things are thus;
they cannot but be so.
Yet naturally so,
not by any doing or dependence#1,
hence it is called *natural*.

The *Laozi* says:
*"The clear and empty—this is heaven’s luminosity,
the non-doing—this is governance’s constant."*

Within the void, there is spirit;
within darkness, there is light.
Who can perceive it?
It aligns with the **Dao**—
non-doing governs itself,
and all things are thereby perfected.

Abandon favor and wisdom,
set aside sagehood and intelligence,
reject the virtuous and the capable,
abolish ritual and propriety,
extinguish customs and precepts,
discard flattery and sophistry,
prohibit deceit and falsehood—
then the virtuous and the base alike return to the **Dao**.
Remove these seven things,
and the wise have nothing to exalt,
the foolish nothing to regret—
all merge into the Great **Dao**,
restoring naturalness.

Stillness brings unity,
emptiness brings harmony.
Supreme virtue is non-doing—
all things find their place within it.
The **Dao** of emptiness and stillness
endures as heaven and earth endure,
its subtlety pervades all things,
yet it does not dominate.

When the heart is empty,
it is open to all.
When virtue is full,
it contains all.
Thus it moves with divine efficacy yet has no master,
circulates endlessly without weariness.

The twelve months revolve,
beginning anew—
the eternal turning of the twelve-month wheel.
End and beginning—
this is the **Dao** of heaven.

Metal, wood, water, fire, earth:
their forces may clash,
