yet their **Dao** depends on one another.
The Five Phases propel each other—
one ascends, one declines.
Cold and heat alternate,
advancing and retreating in their time.
Life and death follow the **Dao**,
never straying from their due.

Thus extreme cold harms things—
yet without cold, it would be impossible;
extreme heat harms things—
yet without heat, it would be impossible.
Thus both what is possible and what is not
are possible.
Hence the Great **Dao** knows no impossibility—
what is fitting is fitting;
see what is fitting and act,
see what is not and cease.
Possible and impossible
are like left and right,
like exterior and interior.

Cold and heat succeed each other—
this is the **Dao** of heaven and earth.
Rites and punishments are the Sage’s laws.
Yet though cold and heat may be harsh,
they cannot be absent.
When timing is right, what harm is there?
Though punishments may be severe,
they cannot be abolished.
What is right is not harmful.
See what is fitting and act,
see what is not and stop.

All things begin with unity,
time marks their measure—
from ancient times to now, unchanging—
this is called *heavenly principle*.
Unity is the **Dao**’s child,
the ruler’s grasp.
Though ages differ,
their operations do not.
Like an axle turning the hub,
inner governs outer—
the wheel revolves endlessly,
matching heaven’s eternal cycle.

Above, it holds the Great Clarity;
below, it wields its light.
The **Dao** generates all things,
its principle lies in yin and yang,
transforming into the four seasons,
dividing into the Five Phases—
each finds its place.
Following time’s flow,
laws have their constants.
Even the lowly are included,
the **Dao** remains unshaken,
ministers act in harmony—
heaven’s **Dao** is complete without doing,
obtained without seeking.
Thus it is known:
non-doing yet beneficial.

Heaven displays its symbols to illuminate the four quarters,
the ruler establishes laws to govern the world.
The *dìchóng*#2 awakens, the people revive with blessings—
yin and yang never err,
all things have their constancy.
Without non-doing,
how could there be benefit to the state?

In ancient times, **Yao** governed the world while perfecting himself.
The *Laozi* says:
*"The most pristine is formless,
the most vast is boundless.
Thus heaven’s roundness does not fit the compass,
earth’s square does not fit the ruler.
Past and future—this is *zhou*,
the four directions and above and below—this is *yu*.
The **Dao** dwells within yet is nowhere known."*

Thus those who see not far cannot speak of the great,
those who know not broadly cannot discuss the ultimate.
He who aligns with the **Dao** and harmonizes with things
cannot be contradicted.
Moral **Dao** is vast and formless,
heaven and earth are boundless yet measureless;
near as a hair’s breadth yet unseen,
far as the cosmos yet unfathomable.
Unless one is broadly enlightened and harmonious with things,
none can clarify the origin of the ultimate **Dao**,
nor penetrate the realm of indistinct right and wrong.

Thus the Three Sovereigns and Five Emperors,
though their laws varied by region,
all won the people’s hearts.
Though their statutes differed,
their reverence for the people was one.

Rules and measures, hooks and lines—
tools of skill,
yet not the skill itself.
Thus *Wén* the lute-maker, though skilled,
could not compose melodies—
only strings could weep alone.
Thus strings are tools of sorrow,
not sorrow itself.
All things, though called natural,
have causes and supports;
none act in isolation.
The hook and line are skill’s contrivance,
yet not skill itself.
Perfection lies in the artisan—
without the line, nothing is straight.
Strings are sorrow’s tool,
yet not sorrow itself—
without strings, there is no sorrow.

When divine harmony plays between heart and hand,
spontaneous expression flows,
melodies arise from the strings—
father cannot teach son,
son cannot learn from father.
This is the **Dao** that cannot be transmitted.

*Wén* played the lute:
his fingers plucked the strings,
his spirit flowed freely between hand and heart,
harmonizing with yin and yang’s rhythms,
thus the music shifted with the seasons.
Qi moved all things—
so profound that even father and son,
though close, could not pass it on.
This is the pinnacle of subtlety.
Thus it is also like the **Dao**—
incapable of transmission.

Reverence is the sovereign of form,
yet silence is the master of sound.
Reverence is stillness—
thus form arises from stillness, ruled by it.
Silence holds sound—
thus emptiness governs it.

The *Laozi* says:
*"Heaven and earth’s **Dao** takes virtue as its master,
the **Dao** gives it its name.
Things regulate themselves;
the most subtle penetrates deeply,
not relying on affairs or rank.
Thus it stands without merit,
is not honored by position,
is not made manifest by name,
does not depend on rites for dignity,
nor on arms for strength."*

The **Dao** gives birth as its decree,
virtue nurtures as its master.

When humans harmonize the **suōng** (冲) qi,
rectify life and nature,
preserve the essence within,
discard external burdens—
why seek name or rank for self-exaltation?
Why rely on arms when all submit?

Thus the **Dao** stands without teaching,
illumination shines without scrutiny.
The **Dao** stands without teaching—
it does not usurp others’ abilities;
illumination shines without scrutiny—
it does not harm their affairs.
When the **Dao** exists, teaching fades;
when clarity is absolute, scrutiny ceases—
only then can one bear great responsibilities without harm.

Those who teach the **Dao** act contrary to virtue,
harming all things.
Thus yin and yang, the four seasons,
metal, wood, water, fire, and earth—
all share the **Dao** yet differ in principle.
All things share sentiment yet differ in form.
The wise do not instruct one another;
the capable do not receive from one another.
Thus the Sage establishes laws to guide the people’s hearts,
letting each return to its natural state.
Thus the living have no cause for virtue,
the dead no cause for grievance.

To act contrary to virtue means
virtue declines while teaching flourishes;
to harm things means
to first diminish oneself while benefiting others.
For the Five Phases differ in nature,
all things vary in form—
teaching has its origins and ends,
people differ in wisdom and folly.
The Sage enacts laws and teachings,
opens the confused and guides the ignorant,
so that the wise may pass knowledge forward,
the capable may not conceal their talents,
each fulfilling their role,
returning to nature.
The living do not boast of their virtue,
the dead do not blame Heaven.

Heaven and Earth are not kind—
they treat all things as sacrificial dogs.
The Sage is not kind—
they treat the people as sacrificial dogs.
Heaven and Earth give birth to all things,
the Sage nourishes the people—
how could they have intentions toward things,
or favoritism toward people?
Viewed as one, they are like sacrificial dogs.

Compassion, love, benevolence, and righteousness—
these are narrow paths.
The narrow confines the great and leads to confusion;
the near obscures the distant and causes bewilderment.
The Sage’s **Dao** enters the great without confusion,
travels far without bewilderment,
constantly empty and self-guarded—
this is the ultimate, called Heaven’s virtue.

**Dao** and virtue are profound and subtle,
benevolence and righteousness are shallow and narrow.
The *Zhongyong* (中庸) takes a minor path to lead to confusion,
the highest Sage ascends the broad way without obstruction.
Unless the spirit is ever-clear,
how could one align with Heaven’s ultimate?

The *Laozi* says:
The Sage covers like Heaven, bears like Earth,
is illuminated by the sun and moon,
harmonizes yin and yang,
transforms through the four seasons,
embraces all things yet does not favor them—
without partiality or preference,
without neglect or closeness.
This is the Sage’s virtue.

Covering and bearing like Heaven and Earth,
illuminating like the sun and moon,
cycling like the four seasons—
all creatures, diverse in kind,
grasses, trees, insects—
each finds rest in its place,
fulfills its nature.
How could there be partiality or preference among them?

Thus those who model Heaven
know Heaven does not act in one season,
Earth does not rely on one material,
humans do not serve one purpose.
Thus undertakings are manifold,
actions are varied.
Speaking of Heaven in one season cannot complete a year,
using Earth with one material exhausts its use.
A single human talent is insufficient for nobility.
Thus in warfare, some are light, some heavy,
some greedy, some frugal—
these four opposites cannot be unified.
Each has its advantage,
so they cannot be one.

The light wish to strike,
the heavy wish to hold firm,
the greedy wish to take,
the frugal find no gain—
not their own.
When armies unite in purpose,
yet the heavy wish to hold,
the light wish to strike,
each pursues their own advantage—
this is not unity.
Without unity, defeat awaits when meeting the enemy.
But if one measures their talents,
balances light and heavy in deployment,
then victory is assured.

Thus the bold may be ordered to advance and fight,
but not to hold firm;
the heavy may be ordered to defend resolutely,
but not to provoke the enemy;
the greedy may be ordered to seize and take,
but not to divide spoils;
the frugal may be ordered to keep their share,
but not to advance and take;
the trustworthy may be ordered to keep agreements,
but not to adapt to changes.

The Sage employs these five talents together,
using each according to their strength—
thus the world has no rival.

Heaven and Earth do not cherish any single thing,
yin and yang do not produce any single kind.
Thus the sea does not refuse the tiniest stream to become vast,
mountains and forests do not refuse the crooked and bent to become towering.
The Sage does not reject the humble words of firewood-gathering
to broaden their renown.

To guard one corner while abandoning the rest,
to take one thing while discarding others—
then what is gained is little,
and what is governed is shallow.
If the **Dao** is not broad,
it cannot embrace all things.
If the Sage’s virtue is not profound,
they cannot receive subtle words.

Thus a single talent is insufficient to serve,
a single region is insufficient to govern.
Those who guard narrowly are limited,
those who serve shallowly are constrained.

The *Laozi* says:
What Heaven covers, what Earth bears,
what the sun and moon illuminate—
forms differ, natures vary,
each finds its place of rest.
What brings joy as joy
is also what brings sorrow.
What brings security as security
is also what brings danger.
To impose one’s joy upon others brings sorrow,
to follow their joy brings joy.
To impose one’s security upon others brings danger,
to follow their life brings security.

Thus the Sage shepherds the people:
letting each follow their nature,
settle where they belong,
act according to their ability,
fulfill their proper role,
apply what is suitable—
then all things align in harmony,
none transgressing.

The Sage shepherds the people:
letting diverse natures and forms
each find their proper place—
though myriad kinds exist,
they function as one body,
none overstepping.
Thus it is called *one harmony*.

Things under Heaven have no inherent nobility or baseness.
To honor what is honored makes all things honored.
To degrade what is degraded makes all things degraded.
Nobility and baseness have no fixed measure,
prosperity and ruin no constant standard—
they depend on fortune and misfortune,
use and disuse.

Thus those who do not exalt the wise
do not cast fish onto trees,
do not drown birds in the deep.
Speak of releasing them to fly in the woods,
of casting them where they swim in water—
then flying and swimming find their place,
through the wise and foolish working together.

The Sage-Kings of old governed the realm:

When Yao ruled the world,
Shun served as Minister of Instruction,
Xiè as Minister of War,
Yu as Minister of Works—
the Three Ministers,
discussing the Dao and administering the state,
harmonizing the principles of yin and yang,
acting as the sovereign’s limbs and voice.

Later, Xièyì cultivated the fields,
teaching the people to sow seeds.
Xī Zhōng became the Master of Works,
crafting tools to meet the people’s needs.
Thus did the Sage employ the worthy.

Their achievements reached to the cosmos,
their virtue flowed to the four seas—
only Heaven was greater,
and Yao followed it alone.

He guided the people:
those dwelling by water fished,
those in the woods gathered,
those in the valleys herded,
those on the hills tilled the land.
The land suited the work,
the work suited the tools,
the tools suited the materials.
Gāo and Zé wove nets,
Líng and Jí plowed the fields—
thus the people exchanged what they lacked
for what they possessed,
their skills for their deficiencies.
So few deserted, many obeyed,
like wind passing through reeds—
suddenly stirring all.

Each responded to purity or turbidity,
seeking what benefited them,
avoiding what harmed them.
Thus neighboring states lay within sight,
the sounds of roosters and dogs carried on the breeze,
yet no footsteps crossed the borders of the lords,
no chariot tracks stretched beyond a thousand li—
all content in their homes.

The Sage’s Way governed the people:
settling them where their strengths lay,
establishing them where it suited them,
so that abundance and scarcity supported one another,
skill and clumsiness complemented each other.
Like wind passing through a reed,
moistening what it touched,
its sound followed what it stirred,
things responded to what benefited them.
Thus neighboring states lay within sight—
weapons unused,
the people living out their days in peace.

So chaotic states seemed full,
well-ordered ones seemed empty,
declining ones seemed lacking,
thriving ones seemed abundant.
The "empty" were not without people,
but each held to their duties;
the "full" were not overpopulated,
but all were fixated on trifles;
the "abundant" were not rich in wealth,
but their desires were restrained and their undertakings few;
the "lacking" were not poor in goods,
but their people were few and their expenditures great.

Understanding these four truths
reveals the roots of order and chaos,
discerns the fate of survival and ruin.

Thus the ancient kings’ laws were not their own creation,
but what they followed.
Their punishments were not their own invention,
but what they upheld—
the Way of Supreme Virtue.

Punishments were not meant to kill,
but to set boundaries.
Yet the foolish did not heed their commands,
and multiplied their transgressions—
thus they invited death.

Laozi said:
To govern the world with the Dao
is not to alter human nature,
but to nurture what already exists,
expanding it fully—
thus following is vast,
forcing is small.

Ancient dike-builders followed the river’s flow,
farmers followed the land’s suitability,
warriors followed the people’s will—
to follow is to be unconquerable under Heaven.

Things must first exist naturally
before human affairs can be ordered.
Observing that all things have inherent natures,
one then aligns with their suitability,
follows the people’s desires—
then undertakings succeed without fail,
actions bring no harm.

Thus the ancient kings’ laws
regulated human nature with measured restraints.
What lacked that nature could not be made to conform,
what had it but lacked resources could not be made to obey the Way.
Wood cannot be made to rise from water,
metal cannot be made to generate fire.

Humans possess the potential for benevolence and righteousness,
yet without the Sage’s guidance,
laws and measures cannot make them align.
By prohibiting what they hate,
delinquency is checked—
punishments unused, authority moves like a god.

Follow their nature, and the world obeys;
contradict it, and laws are issued in vain.
Morality and virtue,
though rooted in human nature,
require the Sage-King’s laws to take form.
By wielding rewards and punishments,
guiding with virtue,
aligning with ritual,
enforcing with law—
only then can nature be bent to purpose,
though it resists.

Follow their nature, and response is divine;
contradict it, and even commands go unheeded.

Morality and virtue
are the foundation of merit and renown,
what the people cherish—
when cherished, merit and renown stand firm.
Without morality and virtue,
no merit or renown can be established.

The ancient wise rulers modeled themselves on the rivers and seas:
the rivers and seas act without effort to become great,
humble themselves to become vast—
thus they endure as Heaven’s valleys,
their virtue sufficient.
Without action, they receive all streams;
without seeking, they obtain;
without striving, they arrive—
thus they hold the world.
They do not claim what they possess,
so they grow rich;
they do not flaunt what they know,
so they become clear;
they do not boast of their strength,
so they endure;
they dwell in what is not theirs,
so they rule the world.
They do not contend,
so none can contend with them.
They do not vie for greatness,
so they achieve it.

The rivers and seas are close to the Dao,
thus they endure alongside Heaven and Earth,
their virtue complete.
Without claiming,
they grow strong;
strong yet not violent toward others,
their Dao deep,
their virtue profound—
thus their merit and renown are fulfilled.
This is called the Mysterious Virtue,
profound and distant,
contrary to all things.

The world values the exalted,
I embrace the lowly;
the world prizes boasting,
I avoid contention;
I dwell where none claim to be—
thus I am contrary to all things.

The world has a beginning,
yet none knows its principle—
only the Sage understands its source.
Neither male nor female,
neither yin nor yang,
it generates yet does not die.

Heaven and Earth were completed,
yin and yang took form,
and all things were born.
Thus yin and yang have round and square,
short and long,
existence and nonexistence—
the Dao names them.

Obscure and deep,
yet without action,
in the heart it is subtle,
in the Dao it is right.
Life and death share the same principle,
all transformations merge into the One Way.
Forgotten in birth,
unaware of death—
where could one not live long?

Abandon deeds and words,
be cautious in non-action.
Guard the Dao with thoroughness,
do not lord over things.
The most subtle, formless—
this is the end.

The beginning of Heaven and Earth,
all things align with the Dao yet manifest in diverse forms.
The most subtle holds nothing within,
thus it can encompass and care for all.
The most vast has no outer limit,
thus it becomes all things.
The most minute contains nothing inside,
thus it is the source of all things’ worth.
The Dao preserves life,
virtue stabilizes form.
The utmost Dao’s measure:
abandoning preference and aversion,
without knowing—
harmonizing intent, calming the heart,
never opposing the Dao.

Heaven and Earth have a beginning—
this is the Dao.
None in the world can recognize it,
for it cannot be distinguished as male or female,
nor traced through form and color.
Yet though sought, it is nowhere found,
though spoken of, it cannot be grasped—
and yet it endures eternally.
Heaven and Earth have high and low positions,
the sun and moon have day and night’s order,
yin and yang have firm and yielding principles,
all things have long and short natures—
yet the Dao is neither obscure nor clear,
neither existent nor nonexistent,
neither vast nor minute,
neither round nor square.
Its revolutions know no end,
its transformations have no fixed pattern.

Yet those who revere it
preserve life and stabilize form,
abandon deeds and words,
vast and effortless, serene and yielding—
thus they return to the Great Primordial,
entering the realm of boundless virtue and longevity.

Heaven and Earth, when unified, become One;
when divided, they become Two.
When reversed, they reunite,
above and below unbroken.
When unified, they become One;
when divided, they become Five.
When reversed, they reunite—
necessarily conforming to the compass and square.

One is the diffusion of *qi*,
Two is the flow of form,
Five are the Five Phases.
Above and below are Heaven and Earth,
and the person between them—
if they harmonize with Heaven and Earth’s virtue,
unify their essence into One,
they necessarily conform to the laws,
and return to the origin.

The Dao is most intimate yet cannot be neglected,
most near yet cannot be distanced.
Seeking it far away,
one goes forth and returns in vain.
Seeking it in things,
none know to seek it within oneself.

Laozi said:
*The Emperor* has a name,
yet none know its essence.
*The Emperor* values virtue,
the King upholds righteousness,
the Conqueror grasps principle.
Virtue nurtures all things,
righteousness rescues the drowning and aids the endangered,
principle responds to circumstances and numbers.

The Sage’s Way in things is nonexistent.
Only when virtue is insufficient does one employ intelligence,
when virtue is thin does one resort to punishment,
when wisdom is shallow does one rely on scrutiny.
Those who rely on intelligence bring chaos to the heart,
those who rely on punishment invite grievance from above and below,
those who rely on scrutiny prompt the lower ranks to feign virtue to please above—
thus harming the truth.
Intelligence spawns deception,
punishment breeds legal trickery,
virtue arises from hypocrisy.
When these three corrupt,
the Sage prohibits them—
none can overcome them.
The harm of straying from the Dao lies here.

Thus the Sage follows Heaven and Earth’s transformations,
adapting virtue accordingly.
Heaven covers, Earth bears,
guiding by season—
its nourishment is profound,
and profound nourishment brings order.
Order and chaos—
though there be sages and gods,
how could they alter it?

Abandon the mind’s schemes,
reduce punishments,
return to clarity and stillness—
things will self-regulate.
The Dao as ruler,
like a solemn corpse in silent repose,
yet the world receives its blessings.
One person benefits without favor,
ten thousand benefit without partiality.

Thus excessive kindness is folly,
excessive severity is tyranny—
both oppose the Dao.
Kindness is giving without measure.
The Sage observes the times’ corruption,
the reliance on deceitful intelligence.
Thus he steadies with virtue and morality,
returns to clarity and stillness,
leaving things to self-regulate,
guarding the mysterious and silent.
Restoring them to simplicity,
kindness is not recklessly bestowed,
punishment not recklessly imposed—
thus tyranny and chaos do not arise,
and all conform to the Dao.

Bestowing rewards without merit,
granting honors without labor—
those who fulfill their duties grow negligent in office,
while the idle rush forward in ambition.
Tyranny is unjust slaughter,
the innocent dying,
those who follow the Dao being punished—
thus those who cultivate themselves are discouraged from virtue,
while the wicked dare to defy above.

Thus excessive kindness breeds deceit,
excessive severity breeds chaos—
deceit and chaos are the customs of a dying state.

Punishment cannot be imposed on the virtuous,
honors cannot be bestowed without merit—
thus those who fulfill their duties show signs of neglect,
those who follow the Dao harbor resentment.
This is the deceit and chaos,
the customs of a dying state.

Thus when the state executes,
the ruler feels no anger;
when the court rewards,
the sovereign takes no credit.
The executed bear no grudge—
punishment is justly meted out—
the rewarded do not feel indebted to above—
merit is naturally achieved.
The people know
that executions and rewards arise from their own actions,
thus they strive for merit and cultivate their endeavors,
not relying on others’ gifts.
Hence the court is uncluttered, with no traces of corruption,
fields lie fallow without filth.

Rewards suffice to encourage virtue,
punishments suffice to deter deceit.
The rewarded feel no danger from arrogance,
the punished feel no pity—
thus the near benefit from its grace,
the distant submit to its virtue.
If they perfect their endeavors and exhaust their strength,
the court knows no disputes,
the fields flourish with crops.

Thus the *Taishang* knows and possesses it.
Speaking of the ancient *Taishang*’s Dao,
later kings took it as their model.

The Way of the King
handles affairs of non-action,
practices teaching without words,
remains serene and unmoved,
measures once and does not waver,
follows naturally without forcing,
achieves results without labor,
plans without error,
acts without fault,
speaks without rhetoric,
moves without display—
advancing and retreating suit the time,
action and stillness follow principle.

Neither praising beauty nor hating ugliness,
neither rewarding nor punishing with joy or anger,
each name is self-bestowed,
each category self-classified,
matters proceed naturally—
none originate from the self.

If one seeks to constrain it,
one only drives it away.
If one seeks to adorn it,
one only robs it of its essence.

The King, unless vast, cannot contain all things,
unless still, cannot harmonize the people.
Abandoning preference and aversion,
cherishing simplicity and authenticity—
narrowness without intimacy,
ornament without harming substance,
all things, diverse in kind,
naturally return to their own.

Heaven’s *qi* is the spirit,
Earth’s *qi* is the vital breath—
reversed, they are profound and mysterious,
each occupying its dwelling,
guarded without loss,
ascending to the Supreme Unity.
The essence of the Supreme Unity
unifies and merges with Heaven.

The Dao of Heaven is silent and indistinct,
without measure or standard.
Its greatness cannot be fathomed,
its depth cannot be plumbed.
It ever transforms with humanity,
yet wisdom cannot grasp it.
Its turning is endless like a wheel,
its transformation as wondrous as the divine.
In emptiness it follows naturally,
always acting after, never before.

The human *hun* is yang, life, received from Heaven.
The *po* is yin, death, received from Earth.
Each guards its own dwelling.
The *hun* is the spirit of yang,
the *po* is the essence of yin.
*Hun* and *po* are the utmost essence of Heaven and Earth—
thus they are called profound and mysterious.
Heaven gains them and remains ever luminous,
humans gain them and remain ever alive—
thus it is said: *guard them lest they be lost*,
and ascend to the Supreme Unity.
The Supreme Unity is the Sovereign of the Highest Dao.
What humans receive from it—
if one guards one’s spirit and essence,
lest they abandon the body,
then one unites above with Heaven.

The Supreme Unity focuses its essence and accumulates its thoughts,
thus it is able to communicate.
The method of guarding it:
only stillness, only silence,
without measure or standard,
without greatness or boundary—
its subtlety is unfathomable.
Thus it is said:
it ever transforms with humanity,
yet wisdom cannot grasp it.
Its turning is like a wheel,
its transformation as wondrous as the divine.
In emptiness it acts after, never before.
In obscurity it enlightens—
thus it is called the utmost truth.

In governing, it maintains a vacant heart and weak will,
clear and bright without darkness,
thus ministers gather like spokes to the hub.
Whether wise or foolish, virtuous or base,
all exhaust their abilities.
The ruler gains the means to govern his ministers,
the ministers gain the means to serve their ruler—
thus the governance of the state becomes manifest.

He who possesses a clear mirror
will see the purest essence.
In governing the state, ministers vie to honor him.
If he does not lightly risk his body,
then ten thousand armies will guard him without abandon.

Laozi said:
He who knows and loves to ask is sage,
he who is courageous and loves to ask is victorious.
He who rides upon the wisdom of the masses
can undertake any task,
he who employs the strength of the masses
can overcome any challenge.
To employ the strength of the masses,
even Wu Ge is insufficient to rely upon.
To ride upon the momentum of the masses,
even the world is insufficient to use.
The skilled user of the masses
finds none stronger than himself.

Employing the masses’ strength,
even mountains and hills, though heavy,
can be moved by their momentum.
Employing the masses’ wisdom,
even ghosts and gods, though hidden,
can be clarified by their reasoning.
Without strategy, no force can prevail,
yet without adhering to principle,
even the sage cannot succeed.

When schemes are laid bare,
even a commoner can wield a crossbow of ten thousand *jin*.
When reason is perverted,
even the sage cannot bend a child’s words.

Thus the sage, in all undertakings,
never fails to utilize their resources.

He who has one talent occupies one post,
he who has one ability serves one task.
If strength exceeds the task, the burden is not heavy,
if ability exceeds the task, the labor is not difficult.
The sage employs all together,
thus no person is discarded,
no material wasted.
Utilizing their talents, all exhaust their abilities.
Utilizing their strengths, all give their utmost.

Laozi said:
So-called *wuwei* is not
that which, when pulled, does not come,
when pushed, does not go,
when pressed, does not respond,
when moved, does not stir,
when stagnant, does not flow,
when gathered, does not disperse.
Only he who adapts and follows the times,
responding to things without obstruction,
is called *wuwei*.
It is said:
his private desires do not intrude upon public duty,
his appetites do not cling to proper conduct.
He acts in accordance with principle,
achieves success through resources,
pushes natural momentum—
artifice finds no place.
When deeds are accomplished, he does not boast,
when merit is established, he does not claim it.

As water uses a boat,
sand uses a plow,
earth uses a cart,
mountains use a ladder—
summer floods, winter pools,
using height to make mountains,
using low to make ponds—
these are not my doings.
Each employs what is advantageous,
all gain their convenience.
Thus it is said: *not my doing*.

The sage does not shame his humble station,
but despises the Dao’s obstruction.
He does not grieve over short life,
but over the people’s hardship.
Thus he remains ever vacant and does nothing,
embracing simplicity, seeing purity,
not mingling with things.
Ever aligning with the Dao,
not mingling with things.

Laozi said:
The ancient emperors were not established
to indulge their desires,
the sages who ascended the throne
did not seek ease for themselves.
For the people of the world,
the strong oppressed the weak,
the many oppressed the few,
the cunning deceived the simple,
the brave intimidated the timid.
Again, the wise hoarded knowledge without teaching,
accumulated wealth without sharing—
thus the Son of Heaven was established to unify them.
For one man’s wisdom could not illuminate the land,
thus the Three Dukes and Nine Ministers were established to assist.
For isolated states and disparate customs could not receive his blessings,
thus the feudal lords were established to instruct them.
Thus Heaven and Earth, the four seasons—
none fail to respond.
No official hides his duties,
no state neglects its benefits.
Thus the cold are clothed, the hungry fed,
the aged and weak cared for,
the weary rested—
none are left unprovided.

The sage in high station
does not seek to exalt his position or delight his body,
but to rest the people and relieve their hardships.
Thus the Son of Heaven holds the One to unify,
the Three Dukes discuss the Dao to rectify,
the Nine Ministers uphold the law to assist,
the feudal lords proclaim teachings to honor.
Thus near and far follow the same winds,
ruler and ministers share one intent,
officials hold no false emoluments,
markets know no deceitful profits.

Thus the *Shijing* says:
*"With enlightened virtue and conduct,
the four quarters submit to it."*

Shennong’s form was emaciated,
Yao was gaunt and withered,
Shun was swarthy and black,
Yu’s hands and feet were calloused.
Yi Yin carried a cauldron to serve Tang,
Jiang Ziya wielded a butcher’s cleaver to enter Zhou,
Bai Li Xi was sold as a slave,
Guan Zhong was bound in chains,
Confucius lacked a black ox for sacrifice,
Mozi had no warm seat—
not for greed of office or lust for rank,
but to awaken the world’s benefits,
to remove the people’s harms.

From the Son of Heaven down to the common people,
their bodies unexerted, their minds untroubled,
yet they seek ease in all matters—
this has never been heard of.

From Shennong onward,
their forms were emaciated, their hands and feet calloused,
not to seek honor by dwelling above the people,
but to fulfill the intent of saving all things.

Never has there been one who sat in ease and awaited emolument,
or who reaped millet without tilling the fields.

Laozi said:
*"What is called the Son of Heaven*
is one who upholds the **Dao/Way** to establish the world.

The **Dao/Way** of establishing the world
is to grasp the One as its foundation,
to return to the root and act without contrivance,
to remain empty and tranquil, without possession,
to drift beyond measure, without boundary,
to extend infinitely, without end—
to observe it, there is no form,
to listen to it, there is no sound.
This is called the **Great Dao/Way’s** essence.

It aligns with the previous explanation.

Laozi said:
The **Dao/Way** is
a rounded body yet square in its methods,
a back that shelters darkness while embracing brightness,
a left that yields softness and a right that wields firmness,
a tread upon the unseen while bearing the luminous—
its transformations are without constancy,
yet it draws from the origin of the One to respond without fixed form.
This is called **Divine Mystery**.

Now, humanity—
its head is round like Heaven,
its feet are square like Earth,
its back shelters darkness while its face embraces brightness,
its left hand holds softness, its right hand holds firmness,
its feet traverse the Nine Abysses,
its head bears the Three Luminaries—
endlessly revolving, its brilliance illuminating the four quarters;
unchanging yet ever-turning,
this is called **Divine Mystery**.
Those who behold it prosper.

Heaven is round yet without end,
so its form cannot be observed.
Earth is square yet without boundary,
so its gate cannot be glimpsed.
Heaven’s transformations follow without defined shape,
Earth’s growth extends without measure.
Endless in their transformations,
even the most enlightened cannot see their forms.
Boundless in their generation,
even the most skilled cannot calculate their numbers.

Among things, there is none that surpasses the **Dao/Way**,
yet the **Dao/Way** itself has no surpassing—
for it is without constant form or measure.
Endlessly revolving,
it mirrors the cycles of the sun and moon,
or the succession of the four seasons—
day and night, the sun and moon,
end and begin again,
brightness and then obscurity,
shaping form yet remaining formless—
thus its work can be accomplished.
It nourishes all things yet is not possessed by them—
thus it prevails without bending.

Form arises from the formless,
so form persists without cessation.
Things arise from the **Dao/Way** yet are not constrained by it,
so it nourishes all things without end.

The **Emperor** is one who wages war in the **Temple of Dao/Way**,
the **King** is one who manifests **Divine Mystery**.
The **Emperor** wages war in the **Temple of Dao/Way** by aligning with Heaven’s **Dao/Way**,
the **King** manifests **Divine Mystery** by illuminating the four seasons,
adjusting governance within his realm,
while distant lands reverence his virtue.
He prevails in battle before it is fought,
and the feudal lords come as guests, submitting in service.

The **Emperor** wages war in the **Temple of Dao/Way** by wielding the **Dao/Way** to conquer,
the **King** manifests **Divine Mystery** by triumphing in battle.
When forced to use them, they are employed.

The ancient ones who attained the **Dao/Way**
were tranquil yet governed Heaven and Earth,
active yet harmonized with the sun and moon,
their joy and anger conformed to the four seasons,
their commands echoed like thunder and lightning,
their breath and **qi** aligned with the Eight Winds,
their yielding and firmness never transgressed the Five Degrees.

Those who attained the **Dao/Way**,
their joy and anger never issued without cause,
their commands never applied without reason—
they governed like Heaven and Earth,
they followed like the sun and moon.
Thus the Eight Winds did not clash,
and the Five Stars did not stray.

They followed the people’s desires,
they harnessed the people’s strength,
to remove their excesses and eliminate their harms.

Those who share the same benefits
perish together,
those who share the same emotions
are fulfilled together,
those who share the same actions
assist each other—
if each follows their own path,
the world becomes a battleground.

Thus, the adept in warfare
uses the people’s own strength for their own ends,
while the incompetent
uses the people’s strength for their own ends.
Using the people’s strength for their own ends,
the world cannot fail to be employed.
Using the people’s strength for one’s own ends,
no single person can be employed.

Remove their harms,
and though the world is vast,
they will serve you of their own accord.
Deny their desires,
and though you are few,
they will not submit to you.

**Volume Eight of the *Tongxuan Zhenjing*** concludes here.

**Volume Nine of the *Tongxuan Zhenjing***—
*Annotated by Mo Xizi*—

#1 **Leaving**: Ho’s version reads *"Borrowing"*.
#2 **Condition**: Huo’s version reads *"Follow"*.
#3 **Honor**: Huo’s version reads *"Uphold"*.
#4 **Noble Just**: Original reads *"King and Duke"*, corrected according to Qu’s version.
#5 **Communicate**: Huo’s version reads *"Press"*.
#6 **Know**: Original reads *"It"*, corrected according to Huo’s version.

The **virtue/power** of lesser attainment
has its times of decline and excess,
hence virtue has its high and low.

The **Dao/Way**-less sovereigns shine with manifest virtue,
yet this does not mean supreme virtue—
thus it is called *lesser virtue*.

Laozi says:
To govern the self, the highest is the ancient, mysterious sovereign.
To nurture the spirit, take emptiness and clarity as the foundation.
Next, to nurture the form, take desires as the foundation.

When the spirit is clear and the mind is even,
all limbs are at peace—
this is the foundation of nurturing life.

Plump flesh, filled intestines,
satisfying desires—
these are the trivialities of nurturing life.

The spirit is the foundation of life,
the form is the triviality of life.
Attaining the foundation preserves the form and aligns with the **Dao/Way**,
while overvaluing the triviality causes the form to perish and return to the earth.

The sages of antiquity prioritized the foundation and did not indulge the trivial,
finding balance in moderation.
Later ages abandoned the spirit and indulged the form—
this is truly excessive.

To govern the realm, the highest is to nurture transformation.
This is to transform through the **Dao/Way**.
Next is to rectify the laws—
meaning penal codes.

When people naturally yield to one another,
strive for humility,
contend for lesser gains,
and take on laborious tasks,
day by day they transform toward the superior,
unaware of how it happens—
this is the foundation of governance,
the highest transformation.

With generous rewards encouraging virtue
and fear of punishment preventing wrongdoing,
when laws are properly established above,
the people submit below—
this is the triviality of governance.

Later ages govern through the trivial,
while earlier ages nurtured the foundation.
First, one discusses governing the self,
then one can govern the realm.

Having a foundation means having a triviality,
just as a whole form brings blessing to the body.
Thus, when one knows the **Dao/Way** and virtue is complete,
the roots of right and wrong are severed,
but when laws are enforced, paths of contention open.

The sage suppresses the trivial and upholds the foundation—
how could this not have its reasons?

Laozi says:
The ruler who seeks to govern does not appear in every age,
the capable minister who can assist does not appear in every thousand.
To seek such a ruler with such a minister—
this is why perfect governance appears only once in a thousand years.

This speaks of the rarity of enlightened rulers and virtuous ministers,
not that they never exist in any dynasty.
To discuss the worthy and the unworthy,
their use or disuse,
is not like the case of King Wen’s teacher, Ji Fang,
or King Wu Ding’s search for the oracle bone master.

If one seeks such figures exhaustively,
one would not encounter a single ruler in ten thousand years,
nor a single minister in a thousand—
who then would govern the world?

The achievements of hegemons and kings
are not established in every age.
This means they do not arise in every generation,
but the wise occupy them.

If one follows their good intentions
and guards against their corrupt hearts,
aligning with the people in the same **Dao/Way**,
then the people can be made virtuous,
and customs refined.

What makes the sage noble is not their imposition of punishments for crimes,
but their understanding of where disorder arises.

If one opens the sharp edges of vice
and lets them run wild in licentiousness,
then abandons them to laws and follows with punishments,
even if they destroy the world,
they cannot suppress their deceit.

Laws prevent what has not yet occurred,
punishments correct what has already happened.
Yet laws must not be乱 (*luàn*—chaotic),
punishments must not be滥 (*làn*—excessive),
for chaos makes them hard to uphold,
and excess harms the innocent—
even if they slaughter ten thousand households,
the wickedness will not cease.

Laozi says:
To stand above the rivers and seas,
yet have the heart beneath the imperial gate—
this is to *heavy life*.
To *heavy life* is to burden one’s existence.
To *heavy life* is to be burdened by desires,
yet still unable to overcome them—
if one follows them, the spirit suffers no harm.
If one cannot overcome them yet forcibly resists,
this is called *double injury*.
Those with *double injury* have no longevity.

The mind cannot serve two purposes at once,
nor can two affairs arise simultaneously.
Just as one stands in a secluded, open place
yet longs for a scene of splendor and honor—
if one abandons the body to follow the mind,
one can avoid burden.
But if one suppresses the body and defies the mind,
the two hearts war within—
this is called *double injury*.
To pray for longevity while bearing *double injury*—
this is no empty boast.

Thus it is said:
To know harmony is to know constancy,
to know constancy is to know enlightenment.
To increase life is auspicious,
to let the mind direct the *qi* is strength—
this is called *mysterious unity*.

Using its radiance,
it returns to its clarity.
Bathing the spirit in harmony,
harmony makes one constant with Heaven and Earth;
unifying the *qi* with enlightenment,
enlightenment makes one luminous with the sun and moon.

Laozi says:
Nothing is easier than doing good,
nothing is harder than doing evil.

What is called *doing good* is
to be still and do nothing,
to follow nature and avoid excess,
to avoid temptations,
to follow one’s true nature and preserve integrity,
to remain unchanging in oneself—
thus it is said that *doing good is easy*.

What is called *doing evil* is difficult—
usurpation, regicide, deceit, and haste,
with many desires—
these are not human nature,
thus it is said that *doing evil is hard*.

Generally, people find it easy to do good
but difficult to do evil.
Yet what is now considered a great calamity
arises from the lack of constancy in moderation.

Thus, in places of gain and loss,
at the juncture of fortune and misfortune,
one must be discerning.

The sage has no desires,
no evasions.
If one desires something,
pursuing it only leads to its loss;
if one avoids something,
avoiding it only leads to its approach.

When the will has desires,
it forgets its purpose.
Thus the sage examines the shifts between action and stillness,
adjusting the measure of giving and receiving,
balancing love and hate,
harmonizing joy and anger.

If action and stillness are balanced,
calamity will not assail.
If giving and receiving are measured,
guilt will not burden.
If love and hate are reasoned,
worry will not approach.
If joy and anger are harmonized,
grievance will not offend.

Those who embody the **Dao/Way**
do not seek gain lightly,
do not shun disaster,
and what they do not abandon, they do not control.
They are always full yet never overflowing,
constantly empty yet easily sustained.

Thus, if one governs by **Dao/Way** and measure,
food fills the empty,
clothing wards off cold,
sufficient for warmth and nourishment in a seven-foot frame.
Without **Dao/Way** and measure,
even with the power of a great state,
it is insufficient for satisfaction;
even with the wealth of the world,
it is insufficient for joy.

If one truly knows contentment,
even a single gourd’s worth is abundant,
but if one is insatiable,
even ruling the world is insufficient.

Thus the sage’s mind is even,
their will is simple,
their spirit internally guarded—
external things cannot deceive them.

When the mind is preserved in tranquility and harmony,
how can external things overwhelm one’s emotions and desires?

Laozi said:
*"He who overcomes others has strength,
but he who overcomes himself is truly powerful."*

One who can be powerful must rely on others’ strength;
one who relies on others’ strength must win their hearts;
one who wins hearts must first attain self-mastery.
There has never been one who gained others while losing themselves,
nor one who lost themselves while gaining others.

This is to say:
*"The soft overcomes the hard,
the weak obtains the strong."*

Strength lies not in oneself but in the multitude—
so I embrace all strength without losing others’ strength.
Dominion lies not in oneself but in the hearts of the people—
so all hearts become mine, and thus I win their hearts.

Therefore, the foundation of governance lies in calming the people;
the means to calm them is in ensuring their sufficiency;
the root of sufficiency is in not disrupting the seasons;
the way to not disrupt the seasons is in reducing labor;
the root of reducing labor is in moderating expenditure;
the root of moderating expenditure is in eliminating arrogance;
the root of eliminating arrogance is in embracing emptiness and nothingness.

This means the ruler does not build lofty pavilions or expand vast parks—
then the people will diligently farm without their time being stolen.

The nature of arrogance and excess,
the craving for splendor and luxury,
cannot be abandoned unless rooted in emptiness and **Dao/Way-virtue**.
Thus, one who understands the nature of life
does not pursue what life cannot provide;
one who understands the nature of fate
does not worry over what fate cannot alter.

Knowing life and fate—
what need is there for worry or fear?
Delighting in the five colors,
indulging in flavors,
listening to the five tones—
the seven orifices contend,
harming the single **qi-nature**.
Daily indulging in perverse desires,
exhausting the natural harmony—
the body cannot even be governed,
how much less the world?

The one who truly gains the world
does not claim it by seizing power or titles,
but by aligning the world’s heart and harnessing its strength.

To have the name of ruler without a single person’s loyalty—
this is to have lost the world.
Thus, Jie and Zhou were not true kings,
while Tang and Wu were not rebels.

When the world follows the **Dao/Way**,
its defense lies in the four frontier regions;
when the world loses the **Dao/Way**,
its defense lies with the feudal lords.
When feudal lords follow the **Dao/Way**,
their defense lies in their borders;
when they lose the **Dao/Way**,
their defense lies with their closest retainers.

Thus it is said:
*"Do not rely on others not taking from you,
but on your inability to be taken from."*
To practice a path that can be seized
while rejecting usurpation and regicide
is of no benefit in holding the world.

The sage governs the world
by cultivating the self and calming the people,
not by chasing titles or power.
Thus, those who follow the **Dao/Way** are revered,
while those without **virtue/power** are abandoned.
The world is not a private possession—
only the good are worthy of it.

Laozi said:
*"The wise ruler does not alter established ways,
nor change what is constant."*

Anger is contrary to **virtue/power**,
weapons are instruments of calamity,
and contention is the source of disorder.
Plotting in secret goes against **virtue/power**,
delighting in weapons exacerbates disorder,
and governing through contention is the height of perversity.

No one can bring calamity upon others
without first cultivating it within themselves.
Instead, blunt their sharpness,
resolve their conflicts,
harmonize their brilliance,
and blend their dust.
Human nature desires to excel over others
and resents those who surpass them.
Desiring to excel breeds contention;
resenting others breeds grievance and strife.
Grievance and strife disrupt the mind and invert the **qi**.
Thus, ancient sage-kings eliminated contention and grievance—
when grievance and strife do not arise,
the mind is ordered and the **qi** flows smoothly.

Therefore it is said:
*"Do not exalt the worthy,
so that the people do not contend."*

To uphold the **Dao/Way** and guard constancy is the sage’s governance;
to indulge in confusion and invert **virtue/power** is the fool’s rule.
If the path of exalting the worthy is not opened,
how can the calamity of resentment be avoided?

Laozi said:
*"To govern things, one does not rely on things to achieve harmony;
to govern harmony, one does not rely on harmony to govern people;
to govern people, one does not rely on people to govern the ruler;
to govern the ruler, one does not rely on the ruler to govern desires;
to govern desires, one does not rely on desires to govern nature;
to govern nature, one does not rely on nature to govern **virtue/power**;
to govern **virtue/power**, one does not rely on **virtue/power** to govern the **Dao/Way**.*

Without harmony, one cannot govern things;
without the ruler, one cannot govern people;
without nature, one cannot fulfill **virtue/power**;
without **virtue/power**, one cannot illuminate the **Dao/Way**.

Rooting the **Dao/Way** in human nature—
untainted, enduring in all things—
this is to return to its origin and align with its true nature.

Clothing, food, rites, and customs
are not inherent to human nature;
they are acquired from without.
The **Dao/Way** serves to tranquilize the spirit,
while things nourish the nature.

Nature is the inner self;
things are the external world.
Seeking external things with the inner nature,
when they arrive, they fulfill its inherent demands,
becoming the natural order of that nature.

Thus, those who follow the **Dao/Way** can abandon things and return to the self.
Returning to the self reveals the sincere desires of one’s original nature—
then all things rest in harmony.

Human nature is inherently balanced;
desires corrupt it.
Only those who follow the **Dao/Way** can abandon things and return to the self.
With self-awareness, one does not lose sight of things’ true essence;
without self-awareness, one is misled by constant scheming.

To indulge desires is to lose one’s nature;
every action is misaligned—
governing life leads to self-ruin,
governing the state leads to chaos.
Thus, without understanding the **Dao/Way**, one cannot return to nature.
From governing things to cultivating the **Dao/Way**,
none have ever been properly aligned.

Illness arises from desire.
Desire is the root of calamity,
the gateway to disaster—
without clarifying **Dao/Way-virtue**, one cannot restore the true self.

Ancient sages attained this from within themselves,
thus their commands were obeyed and prohibitions respected.
In all undertakings, one must first calm the mind and clarify the spirit.
Only when the spirit is clear and the mind composed
can affairs be properly ordered.
If judgment is clouded by praise or blame,
if the eyes are dazzled by colors,
how can affairs be rightly governed?
Thus, emptiness is held in highest regard.

Water, when stirred, creates waves;
**qi**, when disordered, brings confusion.
Confused wisdom cannot be relied upon for rectitude,
turbulent water cannot be still.
Thus, the sage-king holds to the One
to regulate the emotions and nature of things.

The One is the most precious—
it has no fixed place in the world.
The sage-king entrusts himself to this boundlessness,
thus commanding the world’s fate.

「The attainment of **Dao/Way** within oneself lies in calming the mind.
The mind’s composure comes from the heart not being swayed by external things,
the eyes not fixated on colors,
unifying the spirit and harmonizing joy and anger,
thus attaining to the **qi**’s purity untainted by passions,
and the passions’ waves not rising.
All follow their nature in reverse,
yet none dare defy the sovereign’s command.

Laozi said:
Yin and Yang nurture all things,
all born riding upon the **One Qi**.
When ruler and subject are estranged, **qi** ascends and churns above;
When the sovereign and ministers are discordant, the five grains fail to flourish.
Spring’s harshness and autumn’s withering,
winter’s thunder and summer’s frost—
all these are born of the **Dao/Way**’s violators.

Between heaven and earth, a single person’s body;
Within the six extremes, a single person’s form.
One person—**the Son of Heaven**.
One person upright, and the world attains peace;
One person corrupt, and all peoples suffer harm.
Thus, bound to heaven and earth,
penetrating the six extremes,
how can one be too cautious?

Those who are clear about their nature
cannot be coerced by heaven and earth;
Those who are precise with **symbols/rituals**
cannot be misled by strange creatures.
When nature aligns with truth,
thunder and lightning press yet do not frighten.
When clarity illuminates all things,
Yin and Yang confuse yet none dare doubt.

The sage discerns the near to know the far,
treating ten thousand miles as one realm.
What is gained within is made manifest without;
What is mastered in the One permeates all.
When **qi** churns within heaven and earth,
rituals and propriety are not established,
and all peoples dare to encroach and oppress,
arising from the depths of **mixed chaos**.
Accumulated virtue receives the **spirit’s** aid,
accumulated vice incurs the **spirit’s** blame.
Yet **qi** of like kind calls to like—
virtue and vice hold no distinction.
Do not say that within **mixed chaos** there is no effect of retribution.

When shame and modesty decline,
and in times of societal decay,
efforts multiply yet wealth dwindles,
labor is excessive yet sustenance insufficient,
the people grow poor and wrathful, strife arising—
thus, **ren** is held in highest regard.
**Ren** brings peace.

When people are coarse and unaligned,
clustering in factions and cliques,
each advancing their own,
nurturing deceitful and cunning schemes—
thus, **yì/rightness** is held in highest regard.
**Yì** cuts through the excess.

When men and women dwell together in groups,
unrestrained and without distinction—
thus, **lǐ/ritual** is held in highest regard.
**Lǐ** brings order.

When natural impulses grow wild,
pressing upon one another beyond restraint,
disharmony arises—
thus, **yuè/music** is held in highest regard.
**Yuè** brings moderation.

Thus, **ren**, **yì**, **lǐ**, and **yuè**—
these are means to remedy failure,
not universal paths to governance.
To value **ren** alone
is not to save what is flawed—
this is not the **ultimate virtue/power**.

If truly one can establish the **spirit’s** clarity throughout the world,
and return the heart to its origin,
then the people’s nature becomes good.
When the people’s nature is good,
heaven and earth, Yin and Yang, follow and embrace them,
then wealth is abundant and people are nourished,
and the hearts of greed, coarseness, and strife cannot arise.
**Ren** and **yì** are not needed,
yet **virtue/power** and **Dao/Way** are established in the world,
and the people do not indulge in colors.

Thus, when **virtue/power** declines, **ren** and **yì** are adorned;
When harmony is lost, music is praised;
When **ritual/lǐ** grows licentious, outward decorum is emphasized.
Hence, one must first know **virtue/power** and **Dao/Way**
before understanding that **ren** and **yì** are insufficient to practice;
One must first know **ren** and **yì**
before understanding that **ritual/lǐ** and **yuè/music** are insufficient to cultivate.

**Virtue/power** and **Dao/Way** encompass all beneath heaven.
Thus, those who possess the **Dao/Way** include **ren**, **yì**, **lǐ**, and **yuè**,
holding them all in readiness.

Some say to abandon and extinguish these four
and claim to possess the **Dao/Way**—
this is not a comprehensive view.

The sage-king pities the world’s decline,
yet without **virtue/power** and **Dao/Way**,
thus values **ren**, **yì**, **lǐ**, and **yuè**,
restraining nature and harmonizing emotions,
preserving propriety and reverence,
exalting respect and yielding,
so none dare overstep—
to restore **virtue/power** and **Dao/Way**.

Laozi said:
Governance through **qīngjìng**—
harmonious and yielding, yet tranquil and still,
simple and true, natural and unadorned,
quiet and undisturbed—
within, it aligns with the **Dao/Way**;
without, it conforms with **yì/rightness**.
Its words are few yet follow reason;
Its actions are pleasing yet accord with nature;
Its heart is harmonious yet unfeigned;
Its deeds are plain yet not ostentatious.
It does not plot beginnings,
nor debate endings.
At peace, it remains; when stirred, it acts.
Its body permeates heaven and earth,
its essence unites with Yin and Yang,
its harmony aligns with the four seasons,
its clarity shines like the sun and moon.
It follows the **Dao/Way**’s transformations—
this is the **true person**.

This is the **true person** who governs with **qīngjìng**,
aligning with heaven’s **Dao/Way** and harmonizing with the **Way**.
**Ren** is free from **jīqìao**—
deceit and artifice.

Thus, heaven covers with **virtue/power**,
earth bears with **yuè/music**,
the four seasons do not lose their order,
winds and rains do not act as disasters,
the sun and moon remain clear and radiant,
the five stars do not stray in their courses—
this is the clarity of **qīngjìng**.

The **true person**’s governance
responds to heaven and earth.
Thus, the sun and moon shine brightly yet do not force,
and calamities do not arise.

Laozi said:
The duties of governance in a **harmonious age** are easy to uphold,
its affairs are simple to execute,
its **ritual/lǐ** is straightforward to practice,
its rewards are clear to bestow.
Thus, one person does not hold multiple offices,
offices do not overlap with scholars,
scholars, farmers, artisans, and merchants
keep to their own districts and states.

Thus, farmers speak with farmers of **cáng/hidden matters**,
scholars speak with scholars of conduct,
artisans speak with artisans of skill,
merchants speak with merchants of calculations—
so scholars lack no proper action,
artisans suffer no laborious toil,
farmers waste no effort,
merchants lose no goods,
each finding contentment in their nature.

Diverse forms and natures,
simple yet not contradictory,
lose their occupation and become despised,
gain their ambition and become honored.
The *Easy Words of the Yi* say:
**Qián** is easy to know,
**Kūn** is simple in ability.
The sage governs without action,
the people do not weary of their labors,
each finding contentment in their work,
all rejoicing in their lives.

Thus, governance is easy yet not chaotic,
the four classes gain their due—
this is honored;
lose their standing—
this is degraded.

Those with foresight and distant vision
are the most talented,
yet in a **harmonious age**, no blame is assigned to them.
No blame is placed upon them.
Those with vast knowledge and strong resolve,
eloquent and quick-witted,
are those whose understanding overflows,
yet the wise ruler does not demand from those below.
The clever and quick are not necessarily loyal and upright—
this is because the ruler does not seek from those beneath him.

To abandon the world’s coarse and despised,
not conforming to common customs,
is the conduct of scholars,
yet in a **harmonious age**, this is not how the people are transformed.»

The age of extravagance and neglect of customs cannot serve as a model.

Thus, those whose heights are unattainable should not be the measure for all;
those whose conduct is beyond imitation should not be the standard for the realm.

Therefore, talent cannot be monopolized or constrained by rigid measures,
while the Dao/Way and its methods may be passed down through generations.

These principles in a harmonious age—
scholars must discern their talents and employ them accordingly.
Only those who grasp the luminous Dao/Way and adapt with the times
can assist in governance.

Thus, when the realm is well-ordered, even the unlearned can uphold its ways,
and military formations can be standardized by law.

Without awaiting ancient sages, the people suffice in their own right—
utilizing what they possess and combining their strengths.

Governance lies in timely adaptation, not in seeking novelty;
eloquence should emphasize substance, not empty rhetoric or debate.

Yet if one measures ability and assigns positions appropriately,
there will never be a time without capable rulers.

Why wait for ancient worthies when the world is already well-ordered?

The laws of the declining age
set lofty standards yet fail to reach their mark,
impose heavy burdens yet cannot bear the punishment,
and create peril yet dare not execute.
The people, burdened by these three oppressions,
adorn their cunning to deceive the ruler,
pursue vice while courting danger—
though harsh laws and severe punishments are enforced,
they cannot suppress their deceit.

When beasts are cornered, they strike;
when birds are cornered, they peck;
when people are cornered, they deceive—
this is their nature.

To apply the law and punish only when crimes are committed
is to kill by law, not to govern.

Those filled with anxiety and fear
adorn their cunning, distort the truth, and scheme to gain advantage—
like birds and beasts driven to desperation,
their defiant hearts disregard their own lives.

Thus, the Sage values the Dao/Way over laws.

Laozi said:
*The thunder’s roar can be imitated by bells and drums;
the shifts of wind and rain can be understood through music’s tones.
What is vast and visible can be measured;
what is bright and apparent can be exhausted;
what is audible can be tuned;
what is discernible can be distinguished.

Yet the utmost vastness—heaven and earth cannot contain it;
the utmost subtlety—spirit and clarity cannot perceive it.

Wind, rain, thunder, and lightning—
their forms, sounds, colors, and symbols
can be categorized and applied to affairs.

But when it comes to the utmost vastness and subtlety—
this is the Dao/Way,
which heaven and earth cannot encompass,
which spirit and clarity cannot fathom.

When establishing laws and calendars,
distinguishing the five colors,
separating purity and turbidity,
discerning sweet and bitter tastes—
then the undifferentiated is dispersed into tools.

When establishing benevolence and righteousness,
refining rituals and music—
then virtue shifts into artifice.

The people adorn their cunning to astonish the ignorant,
employ deceit to attack their superiors—
though some may uphold these ways,
none can truly govern with them.

The more cleverness abounds, the more virtue declines—
thus, the perfect one remains pure and undivided.

The perfect one’s governance is
empty, silent, and tranquil—
seeing no desires,
the heart and spirit dwell in harmony,
the form and nature align,
stillness embodies virtue,
action flows with principle,
following the Dao/Way of nature
only when necessity arises.

Vague and effortless, yet the world harmonizes;
indifferent and desireless, yet the people return to simplicity.
Without contention, resources suffice.
The giver does not hoard,
the receiver does not refuse—
virtue returns naturally,
yet none can claim credit.

Speech without words,
the Dao/Way beyond explanation—
if one attains this,
it is called the Celestial Storehouse.

Take from it without depletion,
draw from it without exhaustion—
none know its source,
for the undifferentiated disperses and loses its origin.
Thus, the Sage acts and refines,
restoring them to their natural state,
concealing them in the Celestial Storehouse,
taking without diminishing,
giving without excess.

This is called *Yao Guang* [Shaking Brilliance].
*Yao Guang* is the resource for all things.
Its luminous radiance and luminous standard
are displayed within,
governing the ten thousand things of the four quarters,
commanding them as its provision.

Laozi said:
*Heaven cherishes its purity,
earth cherishes its levelness,
humans cherish their sincerity.
Heaven’s purity—
the sun, moon, stars, and constellations,
thunder and wind, rain and mist.
Earth’s levelness—
water, fire, metal, wood, and soil.
Human sincerity—
thought, perception, cleverness, joy, and anger.

Thus, closing the four gates,
halting the five pathways—
this aligns with the Dao/Way.

Spirit and clarity are hidden in the formless,
qi returns to its true essence.
The eyes see clearly yet do not gaze,
the ears hear keenly yet do not listen,
the mouth speaks appropriately yet does not speak,
the heart’s pathways are open yet do not deliberate.
Abandoning action, knowing without arrogance,
following nature’s essence,
one knows yet cannot be harmed.

When essence resides in the eyes, vision is clear;
when it resides in the ears, hearing is acute;
when it lingers in the mouth, speech is precise;
when gathered in the heart, thought is lucid.

Thus, closing the four gates
ensures a life free from calamity—
the limbs and orifices neither die nor live,
this is called the True Person.

Heaven’s four gates are the sun, moon, stars, and constellations.
The five pathways are the five phases.

Speaking of the four seasons having their due,
the five phases having their measure—
then heaven and earth are clear and bright,
and the people and creatures flourish.

Human four gates are the heart, mouth, ears, and eyes.
The five pathways are the five viscera.

When essence and spirit are preserved,
the four gates do not move recklessly;
the five pathways do not receive false stimuli—
vision is clear, hearing is acute,
speech and action are without fault—
thus, calamity and harm cannot reach the body,
and death does not dwell in the mind.
This is the domain where the True Person dwells in the Dao/Way.

Earth’s production of wealth
has no greater foundation than the five phases.
The Sage regulates the five phases,
and thus governance does not falter.

The Sage, by harmonizing the five emotions with the five phases,
prevents chaos in the world—
how much more so for ordinary people?

Laozi said:
*When balanced left and right,
there is no private preference for light or heavy,
thus it can serve as level.
When measured within and without,
there is no private bias for crooked or straight,
thus it can serve as upright.
When the ruler applies law,
there is no private favor or aversion,
thus it can serve as command.

Virtue has no fixed place,
grudges have no place to hide—
this is to entrust oneself to the Dao/Way
and align with human hearts.

These three principles—
relying on impartiality—
establish levelness,
preserve uprightness,
and enact command;

The Sage does not abandon virtue to the external world,
nor conceal grudges within.
To entrust oneself to the Dao/Way even unto death—
the common people remain unaware.

Thus, those who govern know not to interfere.

Water capsizes a boat, wood snaps an axle—
yet one does not blame the wood and stone,
but punishes the clumsy and the skilled.
This is not wisdom.

Water has no intent to capsize a boat,
wood no mind to snap an axle—
yet one blames the clumsy and the skilled,
for this is not the work of wisdom.

To govern a state with wisdom is to be its ruin.

Thus, when the Dao/Way is mixed with wisdom, there is chaos;
when virtue is mixed with intent, there is peril;
when the mind is mixed with perception, there is dizziness.
To cease wisdom is to avoid chaos,
to forget intent is to avoid peril,
to sever perception is to avoid dizziness—
all are acts of forgetting.

The scales and compasses, the square and the circle—
fixed and unchanging,
ever consistent and never crooked,
moving straight without hesitation.
Once shaped, they endure for ten thousand generations—
this is the Way of *wuwei* (non-action).

The scales are not unbalanced,
the plumb line not crooked—
yet those who wield them err.

The Dao/Way is not empty,
virtue not obscure—
yet those who cultivate it stray.

The One is *wuwei*—
used by a hundred kings,
transmitted for ten thousand generations,
yet never altered.

The One is *wuwei*—
used by a hundred kings,
transmitted for ten thousand generations without change.

All who act without emotion or private bias,
meeting the One in all things—
though they act all day,
never once do they act.

This Dao brings governance—
correct and unchanging.

Otherwise, how could the balance and measure
be crafted in a single day
yet never altered for ten thousand generations?

Laozi said:
*"The people say:
'A kingdom has a lost ruler,
an age has lost the Dao/Way.'

A person may reach exhaustion,
yet reason remains unblocked.
Thus, *wuwei* is the essence of the Dao/Way.

To grasp the essence of the Dao/Way
is to respond to the boundless.
Then why do kingdoms perish?
Because they lack the correct Dao/Way.

He who reaches exhaustion yet remains unblocked—
this is the Dao/Way.
To act without clinging—
this is virtue.
The essence of *wuwei* responds to the boundless.

Thus, if one does not follow the numbers of Dao/Way
and instead relies solely on one’s own ability,
exhaustion will come swiftly.

To pride oneself in one’s own virtue
while neglecting the Dao/Way—
ruin and humiliation will soon follow.

The sovereign need not leave his door
to know the world—
by observing things, he knows things;
by observing people, he knows people.

Thus, where strength is gathered, nothing is unconquerable;
where many minds unite, nothing is unachievable.

A thousand-strong force lacks no provisions,
a ten-thousand-strong host lacks no purpose.
Craftsmen have no conflicting skills,
scholars hold no dual offices—
each guards their duty,
none overstepping their bounds.
People find their proper place,
things find their natural rest—
thus tools are not despised,
duties not neglected.

By observing one thing, one knows the nature of another;
by measuring oneself, one discerns the nature of others.

He who skillfully uses the many
can divert rivers and fill seas;
he who skillfully uses people
can exhaust their hearts and strength.

Debt is light when few,
duties are easy when simple,
burdens are light when light—
thus the sovereign enforces minimal demands,
and the people perform with ease.
Hence, ruler and subjects endure without weariness.

This is the virtue of simplicity—
thus there is no labor or fatigue.

Laozi said:
*The Sage embodies the Great Unity.
The Great Unity takes emptiness and nothingness as its origin,
cleanness and stillness as its essence—
thus the Sage and king should embody it.

The king follows the patterns of yin and yang,
moving with measured rhythm.

The hegemon aligns with the four seasons,
never departing from their due.

The ruler applies the six *lü* (musical pitches),
harmonizing with all things in moderation.

To embody the Great Unity is to understand
the nature of Heaven and Earth,
to harmonize with the principles of virtue and the Dao/Way;
to shine with the clarity of sun and moon,
to resonate with the spirit of all things;
to move and rest in accord with yin and yang,
to rejoice and anger in harmony with the four seasons;
to cover all things with the Dao/Way,
spreading broadly without favor—
even the smallest creatures,
creeping or flying,
all depend on virtue to live.

Virtue extends beyond the realm,
and fame endures through the ages.
Thus, the ancient Sage-kings governed the world with the Dao/Way.

To follow yin and yang is to inherit
the harmony of Heaven and Earth,
to share brilliance with Heaven and Earth,
to shine with the radiance of sun and moon,
to resonate with the spirits of gods and ghosts;
to wear the round and tread the square,
to embrace the standard and sleep with the plumb line—
within, to govern the body;
without, to win the hearts of people.
When orders are issued, the world follows like the wind.

The Five Emperors governed the world with virtue.

To align with the four seasons is to
take and give in due measure,
to enter and exit with restraint—
joy and anger, firmness and gentleness,
never straying from principle.
Gentle yet unbreakable,
firm yet unfractured,
broad yet not licentious,
solemn yet not rebellious—
to nurture all things with ease and submission,
to abandon folly and embrace the unworthy,
without private favor.

The Three Kings governed the world with benevolence.

To apply the six *lü* is to
give life and take it,
to reward and punish,
to bestow and withhold—
this is the Dao/Way.
To subdue chaos and restrain violence,
to elevate the worthy and discard the unworthy,
to correct the crooked and establish the straight,
to clarify the paths of opening and closing,
to ride the times and leverage circumstances,
to serve and govern the hearts of people—
this is the way of the Five Hegemons governing with righteousness.

The Sage who does not embody yin and yang will be overrun;
the king who does not follow the four seasons will be diminished;
the hegemon who does not use the six *lü* will be humiliated;
the ruler who loses the plumb line and square will be deposed.

Thus, the small acting as the great
will reach exhaustion without intimacy;
the great acting as the small
will become narrow and unyielding.

In the age of the Sage-kings,
virtue and the Dao/Way had degrees of excellence;
in the age of the Five Hegemons,
benevolence and righteousness had varying depths.
Great and small could not transgress their bounds.

Laozi said:
*"Though a land be vast and its people numerous,
this is not true strength;
though armor be sturdy and weapons sharp,
this is not reliable victory;
though walls be high and moats deep,
this is not true defense;
though punishments be severe and laws harsh,
this is not true authority."*

The three cannot be relied upon—only the virtuous/powerful can rule.

For those who preserve governance, though small, they endure;
for those who abandon governance, though great, they perish.

Thus, the skilled in defense need no resistance—*yin* and *yang* suffice.
The skilled in war need no battle—
they ride the times and follow the people’s desires,
and all under Heaven submits.

Therefore, the skilled in governance accumulate virtue/power;
the skilled in wielding arms cultivate forbearance.
When virtue/power is accumulated, the people may be used;
when anger is restrained, authority may be established.

Where the *wen* (cultural refinement) extends deeply,
the *quan* (authoritative measures) command greatly;
where virtue/power is broadly applied,
the authority controls widely—
this is to strengthen oneself while weakening others.

The skilled in wielding arms first weaken the enemy, then engage—
thus, their expenditures are halved while their achievements are tenfold.

A thousand-chariot state practicing *wen* (cultural governance) rules;
a ten-thousand-chariot state fond of war perishes.

The victorious army wins first, then fights;
the defeated army fights first, then seeks victory—
this is ignorance of the Dao/Way.

Lightly wielding arms, though great, will perish;
skillfully employing governance, though small, will flourish.

Preserving the myriad households in the land of no death—this is victory;
driving the people into the land of corpses—this is certain defeat.

*[End of Volume Nine of the Treatise on the Mysterious and True]*
*[Volume Ten of the Treatise on the Mysterious and True, with annotations by Mo Xi Zi]*

The benevolent sovereign of **upper virtue** and the virtuous/powerful sovereign of **upper Dao** will have all under heaven flock to him;
the sovereign of **upper virtue** will have all within the seas come to him;
the sovereign of **upper righteousness** will have one state unite under him;
the sovereign of **upper propriety** will have one village follow him.
Without these four, the people will not flock to him.
From *"Upper Virtue"* onward, no chapter annotations are given—its meaning is similar.

The *Laozi* says:
The **Dao/Way** of the gentleman is to cultivate stillness to refine oneself,
to practice frugality to nourish life.
Stillness means not disturbing below—
if below is not disturbed, the people will not grumble;
if below is disturbed, governance will falter—
the people grumble when virtue/power is thin.
When governance falters, the wise do not offer counsel;
when virtue/power is thin, the brave do not fight.
The enlightened sovereign cultivates stillness to refine himself,
practices frugality to nourish life,
so that above there is no chaotic governance and below there are no grumbling people.
Then the wise offer counsel of their own accord,
and the brave fight for their own sake.
The chaotic sovereign is not thus:
In one day, he may possess the wealth of all under heaven,
command the power of one sovereign,
exhaust the people’s strength to gratify his senses,
while his ambitions fix on palaces and pavilions,
moats and ponds, gardens and enclosures,
tigers and rare beasts.
The poor suffer hunger and cold,
while the halls are decked in embroidered silks.
Thus, though the sovereign hoards these useless things,
the world does not rest beneath his rule.
This is the dark sovereign in his single day of power,
extorting the wealth of the world,
poisoning the four seas,
exhausting the people’s livelihoods,
indulging his eyes and ears in pleasure—
like the Second Emperor of Qin, whose use of power led only to folly.

The *Laozi* says:
Without simplicity, one cannot clarify virtue/power;
without tranquility, one cannot attain the distant;
without magnanimity, one cannot embrace and cover all;
without fairness, one cannot make decisive judgments.
To see with the eyes of all under heaven,
to hear with the ears of all under heaven,
to deliberate with the hearts of all under heaven,
to contend with the strength of all under heaven—
thus, his commands reach their full extent,
and the sentiments of his ministers ascend to his ears.
Officials diligently perform their duties,
while ministers gather like spokes to the hub.
He does not reward for joy,
nor punish for anger.
His laws are discerning yet not harsh;
his senses are keen yet not obscured.
The truth and falsehood of matters are laid bare before him daily,
and he does not resist them.
Thus, the wise exhaust their intelligence,
the unworthy strain their strength,
the near feel secure in their nature,
the distant cherish his virtue/power—
this is the **Dao/Way** of employing people.
Such is how the enlightened sovereign governs his state,
and how he employs his people—
like the Han’s Emperor Gaozu or the Tang’s Emperor Taizong.

He who rides in the imperial carriage and on fine horses
travels a thousand *li* without labor;
he who sails on ships and steers oars
crosses rivers and seas without swimming.
If one’s words are true, though he be a merchant or a humble laborer,
he should not be discarded;
if one’s words are false, though he be a sovereign, minister, or high official,
he should not be employed.
The judgment of truth and falsehood
cannot be decided by rank or status.
If one’s plans are useful, he need not be ashamed of his station;
if one’s words are practical, he need not be prized for his eloquence.
