When employing people in accord with the **Dao/Way**,
one achieves results without effort,
and reaches understanding without action.
Thus, the Yellow Emperor was moved by the words of a shepherd boy
and brought order to all under heaven—
was he truly of low and weak status?
Hu Hai trusted the schemes of Zhao Gao
and lost all under heaven—
was Zhao Gao truly of noble rank?
The dark sovereign is not thus:
Few ministers offer sincere loyalty,
for he does not employ their talents.
He favors those close and familiar with deceit,
so the wise cannot be seen;
he spurns those distant, humble, and lowly,
so those who strain their strength and offer loyal counsel cannot be heard.
If one speaks, he exhausts them with words;
if one remonstrates, he executes them with charges.

「Thus yet seeking to bring peace to all under heaven,
to preserve the myriad lands,
his distance from wisdom and sagacity is already vast.

Without **saintly** virtue, one cannot still the four seas;
without **sagacity**, one cannot bring peace to the myriad lands.

**Laozi** said:
*"He who can honor life,
though rich and noble does not harm his body with sustenance,
though poor and humble does not burden his form with gain.
Though he spends ten thousand gold daily,
he does not indulge his mouth to harm life;
though his home lacks even a single grain,
he does not begrudgingly seek to injure **virtue/power**.
This may be called honoring life indeed.*

Now, receiving the hereditary noble rank of one’s ancestors,
one must surely place great weight on not losing it—
yet life’s origin has endured for ages,
and to lightly risk losing it, is this not perplexing?

To govern the world with noble rank is to entrust it with the world;
to govern the world with love is to rely upon it with the world.

Those who inherit the legacy of their ancestors yet lose it,
must surely do so through careless neglect.

Thus, if one does not blame oneself,
there is no way to entrust the world with oneself.

**Wenzzi** asked about the foundation of ruling a state,
and **Laozi** replied:
*"The foundation lies in governing oneself.
Never have I heard of a state in disorder while the self is governed,
nor has there ever been a state in order while the self is disordered.
Hence it is said:
Refine oneself, and one’s **virtue/power** shall be true.
If one’s self is not yet governed, how much less the state?"*

The **Dao/Way** is so supremely wondrous,
that a father cannot teach it to his son,
nor can a son receive it from his father.
Thus, the **Dao/Way** that can be spoken of is not the constant **Dao/Way**;
the name that can be named is not the constant name.
The constant **Dao/Way** has no name—
what can be named is not the **Dao/Way**.
Hence, what words and discourse cannot encompass,
fathers and sons cannot pass it on.

**Wenzzi** asked:
*"What conduct ensures the people love their ruler?"*
**Laozi** replied:
*"Lead them with proper timing,
and treat them with reverence and caution,
as though standing at the edge of a deep abyss,
or walking on thin ice.
Between heaven and earth,
the good become my nurtured subjects,
the unworthy become my **enemies**.
In ancient times, the ministers of Xia and Shang,
avenged the wrongs of Jie and Zhou
and served as subjects to Tang and Wu.
The people of Huosha,
attacked their own lord
and joined the followers of **Shennong**.*

Hence it is said:
*"What the people fear,
one must not fail to fear.
What is truly fearsome is not the ruler,
what is truly fearsome is not mere goodness."*

**Laozi** said:
*"To govern the great,
the **Dao/Way** cannot be trifling;
to rule vast lands,
the measures cannot be narrow;
to occupy high station,
the duties cannot be burdensome;
to lead many people,
the teachings cannot be harsh.
Burdensome duties are hard to govern,
harsh laws are hard to implement,
demands that are excessive are hard to satisfy.
Measure an inch with a ruler,
and by a yard it will surely be off;
weigh a **qian** with a balance,
and by a **dan** it will surely be excessive.
Yet weigh with a balance and measure with a ruler,
and even by a **dan** or a yard,
the errors will be few.
It is easy to be wise in broad strokes,
but subtle reasoning is hard to make sagacious.
The **Dao/Way** lies hidden in small things yet achieves greatness,
words lie hidden in simplicity yet reveal superficiality.
Thus, what does not benefit governance
but instead harms it,
the **saintly** does not practice;
what does not benefit utility
but instead wastes it,
the wise do not employ.

Hence, deeds are not to be scorned for being too restrained,
tasks are not to be scorned for being too few,
demands are not to be scorned for being too sparse.
Restrained deeds are easy to accomplish,
few tasks are easy to govern,
sparse demands are easy to satisfy,
and entrusting matters to the people makes it easy.
Thus, petty reasoning harms **righteousness**,
petty **righteousness** breaks the **Dao/Way**,
and the **Dao/Way** must be simple to be comprehensive.
The **saintly** are comprehensive in wisdom,
perceiving what has not yet come to pass,
not allowing small things to obscure the great,
nor burdensome matters to replace simplicity.

The river flows far because of its winding course,
not because it is a single ladle of water.
The mountain rises high because of its gradual ascent,
not because it is a single handful of soil.
The **Dao/Way** transforms because of its ease and flow,
not because of what is achieved in an instant.

He who excels in one skill,
is meticulous in one matter,
and discerning in one ability,
can employ clever arguments,
but cannot broadly apply them.

The tuner of music,
tightens the small strings,
and loosens the large ones;
the establisher of affairs,
makes the lowly labor,
and lets the noble rest.
Words say:
*"Balance the strings, fit the large and small—
in governing a state, noble and lowly alike must be justly treated."*

The words of the **Dao/Way** say:
*"Vague and indistinct,
align with heaven’s might,
and harmonize with heaven’s **qi**.
He who harmonizes with **qi** becomes **Emperor**,
he who harmonizes with **righteousness** becomes **King**,
he who harmonizes with deeds becomes **Hegemon**.
He who lacks all three perishes.
He who harmonizes with **qi** is without **virtue/power** yet is called so;
he who harmonizes with **righteousness** rescues the peril of the age;
he who harmonizes with deeds shares benefits with the people.
Without one of these three,
the peril of ruin is inevitable.
Hence, one does not speak yet is believed,
does not act yet is benevolent,
does not rage yet is awe-inspiring—
thus, the **heavenly heart** moves and transforms.

The Five **Emperors** acted naturally without effort,
harmonizing with heaven’s will.
All things received their life from them,
and responded in transformation.

To act yet be benevolent,
to speak yet be trustworthy,
to rage yet be awe-inspiring—
this is what is achieved through **sincerity** and **inner power**.

The Three **Kings**’ **sincerity** and **inner power** emanated from within,
responding outwardly in action,
yet still left traces,
not yet fully harmonized with the **heartless**.

To act yet lack benevolence,
to speak yet lack trustworthiness,
to rage yet lack awe—
this is what is achieved through mere outward appearance.

The Five **Hegemons**’ **sincerity** did not arise from the heart,
and thus nothing responded to them.
Hence, though they raged, they were not awe-inspiring.

Thus, with the **Dao/Way** to guide them,
even few laws suffice for governance;
without the **Dao/Way** to guide them,
even many laws suffice only for chaos.
Governance depends on the **Dao/Way** being essential,
chaos depends on laws being excessive.

**Laozi** said:
*"A whalefish out of water is controlled by ants;
a ruler who abandons his **Dao/Way**
and disputes matters with his ministers,
is controlled by his officials.
Fish cannot be out of water,
nor can a ruler abandon the **Dao/Way**.
To uphold one’s station with **non-action**,
those who guard their duties take refuge in obedience,
while ministers conceal their wisdom and do not act—
instead, they allow their superiors to monopolize affairs.
A ruler who does not entrust capable ministers
but prefers to act himself,
shall find his wisdom daily constrained
while he bears the blame alone.
If justice is exhausted below,
he cannot extend proper governance;
if his conduct falters in his station,
he cannot maintain authority.
If wisdom is insufficient for governance,
and awe is insufficient to enforce punishment,
then there is no way to interact with his subjects.

If joy and anger manifest in the heart,
and desires appear outwardly,
then those who guard their duties
shall abandon principle to curry favor,
and officials shall pervert the law to follow the wind.
If rewards do not match deeds,
and punishments do not fit crimes,
then ruler and subjects shall be at odds,
and sovereign and ministers shall harbor mutual resentment.»

The myriad officials grow chaotic, yet wisdom cannot disentangle them;
Praise does not arise, yet sagacity cannot illuminate it.
Not one’s own fault, yet he blames himself alone—
Then the sovereign’s labors increase, while ministers grow more negligent.

This is like the artisan who carves in place of the master.
He who carves in place of the master,
Rarely fails to harm his own hands.

When the sovereign entrusts the worthy and selects the capable,
And does not monopolize decisions for himself,
Then wisdom has its source, and sagacity need not always shine—
The guardians of duty shall not flatter above,
And the ministers shall uphold the law.
Yet still the innocent are slain,
And those with merit are not rewarded—
The sovereign’s labors increase, while ministers grow more negligent.

This is like the artisan who carves in place of the master,
And sits, harming his own hands.

To chase horses with their swiftness,
One’s sinews shall snap and fail to keep up—
To seize the reins from the chariot,
The horse shall die beneath the yoke.

The **Bólé** discerns them, the **Wángliáng** drives them,
A wise sovereign rides them—
Without the effort of discernment or driving,
Yet reaches a thousand *lǐ*—
This is the art of leveraging others’ strengths.

The sovereign, seated above, governs the multitude below;
His trust lies in loyalty and rectitude,
Not in outward form or inner spirit.
As with riding a horse,
He relies on the **shì** [discerning official] and the **yù** [driver],
Achieving a thousand *lǐ* without exertion—
This is no hardship.

The **Way** of the sovereign:
To act without attachment, to establish without preference.
To act is to debate,
To prefer is to flatter—
Debate invites usurpation,
Flattery invites corruption.
Corrupting his private inclinations,
Usurping his rightful governance—
He who submits to others’ control,
Cannot sustain the realm.

Thus, the master of governance does not pluck [at will];
This is because governance is formless.
Only those who embody **shénhuà** [transformation of the spirit],
No force can overcome.

The sovereign who governs,
Ever controls others—
Never controlled by others—
This is the master of governance who does not pluck [at will].
To use without visible form,
Hence the term **shénhuà**.

When inner desires do not manifest,
This is called **"jīng"** [restraining];
When outer evils do not enter,
This is called **"bì"** [closing].
To restrain within and close without,
What matter cannot be moderated?
To close without and restrain within,
What task cannot be accomplished?
To restrain within and close without,
There are no desires, no harms.

Thus, he does not act for them,
Nor does he act against them—
Yet they act for him,
And he acts through them.
Words that are not refuted,
Deeds that are not thwarted—
Following names to assess reality,
Letting each minister bear responsibility—
To regard ignorance as the **Way**,
To forbid cruelty as the sovereign’s principle—
Then the myriad officials’ affairs,
Each shall find their proper measure.

The sovereign preserves the grand framework,
Entrusts it to the myriad officials,
Examines their deliberations,
And determines their merits and faults—
That is all.

**Lǎozǐ** said:
*"The nourished are the people’s foundation;
The people are the realm’s base.
Thus the sovereign,
Above, follows the **Dao** of Heaven and its seasons,
Below, exhausts the logic of Earth,
Within, employs the strength of the people.
Hence the myriad creatures thrive—
Growth and abundance flourish.
Spring, he prunes the withered;
Summer, he harvests the fruits;
Autumn, he stores the vegetables and grains;
Winter, he gathers firewood and salt.
As the people’s resources,
They lack nothing in life,
And in death, no orphaned descendants mourn.
The corpse of the sovereign can preserve harmony,
Life and death are both in order."*

The **Way** of the ancient kings:
Does not net the multitude while seeking the **máo** [wild boar],
Nor flood the marshes while hunting fish.
Does not net the multitude while seeking the **máo** [wild boar],
Nor flood the marshes while hunting fish.
Does not exhaust the creatures.

The **yáo** [wolf] has not yet been sacrificed,
Yet the **zhì** [noose] resounds with empty sounds—
It cannot reach the wilds;
The **tǎ** [otter] has not yet been sacrificed,
Yet the nets do not enter the waters—
The **yīng** [eagle] and **sǔn** [hawk] have not yet struck,
Yet the snares are not spread upon the **gāo** [highlands];
The grasses and trees have not yet fallen,
Yet the axes and blades do not enter the forests—
The insects have not yet been roasted,
Nor the pregnant slain—
The **zhèn** [nest] is not yet explored,
The fish not yet grown to a *chǐ* [foot] in length are taken,
Nor the dogs and swine allowed to starve—
The year’s harvest is not yet ripe.

All follow their proper time,
And do not recklessly harm.

Thus, the myriad creatures’ births,
Like the **zhēng** [breath] of **yīn-yáng** [yin-yang],
Are said to rise when their **jīng-qì** [vital essence] is slain,
And their **dào** [proper measure] is taken—
Then the myriad creatures’ vital essence and **qì** surge upward,
Reaching the heavens,
And shall bring harm to mankind.

This is the **Way** of the ancient kings
To respond to the seasons in preparation,
To enrich the realm and benefit the people.

To nourish with propriety,
To take in due measure—
Not to disrupt their **Way**—
This is how the realm is enriched,
And the family preserved.

It is not a matter of what the eyes see
And the feet can reach—
If he desires to benefit the people,
And does not harbor resentment in his heart,
Then the people shall naturally provide.

To measure things by the heart,
To observe others by the body—
What need to raise brows or move feet,
Before all is complete?

**Lǎozǐ** said:
*"The ancient sovereigns,
When taking from below, had moderation;
When nourishing themselves, had restraint.
They calculated the year’s harvest,
Measured the people’s stored wealth,
Knew the surplus and the deficit—
Then they took and offered.
Thus they received what Heaven and Earth bestowed,
And avoided the perils of hunger and cold.
Their grief for the people was profound—
When the realm had famine,
Their food lacked savor;
When the people were cold,
Their winter cloaks were not fur-lined—
Sharing the people’s joys and sorrows,
Thus the world had no grieving subjects—
This was the ancient sovereign’s governance of the realm."*

The **dark sovereign** is not so.
When taking from the people,
He does not limit their strength;
When demanding from below,
He does not measure their stores—
Men and women cannot till and weave
To meet his endless demands.
Their labor is exhausted, their wealth depleted—
They have no dawn without dusk,
And sovereign and ministers alike grow weary.

Moreover, when a man plows,
One *zhǐ* [fathom] of grain does not benefit ten *mǔ* [mu] of land—
The yield of average fields,
Does not exceed four *dàn* [bushels].
His aged wife, children, and infirm dependents,
Rely on him for sustenance.
If calamity or disaster strikes,
They cannot meet the sovereign’s demands.
Then the sovereign pities them—
But the greedy tyrant,
Draining the people like a fisherman in drought,
To satisfy his insatiable desires—
Then the common folk do not enjoy Heaven’s harmony,
Nor walk upon Earth’s **virtue** [de].

This is the **dark sovereign’s** governance of the realm.

**Lǎozǐ** said:
*"The **qì** of Heaven and Earth,
None greater than **harmony**.
**Harmony** is the balance of **yīn-yáng** [yin-yang],
The division of day and night.
Thus, when spring arrives, all things are born,
When autumn comes, all things mature—
Birth and maturity must draw from **harmony’s** essence.
Hence, accumulated **yīn** does not give life,
Accumulated **yáng** does not transform—
Only when **yīn-yáng** intermingle,
Does **harmony** arise."*

This is the **harmony** of Heaven and Earth’s **qì**,
Thus all things attain birth and maturity.

Therefore, the **Dao** of the Sage
Is broad yet cautious,
Stern yet warm,
Soft yet upright,
Fierce yet benevolent.

Excessive rigidity breaks,
Excessive softness curls inward—
The **Dao** lies between **just** and **yielding**.

Like a measuring line,
It can be rolled and held,
Stretched and laid straight—
Long yet not sprawling,
Short yet not confined,
Straight yet not rigid—
Thus the Sage embodies it.

This is the Sage’s **harmony**.

Soft yet able to stand firm—
The carpenter’s square and compass.
Coiled yet able to unfold—
The Sage’s laws and measures.

Push benevolence too far, it becomes weakness—
Weakness lacks authority.
Push sternness too far, it becomes brutality—
Brutality lacks **harmony**.
Push love too far, it becomes indulgence—
Indulgence lacks order.
Push punishment too far, it becomes calamity—
Calamity breeds estrangement.
Thus, **harmony** is most valued.

**Lǎozǐ** said:
*"A realm endures by attaining the **Dao**;
It perishes by obstruction of principle.
Thus the Sage observes transformations to discern their omens.
**Virtue** waxes or wanes—
The winds first stir its signs.
Those who grasp the **Dao** of life,
Though small, shall grow great;
Those who bear signs of ruin,
Though established, shall fall.
A realm’s ruin does not lie in vastness;
The **Dao**’s practice cannot be slighted.
Thus survival lies in attaining the **Dao**,
Not in size;
Ruination lies in losing the **Dao**,
Not in grandeur.
Rulers of chaotic realms
Seek only broad lands,
Yet neglect **virtue** and **righteousness**;
Strive for high stations,
Yet neglect **Dao** and **moral power**.
They abandon what sustains them,
And court their own destruction.

The wise observe the wise and foolish
To judge the rise and fall of states—
**Virtue** and **moral power** endure,
Not in their scale, but in their depth.
If the sovereign above disrupts the **Three Lights**’ radiance,
And loses the people’s hearts below—
Who cannot fail to crumble?

Thus those who scrutinize themselves
Need not fear others.

In ancient times,
Deep practice was called **Dao** and **virtue**,
Shallow practice, **righteousness** and **benevolence**,
Thin practice, **rites** and **wisdom**.
These six are the state’s vital threads.
Deep practice brings thick blessings;
Shallow practice, thin blessings;
Full practice—all under Heaven submits.
In antiquity,
Cultivating **Dao** and **virtue** rectified all under Heaven,
Cultivating **righteousness** and **benevolence** rectified a realm,
Cultivating **rites** and **wisdom** rectified a village.
Thick **virtue** brings greatness,
Thin **virtue** brings smallness—
Thus the **Dao** does not rely on martial prowess,
Nor on strength or greed to prevail,
Nor on competition to gain.
To stand in all under Heaven is to push oneself outward,
To prevail is to let all under Heaven submit of its own accord,
To gain is to give to all under Heaven—
Not to seize for oneself.
Thus the yielding attains firmness,
The weak attain victory,
**Righteousness** and **benevolence** attain success,
The non-contentious cannot be contended with.
Thus the **Dao** in all under Heaven
Is like the great rivers and seas.

Heaven’s **Dao**:
Those who act against it perish,
Those who grasp it lose it.
To covet great fame while striving for it—
I see it as inevitable failure.
Yet even if grasped, it does not remain.
Great fame cannot be seized—
It is given by all under Heaven.
To give is to receive.
All under Heaven’s allegiance is to **virtue**.
Thus it is said:
The **virtue** of the highest **Dao** draws all under Heaven,
The **benevolence** of the highest **Dao** draws all within the seas,
The **righteousness** of the highest **Dao** draws a realm,
The **rites** of the highest **Dao** draw a village.
Without these four, the people will not submit.
If they do not submit, arms are used—
This is the perilous **Dao**.

Thus it is said:
*"Weapons are the most ominous tools.
Used only when unavoidable,
Victory brings no joy in slaughter.
Thus it is said:
In the land of death, thorns and brambles grow—
Mournful weeping fills the air,
Funeral rites mark the dwellings.
Therefore the **Junzi** prioritizes **Dao** and **virtue**,
And rarely resorts to arms.
Practice varies in depth,
**Virtue** in thickness—
**Dao** and **virtue** cannot be momentarily abandoned,
Warlike tools should not be recklessly wielded."*

**Wénzǐ** asked:
*"Why are **righteousness**, **benevolence**, and **rites** inferior to **Dao** and **virtue**?"*

**Lǎozǐ** said:
*"Those who practice **benevolence** must weigh it by sorrow and joy;
Those who practice **righteousness** must clarify giving and taking.
Within the Four Seas, sorrow and joy cannot reach all,
And exhausting the treasury’s wealth cannot suffice for the people.
Thus kindness cannot be universal.
Therefore, it is better to cultivate the **Dao** and practice **virtue**,
Aligning with Heaven and Earth’s nature—
All things then self-regulate,
And all under Heaven is nourished,
While **righteousness** and **benevolence** follow naturally.

Thus the **true man** dwells in the thick,
Not the thin.
Knowing the **Dao**’s **virtue** is profound while **righteousness** is shallow,
The Sage abides in the thick,
Not the thin.

**Rites** are the adornment of substance;
**Benevolence** is the manifestation of kindness.
Thus **rites** follow human nature without excess,
**Benevolence** does not overflow kindness—
Mourning the dead aligns with **benevolence**,
Funeral rites fulfill **rites**.
Nurturing life does not force what cannot be borne,
Nor sever what cannot be abandoned—
Measure and moderation never stray from balance,
And unearned praise finds no root.

Thus music suffices to unite joy,
Yet never strays from **harmony**,
Clarifying the division of life and death,
And the balance of extravagance and frugality.
When human nature loses **harmony**,
**Righteousness** and **benevolence** arise,
Regulated by **rites** and music,
Each made clear in their place without overstepping.

In the later ages, it is not so—
Words and deeds contradict,
Emotions and appearances clash,
**Rites** become burdensome adornments,
Music turns to licentious chaos,
Customs drown in worldly excess,
Praise and blame converge in court,
Thus the **true man** is abandoned and unused.

The later age is marked by:
Music’s licentiousness,
**Rites**’ corruption,
**Rites** becoming burdensome,
Emotions masked in pretense—
When the **true man** sees this,
He embraces simplicity,
Transforming customs.
If he races with the swift horse on foot,
Man cannot outpace the horse;
But mounted in a chariot,
The horse cannot outpace man."*

The **Dao/Way** is well-used when:

One rides upon the people’s strengths to achieve great deeds,
Leveraging what they can do to overcome what they cannot.

When the ruler gives them favorable timing,
The people repay him with wealth;
When the ruler treats them with **rites**,
The people repay him with death.

Thus, there are states in peril yet no secure ruler;
There are rulers in sorrow yet no contented ministers.

If **virtue/power** exceeds one’s station,
He is honored;
If **reward/luck** exceeds one’s **virtue/power**,
He is doomed.

**Virtue/power** is valued without limit,
**Righteousness** is sought without excess.
To hold **virtue/power** yet not be honored is to usurp one’s place;
To take **righteousness** yet not earn it is to steal wealth.

To be honored without **virtue/power** is doomed,
To take without **righteousness** is theft.

The **sage** finds contentment in poverty and joy in the **Dao/Way**,
Does not let desire harm life,
Does not let profit burden himself—
Thus he does not violate **righteousness** nor presumptuously seize what belongs to others.*

Without **virtue/power**, he is not honored;
Without ability, he is not appointed;
Without merit, he is not rewarded;
Without guilt, he is not punished.

In advancing men, he relies on **rites**;
In dismissing men, he relies on **righteousness**.

In the age of the petty man,
To advance him is like ascending to heaven;
To dismiss him is like descending into the abyss.
The **sage** in using men does not lightly advance nor easily dismiss;
The petty man fears advancement may not be lofty enough,
Regrets dismissal may not be deep enough.

To speak of the ancient is to criticize the present.
The present age is shallow and deceitful—
He loves those he wishes to live,
Hates those he wishes to die.

In judging horses, he errs by favoring the fat;
In selecting ministers, he errs by favoring the wealthy.
A plump pig fills the kitchen,
A gaunt one is heard in the temple.
Thus, the unworthy are appointed.

Though a horse be lean, if it is of fine breed,
It can still travel far;
Though a minister be poor, if he is loyal,
He can still accomplish great deeds.

The **noble man** discerns reality,
And does not trust slanderous words.
If a minister does not remonstrate when the ruler errs,
He is not a loyal servant;
If the ruler does not heed remonstrance,
He is not enlightened.

The one who does not remonstrate is called a **living corpse** (taking salary without duty),
The one who does not heed is a **tyrannical ruler**.

If the people sink in dissipation yet the ruler feels no concern,
He is not an **accomplished ruler**.
Thus, to uphold **rites** and die in hardship is the duty of a minister;
To endure cold and hunger is the kindness of a **benevolent father**.

If a ruler is thus, why fear the peril of the state?
If a minister is thus, why fear the lack of rank and reward?

To serve the great with the small is called **perverting** (changing nature);
To offend the great with the small is called **defying the Dao/Way**—
Though one may ascend to heaven beforehand,
He will surely plunge into the abyss thereafter.

Thus, in the village, elders are honored by seniority,
And the aged and destitute are never abandoned;
In court, rank determines precedence,
With distinctions between high and low.

Therefore, when the old and young uphold their proper **rites**,
Calamities have no cause to arise.

The exalted are honored because they are near the ruler;
The aged are revered because they are near kin;
The elders are respected because they are near brothers.
Thus, one honors the noble through the ruler,
Respects the aged through kinship,
And reveres the elder through **rites**.

Those born noble grow arrogant,
Those born rich grow extravagant.
Thus, few who are rich and noble,
Without the **Dao/Way** to reflect upon,
Can avoid wrongdoing.

Nobility without humility invites arrogance,
Wealth without restraint invites extravagance.
To dwell in nobility yet fail to uphold **virtue/power**,
And not commit wrongdoing—how rare!

To study without surfeit is the way to cultivate oneself;
To teach without weariness is the way to govern the people.
With wise mentors and virtuous friends,
Few would abandon **rites** and still not err.

To observe learning is to know the **Dao/Way**,
To receive instruction is to embrace all without distinction—
Entering a garden of orchids and immortals,
One must surely inhale their fragrant **qi**.

To recognize the worthy is **wisdom**,
To love the worthy is **benevolence**,
To honor **benevolence** is **righteousness**,
To revere the worthy is **rites**,
To delight in the worthy is **music**.

The ancients who governed the world well
Did nothing yet accomplished all—
Not creating affairs for the people,
Thus called **non-action**,
Yet following what benefits the people,
Thus nothing was left undone.

Therefore, governing the world requires **tolerance**—
To obtain this **tolerance** is to achieve **non-action** yet fulfill all;
To fail in this **tolerance** is to act and invite disaster.

The **tolerance** of governing the world is thus:
**Cautious**, as if crossing a great river in winter;
**Wary**, as if fearing injury from all sides;
**Dignified**, as if maintaining **rites**;
**Dissolved**, as if ice melting;
**Sincere**, as if uncarved wood;
**Mingled**, as if turbid water;
**Vast**, as if a valley.

This is the **tolerance** of governing the world.
**Tolerance** means encompassing all.

The **Dao/Way**’s appearance is thus:
The ruler governs with diligence, never slacking.

---
**Explanations of the above:**

**Cautious**, as if crossing a great river in winter—
Not daring to act rashly;

**Wary**, as if fearing injury from all sides—
Fearful of harm on every front;

**Dignified**, as if maintaining **rites**—
Humility, respect, and deference;

**Dissolved**, as if ice melting—
Not daring to hoard;

**Sincere**, as if uncarved wood—
Not daring to be artificially perfected;

**Mingled**, as if turbid water—
Not daring to seek clarity alone;

**Vast**, as if a valley—
Not daring to be full to overflowing.

To advance without daring to act,
To retreat without daring to lead—
To fear self-harm,
To cling to gentleness and not presume strength—
To humble oneself and honor others—
To diminish oneself and thus truly endure—
To be deficient and thus remain whole—
To endure filth and humiliation yet remain fresh—
To see insufficiency and thus not claim superiority.

The **Dao/Way** retreats yet leads,
Clings to weakness yet prevails,
Humbles itself yet rises above others,
Diminishes itself yet stands firm,
Lacks yet remains complete,
Endures filth yet remains pure,
Sees insufficiency yet is worthy.

The **Dao/Way** does nothing yet accomplishes all.

These are the inversions of the **Dao/Way**.
To place oneself last is to be first for others,
To diminish oneself is to gain from others.

Thus, the world reveres it without burden,
And the people willingly follow without exhaustion.

The **Dao/Way** of clarity—Laozi says:

All who study the **Dao/Way**,
If they can discern the distinction between Heaven and humanity,
And grasp the root of order and chaos,
If they purify the heart and clarify the mind to preserve it,
And observe its beginning and end,
Returning to the emptiness and stillness—
Then they may be said to have attained it.

Only those who embody purity,
And clearly return to emptiness and tranquility,
Can truly discern the distinction between Heaven and humanity,
And fathom the origins of all confusion.

The root of governance is **virtue/power** and righteousness;
Its branches are laws and statutes.
What gives birth to humanity is the root;
What does not is the branch.
Root and branch are one in essence,
Yet their dual nature is love and desire.

Prioritizing root over branch—this is the **君子** (junzi/noble one);
Prioritizing branch over root—this is the **小人** (xiao ren/commoner).

In the birth of humanity,
Spirit is the root,
Form is the branch.
Thus, in the **太上** (Great Sage) era, nurturing of spirit prevails,
And emotions and inclinations are regulated;
In the **末世** (Later Age), form is cherished,
And appetites and desires run wild.
Regulating desire clarifies the spirit,
Indulging desire harms the body.

When the spirit is clear and the body harmonious,
Root and branch mutually support,
Then one preserves the whole body and safeguards the spirit,
Without diminishing its true essence.
All methods of cultivation and discipline vary,
And inherent capacities differ—
Thus arise the distinctions between noble and common.

When laws arise,
They serve to aid **virtue/power** and righteousness;
If laws are exalted and **virtue/power** abandoned,
This is like valuing one’s cap and shoes
While neglecting one’s head and feet.

In governing a state,
One must first establish **virtue/power** and righteousness,
Then laws and decrees to bring order.
If laws and decrees are emphasized,
And **virtue/power** and righteousness neglected,
This is like neglecting one’s head and feet
While valuing one’s cap and shoes.

**Virtue/power** and righteousness are broad and profound.
To increase their height without broadening their foundation—this is ruin;
To extend their reach without deepening their root—this is destruction.

Thus, without great beams,
One cannot bear heavy burdens;
To bear burdens, none surpass **楝** (yuán, a strong tree);
To govern a state, none surpass **德** (virtue).

The ruler’s people are like the foundation of a city,
Or the roots of a tree.
Deep roots mean a firm foundation,
A thick base means a secure height.
Foundation and roots are like **德** (virtue) and the **Dao/Way**.

When a foundation is deep and broad,
Yet it is destroyed—this has never occurred.
When the **Dao/Way** is high and **德** (virtue) flourishes,
Yet the people do not revere it—this has never been heard.

Thus, what is not rooted in **德** (virtue) and the **Dao/Way**
Cannot be called a true principle;
What does not align with the ancient kings
Cannot be called the **Dao/Way**.

If one does not learn from antiquity,
And does not follow the **Dao/Way**,
Then it cannot be the law of the state.
To merely recite the methods of one deed or one merit,
This is not the universal **Dao/Way**.
To employ bizarre and deceptive techniques,
This is not the great **Dao/Way** of the world.

Laozi says:
The **Dao/Way** of governing people
Is like **造父** (Zào Fǔ, a legendary charioteer) guiding his four horses.
In the time of King **周穆王** (Zhōu Mù Wáng, King Mu of Zhou),
Zào Fǔ drove his horses.
With reins and bit in hand,
He aligned their movements with his own chest,
Gained their trust from within,
And harmonized their wills from without.
Thus he could take the **Dao/Way** and travel far,
With energy and strength to spare.
Advancing or retreating, turning or bending—
All went as he intended.
Truly, he mastered the technique!

Now, **权势** (authority and influence) are the ruler’s carriage;
Great ministers are the ruler’s four horses.
The body cannot abandon the carriage’s stability,
The hands cannot lose the horses’ hearts.
Thus, if the four horses are not attuned,
Zào Fǔ cannot guide them along the **Dao/Way**;
If ruler and ministers are not in harmony,
Even the **圣人** (sage) cannot govern with them.

To grasp the **Dao/Way** and guide with it—
Even mediocre talent can achieve this;
To clearly distinguish and demonstrate—
Even the wicked can be restrained.
Things come to completion while observing their changes,
Affairs unfold while responding to their transformations.
If the near are not disordered,
Then the far will naturally be governed.
Without employing contrived teachings,
Yet attaining the natural **Dao/Way**,
All actions will be without error.

With **造父**’s skill in guiding,
One need not fear the horses’ wild galloping;
With the **圣人**’s supreme governance,
One need not fear the people’s peril and ruin.

Laozi says:
All who practice the **Dao/Way**,
Must block the paths of evil and unseen forces,
And prevent what has not yet occurred.
They value not their own self-righteousness,
But rather their inability to act wrongly.
Thus it is said:
Do not allow what is desired to exist,
Or there will be no day without strife;
Do not allow what is to be taken to exist,
Or there will be no day without contention.
Thus, when desires are released,
The public **Dao/Way** prevails.

Those in excess are stopped by measure;
Those in deficiency are urged by use.
Thus, the world can be governed by one.

If one abandons official duties
And listens to praise and blame,
If one discards merit and labor
And relies on factions and cliques,
Then even with extraordinary talents,
One will not advance in office;
While the people’s customs decay in the state,
And ministers vie for power in court.

Thus, with the **Dao/Way** to guide people,
One is not guided by people;
Without the **Dao/Way**, one is constrained by people.
**舜** (Shùn, a virtuous ruler) as a commoner
Was revered by all under Heaven—
Because his **Dao/Way** endured,
And thus he could guide others.
**纣** (Zhòu, the tyrannical last king of Xia) as a ruler
Lost the loyalty of the four seas—
Because his **Dao/Way** perished,
And thus he was constrained by others.

Laozi says:
In governing a state, there are constants,
Yet benefiting the people is the root;
In governance and teaching, there is a **Dao/Way**,
Yet decrees and conduct follow antiquity.
If it benefits the people,
It need not strictly adhere to the old ways;
If it harmonizes with affairs,
It need not blindly follow custom.

Thus, the **圣人** adapts laws to the times,
And rituals to the customs.
Clothing and tools,
Each serve their purpose;
Laws and decrees,
Each suit their needs.
Thus, changing antiquity is not wrong,
Nor is following custom always commendable.

What is valued in governance
Is only fitting the times.
It is not a matter of frequent alteration.

Reading the books of ancient kings,
Is not as good as hearing their words;
Hearing their words,
Is not as good as understanding their intent.
To grasp their intent—
Their words cannot fully express it.

Thus, the **Dao/Way** that can be spoken of
Is not the constant **Dao/Way**;
The name that can be named
Is not the constant name.
To hold words as the **Dao/Way**,
Is to hold words while not possessing the **Dao/Way**;
To cling to stone as jade,
Is to cling to stone while not possessing jade.

Thus, the **Dao/Way** spoken of by the **圣人**
Is like metal and stone—
Once refined, it cannot be altered.
The principles of sound and rhythm
Are fixed and cannot be changed.
Governance is like the **琴瑟** (qin se, musical instruments);
When the melody ends, the tuning shifts.
Since the notes differ,
Thus it is fitting to change and adapt.
Laws, statutes, rituals, and music
Are the tools of governance,
Not the governance itself.

Thus, those who are skilled in technique
Cannot be consulted on the ultimate **Dao/Way**,
For they are bound by doctrine and awaken only to the common.

The ascent to Mount Langfeng is not within the reach of ordinary means,
Nor can those skilled in technique grasp the ultimate **Dao/Way**.

Laozi said: *"How few are the fixed laws under Heaven!*
When aligning with worldly affairs,
Attaining human principles,
Conforming to Heaven and Earth,
And scrutinizing spirits and gods,
Then governance may be rightly established.

To act conveniently in affairs,
To conform to humanity,
Is sufficient to govern the world.
Naturally harmonizing with the divine—
Why should there be fixed laws at all?

In ancient times, the Three Sovereigns ruled without decrees, yet the people obeyed;
The Five Emperors established decrees but had no punishments.
The Xia ruler did not break his word,
The Yin people took oaths,
The Zhou people made alliances.

The Three Sovereigns were Fu Xi, Shennong, and the Yellow Emperor.
The Five Emperors were Shaohao, Zhuantu, Gaoxin, Tang, and Yu.
The Three Kings were the Xia, Yu, Tang of Yin, and King Wen of Zhou.

In the distant past, without decrees the people obeyed;
In later times, harsh laws bred deceit.
Thus, *wuwei* transforms effortlessly,
While forced action governs with difficulty.
Oaths and alliances, once broken, invite punishment.

In the decadent age, men endure filth yet scorn humiliation,
Hoard gain yet lack shame.
Thus, laws and statutes are crafted according to customs,
Adjusting urgency and moderation.
Tools and implements adapt to the times,
Made suitable for their purpose.

Those bound by law cannot comprehend comprehensive governance;
Those constrained by ritual cannot respond to change.
Only those with singular insight and acute hearing
Can then wield the **Dao/Way** and act accordingly.

Those bound by law and text move stiffly and stagnant;
Those with singular perception and hearing
Do not stray from the **Dao/Way**.

To know the origin of laws is to adapt with the times;
To ignore the source of governance, though obedient, leads only to chaos.

Now, scholars cling to tradition,
Grasp texts and hold fast to doctrine,
Hoping to govern thereby—
As if without this, governance is impossible—
Like forcing a square peg into a round hole,
Seeking suitability is difficult indeed.

To cling to law and text without flexibility,
Believing oneself to govern,
Is like forcing a square into a circle—
How could it ever fit?

To preserve danger and govern chaos,
Requires wisdom beyond the ordinary,
Yet the **Dao/Way** precedes antiquity—
Even the foolish may grasp it.

Thus, laws untested the **圣人** does not employ;
Words unverified the wise ruler does not heed.
If matters demand it, even the foolish must act;
If principles conflict, even the virtuous must abandon them.

Wenzi asked: *"Where do laws derive their authority?"*
Laozi replied: *"Laws arise from righteousness,
Righteousness from what benefits the many.
When benefits align with the people’s heart,
This is the essence of governance.

Laws do not descend from Heaven,
Nor emerge from Earth—
They arise from humanity,
Returning to self to be rectified.
If one attains their root,
Disorder is averted before it begins.
Knowing the essentials,
One is not confounded by doubt;
If it exists within oneself,
One does not criticize others;
If it does not exist within oneself,
One does not blame those who establish it.

Those below must not neglect what is above;
What is forbidden to the people must not be practiced by the ruler.
Thus, when the ruler establishes laws,
First, they must govern themselves.

If restraint begins with the self,
Then the people will be restrained.
The county’s laws—
Laws that are not laws—
Are laws that govern the laws.
Once laws are established,
Those who conform are rewarded,
Those who deviate are punished—
Even the noble must not lightly receive reward,
Even the lowly must not suffer excessive punishment.

Those who violate the law,
Though virtuous, must be punished;
Those who conform,
Though unworthy, are guiltless.
Thus, public virtue prevails while private desires are checked.

In ancient times, officials were appointed
To restrain the people,
So they might not act wantonly;
The ruler was established
To restrain officials,
So they might not act unilaterally.
Laws and the **Dao/Way** restrain the ruler,
So he may not act arbitrarily.

When none act wantonly,
Then the **Dao/Way** prevails and principles are fulfilled.
Thus, return to simplicity and *wuwei*.

*Wuwei* does not mean inaction—
It means action arising from the self.

Establish rulers with officials,
So the sovereign does not act wantonly,
And the people do not act unilaterally.
Thus, the sovereign upholds proper conduct,
And the people do not pervert the law.
How difficult is it to govern the world?

Laozi said:
*The skilled in rewards spend little yet inspire many;
The skilled in punishments reduce crime while deterring vice;
The skilled in encouraging virtue use minimal means yet cultivate **德** (virtue/power);
The skilled in taking resources gather much yet breed no resentment.

Thus, the **圣人** follows what the people naturally favor to encourage virtue,
And what they naturally abhor to restrain vice.
Reward one, and the world rushes to emulate;
Punish one, and the world fears to transgress.
Thus, the greatest rewards cost nothing,
And the greatest punishments are never excessive.
The **圣人** governs with brevity yet achieves vast results—
This is what is meant.

This is how the **圣人** fulfills governance.

Laozi said:
*The **臣道** (subject’s path) is to speak truthfully when appropriate,
To lead in action,
To uphold duty and clarify distinctions,
Thus achieving success.

Thus, when ruler and subject follow distinct paths, governance is established;
When they share the same path, chaos ensues.
Each attaining their proper place,
Acting appropriately,
Then above and below can govern one another.

Thus, branches must not exceed the trunk,
Nor ends surpass the root—
Speech and action must balance in weight and measure.
When ruler and subject are clearly distinguished,
Then great and small do not overstep.

Those who wield authority hold little yet command much,
Stand in a small place yet influence broadly,
Adhere strictly to principle yet govern extensively.
A tree ten *zhang* in girth supports a thousand-*jin* house—
This is the power of leverage.
A five-*cun* hinge controls the opening and closing—
This is the power of position.

What the people must obey today,
Those who conform prosper,
Those who resist perish—
None dare disobey—
This is the power of conformity.

Issuing decrees and enforcing prohibitions
Relies on the multitude for authority.

Righteousness is not about exhausting the world’s benefits for the people—
It is about benefiting one, and the world follows."

The tyrant does not exhaust harm upon the realm—
He harms one, and the world rebels.
Thus, every action and decision must be weighed with care.

Be cautious in your actions and affections,
Grant rewards without partiality,
Inflict punishments without bias.

As Laozi says:
*"Bend an inch to straighten a foot,
A small crookedness yields great rectitude—
The sage acts thus."*

Yet rulers today judge their ministers
Not by their grand achievements or broad conduct,
But by trifling virtues—
Thus losing the way to true merit.

Thus, when a man has great virtue,
His minor flaws are overlooked;
When a man has great renown,
His small faults are forgotten.

Human nature is never without flaw—
What matters is his grand purpose.
Though he has small errors,
They should not be held against him.

If his grand purpose fails,
His common virtues are insignificant.
To speak of a man’s talents as flawless is folly—
One must not doubt his minor faults
But uphold his great use.

Common virtues and slanderous words
Are not to be trusted.
Thus, the overly meticulous fail in great success,
While those who scorn conduct are shunned by the multitude.

The grand in stature has loose restraints,
The vast in measure earns distant praise—
This is the way to judge ministers.

If one judges ministers thus,
He will not lose the right man.

As Laozi says:
*"From ancient times to now,
No one has ever been flawless in conduct."*

Thus, the noble does not demand perfection of one man.
No man is fully capable—
Measure his talents and assign him accordingly.

Square yet not rigid,
Sharp yet not jagged,
Straight yet not reckless,
Broad yet not vulgar—
In virtue, culture, and martial prowess,
Do not demand perfection of others.

Cultivate oneself with the Dao,
And do not demand of others—
This is easy to reward.

Cultivate oneself with the Dao,
And there will be no regrets.

To cultivate oneself without demanding of others,
Yet act in the world—
Is this not possible?

The jade of the Xia sovereigns cannot be without blemish,
The moon’s pearl cannot be without impurity—
Yet the world treasures them,
Not letting minor flaws obscure their great beauty.

Now, if one fixates on another’s shortcomings
And forgets their strengths,
Yet seeks merit in the world—
This is difficult indeed.

The jade of the Xia sovereigns and the moon’s pearl
Still have flaws—
How can the worthy and noble be entirely flawless?

Abandon their flaws,
Take their strengths—
Then no talent will be wasted.

The common view sees only
Lowly station, humble birth,
Humiliating tasks—
Yet fails to grasp their grand purpose.

Most men despise what is lowly and shameful;
The noble judges men by their grand purpose.

Thus, the way to judge a man:
When he rises, observe his actions—
This reveals his talent.
When he is wealthy, observe his generosity—
This reveals his virtue in aiding others.
When he is impoverished, observe his principles—
He does not act against righteousness.
When he is lowly, observe his conduct—
He does not dwell where the Dao is absent.
Observe his response to calamity—
This reveals his courage.
In adversity, true benevolence and bravery are seen.
When he rejoices, observe his restraint—
He does not overstep.
When entrusted with wealth, observe his benevolence—
He does not take what is not his.
When threatened, observe his resolve—
He would sacrifice himself for righteousness.

Thus, one may discern human nature.

If one aspect is present, he is capable;
If all seven are complete, he is a hero of the age.
By this, the worthy and foolish may be known,
And loyalty and sincerity revealed.

As Laozi says:
*"To bend is to seek straightening,
To be crooked is to seek rectitude.
Bend an inch to straighten a foot,
A small crookedness yields great rectitude—
The noble acts thus."*

All rivers flow toward the sea—
Those that do not are not valleys.
Men pursue different paths—
Those who do not return to the good are not noble.

Noble speech must be practicable,
Noble action must be rooted in benevolence and righteousness.

The noble’s errors,
Like eclipses of the sun and moon,
Do not obscure their brilliance.
Errors are seen by all,
But when corrected, all revere them.

Thus, the wise do not act recklessly,
The brave do not kill recklessly—
Choose what must be done,
Act according to ritual propriety,
So that deeds are accomplished and merit is assured,
And though death comes, his name endures.

Do what must be done,
Kill what must be killed—
Then success is achieved and name is established,
Praised by future generations.

Though one may be intelligent,
He must ground himself in benevolence and righteousness
Before he stands firm.

Intelligence and ability must align—
The sage uses benevolence and righteousness as his measure.
Those who meet the measure are noble;
Those who do not are petty.

Though the noble may die,
His name does not perish;
Though the petty may gain power,
Their guilt is not erased.

Thus, Yao and Shun did good,
And the worthy praised them;
Jie and Zhou did evil,
And their names were not established.

The names of good and evil endure—
Thus, the noble is cautious in avoiding evil deeds.

To hold the map of the world in one hand
And slit one’s own throat with the other—
Even the foolish would not do this,
For life is precious above all.

To be a guest of the "Lone Sovereign" at dawn
And a corpse cast aside by dusk—
These are the actions of the foolish and petty,
Not the conduct of the noble.

To die for one’s lord and parents is to return home—
Righteousness is heavier than life itself.

Thus, the greatest benefit in the world,
Compared to the self, is small;
The self, compared to righteousness, is light.

This is to take benevolence and righteousness as the measure.

Is this not a lament for the times lacking in benevolence and righteousness?

Thus, it is fitting to speak of the noble dying for righteousness,
And the petty dying for gain.

As Laozi says:
*"The completeness of virtue and the Dao
Is like the sun and moon—
Even barbarians cannot alter their course.
If one has the Dao,
Even in barbarian lands, it will prevail.
Without the Dao,
What use is the Central States?"*

If aspirations and actions align,
Then praise or blame depends on custom;
If intentions and conduct are balanced,
Then poverty or prosperity depends on fate.

If one’s deeds are complete in the world,
Then success is achieved;
If one’s efforts accord with the times,
Then name is established.

Thus, those who establish merit and name
Are simple in the world yet cautious in the times.
When the time arrives,
Even a breath’s delay is too late.

The **Dao/Way** of rescuing the fallen and the weak while establishing merit and name
Must seize its opportunity—no time for hesitation, not even a breath.

The ancient warriors who wielded arms
Did not covet land or treasure—
They sought to preserve life and quell disorder,
To remove harm from the people.

Men greedy for wealth and pleasure,
Devouring the world’s resources,
Disturb the masses—
None can rest in their homes.

When a **sage** arises in fury,
Punishing the strong and violent,
Restoring order to the chaotic age,
Removing the world’s calamities,
Turning turbidity into clarity,
Transforming peril into peace—
Then they must be cut down.

The **Red Sovereign** became a fire disaster,
So the **Yellow Sovereign** subdued him.
**Gonggong** brought water ruin,
So **Zhuanxu** slew him.

These two **sages** who rose in wrath
Did not seek to exalt their power,
To profit from the land,
To hoard wealth,
Or to spare the wicked—
They removed the world’s cruel afflictions,
Acting only when forced.

Teach the people with the **Dao/Way**,
Guide them with **virtue/power (de)**,
Yet if they do not heed,
Then confront them with martial might.
If they still refuse,
Then suppress them with arms.

To slaughter the innocent,
To sustain the unrighteous—
No greater harm exists.
To amass the world’s wealth,
To satisfy one man’s desires—
No deeper misfortune could be.

To indulge one man’s desires,
Yet prolong the nation’s suffering—
This is what **Heaven’s order (天伦)**** rejects.

When **Heaven** destroys them,
**Jie** and **Zhou** are the examples.

The purpose of establishing rulers
Is to restrain violence and chaos.
Yet now, they exploit the people’s strength,
Only to become the very wickedness—
So, even with wings attached,
How can they claim to be unchecked?

The man who breeds fish
Must remove the **bats (蝙蝠)** and **otters (獭)**,
The man who tends birds and beasts
Must eliminate the **wolves (豺)** and **wild dogs (狼)**.
How much more so the shepherd of the people?

Thus, the reason arms are raised.

No **Dao/Way** is greater than punishing the violent,
No **virtue/power (de)** surpasses calming the people.

When rulers are cruel and without the **Dao/Way**,
The masses are restless.
If they are not removed,
It is like letting ferocious beasts harm humanity,
Or raising fish and turtles to feed the **bats** and **otters**—
No greater crime exists.

Laozi said:
*"The **Dao/Way** of governing a state
Is to have no harsh decrees above,
No burdensome administration in office,
No deceptive conduct among the scholars,
No excessive craftsmanship among the artisans.

Their tasks are fulfilled without disturbance,
Their tools are complete without ornamentation—
This is the age of perfect governance."*

In times of disorder, it is not so.
Those who pursue **achievement (行)**,
Compete to exalt themselves in falsehood;
Those who uphold **ritual (礼)**,
Vainly display their hypocrisy.

Chariots and carriages are over-carved,
Utensils are excessively filigreed.
Seekers of wealth vie for scarce treasures,
Calling them precious;
Those who follow **music (音)**,
Chase after frivolous complications,
Claiming them urgent.

Debates become deceitful,
Cases drag on endlessly—
No benefit to governance,
Only harm to order—
This is the logic of a declining age.

Artisans craft bizarre tools,
Taking years to complete,
Yet they fail to meet practical needs.

Thus, **Shennong’s law** states:
*"If men of prime vigor do not till the fields,
The world will have those who suffer hunger;
If women of their years do not weave,
The world will have those who endure cold."*

Therefore, the **sage** plows in person,
His wife weaves by hand,
Setting an example for all.

In guiding the people,
The **Son of Heaven** plows the fields to encourage farming,
The **Empress** personally spins to inspire weaving—
How much more so the common folk,
Who grow slack in tilling and weaving,
Yet suffer from hunger and cold?

Precious things are not valued for their scarcity,
Useless objects are not prized for their weight.
Thus, the plowman does not strain,
For there is no way to sustain life;
The weaver does not exert herself fully,
For there is no way to clothe the body.

With neither surplus nor lack,
Each returns to their own needs.
When food and clothing are abundant,
Wickedness does not arise.
In peace and ease,
No calamity occurs—
The world is harmonious.

The wise have no schemes to employ,
The brave have no strength to display.
The wise act to avert danger,
The brave act to suppress violence.
When danger and violence cease,
What need is there for them?

Laozi said:
*"The **Dao/Way** of the **Conqueror** and **King**
Is to plan with strategy,
To scheme with foresight.
To act under the banner of righteousness—
Not to preserve his own state,
But to save the doomed.

He who saves the doomed—
Thus, when hearing of a ruler from another state
Who oppresses his people,
He raises his army and marches to their borders,
Accusing them of unrighteousness,
Exposing their misdeeds.

At the enemy’s outskirts,
He commands his generals:
'Do not cut down trees,
Do not dig up graves,
Do not destroy crops,
Do not burn stores,
Do not capture the people,
Do not seize livestock.'

Then he proclaims:
'That ruler
Defies **Heaven and Earth**,
Insults **spirits and gods**,
Judges cases unjustly,
Slaughters the innocent—
**Heaven** condemns him,
The people seek vengeance.

The army comes
To abolish unrighteousness
And enthrone the virtuous.

If any dare defy **Heaven’s order**,
Disturb the people—
Death to themselves,
Extinction of their kin.
If they heed the call as a family,
Reward the family;
If they heed as a neighborhood,
Reward the neighborhood;
If they heed as a village,
Reward the village;
If they heed as a county,
Reward the county.

Conquer the state,
Yet spare its people;
Depose the ruler,
Yet reform his governance;
Honor the talented,
Display the virtuous;
Support the orphaned and widowed,
Relieve the poor;
Free the imprisoned,
Reward the meritorious.

The common folk open their doors to welcome them,
Offer grain to store for them—
Only fearing they will not come.

A **righteous army**, arriving at the border,
Stops without battle.

Where a **righteous army** passes,
**Heaven and Earth** are made aware;
The **spirits and gods** are informed,
**Virtue/power (de)** extends to all things.
They address the multitudes with words of peace,
They question the enemy with charges of guilt—
Yet they do not easily wield weapons of slaughter.

The people rejoice and submit,
Only fearing they will not arrive.

**Yin Tang** and **Wen Wu**
Punished violence with **righteousness**.

An **unrighteous army**
Arrives amid corpses and rivers of blood,
Facing enemies in relentless combat.
Thus, those who fight for land
Cannot achieve kingship;
Those who fight for themselves
Cannot establish merit.

If one raises arms for others,
The multitude will aid them;
If one raises arms for themselves,
The multitude will abandon them.

Where the multitude is moved,
Even the weak will grow strong;
Where the multitude departs,
Even the great will perish.

「The unrighteous army, with its strength, dredges the weak,
relying on its greatness to encroach upon the small,
slaughters the innocent, and harms the virtuous.
Though it may butcher ten thousand in a city,
plunder lands a thousand leagues wide,
its fate shall be like that of Xiang Yu—
whose **Way (Dao)** dominated the seas and lands,
whose **qi** swallowed the cosmos,
whose might towered over hills and valleys—
yet was ultimately crushed by Gaozu.
This is because he did not follow the **Way (Dao)** and righteousness;
though great, he must fall;
though strong, he must perish.
Even the ambition for tyranny could not be fulfilled—
how much less could he achieve kingship?

**Laozi** said:
*"The highest **virtue/power (de)** in governance*
is to rule the state,
order the realm,
practice benevolence and righteousness,
bestow virtue and kindness,
establish just laws,
and block unrighteous paths.
Then the ministers will be loyal and attached,
the people will be harmonious and unified,
and the ruler and subjects will share a single heart,
their ministers working in concert.
The feudal lords will submit to his authority,
and the four quarters will embrace his **virtue/power (de)**.
He will rectify the halls of power,
and vanquish his enemies a thousand leagues away.
When he issues commands,
the world responds in unison—
this is the highest form of governance.

When a state is vast and its people numerous,
its ruler wise and its generals capable,
its wealth abundant and its armies strong,
its laws trusted and its orders clear,
and its enemies balanced in strength,
then even without battle or blade,
the foe will flee in disarray—
this is the second form of governance.

To know the suitability of the land,
to master the advantages of rugged passes,
to discern the flaws of oppressive rule,
and to study the movements of armies.
When blades clash and arrows fly,
to bear the dead and tend the wounded,
to see rivers of blood and corpses strewn across the fields—
this is the lowest form of governance.

To govern through **virtue/power (de)** is the highest;
to govern through law is the middle;
to govern through force is the lowest.

The victory or defeat of armies
lies in governance.
When governance overcomes the people,
the lower ranks submit to the upper—
then the army is strong.
When the people overcome governance,
the lower ranks rebel against the upper—
then the army is weak.

If **righteousness** is sufficient to embrace the people of the world,
if undertakings are sufficient to meet the world’s crises,
if appointments are sufficient to win the hearts of the worthy,
and if deliberations are sufficient to weigh the light and heavy—
then this is the **Way (Dao)** of the highest **virtue/power (de)**.

**Laozi** said:
*"The reason a state is strong*
is because of its resolve to die.
The reason it resolves to die*
is because of **righteousness**.
The reason **righteousness** prevails*
is because of its **威 (威)** [awe/authority].
Thus, to govern with words of **virtue/power (de)**,
and to unify with the might of **威 (威)** [awe/authority]—
this is called inevitable victory.
When **威 (威)** and **righteousness** are both wielded—
this is called inevitable strength.

When blades clash and stones rain down,
yet soldiers vie to strike first—
this is because rewards are trusted and punishments are clear.
If a ruler regards his subjects as his children,
his subjects will serve him as their father;
if a ruler regards his subjects as his younger brothers,
his subjects will serve him as their elder brothers.
If a ruler regards his subjects as his children,
he will surely reign over the four seas;
if his subjects serve him as their father,
he will surely govern the world.
If a ruler regards his subjects as his younger brothers,
then he will surely die for them;
if his subjects serve him as their elder brothers,
then he will surely perish for them.
Thus, when a ruler’s enemies are his own father, son, or brothers,
he cannot fight them.
If a ruler is thus,
then the world is like one family.
Ten thousand armies united in heart,
then even enemies are not feared,
and death is not shunned.
When **恩 (恩)** [favor] and **义 (义)** [righteousness] are felt,
men will regard death as returning home.
Who would see their father in peril
and grudge their own life to save him?
Who would see their ruler in danger
and fear death rather than remain loyal?

Thus, a **righteous ruler** refines his governance within,
to accumulate his **virtue/power (de)**;
and blocks the unrighteous without,
to display his **威 (威)** [awe/authority].
He observes their labor and ease,
to know their hunger and fullness.
When war is decided upon,
he treats death as returning home—
this is the favor bestowed upon them.
When **信 (信)** [trustworthiness] and **义 (义)** [righteousness] are established,
even death is not shunned.
When **恩 (恩)** [favor] and **威 (威)** [awe/authority] are both wielded,
no distance is beyond their reach.

Thus concludes the eleventh volume of the *通玄真经 (Tongxuan Zhenjing)* [The Authentic Scripture of the Perfected Way].」

The Authentic Scripture of the Perfected Way, Volume Twelve
*Commentary by Mo Xi Zi*, Part One: *On Rites*

Laozi said:

In the ancient age, the True Person was the sovereign of the Mysterious Old Dao.
Through his breathing, he harmonized the yin and yang,
and all things born under heaven naturally revered his **virtue/power (de)** for its gentleness and compliance.
At that time, **领理 (lingli)** [natural governance] and **隐密 (yinmi)** [subtle mystery] were spontaneously formed,
and the pure, unrefined essence remained undispersed,
while all things flourished in great ease.
Within, he concealed his bright **德 (virtue)**, without, he harmonized all things,
and the world knew no strife—
each followed its nature, without mutual harm,
thus all lived in perfect ease.

When the age declined to the time of **伏羲 (Fu Xi)**,
the people, dimly enlightened, all sought to abandon their childlike hearts
and awaken to the Dao of heaven and earth.
Their **virtue/power (de)** became complex and inconsistent.
Then the Eight Trigrams were first drawn,
to connect with the divine and to categorize all emotions.
Knotted ropes were made as nets and snares,
to hunt and fish.
The sovereign of the world, having abandoned childlike simplicity,
gradually lost his original Dao—
his **virtue/power (de)** became complex and inconsistent,
compared to the Mysterious Old time, making it an age of decline.

When **神农 (Shennong)** and **黄帝 (Huangdi)** took leadership of the world,
they regulated the four seasons,
harmonized the yin and yang,
and thus all the people refined their bodies and minds,
listened and observed with reverence—
yet governance remained harsh, not harmonious.

**伏羲 (Fu Xi)** created the **未耜 (weiji)** [plowshare],
to teach the people how to sow seeds;
**黄帝 (Huangdi)** invented the **轩冕 (xuānmìan)** [ceremonial robes],
and **窍领 (qiàolǐng)** [to regulate with subtle governance],
meaning to open the blocked and to harmonize the yin and yang,
to stop the rebellious **气 (qi)** and to cease the harming of things.
He established writing and contracts,
established laws and calendars,
regulated the four seasons,
harmonized the Five Phases,
fearing the loss of their essence—
he made all things refine their bodies,
and all focused their ears and eyes,
listening and observing to **德化 (dehua)** [virtuous transformation],
to uphold their sovereign.
Thus, he spoke of governance being unharmonious.

In the ages of **夏 (Xia)** and **殷 (Yin)**,
their desires extended to all things,
their cleverness was enticed by the external world,
and their true nature was lost.
When desires for things arose in **夏 (Xia)** and **殷 (Yin)**,
they were enticed by the external world;
when cleverness was indulged,
their true nature was lost within.
This spread to the **周室 (Zhou Dynasty)**,
where **浇 (Jiao)** and **醇 (chún)** [debauched and refined] diluted the pure,
abandoned the Dao for falsehood,
took **险 (xian)** [deceitful] **德 (virtue)** as conduct,
and gave rise to cleverness and cunning.
They schemed to imitate the sages,
adorned falsehood to please the crowd,
polished poetry and scriptures
to purchase fame and reputation.
Each sought to display their cleverness,
to use falsehood to fit into the world,
and thus lost the true essence of the great Dao.
Hence, the world has long been losing its true nature,
and the decline has been gradual for ages.

By the time of the **周室 (Zhou Dynasty)**,
**Dao/Way (Dao)** and **virtue/power (de)** were completely lost.
**浇 (Jiao)** [debauched] and **酿 (niàng)** [refined] grew even more extreme,
taking falsehood and deceit as the Dao,
taking cleverness and cunning as **贤 (xian)** [virtuous excellence],
displaying the Dao and boasting of **德 (virtue)**,
purchasing fame and seeking reputation—
thus losing the true essence of the great Dao.
The decline of the age began from **Fu Xi (伏羲)** and **Xuānmìan (轩冕)**,
counting thousands of years—
thus it is said that the decline has been long,
not that it suddenly began in the **周室 (Zhou Dynasty)**.

Therefore, the study of the **至人 (zhiren)** [perfected/sage] is to return to the **无 (wu)** [non-being/emptiness] of true nature,
and to wander the mind in the **虚 (xu)** [void].
The study of the common world, however,
is to **擢德 (zhuóde)** [flaunt virtue] and **攓性 (nièxìng)** [constrain nature],
to torment the five **藏 (zang)** [organs],
to act rashly beyond wisdom,
and thus to **譊 (yǒu)** [create discord] with their cleverness and cunning.
They clamor and shout for fame in the world—
this is what the perfected do not do.
**擢德 (zhuóde)** [flaunting virtue] is self-promotion,
**攓性 (nièxìng)** [constraining nature] is to sever life.
If the perfected are steadfast in the meaning of death and life,
and comprehend the logic of honor and disgrace,
then even if the whole world praises them, they are not further encouraged,
and even if the whole world criticizes them, they are not discouraged.
This is the essence of attaining the **至道 (zhiDao)** [perfected Dao/Way].

The **至人 (zhiren)** [perfected/sage] exist in every age,
but they are rare.
One in ten thousand is already considered many.
The study of the perfected is to return to the **华伪 (huapí)** [false and ornate] realm of nature,
and to bathe in the divine within the **虚静 (xūjìng)** [void and tranquil] domain.
They do not boast of their **德 (virtue)**,
nor do they flaunt their talents—
death and life bring no change to them,
and harm and benefit do not affect their hearts.
Now, the scholars of the world
abandon true nature to seek enlightenment,
pursue fame to elevate their reputation—
thus, the rise of deceit and decline of loyalty,
and the concealment of the true and upright.
If one seeks governance of the world
or personal peace and comfort,
it cannot be attained.

Laozi said:

In ancient times, those who ruled wore their hair unbound and had no **卷领 (juǎnlǐng)** [collared garments],
yet they governed all under heaven.
Their **德 (virtue)** was born without killing,
and they gave without taking.
Though the people did not wear their ceremonial robes,
they all shared the same heart of **德 (virtue)**.
At that time, yin and yang were in harmony,
and all things thrived and grew.
The nests of flying birds could be approached without alarm,
and wild beasts could be tethered and followed.
The sovereign of the Mysterious Old Dao
did not wear a hat or comb his hair,
but let his hair flow unbound,
and thus governed all under heaven.
He allowed the people to live in peace,
did not take their benefits—
thus the four seasons were harmonious,
and all things followed their nature.
Hence, the birds nested without fear,
and the beasts were tethered without alarm.
This was the peak of **德 (virtue)**.

When the age declined,
birds, beasts, insects, and serpents all became harm to the people,
so iron was cast and blades were forged to repel their dangers.
Thus, when the people were pressed by their difficulties,
they sought convenience and ease,
and when faced with calamities,
they took precautions.
Each used their cleverness to avoid what harmed them,
and to seek what benefited them.
Customs were no longer fixed,
and tools could not be relied upon as before—
thus the laws of the ancient kings were subject to change.
Hence it is said:
*"The nameable can be named,
but it is not the constant name."*
In the decline of the age,
things were no longer pure and singular,
but each gave rise to strange emotions,
harming one another in succession.
Cleverness and deceit were used to deceive,
and tools were made for defense,
to avoid what harmed and to seek what benefited.
Thus the ancient kings changed their laws,
not because there was a fixed standard—
hence, laws have no constant name.

The Five Emperors held different **Dao/Way (Dao)**,
yet their **德 (virtue)** covered the world;
the Three Kings had distinct affairs,
yet their names endured in posterity—
all adapted to the times.
It is like the **师旷 (Shiguang)** [Master Musician] tuning the Five Notes,
where the adjustments rise and fall without fixed measurement,
yet all are in harmony.
Thus, those who understand the emotions of music
can compose the sounds.

The root principle lies within,
and though the Five Emperors and Three Kings differed in their laws,
they were like Shiguang tuning the Five Notes,
mastering the essence of music,
and knowing the essence of harmony.

The Five Notes take *gong* as their root,
and the myriad households take the sovereign as their root.
Without a central root, there is chaos—
thus, a root is established to unify them.

Those who understand the use of compasses, squares, hooks, and cords
can govern the people.

Hence, when the ancient kings’ institutions were no longer fitting,
they were discarded;
when later ages’ affairs were good,
they were recorded.

Thus, the Sage’s establishment of rites and music
was not constrained by rites and music,
those who govern things are not constrained by things,
those who establish laws are not constrained by laws—
to establish rites contrary to rites,
to establish music contrary to music,
is to go against the Dao.

To let things be governed by things is tolerable;
to let laws be established contrary to laws
is to exceed proper governance.

Hence it is said:
*The Dao that can be spoken of is not the constant Dao.*

Laozi said:
*The ancient Sage-Kings looked upward to the patterns of Heaven,
looked downward to the measures of Earth,
and took their laws from humanity.
They harmonized the yin and yang qi,
balanced the seasons’ rhythms,
and observed the suitability of highlands, lowlands, waters, marshes, and fertile plains.
They established affairs and generated wealth according to elevation and terrain,
eliminating the calamities of hunger and cold,
and warding off the disasters of sickness and misfortune.
Within, they received human affairs to establish rites and music,
practiced the Way of benevolence and righteousness to govern human relations;
they arranged the natures of metal, wood, water, fire, and earth
to establish filial piety and form the family;
they listened to the clear and turbid tones of the Five Notes,
and the generative cycles of the Six *Lü*,
to establish the proper relations of ruler and subject and form the state;
they observed the sequence of the four seasons—beginning, middle, and end—
to establish the order of seniority and juniority and form the bureaucracy;
they divided the land into regions and governed the states;
they established the Grand Academy to teach—
these are the fundamental principles of governance.

These are the six fundamental principles for governing the world.
When the Dao is attained, they are implemented;
when the Dao is lost, they are abandoned.

Things have never existed without tension and relaxation,
without flourishing and decline—
only the Sage can flourish without decline.

When the Sage first composed music,
it was to return the spirit and suppress licentiousness,
restoring the natural heart.
But when it declined,
it became excessive and unreturning,
indulging in sensuality and disregarding proper law,
spreading to later ages until kingdoms fell.

When they established writing,
it was to organize all affairs,
so the foolish would not forget,
and the wise would record events;
but when it declined,
it became a tool for deceit,
excusing the guilty and condemning the innocent.

When they established hunting grounds,
it was to prepare for ancestral temples
and to train soldiers against unforeseen dangers;
but when it declined,
they indulged in hunting and falconry,
wasting the people’s time
and exhausting their strength in leather and fur.

When they honored the worthy,
it was to spread teaching and justice,
placing the capable in office,
the skilled in their posts,
and letting their virtue descend to the people,
so all under Heaven cherished their virtue;
but when it declined,
they formed cliques and factions,
each promoting their own,
abandoning the public for private gain,
with outsiders and insiders aiding one another,
the unworthy in office,
and the worthy in hiding.

The Dao of Heaven and Earth:
when pushed to extremes, it reverses;
when increased, it diminishes.
Thus, the Sage corrects flaws and changes institutions,
and when matters conclude, they are renewed.
Their excellence lies in harmony,
their failure in expedience.

The Sage’s Way states:
Without cultivating rites and righteousness,
there is no establishment of propriety and shame—
and without propriety and shame, the people cannot be governed.
Without knowing rites and righteousness,
laws cannot be properly enforced.
Without elevating the good and abolishing the base,
one cannot turn toward rites and righteousness.
Without law, governance is impossible,
and without knowing rites and righteousness,
one cannot implement law.

Law can punish the unfilial,
but cannot make people filial;
it can punish thieves,
but cannot make people virtuous.

When the Sage-King rules above,
he clearly demonstrates good and evil to guide the people,
praises the worthy and censures the unworthy,
advances the worthy and demotes the unworthy,
and though punishments are set,
rites and virtue are cultivated,
and the worthy are entrusted with governance.

Thus, the highest in the world are the Three Dukes,
the highest in a region are the Nine Ministers,
the highest in a state are the Twenty-Seven Great Officers,
and the highest in a village are the Eighty-One Commoners.

Officials are appointed and duties divided,
the worthy are entrusted, and the corrupt removed,
to serve as the king’s limbs and senses,
harmonizing the myriad households
and pacifying the four quarters.

One who surpasses ten thousand is called *ying* (heroic),
one who surpasses a thousand is called *jun* (exceptional),
one who surpasses a hundred is called *jie* (outstanding),
one who surpasses ten is called *hao* (talented).

To understand the Dao of Heaven and Earth,
to grasp the principles of human nature,
to be vast enough to contain the many,
to be benevolent enough to embrace the distant,
to be wise enough to know expedience—
this is the *ying* person.

To have virtue sufficient for teaching,
conduct sufficient to uphold righteousness,
trust sufficient to win the people,
clarity sufficient to illuminate below—
this is the *jun* person.

To have conduct worthy of emulation,
wisdom sufficient to resolve doubts,
trust sufficient to keep agreements,
integrity sufficient to distribute wealth fairly,
actions worthy of imitation,
words worthy of following—
this is the *jie* person.

To fulfill one’s duties without neglect,
to act righteously without cliquishness,
to face danger without cowardice,
to reject gain without greed—
this is the *hao* person.

This is the method of selecting talent,
the means of knowing people.
If one possesses even one of these,
they can be used.
If they possess them all,
they are a Sage.

The heroic, exceptional, outstanding, and talented
are placed according to their greatness and smallness,
from root to branch,
with the heavy governing the light,
the ruler setting the tone and the people responding in harmony,
so all under Heaven unite in one heart,
turning away from greed and vulgarity,
toward benevolence and righteousness—
their influence on the people
is like the wind swaying the grass.

Now, if the unworthy rule over the worthy,
even severe punishments cannot restrain their deceit.
The weak cannot control the strong,
the small cannot govern the great—
this is the nature of Heaven and Earth.

Thus, the Sage selects the worthy to establish merit,
while the unworthy ruler promotes those like themselves.
By observing whom they promote,
one can discern order or chaos;
by examining their factions,
one can judge the worthy from the unworthy.

The Sage’s use of people
matches their talents,
and when officials do not overstep their ranks,
the world is governed.

The Master Laozi says:

Those who make **rites** carve and polish human nature,
straightening and restraining their feelings—
though the eyes may covet them,
they are checked by measure.
Though the heart may delight in them,
they are moderated by **rites**—
circumambulating, bowing low,
flesh stiff with hunger yet not eating,
