wine offered yet not drinking,
the body bound in form,
the mind troubled, its voice imprisoned.
Their **virtue** restrains the harmony of yin and yang,
forcing the natural inclinations of life and death—
thus, they become sorrowful men all their days.

Why so? They do not root out the source of what is desired,
yet they restrain what is desired;
they do not trace the origin of what brings delight,
yet they prevent what brings delight—
this is like enclosing a beast without blocking its den,
restraining its wild heart,
or damning the flow of a river while trying to stop it with one’s hand.
Hence it is said:
*"Open its mouth, aid its work—
yet its life will never be saved."*

When the flaws of **rites** and **music** arise,
and the artifice of feelings is exposed,
they do not root out the absence of desire,
yet they restrict what is desired;
they do not trace the absence of delight,
yet they prevent what is delighted in—
then desire cannot be stopped,
delight cannot be restrained.
Delight, when unrestrained, leads to excess;
**rites**, when not properly observed, lead to neglect.
And from opening the pen to release the beasts,
from damning the river to halt its flow,
once truth is lost, all are bewildered and cannot return.

Now, **rites** are meant to curb feelings and close desires,
to defend oneself with **righteousness**—
though the feelings and heart may choke,
and desires may burst forth,
the body and nature hunger and thirst,
yet they endure what cannot be avoided—
thus, none can complete their natural lifespan.

To uphold **rites** is to be bound and constrained,
forced by feelings and nature,
all striving to endure—
thus, none complete their natural lifespan.

**Rites** do not make people not desire,
but they restrain desire;
**music** does not make people not delight,
but it restrains delight.

If one makes all under Heaven fear punishment
and thus dare not steal or rob,
is it not better to make them have no thieving heart?
If one does not open their cravings,
what need is there for dikes and walls?
If one does not amass wealth and goods,
there will be no thieves to steal or rob.

Thus, when one knows what is truly useless,
even the greedy will abandon it;
when one does not know what is truly useful,
the virtuous cannot yield to it.

That which causes the ruin of states,
death at the hands of others,
and laughter from all under Heaven—
has it not been desire?
The harm of desire is so great.

Who knows the uselessness of summer furs
and winter fans
will see all things turn to dust and filth.
Who knows the uselessness of all things
will see them become dung and soil.

Hence the Way teaches:
*"The harm lies with those who have desire."*

Thus, to cool boiling water with a ladle,
the boiling only worsens—
but to know its root,
one need only remove the fire.
None know the Way better than those who have no desire;
to stop the boiling lies in removing the kindling.

Laozi says:
*"To follow one’s nature and act is called the Dao;
to attain one’s natural essence is called **virtue**.*

Once nature is lost,
then **righteousness** and **benevolence** are prized—
but when **righteousness** and **benevolence** stand,
the Dao and **virtue** are abandoned.
Purity is scattered, **rites** and **music** take their place—
distinctions arise, the people are dazzled,
and jade and pearls are treasured—
all under Heaven contend.

When the Dao and **virtue** are gone,
**righteousness** and **benevolence** are insufficient to restrain their feelings,
**rites** and **music** are insufficient to curb their desires—
one man upholds them, yet a hundred men contend—
then chaos ensues.

Now, **rites** are meant to distinguish high and low, noble and base;
**righteousness** is meant to harmonize the relations of
ruler and subject, father and son, elder and younger brothers,
husband and wife, and all human interactions.

In the **end times**, **rites** become mere etiquette,
and **righteousness** is practiced through charity—
rulers and subjects criticize each other,
and kin bear grudges.

Thus, when water accumulates, it breeds devouring insects;
when earth accumulates, it spawns self-consuming hunters—
when **rites** and **music** are merely decorative,
deceit and falsehood arise.
Like one who fans a fire to burn oneself,
or throws oneself into water to drown.

Moreover, **rites** and **righteousness** originally have no nature of harming others;
water and fire, too, have no intent to harm—
but when they are used contrary to their proper Way,
they bring disaster;
when they are used in accord with their proper Way,
they bring blessing.

In the **end times**, governance does not accumulate means for nurturing life,
but pours out the pure and scatters the genuine—
it confuses the people,
taking what is clear for what is turbid,
and life and nature are scattered.
All strive to create chaos,
faith and sincerity decay,
people lose their nature—
law contradicts **righteousness**, actions oppose profit,
the poor and rich vie with each other,
and rulers and commoners are no longer distinguishable.

When there is excess, there is yielding;
when there is lack, there is contention—
yielding gives rise to **rites** and **righteousness**,
contention spawns violence and disorder.
Thus, many desires lead to unchecked actions,
and seeking indulgence brings endless strife.

Hence, in times of order, the petty man upholds propriety,
and profit cannot tempt him;
in times of chaos, the noble man becomes deceitful,
and law cannot restrain him.

Laozi says:
*"The rulers of the **end times**,
drill through mountains for stones,
pluck gold and jade,
dig up tortoises and turtles,
melt copper and iron—
yet all things do not flourish.

They hollow out wombs and burn the fields,
upset nests and destroy eggs—
phoenixes do not fly,
qilin do not roam.

They pile wood to build high platforms,
burn forests for hunting,
drain ponds for fishing,
heap earth to make artificial hills,
dig wells for drinking,
dredge rivers to make pools,
build walls for defense—
they trap beasts as livestock—
then yin and yang are disrupted,
the four seasons lose their order,
thunder and lightning are shattered,
hail and frost bring harm—
all things wither and die,
half of them perish,
and grasses and trees dry up in summer.*

The rulers of the **end times**,
split rocks to find jade,
dig clamshells for pearls,
burn the fields and drain the ponds,
hollow out wombs and destroy eggs—
they indulge the desires of their nature,
satisfy the pleasures of their ears and eyes—
thus, they bring about discord,
and all things lose their proper place.
Thunder and lightning strike in terror,
hail and frost bring ruin—
not the fault of all the lands,
but the crime of one man alone.

Hence, Xia Jie and Shang Zhou,
though their cities were not unhigh,
their jade and silk not few,
their allure not lacking,
their granaries not empty—
suddenly their ancestral lines were cut off,
their states ruined,
they died at the hands of others,
became the laughingstock of all under Heaven—
how painful was it not?

The **Way of Heaven** is not distant—it was in the **Xia** and **Shang** dynasties.

The Three Rivers ceased to flow.
The mountains and valleys were divided,
giving them boundaries and limits—
when the **Zhou** declined, the Three Rivers dried up.
When the **Jin** weakened, the **Sandu** and **Lu** lands collapsed.

When a state is about to rise,
it must have auspicious omens;
when a state is about to fall,
it must have **demonic portents** and calamities.

Counting the people’s numbers,
establishing ranks and divisions,
setting up traps and obstacles as defenses,
regulating the colors of clothing,
distinguishing noble and ignoble,
differentiating the worthy from the unworthy,
and enacting rewards and punishments—
then wars arise and contentious strife is born,
the innocent are slaughtered,
the guilty are executed without crime,
and thus, decline begins.

The **Heaven and Earth** cannot help but shift,
and the people cannot help but suffer disaster and peril.

**Laozi** said:
*"When the world is about to lose its **Dao/Way** and its life,
it is like the rise of the **yin** qi—
the ruler is dark and unclear,
the **Dao/Way** is abandoned and not followed,
**virtue/power** is extinguished and not displayed.
Actions go against **Heaven**,
commands defy the four seasons.
Spring and autumn shrink their harmony,
**Heaven and Earth** remove their **virtue/power**,
the sovereign sits uneasily in his position,
the ministers withdraw in seclusion and speak no more,
the officials twist the ruler’s intentions to ruin the norms,
they sever ties with kin to preserve their own safety,
wicked men flatter while secretly plotting,
they load the arrogant ruler with his own chaos,
and people act to fulfill his schemes.
Therefore, the ruler and his ministers are estranged and not affectionate,
kin are distant and not attached.
Fields yield no sprouts,
roads have no leisurely travelers,
stockpiled gold becomes tarnished,
walls crumble without repair,
tortoiseshells have no inner lining,
divination stalks are used daily,
and the world is divided yet made into one household.*

The feudal lords enact laws that differ,
customs become perverse,
they uproot their foundations and abandon their roots,
engrave the Five Punishments like carvings,
quibbling over the minutest details,
they slaughter and reap their people, taking half of them,
raise armies for destruction,
assault cities with indiscriminate killing,
overthrow the high and secure the low,
use heavy chariots,
build tall ramparts,
dismantle battle formations,
force soldiers into death paths,
confront stern enemies,
with ten attacks and one retreat,
their reputation may be grand,
they conquer lands and hold territories,
but the number of fallen corpses reaches tens of thousands,
and those who die from hunger and cold in their old age cannot be counted.

From this point onward,
the world has never known peace in its **Dao/Way** and life,
nor joy in its customs."*

The degenerate rulers of the end times,
relying on their might and expanding their lands,
exhausting the lives of the common folk,
quibbling over trifles,
with corpses piled and blood flowing without respite,
until this very day—
how could this be the **Dao/Way**?

The worthy and sagely rise in vigor,
upholding **virtue/power** and supplementing with **ren** and **yi**,
drawing near to enlighten the wise,
winning over the distant with their **virtue/power**,
unifying the world into one.
Their descendants succeed in assisting one another,
banishing the origins of slanderous flatterers,
silencing unproven arguments,
abolishing harsh laws,
removing burdensome regulations,
shutting out idle gossip,
sealing the gates of factions,
dulling sharp intellects,
following the Great Constant,
demolishing superficial forms,
discarding cleverness,
greatly harmonizing the obscure,
allowing all things to return to their roots.

The Sage does not create the times;
when the times arrive, they do not fail—
thus they cannot be interrupted.
The Sage perceives the moment,
seizes the time,
rises in vigor,
rectifying Heaven and Earth,
extinguishing brutality,
greatly sheltering the common folk.

When Heaven bestows great responsibility upon one,
they cannot be interrupted—
words must have their master.

Laozi says:
*"The depth of the Ying River,
ten *ren* deep yet untainted by dust,
gold and stone within reveal their forms without—
not because it is not deep and pure.
Turtles, fish, dragons, and serpents do not dwell there.
For water too pure, fish will not swim;
a man too scrutinizing, none will follow him.
No grains grow on barren rock,
no deer roam the bald mountain—
there is no shelter.
Thus, governing with harshness as scrutiny,
severity as clarity,
cruelty as loyalty,
and scheming as merit—
such methods are like stretching leather too wide,
the path to great ruin and splitting.
Governing with poetry leads to defeat,
stretching leather too wide leads to splitting.
When governance is stifling, the people are sincere;
when governance is probing, the people are depleted.
Excessive scrutiny above brings unrest below."*

Laozi says:
*"To govern a state, to employ strategy in war,
first establish an unbeatable governance,
then seek victory over the enemy—
to attack disorder with what is untamed
is like answering fire with fire, water with water.
The same cannot govern itself.
Thus, the strange is made effective:
strange stillness as agitation,
strange order as chaos,
strange abundance as hunger,
strange ease as toil,
strange rectitude clashing like water, fire, metal, wood—
where can one not prevail?

Thus, when **virtue/power** is balanced, the many defeat the few;
when strength is equal, the wise overcome the foolish;
when wisdom is matched, the calculated ensnare the incalculable.
This shows how rectitude and the strange attack each other,
the worthy and foolish contend.
Though great gains may be equal,
those who lead the multitude prevail;
though strength may be matched,
wisdom subdues the foolish;
though wisdom may be equal,
the cunning ensnare the uncunning.
Strategy and calculation—
these are all arts of mutual conquest,
alternately capturing and controlling,
yet not the **Dao/Way**—
this is the art of expedience."

The *Tongxuan Zhenjing* concludes its twelfth scroll.
